Shell, Charles Franks, and the\r\nOnline Distributed Proofreading Team\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE SHEIK\r\n\r\nA Novel\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nby E. M. HULL\r\n\r\n1921\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I\r\n\r\n\r\n"Are you coming in to watch the dancing, Lady Conway?"\r\n\r\n"I most decidedly am not. I thoroughly disapprove of the expedition of\r\nwhich this dance is the inauguration. I consider that even by\r\ncontemplating such a tour alone into the desert with no chaperon or\r\nattendant of her own sex, with only native camel drivers and servants,\r\nDiana Mayo is behaving with a recklessness and impropriety that is\r\ncalculated to cast a slur not only on her own reputation, but also on\r\nthe prestige of her country. I blush to think of it. We English cannot\r\nbe too careful of our behavior abroad. No opportunity is slight enough\r\nfor our continental neighbours to cast stones, and this opportunity is\r\nvery far from being slight. It is the maddest piece of unprincipled\r\nfolly I have ever heard of."\r\n\r\n"Oh, come, Lady Conway! It\'s not quite so bad as all that. It is\r\ncertainly unconventional and--er--probably not quite wise, but remember\r\nMiss Mayo\'s unusual upbringing----"\r\n\r\n"I am not forgetting her unusual upbringing," interrupted Lady Conway.\r\n"It has been deplorable. But nothing can excuse this scandalous\r\nescapade. I knew her mother years ago, and I took it upon myself to\r\nexpostulate both with Diana and her brother, but Sir Aubrey is hedged\r\naround with an egotistical complacency that would defy a pickaxe to\r\npenetrate. According to him a Mayo is beyond criticism, and his\r\nsister\'s reputation her own to deal with. The girl herself seemed,\r\nfrankly, not to understand the seriousness of her position, and was\r\nvery flippant and not a little rude. I wash my hands of the whole\r\naffair, and will certainly not countenance to-night\'s entertainment by\r\nappearing at it. I have already warned the manager that if the noise is\r\nkept up beyond a reasonable hour I shall leave the hotel to-morrow."\r\nAnd, drawing her wrap around her with a little shudder, Lady Conway\r\nstalked majestically across the wide verandah of the Biskra Hotel.\r\n\r\nThe two men left standing by the open French window that led into the\r\nhotel ballroom looked at each other and smiled.\r\n\r\n"Some peroration," said one with a marked American accent. "That\'s the\r\nway scandal\'s made, I guess."\r\n\r\n"Scandal be hanged! There\'s never been a breath of scandal attached to\r\nDiana Mayo\'s name. I\'ve known the child since she was a baby. Rum\r\nlittle cuss she was, too. Confound that old woman! She would wreck the\r\nreputation of the Archangel Gabriel if he came down to earth, let alone\r\nthat of a mere human girl."\r\n\r\n"Not a very human girl," laughed the American. "She was sure meant for\r\na boy and changed at the last moment. She looks like a boy in\r\npetticoats, a damned pretty boy--and a damned haughty one," he added,\r\nchuckling. "I overheard her this morning, in the garden, making\r\nmincemeat of a French officer."\r\n\r\nThe Englishman laughed.\r\n\r\n"Been making love to her, I expect. A thing she does not understand and\r\nwon\'t tolerate. She\'s the coldest little fish in the world, without an\r\nidea in her head beyond sport and travel. Clever, though, and plucky as\r\nthey are made. I don\'t think she knows the meaning of the word fear."\r\n\r\n"There\'s a queer streak in the family, isn\'t there? I heard somebody\r\nyapping about it the other night. Father was mad and blew his brains\r\nout, so I was told."\r\n\r\nThe Englishman shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n"You can call it mad, if you like," he said slowly. "I live near the\r\nMayos\' in England, and happen to know the story. Sir John Mayo was\r\npassionately devoted to his wife; after twenty years of married life\r\nthey were still lovers. Then this girl was born, and the mother died.\r\nTwo hours afterwards her husband shot himself, leaving the baby in the\r\nsole care of her brother, who was just nineteen, and as lazy and as\r\nselfish then as he is now. The problem of bringing up a girl child was\r\ntoo much trouble to be solved, so he settled the difficulty by treating\r\nher as if she was a boy. The result is what you see."\r\n\r\nThey moved nearer to the open window, looking into the brilliantly lit\r\nballroom, already filled with gaily chattering people. On a slightly\r\nraised platform at one end of the room the host and hostess were\r\nreceiving their guests. The brother and sister were singularly unlike.\r\nSir Aubrey Mayo was very tall and thin, the pallor of his face\r\naccentuated by the blackness of his smoothly brushed hair and heavy\r\nblack moustache. His attitude was a mixture of well-bred courtesy and\r\nlanguid boredom. He seemed too tired even to keep the single eye-glass\r\nthat he wore in position, for it dropped continually. By contrast the\r\ngirl at his side appeared vividly alive. She was only of medium height\r\nand very slender, standing erect with the easy, vigorous carriage of an\r\nathletic boy, her small head poised proudly. Her scornful mouth and\r\nfirm chin showed plainly an obstinate determination, and her deep blue\r\neyes were unusually clear and steady. The long, curling black lashes\r\nthat shaded her eyes and the dark eyebrows were a foil to the thick\r\ncrop of loose, red-gold curls that she wore short, clubbed about her\r\nears.\r\n\r\n"The result is worth seeing," said the American admiringly, referring\r\nto his companion\'s last remark.\r\n\r\nA third and younger man joined them.\r\n\r\n"Hallo, Arbuthnot. You\'re late. The divinity is ten deep in would-be\r\npartners already."\r\n\r\nA dull red crept into the young man\'s face, and he jerked his head\r\nangrily.\r\n\r\n"I got waylaid by Lady Conway--poisonous old woman! She had a great\r\ndeal to say on the subject of Miss Mayo and her trip. She ought to be\r\ngagged. I thought she was going on talking all night, so I fairly\r\nbolted in the end. All the same, I agree with her on one point. Why\r\ncan\'t that lazy ass Mayo go with his sister?"\r\n\r\nNobody seemed to be able to give an answer. The band had begun playing,\r\nand the floor was covered with laughing, talking couples.\r\n\r\nSir Aubrey Mayo had moved away, and his sister was left standing with\r\nseveral men, who waited, programme in hand, but she waved them away\r\nwith a little smile and a resolute shake of her head.\r\n\r\n"Things seem to be getting a hustle on," said the American.\r\n\r\n"Are you going to try your luck?" asked the elder of the two\r\nEnglishmen.\r\n\r\nThe American bit the end off a cigar with a little smile.\r\n\r\n"I sure am not. The haughty young lady turned me down as a dancer very\r\nearly in our acquaintance. I don\'t blame her," he added, with a rueful\r\nlaugh, "but her extreme candour still rankles. She told me quite\r\nplainly that she had no use for an American who could neither ride nor\r\ndance. I did intimate to her, very gently, that there were a few little\r\nopenings in the States for men beside cattle-punching and cabaret\r\ndancing, but she froze me with a look, and I faded away. No, Sir\r\nEgotistical Complacency will be having some bridge later on, which will\r\nsuit me much better. He\'s not a bad chap underneath if you can swallow\r\nhis peculiarities, and he\'s a sportsman. I like to play with him. He\r\ndoesn\'t care a durn if he wins or loses."\r\n\r\n"It doesn\'t matter when you have a banking account the size of his,"\r\nsaid Arbuthnot. "Personally, I find dancing more amusing and less\r\nexpensive. I shall go and take my chance with our hostess."\r\n\r\nHis eyes turned rather eagerly towards the end of the room where the\r\ngirl was standing alone, straight and slim, the light from an\r\nelectrolier gilding the thick bright curls framing her beautiful,\r\nhaughty little face. She was staring down at the dancers with an absent\r\nexpression in her eyes, as if her thoughts were far away from the\r\ncrowded ballroom.\r\n\r\nThe American pushed Arbuthnot forward with a little laugh.\r\n\r\n"Run along, foolish moth, and get your poor little wings singed. When\r\nthe cruel fair has done trampling on you I\'ll come right along and mop\r\nup the remains. If, on the other hand, your temerity meets with the\r\nsuccess it deserves, we can celebrate suitably later on." And, linking\r\nhis arm in his friend\'s, he drew him away to the card-room.\r\n\r\nArbuthnot went through the window and worked slowly round the room,\r\nhugging the wall, evading dancers, and threading his way through groups\r\nof chattering men and women of all nationalities. He came at last to\r\nthe raised dais on which Diana Mayo was still standing, and climbed up\r\nthe few steps to her side.\r\n\r\n"This is luck, Miss Mayo," he said, with an assurance that he was far\r\nfrom feeling. "Am I really fortunate enough to find you without a\r\npartner?"\r\n\r\nShe turned to him slowly, with a little crease growing between her\r\narched eyebrows, as if his coming were inopportune and she resented the\r\ninterruption to her thoughts, and then she smiled quite frankly.\r\n\r\n"I said I would not dance until everybody was started," she said rather\r\ndoubtfully, looking over the crowded floor.\r\n\r\n"They are all dancing. You\'ve done your duty nobly. Don\'t miss this\r\nripping tune," he urged persuasively.\r\n\r\nShe hesitated, tapping her programme-pencil against her teeth.\r\n\r\n"I refused a lot of men," she said, with a grimace. Then she laughed\r\nsuddenly. "Come along, then. I am noted for my bad manners. This will\r\nonly be one extra sin."\r\n\r\nArbuthnot danced well, but with the girl in his arms he seemed suddenly\r\ntongue-tied. They swung round the room several times, then halted\r\nsimultaneously beside an open window and went out into the garden of\r\nthe hotel, sitting down on a wicker seat under a gaudy Japanese hanging\r\nlantern. The band was still playing, and for the moment the garden was\r\nempty, lit faintly by coloured lanterns, festooned from the palm trees,\r\nand twinkling lights outlining the winding paths.\r\n\r\nArbuthnot leaned forward, his hands clasped between his knees.\r\n\r\n"I think you are the most perfect dancer I have ever met," he said a\r\nlittle breathlessly.\r\n\r\nMiss Mayo looked at him seriously, without a trace of\r\nself-consciousness.\r\n\r\n"It is very easy to dance if you have a musical ear, and if you have\r\nbeen in the habit of making your body do what you want. So few people\r\nseem to be trained to make their limbs obey them. Mine have had to do\r\nas they were told since I was a small child," she answered calmly.\r\n\r\nThe unexpectedness of the reply acted as a silencer on Arbuthnot for a\r\nfew minutes, and the girl beside him seemed in no hurry to break the\r\nsilence. The dance was over and the empty garden was thronged for a\r\nlittle time. Then the dancers drifted back into the hotel as the band\r\nstarted again.\r\n\r\n"It\'s rather jolly here in the garden," Arbuthnot said tentatively. His\r\nheart was pounding with unusual rapidity, and his eyes, that he kept\r\nfixed on his own clasped hands, had a hungry look growing in them.\r\n\r\n"You mean that, you want to sit out this dance with me?" she said with\r\na boyish directness that somewhat nonplussed him.\r\n\r\n"Yes," he stammered rather foolishly.\r\n\r\nShe held her programme up to the light of the lantern. "I promised this\r\none to Arthur Conway. We quarrel every time we meet. I cannot think why\r\nhe asked me; he disapproves of me even more than his mother does--such\r\nan interfering old lady. He will be overjoyed to be let off. And I\r\ndon\'t want to dance to-night. I am looking forward so tremendously to\r\nto-morrow. I shall stay and talk to you, but you must give me a\r\ncigarette to keep me in a good temper."\r\n\r\nHis hand shook a little as he held the match for her. "Are you really\r\ndetermined to go through with this tour?"\r\n\r\nShe stared at him in surprise. "Why not? My arrangements have been made\r\nsome time. Why should I change my mind at the last moment?"\r\n\r\n"Why does your brother let you go alone? Why doesn\'t he go with you?\r\nOh, I haven\'t any right to ask, but I do ask," he broke out vehemently.\r\n\r\nShe shrugged her shoulders with a little laugh. "We fell out, Aubrey\r\nand I. He wanted to go to America. I wanted a trip into the desert. We\r\nquarrelled for two whole days and half one night, and then we\r\ncompromised. I should have my desert tour, and Aubrey should go to New\r\nYork; and to mark his brotherly appreciation of my gracious promise to\r\nfollow him to the States without fail at the end of a month he has\r\nconsented to grace my caravan for the first stage, and dismiss me on my\r\nway with his blessing. It annoyed him so enormously that he could not\r\norder me to go with him, this being the first time in our wanderings\r\nthat our inclinations have not jumped in the same direction. I came of\r\nage a few months ago, and, in future, I can do as I please. Not that I\r\nhave ever done anything else," she conceded, with another laugh,\r\n"because Aubrey\'s ways have been my ways until now."\r\n\r\n"But for the sake of one month! What difference could it make to him?"\r\nhe asked in astonishment.\r\n\r\n"That\'s Aubrey," replied Miss Mayo drily.\r\n\r\n"It isn\'t safe," persisted Arbuthnot.\r\n\r\nShe flicked the ash from her cigarette carelessly. "I don\'t agree with\r\nyou. I don\'t know why everybody is making such a fuss about it. Plenty\r\nof other women have travelled in much wilder country than this desert."\r\n\r\nHe looked at her curiously. She seemed to be totally unaware that it\r\nwas her youth and her beauty that made all the danger of the\r\nexpedition. He fell back on the easier excuse.\r\n\r\n"There seems to be unrest amongst some of the tribes. There have been a\r\nlot of rumours lately," he said seriously.\r\n\r\nShe made a little movement of impatience. "Oh, that\'s what they always\r\ntell you when they want to put obstacles in your way. The authorities\r\nhave already dangled that bogey in front of me. I asked for facts and\r\nthey only gave me generalities. I asked definitely if they had any\r\npower to stop me. They said they had not, but strongly advised me not\r\nto make the attempt. I said I should go, unless the French Government\r\narrested me.... Why not? I am not afraid. I don\'t admit that there is\r\nanything to be afraid of. I don\'t believe a word about the tribes being\r\nrestless. Arabs are always moving about, aren\'t they? I have an\r\nexcellent caravan leader, whom even the authorities vouch for, and I\r\nshall be armed. I am perfectly able to take care of myself. I can shoot\r\nstraight and I am used to camping. Besides, I have given my word to\r\nAubrey to be in Oran in a month, and I can\'t get very far away in that\r\ntime."\r\n\r\nThere was an obstinate ring in her voice, and when she stopped speaking\r\nhe sat silent, consumed with anxiety, obsessed with the loveliness of\r\nher, and tormented with the desire to tell her so. Then he turned to\r\nher suddenly, and his face was very white. "Miss Mayo--Diana--put off\r\nthis trip only for a little, and give me the right to go with you. I\r\nlove you. I want you for my wife more than anything on earth. I shan\'t\r\nalways be a penniless subaltern. One of these days I shall be able to\r\ngive you a position that is worthy of you; no, nothing could be that,\r\nbut one at least that I am not ashamed to offer to you. We\'ve been very\r\ngood friends; you know all about me. I\'ll give my whole life to make\r\nyou happy. The world has been a different place to me since you came\r\ninto it. I can\'t get away from you. You are in my thoughts night and\r\nday. I love you; I want you. My God, Diana! Beauty like yours drives a\r\nman mad!"\r\n\r\n"Is beauty all that a man wants in his wife?" she asked, with a kind of\r\ncold wonder in her voice. "Brains and a sound body seem much more\r\nsensible requirements to me."\r\n\r\n"But when a woman has all three, as you have, Diana," he whispered\r\nardently, his hands closing over the slim ones lying in her lap.\r\n\r\nBut with a strength that seemed impossible for their smallness she\r\ndisengaged them from his grasp. "Please stop. I am sorry. We have been\r\ngood friends, and it has never occurred to me that there could be\r\nanything beyond that. I never thought that you might love me. I never\r\nthought of you in that way at all, I don\'t understand it. When God made\r\nme He omitted to give me a heart. I have never loved any one in my\r\nlife. My brother and I have tolerated each other, but there has never\r\nbeen any affection between us. Would it be likely? Put yourself in\r\nAubrey\'s place. Imagine a young man of nineteen, with a cold, reserved\r\nnature, being burdened with the care of a baby sister, thrust into his\r\nhands unwanted and unexpected. Was it likely that he would have any\r\naffection for me? I never wanted it. I was born with the same cold\r\nnature as his. I was brought up as a boy, my training was hard. Emotion\r\nand affection have been barred out of my life. I simply don\'t know what\r\nthey mean. I don\'t want to know. I am very content with my life as it\r\nis. Marriage for a woman means the end of independence, that is,\r\nmarriage with a man who is a man, in spite of all that the most modern\r\nwoman may say. I have never obeyed any one in my life; I do not wish to\r\ntry the experiment. I am very sorry to have hurt you. You\'ve been a\r\nsplendid pal, but that side of life does not exist for me. If I had\r\nthought for one moment that my friendship was going to hurt you I need\r\nnot have let you become so intimate, but I did not think, because it is\r\na subject that I never think of. A man to me is just a companion with\r\nwhom I ride or shoot or fish; a pal, a comrade, and that\'s just all\r\nthere is to it. God made me a woman. Why, only He knows."\r\n\r\nHer quiet, even voice stopped. There had been a tone of cold sincerity\r\nin it that Arbuthnot could not help but recognise. She meant everything\r\nthat she said. She said no more than the truth. Her reputation for\r\ncomplete indifference to admiration and her unvarying attitude towards\r\nmen were as well known as her dauntless courage and obstinate\r\ndetermination. With Sir Aubrey Mayo she behaved like a younger brother,\r\nand as such entertained his friends. She was popular with everybody,\r\neven with the mothers of marriageable daughters, for, in spite of her\r\nwealth and beauty, her notorious peculiarities made her negligible as a\r\nrival to plainer and less well-dowered girls.\r\n\r\nArbuthnot sat in silence. It was hardly likely, he thought bitterly,\r\nthat he should succeed where other and better men had failed. He had\r\nbeen a fool to succumb to the temptation that had been too hard for him\r\nto resist. He knew her well enough to know beforehand what her answer\r\nwould be. The very real fear for her safety that the thought of the\r\ncoming expedition gave him, her nearness in the mystery of the Eastern\r\nnight, the lights, the music, had all combined to rush to his lips\r\nwords that in a saner moment would never have passed them. He loved\r\nher, he would love her always, but he knew that his love was as\r\nhopeless as it was undying. But it was men who were men whom she wanted\r\nfor her friends, so he must take his medicine like a man.\r\n\r\n"May I still be the pal, Diana?" he said quietly.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him a moment, but in the dim light of the hanging\r\nlanterns his eyes were steady under hers, and she held out her hand\r\nfrankly. "Gladly," she said candidly. "I have hosts of acquaintances,\r\nbut very few friends. We are always travelling, Aubrey and I, and we\r\nnever seem to have time to make friends. We rarely stay as long in one\r\nplace as we have stayed in Biskra. In England they call us very bad\r\nneighbours, we are so seldom there. We generally go home for three\r\nmonths in the winter for the hunting, but the rest of the year we\r\nwander on the face of the globe."\r\n\r\nHe held her slender fingers gripped in his for a moment, smothering an\r\ninsane desire to press them to his lips, which he knew would be fatal\r\nto the newly accorded friendship, and then let them go. Miss Mayo\r\ncontinued sitting quietly beside him. She was in no way disturbed by\r\nwhat had happened. She had taken him literally at his word, and was\r\ntreating him as the pal he had asked to be. It no more occurred to her\r\nthat she might relieve him of her society than it occurred to her that\r\nher continued presence might be distressing to him. She was totally\r\nunembarrassed and completely un-self-conscious. And as they sat silent,\r\nher thoughts far away in the desert, and his full of vain longings and\r\nregrets, a man\'s low voice rose in the stillness of the night. "_Pale\r\nhands I loved beside the Shalimar. Where are you now? Who lies beneath\r\nyour spell_?" he sang in a passionate, vibrating baritone. He was\r\nsinging in English, and yet the almost indefinite slurring from note to\r\nnote was strangely un-English. Diana Mayo leaned forward, her head\r\nraised, listening intently, with shining eyes. The voice seemed to come\r\nfrom the dark shadows at the end of the garden, or it might have been\r\nfurther away out in the road beyond the cactus hedge. The singer sang\r\nslowly, his voice lingering caressingly on the words; the last verse\r\ndying away softly and clearly, almost imperceptibly fading into\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nFor a moment there was utter stillness, then Diana lay back with a\r\nlittle sigh. "The Kashmiri Song. It makes me think of India. I heard a\r\nman sing it in Kashmere last year, but not like that. What a wonderful\r\nvoice! I wonder who it is?"\r\n\r\nArbuthnot looked at her curiously, surprised at the sudden ring of\r\ninterest in her tone, and the sudden animation of her face.\r\n\r\n"You say you have no emotion in your nature, and yet that unknown man\'s\r\nsinging has stirred you deeply. How do you reconcile the two?" he\r\nasked, almost angrily.\r\n\r\n"Is an appreciation of the beautiful emotion?" she challenged, with\r\nuplifted eyes. "Surely not. Music, art, nature, everything beautiful\r\nappeals to me. But there is nothing emotional in that. It is only that\r\nI prefer beautiful things to ugly ones. For that reason even pretty\r\nclothes appeal to me," she added, laughing.\r\n\r\n"You are the best-dressed woman in Biskra," he acceded. "But is not\r\nthat a concession to the womanly feelings that you despise?"\r\n\r\n"Not at all. To take an interest in one\'s clothes is not an exclusively\r\nfeminine vice. I like pretty dresses. I admit to spending some time in\r\nthinking of colour schemes to go with my horrible hair, but I assure\r\nyou that my dressmaker has an easier life than Aubrey\'s tailor."\r\n\r\nShe sat silent, hoping that the singer might not have gone, but there\r\nwas no sound except a cicada chirping near her. She swung round in her\r\nchair, looking in the direction from which it came. "Listen to him.\r\nJolly little chap! They are the first things I listen for when I get to\r\nPort Said. They mean the East to me."\r\n\r\n"Maddening little beasts!" said Arbuthnot irritably.\r\n\r\n"They are going to be very friendly little beasts to me during the next\r\nfour weeks.... You don\'t know what this trip means to me. I like wild\r\nplaces. The happiest times of my life have been spent camping in\r\nAmerica and India, and I have always wanted the desert more than either\r\nof them. It is going to be a month of pure joy. I am going to be\r\nenormously happy."\r\n\r\nShe stood up with a little laugh of intense pleasure, and half turned,\r\nwaiting for Arbuthnot. He got up reluctantly and stood silent beside\r\nher for a few moments. "Diana, I wish you\'d let me kiss you, just\r\nonce," he broke out miserably.\r\n\r\nShe looked up swiftly with a glint of anger in her eyes, and shook her\r\nhead. "No. That\'s not in the compact. I have never been kissed in my\r\nlife. It is one of the things that I do not understand." Her voice was\r\nalmost fierce.\r\n\r\nShe moved leisurely towards the hotel, and he paced beside her\r\nwondering if he had forfeited her friendship by his outburst, but on\r\nthe verandah she halted and spoke in the frank tone of camaraderie in\r\nwhich she had always addressed him. "Shall I see you in the morning?"\r\n\r\nHe understood. There was to be no more reference to what had passed\r\nbetween them. The offer of friendship held, but only on her own terms.\r\nHe pulled himself together.\r\n\r\n"Yes. We have arranged an escort of about a dozen of us to ride the\r\nfirst few miles with you, to give you a proper send-off."\r\n\r\nShe made a laughing gesture of protest. "It will certainly need four\r\nweeks of solitude to counteract the conceit I shall acquire," she said\r\nlightly, as she passed into the ballroom.\r\n\r\nA few hours later Diana came into her bedroom, and, switching on the\r\nelectric lights, tossed her gloves and programme into a chair. The room\r\nwas empty, for her maid had had a _vertige_ at the suggestion that\r\nshe should accompany her mistress into the desert, and had been sent\r\nback to Paris to await Diana\'s return. She had left during the day, to\r\ntake most of the heavy luggage with her.\r\n\r\nDiana stood in the middle of the room and looked at the preparations\r\nfor the early start next morning with a little smile of satisfaction.\r\nEverything was _en train_; the final arrangements had all been\r\nconcluded some days before. The camel caravan with the camp equipment\r\nwas due to leave Biskra a few hours before the time fixed for the Mayos\r\nto start with Mustafa Ali, the reputable guide whom the French\r\nauthorities had reluctantly recommended. The two big suit-cases that\r\nDiana was taking with her stood open, ready packed, waiting only for\r\nthe last few necessaries, and by them the steamer trunk that Sir Aubrey\r\nwould take charge of and leave in Paris as he passed through. On a\r\nchaise-longue was laid out her riding kit ready for the morning. Her\r\nsmile broadened as she looked at the smart-cut breeches and high brown\r\nboots. They were the clothes in which most of her life had been spent,\r\nand in which she was far more at home than in the pretty dresses over\r\nwhich she had laughed with Arbuthnot.\r\n\r\nShe was glad the dance was over; it was not a form of exercise that\r\nappealed to her particularly. She was thinking only of the coming tour.\r\nShe stretched her arms out with a little happy laugh.\r\n\r\n"It\'s the life of lives, and it\'s going to begin all over again\r\nto-morrow morning." She crossed over to the dressing-table, and,\r\npropping her elbows on it, looked at herself in the glass, with a\r\nlittle friendly smile at the reflection. In default of any other\r\nconfidant she had always talked to herself, with no thought for the\r\nbeauty of the face staring back at her from the glass. The only comment\r\nshe ever made to herself on her own appearance was sometimes to wish\r\nthat her hair was not such a tiresome shade. She looked at herself now\r\nwith a tinge of curiosity. "I wonder why I\'m so especially happy\r\nto-night. It must be because we have been so long in Biskra. It\'s been\r\nvery jolly, but I was beginning to get very bored." She laughed again\r\nand picked up her watch to wind. It was one of her peculiarities that\r\nshe would wear no jewellery of any kind. Even the gold repeater in her\r\nhand was on a plain leather strap. She undressed slowly and each moment\r\nfelt more wide-awake. Slipping a thin wrap over her pyjamas and\r\nlighting a cigarette she went out on to the broad balcony on to which\r\nher bedroom gave. The room was on the first floor, and opposite her\r\nwindow rose one of the ornately carved and bracketed pillars that\r\nsupported the balcony, stretching up to the second story above her\r\nhead. She looked down into the gardens below. It was an easy climb, she\r\nthought, with a boyish grin--far easier than many she had achieved\r\nsuccessfully when the need of a solitary ramble became imperative. But\r\nthe East was inconvenient for solitary ramble; native servants had a\r\ndisconcerting habit of lying down to sleep wherever drowsiness overcame\r\nthem, and it was not very long since she had slid down from her balcony\r\nand landed plumb on a slumbering bundle of humanity who had roused half\r\nthe hotel with his howls. She leant far over the rail, trying to see\r\ninto the verandah below, and she thought she caught a glimpse of white\r\ndrapery. She looked again, and this time there was nothing, but she\r\nshook her head with a little grimace, and swung herself up on to the\r\nbroad ledge of the railing. Settling herself comfortably with her back\r\nagainst the column she looked out over the hotel gardens into the\r\nnight, humming softly the Kashmiri song she had heard earlier in the\r\nevening.\r\n\r\nThe risen moon was full, and its cold, brilliant light filled the\r\ngarden with strong black shadows. She watched some that seemed even to\r\nmove, as if the garden were alive with creeping, hurrying figures, and\r\namused herself tracking them until she traced them to the palm tree or\r\ncactus bush that caused them. One in particular gave her a long hunt\r\ntill she finally ran it to its lair, and it proved to be the shadow of\r\na grotesque lead statue half hidden by a flowering shrub. Forgetting\r\nthe hour and the open windows all around her, she burst into a rippling\r\npeal of laughter, which was interrupted by the appearance of a figure,\r\nimperfectly seen through the lattice-work which divided her balcony\r\nfrom the next one, and the sound of an irritable voice.\r\n\r\n"For Heaven\'s sake, Diana, let other people sleep if you can\'t."\r\n\r\n"Which, being interpreted, is let Sir Aubrey Mayo sleep," she retorted,\r\nwith a chuckle. "My dear boy, sleep if you want to, but I don\'t know\r\nhow you can on a night like this. Did you ever see such a gorgeous\r\nmoon?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, damn the moon!"\r\n\r\n"Oh, very well. Don\'t get cross about it. Go back to bed and put your\r\nhead under the clothes, and then you won\'t see it. But I\'m going to sit\r\nhere."\r\n\r\n"Diana, don\'t be an idiot! You\'ll go to sleep and fall into the garden\r\nand break your neck."\r\n\r\n"_Tant pis pour moi. Tant mieux pour toi,_" she said flippantly.\r\n"I have left you all that I have in the world, dear brother. Could\r\ndevotion go further?"\r\n\r\nShe paid no heed to his exclamation of annoyance, and looked back into\r\nthe garden. It was a wonderful night, silent except for the cicadas\'\r\nmonotonous chirping, mysterious with the inexplicable mystery that\r\nhangs always in the Oriental night. The smells of the East rose up all\r\naround her; here, as at home, they seemed more perceptible by night\r\nthan by day. Often at home she had stood on the little stone balcony\r\noutside her room, drinking in the smells of the night--the pungent,\r\nearthy smell after rain, the aromatic smell of pine trees near the\r\nhouse. It was the intoxicating smells of the night that had first\r\ndriven her, as a very small child, to clamber down from her balcony,\r\nclinging to the thick ivy roots, to wander with the delightful sense of\r\nwrong-doing through the moonlit park and even into the adjoining gloomy\r\nwoods. She had always been utterly fearless.\r\n\r\nHer childhood had been a strange one. There had been no near relatives\r\nto interest themselves in the motherless girl left to the tender\r\nmercies of a brother nearly twenty years her senior, who was frankly\r\nand undisguisedly horrified at the charge that had been thrust upon\r\nhim. Wrapped up in himself, and free to indulge in the wander hunger\r\nthat gripped him, the baby sister was an intolerable burden, and he had\r\nshifted responsibility in the easiest way possible. For the first few\r\nyears of her life she was left undisturbed to nurses and servants who\r\nspoiled her indiscriminately. Then, when she was still quite a tiny\r\nchild, Sir Aubrey Mayo came home from a long tour, and, settling down\r\nfor a couple of years, fixed on his sister\'s future training, modelled\r\nrigidly on his own upbringing. Dressed as a boy, treated as a boy, she\r\nlearned to ride and to shoot and to fish--not as amusements, but\r\nseriously, to enable her to take her place later on as a companion to\r\nthe man whose only interests they were. His air of weariness was a\r\nmannerism. In reality he was as hard as nails, and it was his intention\r\nthat Diana should grow up as hard. With that end in view her upbringing\r\nhad been Spartan, no allowances were made for sex or temperament and\r\nnothing was spared to gain the desired result. And from the first Diana\r\nhad responded gallantly, throwing herself heart and soul into the\r\narduous, strenuous life mapped out for her. The only drawback to a\r\nperfect enjoyment of life were the necessary lessons that had to be\r\ngone through, though even these might have been worse. Every morning\r\nshe rode across the park to the rectory for a couple of hours\' tuition\r\nwith the rector, whose heart was more in his stable than in his parish,\r\nand whose reputation was greater across country than it was in the\r\npulpit. His methods were rough and ready, but she had brains, and\r\nacquired an astonishing amount of diverse knowledge. But her education\r\nwas stopped with abrupt suddenness when she was fifteen by the arrival\r\nat the rectory of an overgrown young cub who had been sent by a\r\ndespairing parent, as a last resource, to the muscular rector, and who\r\nquickly discovered what those amongst whom she had grown up had hardly\r\nrealised, that Diana Mayo, with the clothes and manners of a boy, was\r\nreally an uncommonly beautiful young woman. With the assurance\r\nbelonging to his type, he had taken the earliest opportunity of telling\r\nher so, following it with an attempt to secure the kiss that up to now\r\nhis own good looks had always secured for him. But in this case he had\r\nto deal with a girl who was a girl by accident of birth only, who was\r\nquicker with her hands and far finer trained than he was, and whose\r\nnatural strength was increased by furious rage. She had blacked his\r\neyes before he properly understood what was happening, and was dancing\r\naround him like an infuriated young gamecock when the rector had burst\r\nin upon them, attracted by the noise.\r\n\r\nWhat she left he had finished, and then, breathless and angry, had\r\nridden back across the park with her and had briefly announced to Sir\r\nAubrey, who happened to be at home upon one of his rare visits, that\r\nhis pupil was both too old and too pretty to continue her studies at\r\nthe rectory, and had taken himself off as hurriedly as he had come,\r\nleaving Sir Aubrey to settle for himself the new problem of Diana. And,\r\nas before, it was settled in the easiest possible way. Physically she\r\nwas perfectly able to take up the role for which he had always intended\r\nher; mentally he presumed that she knew as much as it was necessary for\r\nher to know, and, in any case, travelling itself was an education, and\r\na far finer one than could be learned from books. So Diana grew up in a\r\nday, and in a fortnight the old life was behind her and she had started\r\nout on the ceaseless travels with her brother that had continued for\r\nthe last six years--years of perpetual change, of excitements and\r\ndangers.\r\n\r\nShe thought of it all, sitting on the broad rail of the balcony, her\r\nhead slanted against the column on which she leaned. "It\'s been a\r\nsplendid life," she murmured, "and to-morrow--to-day begins the most\r\nperfect part of it." She yawned and realised suddenly that she was\r\ndesperately sleepy. She turned back into her room, leaving the windows\r\nwide, and, flinging off her wrap, tumbled into bed and slept almost\r\nbefore her head was on the pillow.\r\n\r\nIt must have been about an hour later when she awoke, suddenly wide\r\nawake. She lay quite still, looking cautiously under her thick lashes.\r\nThe room was flooded with moonlight, there was nothing to be seen, but\r\nshe had the positive feeling that there was another presence in the\r\nroom beside her own; she had had a half-conscious vision in the moment\r\nof waking of a shadowy something that had seemed to fade away by the\r\nwindow. As the actual reality of this thought pierced through the sleep\r\nthat dulled her brain and became a concrete suggestion, she sprang out\r\nof the bed and ran on to the balcony. It was empty. She leaned over the\r\nrailing, listening intently, but she could see nothing and hear\r\nnothing. Puzzled, she went back into her room and turned on the lights.\r\nNothing seemed to be missing: her watch lay where she had left it on\r\nthe dressing table; and the suit-cases had apparently not been tampered\r\nwith. By the bedside the ivory-mounted revolver that she always carried\r\nwas lying as she had placed it. She looked around the room again,\r\nfrowning. "It must have been a dream," she said doubtfully, "but it\r\nseemed very real. It looked tall and white and solid, and I _felt_\r\nit there." She waited a moment or two, then shrugged her shoulders,\r\nturned out the lights, and got into bed. Her nerves were admirable, and\r\nin five minutes she was asleep again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\n\r\n\r\nThe promised send-off had been enthusiastic. The arrangements for the\r\ntrip had been perfect; there had been no hitch anywhere. The guide,\r\nMustafa Ali, appeared capable and efficient, effacing himself when not\r\nwanted and replying with courteous dignity when spoken to. The day had\r\nbeen full of interest, and the long, hot ride had for Diana been the\r\nheight of physical enjoyment. They had reached the oasis where the\r\nfirst night was to be passed an hour before, and found the camp already\r\nestablished, tents pitched, and everything so ordered that Sir Aubrey\r\ncould find nothing to criticise; even Stephens, his servant, who had\r\ntravelled with him since Diana was a baby, and who was as critical as\r\nhis master on the subject of camps, had no fault to find.\r\n\r\nDiana glanced about her little travelling tent with complete content.\r\nIt was much smaller than the ones to which she had always been\r\naccustomed, ridiculously so compared with the large one she had had in\r\nIndia the previous year, with its separate bath--and dressing-rooms.\r\nServants, too, had swarmed in India. Here service promised to be\r\ninadequate, but it had been her whim on this tour to dispense with the\r\nelaborate arrangements that Sir Aubrey cultivated and to try\r\ncomparative roughing it. The narrow camp cot, the tin bath, the little\r\nfolding table and her two suit-cases seemed to take up all the\r\navailable space. But she laughed at the inconvenience, though she had\r\ndrenched her bed with splashing, and the soap had found its way into\r\nthe toe of one of her long boots. She had changed from her riding\r\nclothes into a dress of clinging jade-green silk, swinging short above\r\nher slender ankles, the neck cut low, revealing the gleaming white of\r\nher soft, girlish bosom. She came out of the tent and stood a moment\r\nexchanging an amused smile with Stephens, who was hovering near\r\ndubiously, one eye on her and the other on his master. She was late,\r\nand Sir Aubrey liked his meals punctually. The baronet was lounging in\r\none deck-chair with his feet on another.\r\n\r\nDiana wagged an admonishing forefinger. "Fly, Stephens, and fetch the\r\nsoup! If it is cold there will be a riot." She walked to the edge of\r\nthe canvas cloth that had been thrown down in front of the tents and\r\nstood revelling in the scene around her, her eyes dancing with\r\nexcitement as they glanced slowly around the camp spread out over the\r\noasis--the clustering palm trees, the desert itself stretching away\r\nbefore her in undulating sweeps, but seemingly level in the evening\r\nlight, far off to the distant hills lying like a dark smudge against\r\nthe horizon. She drew a long breath. It was the desert at last, the\r\ndesert that she felt she had been longing for all her life. She had\r\nnever known until this moment how intense the longing had been. She\r\nfelt strangely at home, as if the great, silent emptiness had been\r\nwaiting for her as she had been waiting for it, and now that she had\r\ncome it was welcoming her softly with the faint rustle of the\r\nwhispering sand, the mysterious charm of its billowy, shifting surface\r\nthat seemed beckoning to her to penetrate further and further into its\r\nunknown obscurities.\r\n\r\nHer brother\'s voice behind her brought her down to earth suddenly.\r\n"You\'ve been a confounded long time."\r\n\r\nShe turned to the table with a faint smile. "Don\'t be a bear, Aubrey.\r\nIt\'s all very well for you. You have Stephens to lather your chin and\r\nto wash your hands, but thanks to that idiot Marie, I have to look\r\nafter myself."\r\n\r\nSir Aubrey took his heels down leisurely from the second chair, pitched\r\naway his cigar, and, screwing his eyeglass into his eye with more than\r\nusual truculence, looked at her with disapproval. "Are you going to rig\r\nyourself out like that every evening for the benefit of Mustafa Ali and\r\nthe camel-drivers?"\r\n\r\n"I do not propose to invite the worthy Mustafa to meals, and I am not\r\nin the habit of \'rigging myself out,\' as you so charmingly put it, for\r\nany one\'s benefit. If you think I dress in camp to please you, my dear\r\nAubrey, you flatter yourself. I do it entirely to please myself. That\r\nexplorer woman we met in London that first year I began travelling with\r\nyou explained to me the real moral and physical value of changing into\r\ncomfortable, pretty clothes after a hard day in breeches and boots. You\r\nchange yourself. What\'s the difference?"\r\n\r\n"All the difference," he snapped. "There is no need for you to make\r\nyourself more attractive than you are already."\r\n\r\n"Since when has it occurred to you that I am attractive? You must have\r\na touch of the sun, Aubrey," she replied, with uplifted eyebrows,\r\ndrumming impatiently with her fingers on the table.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t quibble. You know perfectly well that you are good-looking--too\r\ngood-looking to carry through this preposterous affair."\r\n\r\n"Will you please tell me what you are driving at?" she asked quietly.\r\nBut the dark blue eyes fixed on her brother\'s face were growing darker\r\nas she looked at him.\r\n\r\n"I\'ve been doing some hard thinking to-day, Diana. This tour you\r\npropose is impossible."\r\n\r\n"Isn\'t it rather late in the day to find that out?" she interrupted\r\nsarcastically; but he ignored the interruption.\r\n\r\n"You must see for yourself, now that you are face to face with the\r\nthing, that it is impossible. It\'s quite unthinkable that you can\r\nwander for the next month all alone in the desert with those damned\r\nniggers. Though my legal guardianship over you terminated last\r\nSeptember I still have some moral obligations towards you. Though it\r\nhas been convenient to me to bring you up as a boy and to regard you in\r\nthe light of a younger brother instead of a sister, we cannot get away\r\nfrom the fact that you are a woman, and a very young woman. There are\r\ncertain things a young woman cannot do. If you had been the boy I\r\nalways wished you were it would have been a different matter, but you\r\nare not a boy, and the whole thing is impossible--utterly impossible."\r\nThere was a fretful impatience in his voice.\r\n\r\nDiana lit a cigarette slowly, and swung round on her chair with a hard\r\nlaugh. "If I had not lived with you all my life, Aubrey, I should\r\nreally be impressed with your brotherly solicitude; I should think you\r\nreally meant it. But knowing you as I do, I know that it is not anxiety\r\non my behalf that is prompting you, but the disinclination that you\r\nhave to travel alone without me. You have come to depend on me to save\r\nyou certain annoyances and inconveniences that always occur in\r\ntravelling. You were more honest in Biskra when you only objected to my\r\ntrip without giving reasons. Why have you waited until to-night to give\r\nme those reasons?"\r\n\r\n"Because I thought that here, at least, you would have sense enough to\r\nsee them. In Biskra it was impossible to argue with you. You made your\r\nown arrangements against my wishes. I left it, feeling convinced that\r\nthe impossibility of it would be brought home to you here, and that you\r\nwould see for yourself that it was out of the question. Diana, give up\r\nthis insane trip."\r\n\r\n"I will not."\r\n\r\n"I\'ve a thundering good mind to make you."\r\n\r\n"You can\'t. I\'m my own mistress. You have no right over me at all. You\r\nhave no claim on me. You haven\'t even that of ordinary brotherly\r\naffection, for you have never given me any, so you cannot expect it\r\nfrom me. We needn\'t make any pretence about it, I am not going to argue\r\nany more. I will not go back to Biskra."\r\n\r\n"If you are afraid of being laughed at----" he sneered; but she took him\r\nup swiftly.\r\n\r\n"I am not afraid of being laughed at. Only cowards are afraid of that,\r\nand I am not a coward."\r\n\r\n"Diana, listen to reason!"\r\n\r\n"Aubrey! I have said my last word. Nothing will alter my determination\r\nto go on this trip. Your arguments do not convince me, who know you. It\r\nis your own considerations and not mine that are at the bottom of your\r\nremonstrances. You do not deny it, because you can\'t, because it is\r\ntrue."\r\n\r\nThey were facing each other across the little table. An angry flush\r\nrose in Sir Aubrey\'s face, and his eyeglass fell with a little sharp\r\ntinkle against a waistcoat button.\r\n\r\n"You\'re a damned obstinate little devil!" he said furiously.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him steadily, her scornful mouth firm as his own. "I am\r\nwhat you have made me," she said slowly. "Why quarrel with the result?\r\nYou have brought me up to ignore the restrictions attached to my sex;\r\nyou now round on me and throw them in my face. All my life you have set\r\nme an example of selfishness and obstinacy. Can you wonder that I have\r\nprofited by it? You have made me as hard as yourself, and you now\r\nprofess surprise at the determination your training has forced upon me.\r\nYou are illogical. It is your fault, not mine. There was bound to be a\r\nclash some day. It has come sooner than I expected, that\'s all. Up till\r\nnow my inclinations have gone with yours, but this seems to be the\r\nparting of the ways. As I reminded you before, I am my own mistress,\r\nand I will submit to no interference with my actions. Please understand\r\nthat clearly, Aubrey. I don\'t want to wrangle any more. I will join you\r\nin New York as I promised. I am not in the habit of breaking my\r\npromises, but my life is my own to deal with, and I will deal with it\r\nexactly as I wish and not as any one else wishes. I will do what I\r\nchoose when and how I choose, and I will _never_ obey any will but\r\nmy own."\r\n\r\nSir Aubrey\'s eyes narrowed suddenly. "Then I hope to Heaven that one\r\nday you will fall into the hands of a man who will make you obey," he\r\ncried wrathfully.\r\n\r\nHer scornful mouth curled still more scornfully. "Then Heaven help\r\nhim!" she retorted scathingly, and turned away to her tent.\r\n\r\nBut, alone, her anger gave way to amusement. It had been something,\r\nafter all, to rouse the lazy Aubrey to wrath. She knew exactly the\r\ngrievance he had been nursing against her during the last few weeks in\r\nBiskra. Though he travelled perpetually and often in remote and\r\ndesolate places, he travelled with the acme of comfort and the minimum\r\nof inconveniences. He put himself out for nothing, and the inevitable\r\ndifficulties that accrued fell on Diana\'s younger and less blase\r\nshoulders. She had always known the uses he put her to and the\r\nconvenience she was to him. He might have some latent feelings with\r\nregard to the inadvisability of her behavior, he might even have some\r\nprickings of conscience on the subject of his upbringing of her, but it\r\nwas thoughts of his own comfort that were troubling him most. That she\r\nknew, and the knowledge was not conducive to any kinder feeling towards\r\nhim. He always had been and always would be supremely selfish. The\r\nwhole of their life together had been conducted to suit his\r\nconveniences and not hers. She knew, too, why her company was\r\nparticularly desired on his visit to America. It was a hunting trip,\r\nbut not the kind that they were usually accustomed to: it was a wife\r\nand not big game that was taking Sir Aubrey across the ocean on this\r\noccasion. It had been in his mind for some time as an inevitable and\r\nsomewhat unpleasant necessity. Women bored him, and the idea of\r\nmarriage was distasteful, but a son to succeed him was imperative--a\r\nMayo must be followed by a Mayo. An heir was essential for the big\r\nproperty that the family had held for hundreds of years. No woman had\r\never attracted him, but of all women he had met American women were\r\nless actively irritating to him, and so it was to America that he\r\nturned in search of a wife. He proposed to take a house in New York for\r\na few months and later on in Newport, and it was for that that Diana\'s\r\ncompany was considered indispensable. She would save him endless\r\ntrouble, as all arrangements could be left in her hands and Stephens\'.\r\nHaving made up his mind to go through with a proceeding that he\r\nregarded in the light of a sacrifice on the family altar, his wish was\r\nto get it over and done with as soon as possible, and Diana\'s\r\ninterference in his plans had exasperated him. It was the first time\r\nthat their wills had crossed, and she shrugged her shoulders\r\nimpatiently, with a grimace at the recollection. A little more and it\r\nwould have degenerated into a vulgar quarrel. She banished Aubrey and\r\nhis selfishness resolutely from her mind. It was very hot, and she lay\r\nvery still in the narrow cot, wishing she had not been so rigid in the\r\nmatter of its width, and wondering if a sudden movement in the night\r\nwould precipitate her into the bath that stood alongside. She thought\r\nregretfully of a punkah, and then smiled derisively at herself.\r\n\r\n"Sybarite!" she murmured sleepily. "You need a few discomforts."\r\n\r\nShe was almost aggressively cheerful next morning at breakfast and for\r\nthe time that they lingered at the oasis after the baggage camels had\r\nstarted. Sir Aubrey was morose and silent, and she exchanged most of\r\nher badinage with Stephens, who was superintending the packing of the\r\ntiffin basket that would accompany her in charge of the man who had\r\nbeen selected as her personal servant, and who was waiting, with\r\nMustafa Ali and about ten men, to ride with her.\r\n\r\nThe time for starting came. Stephens was fussing about the horse that\r\nDiana was to ride.\r\n\r\n"Everything all right, Stephens? Up to your standard? Don\'t look so\r\nglum. I wish you were coming to look after me, but it couldn\'t be done.\r\nSir Aubrey would be lost without you."\r\n\r\nThe idea of a tour without Stephens in the background seemed suddenly\r\nmomentous, and the smile she gave him was more serious than she meant\r\nit to be. She went back to her brother, who was pulling his moustache\r\nsavagely. "I don\'t think there\'s any use waiting any longer. You won\'t\r\nwant to hurry yourself too much, and you will want to be in Biskra in\r\ntime for dinner," she said as casually as she could.\r\n\r\nHe swung towards her. "Diana, it\'s still not too late to change your\r\nmind. For Heaven\'s sake give up this folly. It\'s tempting Providence."\r\nFor the first time there was a genuine ring in his voice, and for a\r\nmoment Diana wavered, but only for a moment. Then she looked at him\r\nwith a slow smile.\r\n\r\n"Do I fall on your neck and say, \'Take me back, dear Guardian; I will\r\nbe good,\' or do I prostrate myself at your feet and knock my head on\r\nyour boots, and whine, in the language of the country, \'Hearing is\r\nobeying\'? Don\'t be ridiculous, Aubrey. You can\'t expect me to change my\r\nmind at the eleventh hour. It\'s perfectly safe. Mustafa Ali will take\r\ncare that everything goes smoothly. He has his reputation in Biskra to\r\nthink of. You know the character the authorities gave him. He is not\r\nlikely to throw that away. In any case I can take care of myself,\r\nthanks to your training. I don\'t mind owning to being conceited about\r\nmy shooting. Even you admit that I am a credit to your teaching."\r\n\r\nWith a gay little laugh she whipped out the ivory-mounted revolver, and\r\naiming at a low flat rock, some distance away, fired. She was an\r\nunusually good revolver shot, but this time she seemed to have missed.\r\nThere was no mark on the stone. Diana stared at it stupidly, a frown of\r\nperplexity creasing her forehead. Then she looked at her brother, and\r\nback to the revolver in her hand.\r\n\r\nSir Aubrey swore. "Diana! What a senseless piece of bravado!" he cried\r\nangrily.\r\n\r\nShe took no notice of him. She was still staring at the smooth rock\r\nfate. "I don\'t understand it. How could I miss? It\'s as big as a\r\nhouse," she murmured thoughtfully, and raised the revolver again.\r\n\r\nBut Sir Aubrey caught her wrist. "For God\'s sake don\'t make a fool of\r\nyourself a second time. You have lowered your prestige quite enough\r\nalready," he said in a low voice, with a glance at the group of\r\nwatching Arabs.\r\n\r\nDiana jerked the little weapon back into its place reluctantly. "I\r\ndon\'t understand it," she said again. "It must be the light." She\r\nmounted and wheeled her horse alongside of Sir Aubrey\'s, and held out\r\nher hand. "Good-bye, Aubrey. Expect me a month after you arrive. I will\r\ncable to you from Cherbourg. Good luck! I shall roll up in time to be\r\nbest man," she added, laughing, and with a nod to Mustafa Ali she\r\nturned her horse\'s head southwards.\r\n\r\nFor a long time she rode in silence. The quarrel with Aubrey had left a\r\nnasty taste in her mouth. She knew that what she was doing was\r\nconsidered unconventional, but she had been brought up to be\r\nunconventional. She had never even thought, when she planned her tour,\r\nof possible criticism; it would have made no difference to her if she\r\nhad thought, and she had been amazed and amused at the sensation that\r\nher proposed trip had caused. The publicity to which it had given rise\r\nhad annoyed her intensely; she had been scornful that people could not\r\noccupy themselves with their own affairs and leave her to deal with\r\nhers. But that Aubrey should join in the general criticism and present\r\nsuch a complete _volte-face_ to the opinions he had always held\r\nwas beyond her comprehension. She was angry with him, and contempt was\r\nmingled with her anger. It was inconsistent with the whole of his\r\nlifelong attitude toward her, and the discovery of his altered ideas\r\nleft her rather breathless and more than ever determined to adhere to\r\nher own deeply-rooted convictions. Aubrey was responsible for them, he\r\nhad instilled them, and if he chose now to abandon them that was his\r\nlook-out. For her own part she saw no reason to change principles she\r\nhad been brought up in. If Aubrey really thought there was danger in\r\nthis expedition he could have sacrificed himself for once and come with\r\nher. As Jim Arbuthnot had said, it was only a month, a negligible\r\nlength of time, but Aubrey\'s selfishness would not allow him to make\r\nthat concession any more than her own obstinacy would allow her to give\r\nway. It was too much to expect. And this was the desert! It was the\r\nexpedition that she had dreamed of and planned for years. She could not\r\ngive it up. The idea of danger brought a little laugh to her lips. How\r\ncould anything in the desert hurt her? It had been calling to her\r\nalways. There was nothing strange about the scene that lay all around\r\nher. Her surroundings seemed oddly familiar. The burning sun overhead\r\nin the cloudless sky, the shimmering haze rising from the hot, dry\r\nground, the feathery outline of some clustering palm trees in a tiny\r\ndistant oasis were like remembrances that she watched again with a\r\nfeeling of gladness that was fuller and deeper than anything that she\r\nhad been conscious of before. She was radiantly happy--happy in the\r\nsense of her youth and strength, her perfect physical fitness, happy in\r\nthe capacity of her power of enjoyment, happy with the touch of the\r\nkeen, nervous horse between her knees, exhilarated with her new\r\nauthority. She had looked forward so eagerly, and realisation was\r\nproving infinitely greater than anticipation. And for a whole month\r\nthis perfect happiness was to be hers. She thought of her promise to\r\nAubrey with impatience. To give up the joyous freedom of the desert for\r\nthe commonplace round of American social life seemed preposterous. The\r\nthought of the weeks in New York were frankly tedious; Newport would be\r\na little less bad, for there were alleviations. The only hope was that\r\nAubrey would find the wife he was looking for quickly and release her\r\nfrom an obligation that was going to be very wearisome. Aubrey was\r\ncounting on her, and it would be unsporting to let him down; she would\r\nhave to keep her promise, but she would be glad when it was over.\r\nAubrey married would settle definitely the possibility of any further\r\ndisagreements between them. She wondered vaguely what the future Lady\r\nMayo would be like, but she did not expend much pity on her. American\r\ngirls as a rule were well able to care for themselves. She stroked her\r\nhorse with a little smile. Aubrey and his possible wife seemed\r\nsingularly uninteresting beside the vivid interest of the moment. A\r\ncaravan that had been visible for a long time coming towards them drew\r\nnearer, and Diana reined in to watch the long line of slow, lurching\r\ncamels passing. The great beasts, with their disdainful tread and long,\r\nswaying necks, never failed to interest her. It was a large caravan;\r\nthe bales on the camels\' backs looked heavy, and beside the merchants\r\non riding camels and a motley crowd of followers--some on lean little\r\ndonkeys and others on foot--there was an armed guard of mounted men. It\r\ntook some time to pass. One of two of the camels carried huddled\r\nfigures, swathed and shapeless with a multitude of coverings, that\r\nDiana knew must be women. The contrast between them and herself was\r\nalmost ridiculous. It made her feel stifled even to look at them. She\r\nwondered what their lives were like, if they ever rebelled against the\r\ndrudgery and restrictions that were imposed upon them, if they ever\r\nlonged for the freedom that she was revelling in, or if custom and\r\nusage were so strong that they had no thoughts beyond the narrow life\r\nthey led. The thought of those lives filled her with aversion. The idea\r\nof marriage--even in its highest form, based on mutual consideration\r\nand mutual forbearance--was repugnant to her. She thought of it with a\r\nshiver of absolute repulsion. To Aubrey it was distasteful, but to her\r\ncold, reserved temperament it was a thing of horror and disgust. That\r\nwomen could submit to the degrading intimacy and fettered existence of\r\nmarried life filled her with scornful wonder. To be bound irrevocably\r\nto the will and pleasure of a man who would have the right to demand\r\nobedience in all that constituted marriage and the strength to enforce\r\nthose claims revolted her. For a Western woman it was bad enough, but\r\nfor the women of the East, mere slaves of the passions of the men who\r\nowned them, unconsidered, disregarded, reduced to the level of animals,\r\nthe bare idea made her quiver and bring her hand down heavily on her\r\nhorse\'s neck. The nervous creature started sharply and she let him go,\r\ncalling to Mustafa Ali as she cantered past him. He had ridden to meet\r\nthe caravan and was dismounted, deep in conversation with the chief of\r\nthe armed guard. With the thoughts that it had provoked the caravan had\r\nlost all interest for Diana. She wanted to get away from it, to forget\r\nit, and she rode on unmindful of her escort, who, like her guide, had\r\nstopped to speak with the traders. Diana\'s horse was fleet, and it was\r\nsome time before they caught her up. There was a look of annoyance on\r\nMustafa Ali\'s face as she turned on hearing them behind her and signed\r\nto him to ride beside her.\r\n\r\n"Mademoiselle is not interested in the caravan?" he asked curiously.\r\n\r\n"No," she replied shortly, and asked for some details connected with\r\nher own expedition. The man talked easily and well, in fluent French,\r\nand after giving the required information, volunteered anecdotes\r\nrelating to various well-known people whom he had guided in the desert.\r\nDiana watched him interestedly. He seemed a man of about middle age,\r\nthough it was difficult to guess more than approximately, for the\r\nthick, peaked beard that hid both mouth and chin made him look older\r\nthan he really was. His beard had been his only drawback from Diana\'s\r\npoint of view, for she judged men by their mouths. Eyes were\r\nuntrustworthy evidences of character in an Oriental, for they usually\r\nwavered under a European\'s. Mustafa Ali\'s were wavering now as she\r\nlooked at him, and it occurred to her that they had not seemed nearly\r\nso shifty in Biskra when she had engaged him. But she attached no\r\nimportance to the thought, and dismissed it as much less interesting\r\nthan the great difference displayed in their respective modes of\r\nriding. The Arab\'s exaggeratedly short stirrup would have given her\r\nagonies of cramp. She pointed the difference with a laugh of amusement\r\nand drew the man on to speak of his horses. The one Diana was riding\r\nwas an unusually fine beast, and had been one of the greatest points in\r\nthe guide\'s favour when he had brought it for her inspection. He was\r\nenthusiastic in its praise, but volubly vague as to its antecedents,\r\nwhich left Diana with the conviction that the animal had either been\r\nstolen or acquired in some irregular manner and that it would be\r\ntactless to pursue further inquiries. After all it was no business of\r\nhers. It was enough that her trip was to be conducted on the back of a\r\nhorse that it was a pleasure to ride and whose vagaries promised to\r\ngive interest to what otherwise might have been monotonous. Some of the\r\nhorses that she had seen in Biskra had been the veriest jades.\r\n\r\nShe asked Mustafa Ali about the country through which they were\r\npassing, but he did not seem to have much information that was really\r\nof interest, or what seemed important to him appeared trivial to her,\r\nand he constantly brought the conversation back to Biskra, of which she\r\nwas tired, or to Oran, of which she knew nothing. The arrival at a\r\nlittle oasis where the guide suggested that the midday halt might be\r\nmade was opportune. Diana swung to the ground, and, tossing down her\r\ngloves, gave herself a shake. It was hot work riding in the burning sun\r\nand the rest would be delightful. She had a thoroughly healthy\r\nappetite, and superintended the laying out of her lunch with interest.\r\nIt was the last time that it would be as daintily packed. Stephens was\r\nan artist with a picnic basket. She was going to miss Stephens. She\r\nfinished her lunch quickly, and then, with her back propped against a\r\npalm tree, a cigarette in her mouth, her arms clasped round her knees,\r\nshe settled down happily, overlooking the desert. The noontime hush\r\nseemed over everything. Not a breath of wind stirred the tops of the\r\npalms; a lizard on a rock near her was the only living thing she could\r\nsee. She glanced over her shoulder. The men, with their big cloaks\r\ndrawn over their heads, were lying asleep, or at any rate appeared to\r\nbe so; only Mustafa Ali was on foot, standing at the edge of the oasis,\r\nstaring fixedly in the direction in which they would ride later.\r\n\r\nDiana threw the end of her cigarette at the lizard and laughed at its\r\nprecipitant flight. She had no desire to follow the example of her\r\nescort and sleep. She was much too happy to lose a minute of her\r\nenjoyment by wasting it in rest that she did not require. She was\r\nperfectly content and satisfied with herself and her outlook. She had\r\nnot a care or a thought in the world. There was not a thing that she\r\nwould have changed or altered. Her life had always been happy; she had\r\nextracted the last ounce of pleasure out of every moment of it. That\r\nher happiness was due to the wealth that had enabled her to indulge in\r\nthe sports and constant travel that made up the sum total of her\r\ndesires never occurred to her. That what composed her pleasure in life\r\nwas possible only because she was rich enough to buy the means of\r\ngratifying it did not enter her head. She thought of her wealth no more\r\nthan of her beauty. The business connected with her coming of age, when\r\nthe big fortune left to her by her father passed unreservedly into her\r\nown hands, was a wearisome necessity that had been got through as\r\nexpeditiously as possible, with as little attention to detail as the\r\nold family lawyer had allowed, and an absence of interest that was\r\nevidenced in the careless scrawl she attached to each document that was\r\ngiven her to sign. The mere money in itself was nothing; it was only a\r\nmeans to an end. She had never even realised how much was expended on\r\nthe continuous and luxurious expeditions that she had made with Sir\r\nAubrey; her own individual tastes were simple, and apart from the\r\nexpensive equipment that was indispensable for their hunting trips, and\r\nwhich was Aubrey\'s choosing, not hers, she was not extravagant. The\r\nlong list of figures that had been so boring during the tedious hours\r\nthat she had spent with the lawyer, grudging every second of the\r\nglorious September morning that she had had to waste in the library\r\nwhen she was longing to be out of doors, had conveyed nothing to her\r\nbeyond the fact that in future when she wanted anything she would be\r\nput to the trouble of writing out an absurd piece of paper herself,\r\ninstead of leaving the matter in Aubrey\'s hands, as she had done\r\nhitherto.\r\n\r\nShe had hardly understood and had been much embarrassed by the formal\r\nand pedantic congratulations with which the lawyer had concluded his\r\nbusiness statement. She was not aware that she was an object of\r\ncongratulation. It all seemed very stupid and uninteresting. Of real\r\nlife she knew nothing and of the ordinary ties and attachments of\r\nfamily life less than nothing. Aubrey\'s cold, loveless training had\r\ndebarred her from all affection; she had grown up oblivious of it. Love\r\ndid not exist for her; from even the thought of passion she shrank\r\ninstinctively with the same fastidiousness as she did from actual\r\nphysical uncleanliness.\r\n\r\nThat she had awakened an emotion that she did not understand herself in\r\ncertain men had been an annoyance that had become more intolerable with\r\nrepetition. She had hated them and herself impartially, and she had\r\nscorned them fiercely. She had never been so gentle and so human with\r\nany one as she had been with Jim Arbuthnot, and that only because she\r\nwas so radiantly happy that night that not even the distasteful\r\nreminder that she was a woman whom a man coveted was able to disturb\r\nher happiness. But here there was no need to dwell on annoyances or\r\ndistasteful reminders.\r\n\r\nDiana dug her heels into the soft ground with a little wriggle of\r\ncontent; here she would be free from anything that could mar her\r\nperfect enjoyment of life as it appeared to her. Here there was nothing\r\nto spoil her pleasure. Her head had drooped during her thoughts, and\r\nfor the last few minutes her eyes had been fixed on the dusty tips of\r\nher riding-boots. But she raised them now and looked up with a great\r\ncontent in them. It was the happiest day of her life. She had forgotten\r\nthe quarrel with Aubrey. She had put from her the chain of ideas\r\nsuggested by the passing caravan. There was nothing discordant to\r\ndisturb the perfect harmony of her mind.\r\n\r\nA shade beside her made her turn her head. Mustafa Ali salaamed\r\nobsequiously. "It is time to start, Mademoiselle."\r\n\r\nDiana looked up in surprise and then back over her shoulder at the\r\nescort. The men were already mounted. The smile faded from her eyes.\r\nMustafa Ali was guide, but she was head of this expedition; if her\r\nguide had not realised this he would have to do so now. She glanced at\r\nthe watch on her wrist.\r\n\r\n"There is plenty of time," she said coolly.\r\n\r\nMustafa Ali salaamed again. "It is a long ride to reach the oasis where\r\nwe must camp to-night," he insisted hurriedly.\r\n\r\nDiana crossed one brown boot over the other, and scooping up some sand\r\nin the palm of her hand trickled it through her fingers slowly. "Then\r\nwe can ride faster," she replied quietly, looking at the shining\r\nparticles glistening in the sun.\r\n\r\nMustafa Ali made a movement of impatience and persisted doggedly.\r\n"Mademoiselle would do well to start."\r\n\r\nDiana looked up swiftly with angry eyes. Under the man\'s suave manner\r\nand simple words a peremptory tone had crept into his voice. She sat\r\nquite still, her fingers raking the warm sand, and under her haughty\r\nstare the guide\'s eyes wavered and turned away. "We will start when I\r\nchoose, Mustafa Ali," she said brusquely. "You may give orders to your\r\nmen, but you will take your orders from me. I will tell you when I am\r\nready. You may go."\r\n\r\nStill he hesitated, swaying irresolutely backwards and forwards on his\r\nheels.\r\n\r\nDiana snapped her fingers over her shoulder, a trick she had learned\r\nfrom a French officer in Biskra. "I said go!" she repeated sharply. She\r\ntook no notice of his going and did not look back to see what orders he\r\ngave the men. She glanced at her watch again. Perhaps it was growing\r\nlate, perhaps the camp was a longer ride than she had thought; but\r\nMustafa Ali must learn his lesson if they rode till midnight to reach\r\nthe oasis. She pushed her obstinate chin out further and then smiled\r\nagain suddenly. She hoped that the night would fall before they reached\r\ntheir destination. There had been one or two moonlight riding picnics\r\nout from Biskra, and the glamour of the desert nights had gone to\r\nDiana\'s head. This riding into the unknown away from the noisy,\r\nchattering crowd who had spoiled the perfect stillness of the night\r\nwould be infinitely more perfect. She gave a little sigh of regret as\r\nshe thought of it. It was not really practical. Though she would wait\r\nnearly another hour to allow the fact of her authority to sink into\r\nMustafa Ali\'s brain she would have to hasten afterwards to arrive at\r\nthe camp before darkness set in. The men were unused to her ways and\r\nshe to theirs. She would not have Stephens\' help to-night; she would\r\nhave to depend on herself to order everything as she wished it, and it\r\nwas easier done in daylight. One hour would not make much difference.\r\nThe horses had more in them than had been taken out of them this\r\nmorning; they could be pushed along a bit faster with no harm happening\r\nto them. She eyed her watch from time to time with a grin of amusement,\r\nbut suppressed the temptation to look and see how Mustafa Ali was\r\ntaking it, for her action might be seen and misconstrued.\r\n\r\nWhen the time she had set herself was up she rose and walked slowly\r\ntowards the group of Arabs. The guide\'s face was sullen, but she took\r\nno notice, and, when they started, motioned him to her side again with\r\na reference to Biskra that provoked a flow of words. It was the last\r\nplace she wanted to hear of, but it was one of which he spoke the\r\nreadiest, and she knew it was not wise to allow him to remain silent to\r\nsulk. His ill-temper would evaporate with the sound of his own voice.\r\nShe rode forward steadily, silent herself, busy with her own thoughts,\r\nheedless of the voice beside her, and unconscious of the fact when it\r\nbecame silent.\r\n\r\nShe had been quite right about the capabilities of the horses. They\r\nresponded without any apparent effort to the further demand made of\r\nthem. The one in particular that Diana was riding moved in a swift,\r\neasy gallop that was the perfection of motion.\r\n\r\nThey had been riding for some hours when they came to the first oasis\r\nthat had been sighted since leaving the one where the midday halt was\r\nmade. Diana pulled up her horse to look at it, for it was unusually\r\nbeautiful in the luxuriousness and arrangement of its group of palms\r\nand leafy bushes. Some pigeons were cooing softly, hidden from sight\r\namongst the trees, with a plaintive melancholy that somehow seemed in\r\nkeeping with the deserted spot. Beside the well, forming a triangle,\r\nstood what had been three particularly fine palm trees, but the tops\r\nhad been broken off about twenty feet up from the ground, and the\r\nmutilated trunks reared themselves bare and desolate-looking. Diana\r\ntook off her heavy helmet and tossed it to the man behind her, and sat\r\nlooking at the oasis, while the faint breeze that had sprung up stirred\r\nher thick, short hair, and cooled her hot head. The sad notes of the\r\npigeons and the broken palms, that with their unusualness vaguely\r\nsuggested a tragedy, lent an air of mystery to the place that pleased\r\nher.\r\n\r\nShe turned eagerly to Mustafa Ali. "Why did you not arrange for the\r\ncamp to be here? It would have been a long enough ride."\r\n\r\nThe man fidgeted in his saddle, fingering his beard uneasily, his eyes\r\nwandering past Diana\'s and looking at the broken trees. "No man rests\r\nhere, Mademoiselle. It is the place of devils. The curse of Allah is\r\nupon it," he muttered, touching his horse with his heel, and making it\r\nsidle restlessly--an obvious hint that Diana ignored.\r\n\r\n"I like it," she persisted obstinately.\r\n\r\nHe made a quick gesture with his fingers. "It is accursed. Death lurks\r\nbeside those broken palm trees," he said, looking at her curiously.\r\n\r\nShe jerked her head with a sudden smile. "For you, perhaps, but not for\r\nme. Allah\'s curse rests only upon those who fear it. But since you are\r\nafraid, Mustafa Ali, let us go on." She gave a little light laugh, and\r\nMustafa Ali kicked his horse savagely as he followed.\r\n\r\nThe distance before her spread out cleanly with the sharp distinctness\r\nthat precedes the setting sun. She rode on until she began to wonder if\r\nit would indeed be night-fall before she reached her destination. They\r\nhad ridden longer and faster than had ever been intended. It seemed odd\r\nthat they had not overtaken the baggage camels. She looked at her watch\r\nwith a frown. "Where is your caravan, Mustafa Ali?" she called. "I see\r\nno sign of an oasis, and the darkness will come."\r\n\r\n"If Mademoiselle had started earlier----" he said sullenly.\r\n\r\n"If I had started earlier it would still have been too far. To-morrow\r\nwe will arrange it otherwise," she said firmly.\r\n\r\n"To-morrow----" he growled indistinctly.\r\n\r\nDiana looked at him keenly. "What did you say?" she asked haughtily.\r\n\r\nHis hand went to his forehead mechanically. "To-morrow is with Allah!"\r\nhe murmured with unctuous piety.\r\n\r\nA retort trembled on Diana\'s lips, but her attention was distracted\r\nfrom her annoying guide to a collection of black specks far off across\r\nthe desert. They were too far away for her to see clearly, but she\r\npointed to them, peering at them intently. "See!" she cried. "Is that\r\nthe caravan?"\r\n\r\n"As Allah wills!" he replied more piously than before, and Diana\r\nwished, with a sudden feeling of irritation, that he would stop\r\nrelegating his responsibilities to the Deity and take a little more\r\nactive personal interest in his missing camel train.\r\n\r\nThe black specks were moving fast across the level plain. Very soon\r\nDiana saw that it was not the slow, leisurely camels that they were\r\novertaking, but a band of mounted men who were moving swiftly towards\r\nthem. They had seen nobody since the traders\' caravan had passed them\r\nin the morning. For Diana the Arabs that were approaching were even\r\nmore interesting than the caravan had been. She had seen plenty of\r\ncaravans arriving and departing from Biskra, but, though she had seen\r\nsmall parties of tribesmen constantly in the vicinity of the town, she\r\nhad never seen so large a body of mounted men before, nor had she seen\r\nthem as they were here, one with the wild picturesqueness of their\r\nsurroundings. It was impossible to count how many there were, for they\r\nwere riding in close formation, the wind filling their great white\r\ncloaks, making each man look gigantic. Diana\'s interest flamed up\r\nexcitedly. It was like passing another ship upon a hitherto empty sea.\r\nThey seemed to add a desired touch to the grim loneliness of the scene\r\nthat had begun to be a little awe-inspiring. Perhaps she was hungry,\r\nperhaps she was tired, or perhaps she was only annoyed by the bad\r\narrangements of her guide, but before the advent of the mounted Arabs\r\nDiana had been conscious of a feeling of oppression, as if the silent\r\ndesolation of the desert was weighing heavily upon her, but the body of\r\nswiftly moving men and horses had changed the aspect utterly. An\r\natmosphere of life and purpose seemed to have taken the place of the\r\nquiet stagnation that had been before their coming.\r\n\r\nThe distance between the two parties decreased rapidly. Diana, intent\r\non the quickly advancing horsemen, spurred ahead of her guide with\r\nsparkling eyes. They were near enough now to see that the horses were\r\nbeautiful creatures and that each man rode magnificently. They were\r\narmed too, their rifles being held in front of them, not slung on their\r\nbacks as she had seen in Biskra. They passed quite close to her, only a\r\nfew yards away--a solid square, the orderly ranks suggesting training\r\nand discipline that she had not looked for. Not a head turned in her\r\ndirection as they went by and the pace was not slackened. Fretted by\r\nthe proximity of the galloping horses, her own horse reared\r\nimpatiently, but Diana pulled him in, turning in her saddle to watch\r\nthe Arabs pass, her breath coming quick with excitement.\r\n\r\n"What are they?" she called out to Mustafa Ali, who had dropped some\r\nway behind her. But he, too, was looking back at the horsemen, and did\r\nnot seem to hear her question. Her escort had lagged still further\r\nbehind her guide and were some distance away. Diana watched the rapidly\r\nmoving, compact square eagerly with appreciatory eyes--it was a\r\nbeautiful sight. Then she gave a little gasp. The galloping horses had\r\ndrawn level with the last stragglers of her own party, and just beyond\r\nthey stopped suddenly.
