Diana would not have believed it possible that\r\nthey could have stopped so suddenly and in such close formation while\r\ntravelling at such a pace. The tremendous strain on the bridles flung\r\nthe horses far back on their haunches. But there was no time to dwell\r\non the wonderful horsemanship or training of the men. Events moved too\r\nrapidly. The solid square split up and lengthened out into a long line\r\nof two men riding abreast. Wheeling behind the last of Mustafa\'s men\r\nthey came back even faster than they had passed, and circled widely\r\nround Diana and her attendants. Bewildered by this manoeuvre she\r\nwatched them with a puzzled frown, striving to soothe her horse, who\r\nwas nearly frantic with excitement. Twice they galloped round her\r\nlittle band, their long cloaks fluttering, their rifles tossing in\r\ntheir hands. Diana was growing impatient. It was very fine to watch,\r\nbut time and the light were both going. She would have been glad if the\r\ndemonstration had occurred earlier in the day, when there would have\r\nbeen more time to enjoy it. She turned again to Mustafa Ali to suggest\r\nthat they had better try to move on, but he had gone further from her,\r\nback towards his own. She wrestled with her nervous mount, trying to\r\nturn him to join her guide, when a sudden burst of rifle shots made her\r\nstart and her horse bound violently. Then she laughed. That would be\r\nthe end of the demonstration, a parting salute, the _decharge de\r\nmousqueterie_ beloved of the Arab. She turned her head from her\r\nrefractory horse to look at them ride off, and the laugh died away on\r\nher lips. It was not a farewell salute. The rifles that the Arabs were\r\nfiring were not pointing up into the heavens, but aiming straight at\r\nher and her escort. And as she stared with suddenly startled eyes,\r\nunable to do anything with her plunging horse, Mustafa Ali\'s men were\r\nblotted out from her sight, cut off by a band of Arabs who rode between\r\nher and them. Mustafa Ali himself was lying forward on the neck of his\r\nhorse, who was standing quiet amidst the general confusion. Then there\r\ncame another volley, and the guide slid slowly out of his saddle on to\r\nthe ground, and at the same time Diana\'s horse went off with a wild\r\nleap that nearly unseated her.\r\n\r\nUntil they started shooting the thought that the Arabs could be hostile\r\nhad not crossed her mind. She imagined that they were merely showing\r\noff with the childish love of display which she knew was\r\ncharacteristic. The French authorities had been right after all.\r\nDiana\'s first feeling was one of contempt for an administration that\r\nmade possible such an attempt so near civilisation. Her second a\r\nfleeting amusement at the thought of how Aubrey would jeer. But her\r\namusement passed as the real seriousness of the attack came home to\r\nher. For the first time it occurred to her that her guide\'s descent\r\nfrom his saddle was due to a wound and not to the fear that she had at\r\nfirst disgustedly attributed to him. But nobody had seemed to put up\r\nany kind of a fight, she thought wrathfully. She tugged angrily at her\r\nhorse\'s mouth, but the bit was between his teeth and he tore on\r\nfrantically. Her own position made her furious. Her guide was wounded,\r\nhis men surrounded, and she was ignominiously being run away with by a\r\nbolting horse. If she could only turn the wretched animal. It would\r\nonly be a question of ransom, of that she was positive. She must get\r\nback somehow to the others and arrange terms. It was an annoyance, of\r\ncourse, but after all it added a certain piquancy to her trip, it would\r\nbe an experience. It was only a "hold-up." She did not suppose the\r\nArabs had even really meant to hurt any one, but they were excited and\r\nsome one\'s shot, aimed wide, had found an unexpected billet. It could\r\nonly be that. It was too near Biskra for any real danger, she argued\r\nwith herself, still straining on the reins. She would not admit that\r\nthere was any danger, though her heart was beating in a way that it had\r\nnever done before. Then as she hauled ineffectually at the bridle with\r\nall her strength there came from behind her the sound of a long, shrill\r\nwhistle. Her horse pricked up his ears and she was conscious that his\r\npace sensibly lessened. Instinctively she looked behind. A solitary\r\nArab was riding after her and as she looked she realised that his horse\r\nwas gaining on hers. The thought drove every idea of stopping her\r\nrunaway from her and made her dig her spurs into him instead. There was\r\na sinister air of deliberation in the way in which the Arab was\r\nfollowing her; he was riding her down.\r\n\r\nDiana\'s mouth closed firmly and a new keenness came into her steady\r\neyes. It was one thing to go back voluntarily to make terms with the\r\nmen who had attacked her party; it was quite another thing to be\r\ndeliberately chased across the desert by an Arab freebooter. Her\r\nobstinate chin was almost square. Then the shadow of a laugh flickered\r\nin her eyes and curved her mouth. New experiences were crowding in upon\r\nher to-day. She had often wondered what the feelings of a hunted\r\ncreature were. She seemed in a fair way of finding out. She had always\r\nstoutly maintained that the fox enjoyed the run as much as the hounds;\r\nthat remained to be proved, but, in any case, she would give this hound\r\na run for his money. She could ride, and there seemed plenty yet in the\r\nfrightened animal under her. She bent down, lying low against his neck\r\nwith a little, reckless laugh, coaxing him with all her knowledge and\r\nspurring him alternately. But soon her mood changed. She frowned\r\nanxiously as she looked at the last rays of the setting sun. It would\r\nbe dark very soon. She could not go chasing through the night with this\r\ntiresome Arab at her heels. The humour seemed to have died out of the\r\nsituation and Diana began to get angry. In the level country that\r\nsurrounded her there were no natural features that could afford cover\r\nor aid in any way; there seemed nothing for it but to own herself\r\ndefeated and pull up--if she could. An idea of trying to dodge him and\r\nof returning of her own free will was dismissed at once as hopeless.\r\nShe had seen enough in her short glimpse of the Arabs\' tactics when\r\nthey had passed her to know that she was dealing with a finished\r\nhorseman on a perfectly trained horse, and that her idea could never\r\nsucceed. But, perversely, she felt that to that particular Arab\r\nfollowing her she would never give in. She would ride till she dropped,\r\nor the horse did, before that.\r\n\r\nThe whistle came again, and again, in spite of her relentless spurring,\r\nher horse checked his pace. A sudden inspiration came to her. Perhaps\r\nit was the horse she was riding that was the cause of all the trouble.\r\nIt was certainly the Arab\'s whistle that had made it moderate its\r\nspeed; it was responding clearly to a signal that it knew. Her guide\'s\r\nreluctance to give any particulars of his acquisition of the horse came\r\nback to her. There could not be much doubt about it. The animal had\r\nunquestionably been stolen, and either belonged to or was known to the\r\nparty of Arabs who had met them.\r\n\r\nThe _naivete_ that paraded a stolen horse through the desert at\r\nthe risk of meeting its former owner made her smile in spite of her\r\nannoyance, but it was not a pleasant smile, as her thoughts turned from\r\nthe horse to its present owner. The sum of Mustafa Ali\'s delinquencies\r\nwas mounting up fast. But it was his affair, not hers. In the meantime\r\nshe had paid for the horse to ride through the desert, not to be\r\nwaylaid by Arab bandits. Her temper was going fast.\r\n\r\nShe urged the horse on with all her power, but perceptibly he was\r\nslowing up. She flashed another backward look. The Arab was close\r\nbehind her--closer than she had been aware. She had a momentary glimpse\r\nof a big white figure, dark piercing eyes, and white gleaming teeth,\r\nand passionate rage filled her. With no thought of what the\r\nconsequences or retaliation might be, with no thought at all beyond a\r\nwild desire to rid herself of her pursuer, driven by a sudden madness\r\nwhich seemed to rise up in her and which she could not control, she\r\nclutched her revolver and fired twice, full in the face of the man who\r\nwas following her. He did not even flinch and a low laugh of amusement\r\ncame from him. And at the sound of his laugh Diana\'s mouth parched\r\nsuddenly, and a cold shiver rippled across her spine. A strange feeling\r\nthat she had never experienced before went through her. She had missed\r\nagain as she had missed this morning. How, she did not know; it was\r\ninexplicable, but it was a fact, and a fact that left her with a\r\nfeeling of powerlessness. She dropped the useless revolver, trying\r\nvainly to force her horse\'s pace, but inch by inch the fiery chestnut\r\nthat the Arab was riding crept up nearer alongside. She would not turn\r\nto look again, but glancing sideways she could see its small,\r\nwicked-looking head, with flat laid ears and vicious, bloodshot eyes,\r\nlevel with her elbow. For a moment or two it remained there, then with\r\na sudden spurt the chestnut forged ahead, and as it shot past it\r\nswerved close in beside her, and the man, rising in his stirrups and\r\nleaning towards her, flung a pair of powerful arms around her, and,\r\nwith a jerk, swung her clear of the saddle and on to his own horse in\r\nfront of him. His movement had been so quick she was unprepared and\r\nunable to resist. For a moment she was stunned, then her senses came\r\nback to her and she struggled wildly, but, stifled in the thick folds\r\nof the Arab\'s robes, against which her face was crushed, and held in a\r\ngrip that seemed to be slowly suffocating her, her struggles were\r\nfutile. The hard, muscular arm round her hurt her acutely, her ribs\r\nseemed to be almost breaking under its weight and strength, it was\r\nnearly impossible to breathe with the close contact of his body. She\r\nwas unusually strong for a girl, but against this steely strength that\r\nheld her she was helpless. And for a time the sense of her helplessness\r\nand the pain that any resistance to the arm wrapped round her gave her\r\nmade her lie quiet. She felt the Arab check his horse, felt the\r\nchestnut wheel, spinning high on his hind legs, and then bound forward\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nHer feelings were indescribable. She did not know what to think. Her\r\nmind felt jarred. She was unable to frame any thoughts coherently. What\r\nhad happened was so unexpected, so preposterous, that no conclusion\r\nseemed adequate. Only rage filled her--blind, passionate rage against\r\nthe man who had dared to touch her, who had dared to lay his hands on\r\nher, and those hands the hands of a native. A shiver of revulsion ran\r\nthrough her. She was choking with fury, with anger and with disgust.\r\nThe ignominy of her plight hurt her pride badly. She had been\r\noutridden, swept from her saddle as if she were a puppet, and compelled\r\nto bear the proximity of the man\'s own hateful body and the restraint\r\nof his arms. No one had ever dared to touch her before. No one had ever\r\ndared to handle her as she was being handled now. How was it going to\r\nend? Where were they going? With her face hidden she had lost all sense\r\nof direction. She had no idea to what point the horse had turned when\r\nhe had wheeled so suddenly. He was galloping swiftly with continual\r\ndisconcerting bounds that indicated either temper or nerves, but the\r\nman riding him seemed in no way disturbed by his horse\'s behavior. She\r\ncould feel him swaying easily in the saddle, and even the wildest leaps\r\ndid not cause any slackening of the arm around her.\r\n\r\nBut by degrees as she continued to lie still the pressure on her body\r\nwas relieved slightly, and she was able to turn her head a little\r\ntowards the air for which she was almost fainting, but not enough to\r\nenable her to see what was passing around her. She drank in the cool\r\nair eagerly. Though she could not see she knew that the night had come,\r\nthe night that she had hoped would fall before she reached her\r\ndestination, but which now seemed horrible. The fresh strength that the\r\nair gave her fanned the courage that still remained with her.\r\nCollecting all her force she made a sudden desperate spring, trying to\r\nleap clear of the arm that now lay almost loosely about her, her\r\nspurred heels tearing the chestnut\'s flank until he reared\r\nperpendicularly, snorting and trembling. But with a quick sweep of his\r\nlong arm the Arab gathered her back into his hold, still struggling\r\nfiercely. His arms were both round her; he was controlling the maddened\r\nhorse only with the pressure of his knees.\r\n\r\n"Doucement, doucement." She heard the slow, soft voice indistinctly,\r\nfor he was pressing her head again closely to him, and she did not know\r\nif the words were applied to herself or to the horse. She fought to\r\nlift her head, to escape the grip that held her, straining, striving\r\nuntil he spoke again.\r\n\r\n"Lie still, you little fool!" he snarled with sudden vehemence, and\r\nwith brutal hands he forced her to obey him, until she wondered if he\r\nwould leave a single bone unbroken in her body, till further resistance\r\nwas impossible. Gasping for breath she yielded to the strength that\r\noverpowered her, and ceased to struggle. The man seemed to know\r\nintuitively that she was beaten, and turned his undivided attention to\r\nhis horse with the same low laugh of amusement that had sent the\r\nstrange feeling through her when her shots had missed him. It had\r\npuzzled her then, but it grew now with a horrible intensity, until she\r\nknew that it was fear that had come to her for the first time in her\r\nlife--a strange fear that she fought against desperately, but which was\r\ngaining on her with a force that was sapping her strength from her and\r\nmaking her head reel. She did not faint, but her whole body seemed to\r\ngrow nerveless with the sudden realisation of the horror of her\r\nposition.\r\n\r\nAfter that Diana lost all sense of time, as she had already lost all\r\nsense of direction. She did not know if it was minutes or hours that\r\npassed as they still galloped swiftly through the night. She did not\r\nknow if they were alone or if the band of Arabs to which this man\r\nbelonged were riding with them, noiseless over the soft ground. What\r\nhad happened to her guide and his men? Had they been butchered and left\r\nwhere they fell, or were they, too, being hurried unwillingly into some\r\nobscure region of the desert? But for the moment the fate of Mustafa\r\nAli and his companions did not trouble her very much; they had not\r\nplayed a very valiant part in the short encounter, and her own\r\nsituation swamped her mind to the exclusion of everything else.\r\n\r\nThe sense of fear was growing on her. She scorned and derided it. She\r\ntried to convince herself it did not exist, but it did exist, torturing\r\nher with its strangeness and with the thoughts that it engendered. She\r\nhad anticipated nothing like this. She had never thought of a\r\ncontingency that would end so, that would induce a situation before\r\nwhich her courage was shuddering into pieces with the horror that was\r\nopening up before her--a thing that had always seemed a remote\r\nimpossibility that could never touch her, from even the knowledge of\r\nwhich her life with Aubrey had almost shielded her, but which now\r\nloomed near her, forcing its reality upon her till she trembled and\r\ngreat drops of moisture gathered on her forehead.\r\n\r\nThe Arab moved her position once, roughly, but she was glad of the\r\nchange for it freed her head from the stifling folds of his robes. He\r\ndid not speak again--only once when the chestnut shied violently he\r\nmuttered something under his breath. But her satisfaction was\r\nshort-lived. A few minutes afterwards his arm tightened round her once\r\nmore and he twined a fold of his long cloak round her head, blinding\r\nher. And then she understood. The galloping horse was pulled in with\r\nalmost the same suddenness that had amazed her when she had first seen\r\nthe Arabs. She felt him draw her close into his arms and slip down on\r\nto the ground; there were voices around her--confused, unintelligible;\r\nthen they died away as she felt him carry her a few paces. He set her\r\ndown and unwound the covering from her face. The light that shone\r\naround her seemed by contrast dazzling with the darkness that had gone\r\nbefore. Confused, she clasped her hands over her eyes for a moment and\r\nthen looked up slowly. She was in a big, lofty tent, brightly lit by\r\ntwo hanging lamps. But she took no heed of her surroundings; her eyes\r\nwere fixed on the man who had brought her there. He had flung aside the\r\nheavy cloak that enveloped him from head to foot and was standing\r\nbefore her, tall and broad-shouldered, dressed in white flowing robes,\r\na waistcloth embroidered in black and silver wound several times about\r\nhim, and from the top of which showed a revolver that was thrust into\r\nthe folds.\r\n\r\nDiana\'s eyes passed over him slowly till they rested on his brown,\r\nclean-shaven face, surmounted by crisp, close-cut brown hair. It was\r\nthe handsomest and cruellest face that she had ever seen. Her gaze was\r\ndrawn instinctively to his. He was looking at her with fierce burning\r\neyes that swept her until she felt that the boyish clothes that covered\r\nher slender limbs were stripped from her, leaving the beautiful white\r\nbody bare under his passionate stare.\r\n\r\nShe shrank back, quivering, dragging the lapels of her riding jacket\r\ntogether over her breast with clutching hands, obeying an impulse that\r\nshe hardly understood.\r\n\r\n"Who are you?" she gasped hoarsely.\r\n\r\n"I am the Sheik Ahmed Ben Hassan."\r\n\r\nThe name conveyed nothing. She had never heard it before. She had\r\nspoken without thinking in French, and in French he replied to her.\r\n\r\n"Why have you brought me here?" she asked, fighting down the fear that\r\nwas growing more terrible every moment.\r\n\r\nHe repeated her words with a slow smile. "Why have I brought you here?\r\nBon Dieu! Are you not woman enough to know?"\r\n\r\nShe shrank back further, a wave of colour rushing into her face that\r\nreceded immediately, leaving her whiter than she had been before. Her\r\neyes fell under the kindling flame in his. "I don\'t know what you\r\nmean," she whispered faintly, with shaking lips.\r\n\r\n"I think you do." He laughed softly, and his laugh frightened her more\r\nthan anything he had said. He came towards her, and although she was\r\nswaying on her feet, desperately she tried to evade him, but with a\r\nquick movement he caught her in his arms.\r\n\r\nTerror, agonising, soul-shaking terror such as she had never imagined,\r\ntook hold of her. The flaming light of desire burning in his eyes\r\nturned her sick and faint. Her body throbbed with the consciousness of\r\na knowledge that appalled her. She understood his purpose with a horror\r\nthat made each separate nerve in her system shrink against the\r\nunderstanding that had come to her under the consuming fire of his\r\nardent gaze, and in the fierce embrace that was drawing her shaking\r\nlimbs closer and closer against the man\'s own pulsating body. She\r\nwrithed in his arms as he crushed her to him in a sudden access of\r\npossessive passion. His head bent slowly down to her, his eyes burned\r\ndeeper, and, held immovable, she endured the first kiss she had ever\r\nreceived. And the touch of his scorching lips, the clasp of his arms,\r\nthe close union with his warm, strong body robbed her of all strength,\r\nof all power of resistance.\r\n\r\nWith a great sob her eyes closed wearily, the hot mouth pressed on hers\r\nwas like a narcotic, drugging her almost into insensibility. Numbly she\r\nfelt him gather her high up into his arms, his lips still clinging\r\nclosely, and carry her across the tent through curtains into an\r\nadjoining room. He laid her down on soft cushions. "Do not make me wait\r\ntoo long," he whispered, and left her.\r\n\r\nAnd the whispered words sent a shock through her that seemed to wrench\r\nher deadened nerves apart, galvanising her into sudden strength. She\r\nsprang up with wild, despairing eyes, and hands clenched frantically\r\nacross her heaving breast; then, with a bitter cry, she dropped on to\r\nthe floor, her arms flung out across the wide, luxurious bed. It was\r\nnot true! It was not true! It could not be--this awful thing that had\r\nhappened to her--not to her, Diana Mayo! It was a dream, a ghastly\r\ndream that would pass and free her from this agony. Shuddering, she\r\nraised her head. The strange room swam before her eyes. Oh, God! It was\r\nnot a dream. It was real, it was an actual fact from which there was no\r\nescape. She was trapped, powerless, defenceless, and behind the heavy\r\ncurtains near her was the man waiting to claim what he had taken. Any\r\nmoment he might come; the thought sent her shivering closer to the\r\nground with limbs that trembled uncontrollably. Her courage, that had\r\nfaced dangers and even death without flinching, broke down before the\r\nhorror that awaited her. It was inevitable; there was no help to be\r\nexpected, no mercy to be hoped for. She had felt the crushing strength\r\nagainst which she was helpless. She would struggle, but it would be\r\nuseless; she would fight, but it would make no difference. Within the\r\ntent she was alone, ready to his hand like a snared animal; without,\r\nthe place was swarming with the man\'s followers. There was nowhere she\r\ncould turn, there was no one she could turn to. The certainty of the\r\naccomplishment of what she dreaded crushed her with its surety. All\r\npower of action was gone. She could only wait and suffer in the\r\ncomplete moral collapse that overwhelmed her, and that was rendered\r\ngreater by her peculiar temperament. Her body was aching with the grip\r\nof his powerful arms, her mouth was bruised with his savage kisses. She\r\nclenched her hands in anguish. "Oh, God!" she sobbed, with scalding\r\ntears that scorched her cheeks. "Curse him! Curse him!"\r\n\r\nAnd with the words on her lips he came, silent, noiseless, to her side.\r\nWith his hands on her shoulders he forced her to her feet. His eyes\r\nwere fierce, his stern mouth parted in a cruel smile, his deep, slow\r\nvoice half angry, half impatiently amused. "Must I be valet, as well as\r\nlover?"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\n\r\n\r\nThe warm sunshine was flooding the tent when Diana awoke from the deep\r\nsleep of exhaustion that had been almost insensibility, awoke to\r\nimmediate and complete remembrance. One quick, fearful glance around\r\nthe big room assured her that she was alone. She sat up slowly, her\r\neyes shadowy with pain, looking listlessly at the luxurious\r\nappointments of the tent. She looked dry-eyed, she had no tears left.\r\nThey had all been expended when she had grovelled at his feet imploring\r\nthe mercy he had not accorded her. She had fought until the unequal\r\nstruggle had left her exhausted and helpless in his arms, until her\r\nwhole body was one agonised ache from the brutal hands that forced her\r\nto compliance, until her courageous spirit was crushed by the\r\nrealisation of her own powerlessness, and by the strange fear that the\r\nman himself had awakened in her, which had driven her at last moaning\r\nto her knees. And the recollection of her abject prayers and weeping\r\nsupplications filled her with a burning shame. She loathed herself with\r\nbitter contempt. Her courage had broken down; even her pride had failed\r\nher.\r\n\r\nShe wound her arms about her knees and hid her face against them.\r\n"Coward! Coward!" she whispered fiercely. Why had she not scorned him?\r\nOr why had she not suffered all that he had done to her in silence? It\r\nwould have pleased him less than the frenzied entreaties that had only\r\nprovoked the soft laugh that made her shiver each time she heard it.\r\nShe shivered now. "I thought I was brave," she murmured brokenly. "I am\r\nonly a coward, a craven."\r\n\r\nShe lifted her head at last and looked around her. The room was a\r\ncurious mixture of Oriental luxury and European comfort. The lavish\r\nsumptuousness of the furnishings suggested subtly an unrestrained\r\nindulgence, the whole atmosphere was voluptuous, and Diana shrank from\r\nthe impression it conveyed without exactly understanding the reason.\r\nThere was nothing that jarred artistically, the rich hangings all\r\nharmonised, there were no glaring incongruities such as she had seen in\r\nnative palaces in India. And everything on which her eyes rested drove\r\nhome relentlessly the hideous fact of her position. His things were\r\neverywhere. On a low, brass-topped table by the bed was the half-smoked\r\ncigarette he had had between his lips when he came to her. The pillow\r\nbeside her still bore the impress of his head. She looked at it with a\r\ngrowing horror in her eyes until an uncontrollable shuddering seized\r\nher and she cowered down, smothering the cry that burst from her in the\r\nsoft pillows and dragging the silken coverings up around her as if\r\ntheir thin shelter were a protection. She lived again through every\r\nmoment of the past night until thought was unendurable, until she felt\r\nthat she would go mad, until at last, worn out, she fell asleep.\r\n\r\nIt was midday when she awoke again. This time she was not alone. A\r\nyoung Arab girl was sitting on the rug beside her looking at her with\r\nsoft brown eyes of absorbed interest As Diana sat up she rose to her\r\nfeet, salaaming, with a timid smile.\r\n\r\n"I am Zilah, to wait on Madame," she said shyly in stumbling French,\r\nholding out a wrap that Diana recognised with wonder as her own. She\r\nlooked behind her. Her suit-cases were lying near her, open, partially\r\nunpacked. The missing baggage camels had been captured first, then. She\r\nwas at least to be allowed the use of her own belongings. A gleam of\r\nanger shot into her tired eyes and she swung round with a sharp\r\nquestion; but the Arab girl shook her head uncomprehendingly, drawing\r\nback with frightened eyes; and to all further questions she remained\r\nsilent, with down-drooping mouth like a scared child. She was little\r\nmore. She evidently only half understood what was said to her and could\r\ngive no answer to what she did understand, and turned away with obvious\r\nrelief when Diana stopped speaking. She went across the tent and pulled\r\naside a curtain leading into a bathroom that was as big and far better\r\nequipped than the one that Diana had had in the Indian tent, and which,\r\nup to now, had seemed the last word in comfort and luxury. Though the\r\ngirl\'s knowledge of French was limited her hands were deft enough, but\r\nher ignorance of the intricacies of a European woman\'s toilette was\r\nvery apparent, and constantly provoked in her a girlish giggle that\r\nchanged hurriedly to a startled gravity when Diana looked at her.\r\nLaughter was very far from Diana, but she could not help smiling now\r\nand again at her funny mistakes.\r\n\r\nThe girl, with her big, wondering eyes, her shy, hesitating French and\r\nchildish curiosity, in some indefinable way gave back to Diana the\r\nself-control that had slipped from her. Her pride reasserted itself,\r\nrigidly suppressing any sign of feeling or emotion that could be\r\nnoticed by the gentle, inquisitive eyes fixed on her.\r\n\r\nThe hot bath that took the soreness out of her limbs brought back the\r\ncolour to her face and lips. She even tubbed her head, rubbing the\r\nglistening curls dry with fierce vigour, striving to rid herself of the\r\ncontamination that seemed to have saturated her. Yet the robes against\r\nwhich they had been pressed were spotless, and the hands that had held\r\nher were fastidiously clean, even to the well-kept nails.\r\n\r\nShe came back into the bedroom to find Zilah on her knees poring over\r\nher scanty but diverse wardrobe with bewilderment, fingering the\r\nevening dresses with shy hands, and finally submitting tentatively to\r\nDiana the tweed skirt that had been packed with her other things for\r\nthe journey when Oran should be reached. But Diana put it aside, and\r\npointed to the riding clothes she had worn the previous day. In them\r\nshe felt more able to face what might be before her, the associations\r\nconnected with them seemed to give her moral strength, in them she\r\nwould feel herself again--Diana the boy, not the shivering piece of\r\nwomanhood that had been born with tears and agony last night. She bit\r\nher lip as she stamped her foot down into the long boot.\r\n\r\nShe sent the girl away at last, and noticed that she avoided passing\r\ninto the adjoining room, but vanished instead through the curtains\r\nleading into the bathroom. Did that mean that in the outer room the\r\nArab Sheik was waiting? The thought banished the self-control she had\r\nregained and sent her weakly on to the side of the bed with her face\r\nhidden in her hands. Was he there? Her questions to the little\r\nwaiting-girl had only been concerned with the whereabouts of the camp\r\nto which she had been brought and also of the fate of the caravan; of\r\nthe man himself she had not been able to bring herself to speak. The\r\nstrange fear that he had inspired in her filled her with rage and\r\nhumiliation. The thought of seeing him again brought a shame that was\r\nunspeakable. But she conquered the agitation that threatened to grow\r\nbeyond restraint, pride helping her again. It was better to face the\r\ninevitable of her own free will than be fetched whether she would or\r\nnot. For she knew now the strength of the man who had abducted her,\r\nknew that physically she was helpless against him. She raised her head\r\nand listened. It was very silent in the next room. Perhaps she was to\r\nbe allowed a further respite. She jerked her head impatiently at her\r\nown hesitation. "Coward!" she whispered again contemptuously, and flung\r\nacross the room. But at the curtains she halted for a moment, then with\r\nset face drew them aside and went through.\r\n\r\nThe respite had been granted, the room appeared to be empty. But as she\r\ncrossed the thick rugs her heart leapt suddenly into her throat, for\r\nshe became aware of a man standing in the open doorway. His back was\r\nturned to her, but in a moment she saw that the short, slim figure in\r\nwhite linen European clothes bore no resemblance to the tall Arab she\r\nhad expected to see. She thought her footsteps were noiseless, but he\r\nturned with a little quick bow. A typical Frenchman with narrow, alert,\r\nclean-shaven face, sleek black hair and dark restless eyes. His legs\r\nwere slightly bowed and he stooped a little; his appearance was that of\r\na jockey with the manners of a well-trained servant. Diana coloured\r\nhotly under his glance, but his eyes were lowered instantly.\r\n\r\n"Madame is doubtless ready for lunch." He spoke rapidly, but his voice\r\nwas low and pleasant. His movements were as quick and as quiet as his\r\nvoice, and in a dream Diana found herself in a few moments before a\r\nlunch that was perfectly cooked and daintily served. The man hovered\r\nabout her solicitously, attending to her wants with dexterous hands and\r\nwatchful eyes that anticipated every need. She was bewildered, faint\r\nfrom want of food, everything seemed unreal. For the moment she could\r\njust sit still and be waited on by the soft-footed, soft-spoken\r\nmanservant who seemed such a curious adjunct to the household of an\r\nArab chief.\r\n\r\n"Monseigneur begs that you will excuse him until this evening. He will\r\nreturn in time for dinner," he murmured as he handed her a cous-cous.\r\n\r\nDiana looked up blankly. "Monseigneur?"\r\n\r\n"My master. The Sheik."\r\n\r\nShe flushed scarlet and her face hardened. Hypocritical, Oriental beast\r\nwho "begged to be excused"! She refused the last dish curtly, and as\r\nthe servant carried it away she propped her elbows on the table and\r\nrested her aching head on her hands. A headache was among the new\r\nexperiences that had overwhelmed her since the day before. Suffering in\r\nany form was new to her, and her hatred of the man who had made her\r\nsuffer grew with every breath she drew.\r\n\r\nThe Frenchman came back with coffee and cigarettes. He held a match for\r\nher, coaxing the reluctant flame with patience that denoted long\r\nexperience with inferior sulphur.\r\n\r\n"Monseigneur dines at eight. At what hour will Madame have tea?" he\r\nasked, as he cleared away and folded up the table.\r\n\r\nDiana choked back the sarcastic retort that sprang to her lips. The\r\nman\'s quiet, deferential manner, that refused to see anything\r\nextraordinary in her presence in his master\'s camp, was almost harder\r\nto bear than flagrant impertinence would have been. That she could have\r\ndealt with; this left her tingling with a feeling of impotence, as if a\r\nnet were gradually closing round her in whose entangling meshes her\r\nvaunted liberty was not only threatened, but which seemed destined even\r\nto stifle her very existence. She pulled her racing thoughts up with a\r\njerk. She must not think if she was going to keep any hold over herself\r\nat all. She gave him an answer indifferently and turned her back on\r\nhim. When she looked again he was gone, and she heaved a sigh of\r\nrelief. She had chafed under his watchful eyes until the feeling of\r\nrestraint had grown unbearable.\r\n\r\nShe breathed more freely now that he was gone, flinging up her head and\r\njerking her shoulders back with an angry determination to conquer the\r\nfear that made her ashamed. Natural curiosity had been struggling with\r\nher other emotions, and she gave way to it now to try and turn the\r\nchannel of her thoughts from the fixed direction in which they tended,\r\nand wandered round the big room. The night before she had taken in\r\nnothing of her surroundings, her eyes had been held only by the man who\r\nhad dominated everything. Here, also, were the same luxurious\r\nappointments as in the sleeping-room. She had knowledge enough to\r\nappreciate that the rugs and hangings were exquisite, the former were\r\nPersian and the latter of a thick black material, heavily embroidered\r\nin silver. The main feature of the room was a big black divan heaped\r\nwith huge cushions covered with dull black silk. Beside the divan,\r\nspread over the Persian rugs, were two unusually large black bearskins,\r\nthe mounted heads converging. At one end of the tent was a small\r\ndoorway, a little portable writing-table. There were one or two Moorish\r\nstools heaped with a motley collection of ivories and gold and silver\r\ncigarette cases and knick-knacks, and against the partition that\r\nseparated the two rooms stood a quaintly carved old wooden chest.\r\nThough the furniture was scanty and made the tent seem even more\r\nspacious than it really was, the whole room had an air of barbaric\r\nsplendour. The somber hangings gleaming with thick silver threads\r\nseemed to Diana like a studied theatrical effect, a setting against\r\nwhich the Arab\'s own white robes should contrast more vividly; she\r\nremembered the black and silver waistcloth she had seen swathed round\r\nhim, with curling scornful lip. There was a strain of vanity in all\r\nnatives, she generalised contemptuously. Doubtless it pleased this\r\nnative\'s conceit to carry out the colour scheme of his tent even in his\r\nclothes, and pose among the sable cushions of the luxurious divan to\r\nthe admiration of his retainers. She made a little exclamation of\r\ndisgust, and turned from the soft seductiveness of the big couch with\r\ndisdain.\r\n\r\nShe crossed the tent to the little bookcase and knelt beside it\r\ncuriously. What did a Francophile-Arab read? Novels, probably, that\r\nwould harmonise with the atmosphere that she dimly sensed in her\r\nsurroundings. But it was not novels that filled the bookcase. They were\r\nbooks of sport and travel with several volumes on veterinary surgery.\r\nThey were all in French, and had all been frequently handled, many of\r\nthem had pencilled notes in the margins written in Arabic. One shelf\r\nwas filled entirely with the works of one man, a certain Vicomte Raoul\r\nde Saint Hubert. With the exception of one novel, which Diana only\r\nglanced at hastily; they were all books of travel. From the few\r\nscribbled words in the front of each Diana could see that they had all\r\nbeen sent to the Arab by the author himself--one even was dedicated to\r\n"My friend, Ahmed Ben Hassan, Sheik of the Desert." She put the books\r\nback with a puzzled frown. She wished, with a feeling that she could\r\nnot fathom, that they had been rather what she had imagined. The\r\nevidence of education and unlooked-for tastes in the man they belonged\r\nto troubled her. It was an unexpected glimpse into the personality of\r\nthe Arab that had captured her was vaguely disquieting, for it\r\nsuggested possibilities that would not have existed in a raw native, or\r\none only superficially coated with a veneer of civilisation. He seemed\r\nto become infinitely more sinister, infinitely more horrible. She\r\nlooked at her watch with sudden apprehension. The day was wearing away\r\nquickly. Soon he would come. Her breath came quick and short and the\r\ntears welled up in her eyes.\r\n\r\n"I mustn\'t! I mustn\'t!" she whispered in a kind of desperation. "If I\r\ncry again I shall go mad." She forced them back, and crossing to the\r\nbig black divan that she had scorned before dropped down among the soft\r\ncushions. She was so tired, and her head throbbed persistently.\r\n\r\nShe was asleep when the servant brought tea, but she started up as he\r\nput the tray on a stool beside her.\r\n\r\n"It is Madame\'s own tea. If she will be good enough to say if it is\r\nmade to her taste," he said anxiously, as if his whole happiness was\r\ncontained in the tiny teapot at which he was frowning deprecatingly.\r\n\r\nHis assiduity jarred on Diana\'s new-found jangling nerves. She\r\nrecognised that he was sincere in his efforts to please her, but just\r\nnow they only seemed an added humiliation. She longed to shout "Go\r\naway!" like an angry schoolboy, but she managed to give him the\r\ninformation he wanted, and putting cigarettes and matches by her he\r\nwent out with a little smile of satisfaction. The longing for fresh air\r\nand the desire to see what place she had been brought to grew\r\nirresistible as the evening came nearer. She went to the open doorway.\r\nA big awning stretched before it, supported on lances. She stepped out\r\nfrom under its shade and looked about her wonderingly. It was a big\r\noasis--bigger than any she had seen. In front of the tent there was an\r\nopen space with a thick belt of palm trees beyond. The rest of the camp\r\nlay behind the Chief\'s tent. The place was alive with men and horses.\r\nThere were some camels in the distance, but it was the horses that\r\nstruck Diana principally. They were everywhere, some tethered; some\r\nwandering loose, some exercising in the hands of grooms. Mounted Arabs\r\non the outskirts of the oasis crossed her view occasionally. There were\r\ngroups of men engaged on various duties all around her. Those who went\r\nby near her salaamed as they passed, but took no further notice of her.\r\nA strange look came into Diana\'s eyes. This was the desert indeed, the\r\ndesert as she had never expected to see it, the desert as few could\r\nexpect to see it. But the cost! She shuddered, then turned at a sudden\r\nnoise near her. A biting, screaming chestnut fury was coming past close\r\nto the tent, taking complete charge of the two men who clung, yelling,\r\nto his head. He was stripped, but Diana recognised him at once. The one\r\nbrief view she had had of his small, vicious head as he shot past her\r\nelbow the evening before was written on her brain for all time. He came\r\nto a halt opposite Diana, refusing to move, his ears laid close to his\r\nhead, quivering all over, snatching continually at his grooms, who\r\nseemed unable to cope with him. Once he swung up on his hind legs and\r\nhis cruel teeth flashed almost into the face of one of the men, who was\r\ntaken off his guard, and who dropped on to the ground, rolling out of\r\nthe way with a howl that provoked a shout of laughter from a knot of\r\nArabs who had gathered to watch the usual evening eccentricities of the\r\nchestnut. The French servant, coming from behind the tent, stopped to\r\nspeak to the man as he picked himself up and made a grab at the horse\'s\r\nhead, and then turned to Diana with his pleasant smile.\r\n\r\n"He is rightly named Shaitan, Madame, for he is assuredly possessed of\r\na devil," he said, indicating the chestnut, who, at that moment, with a\r\nviolent plunge, broke away from the men who were holding him and headed\r\nfor the edge of the oasis with the Arabs streaming after him. "The\r\nmounted men will catch him," he added with a little laugh, in response\r\nto Diana\'s exclamation.\r\n\r\n"Is he amusing himself, or is it really vice?" she asked.\r\n\r\n"Pure vice, Madame. He has killed three men."\r\n\r\nDiana looked at him incredulously, for his tone was casual and his\r\nmanner did not indicate any undue feeling.\r\n\r\n"He ought to be shot," she said indignantly.\r\n\r\nThe man shrugged. "Monseigneur is fond of him," he said quietly.\r\n\r\nAnd so because Monseigneur was fond of him the vicious animal was\r\nsurrounded with every care that his master\'s pleasure might not be\r\ninterfered with. Evidently the lives of his wretched people were of\r\nless value to him than that of a favourite horse. It sounded compatible\r\nwith the mercilessness she had herself experienced. What she would not\r\nhave believed yesterday to-day seemed terribly credible. The courage\r\nthat the relief of his absence brought back was sinking fast, as fast\r\nas the red ball glowing in the heavens was sinking down towards the\r\nhorizon. She turned from her own fearful thoughts to look at some more\r\nhorses that were being led away to the lines on the other side of the\r\ncamp.\r\n\r\n"The horses are magnificent, but they are bigger than any Arabs I have\r\nseen before."\r\n\r\n"They are a special breed, Madame," replied the Frenchman. "The tribe\r\nhas been famous for them for generations. Monseigneur\'s horses are\r\nknown through all the Barbary States, and as far as France," he added,\r\nwith a little accent of pride creeping into his voice.\r\n\r\nDiana looked at him speculatively. There was an inflection in his voice\r\neach time he mentioned his master that indicated a devotion that she\r\nwas unable to accredit to the brute for whose treatment she was still\r\nsuffering. But her thoughts were broken into abruptly.\r\n\r\n"There is Monseigneur," said the servant suddenly. He spoke as if she,\r\ntoo, must be glad of his coming. Did the valet imagine for one moment\r\nthat she was here of her own free will? Or was it all a part of the\r\nhypocrisy in which she seemed to be enveloped? She flashed one glance\r\nat the horseman riding through the belt of trees that fringed the oasis\r\nand an icy perspiration chilled her from head to foot. She shrank back\r\nunder the awning and into the coolness of the tent, raging against the\r\nmastering fear that she could not overcome. But just inside the open\r\ndoorway she stood firm; even her fear could make her go no further. She\r\nwould meet him here, not cowering into the inner room like a trembling\r\ncreature skulking in the furthest corner of its cage. That much pride\r\nat least was left.\r\n\r\nFrom the shelter of the tent she watched the troop arrive at the open\r\nspace before her. The horse the Sheik was riding was jet black, and\r\nDiana looked from the beautiful creature\'s satiny coat to the man\'s\r\nwhite robes with angry contempt.\r\n\r\n"Black and white! Black and white! Mountebank!" she muttered through\r\nher clenched teeth. Then as he swung to the ground every thought fell\r\nfrom her but the terror he inspired. She waited, breathless, the swift\r\nracing of her heart an actual physical pain.\r\n\r\nHe lingered, fondling the great black horse, and even after it had been\r\nled away he stood looking after it, talking to a tall young Arab who\r\nhad ridden in with him. At last he turned and came leisurely towards\r\nthe tent. He paused at the door to speak to the Frenchman, a\r\npicturesque, barbaric figure, with flowing robes and great white cloak,\r\nthe profile of his lean face clean cut against the evening sky, the\r\nhaughty poise of his head emphasised by the attitude in which he was\r\nstanding, arrogant, dominating. He moved his hands when he spoke with\r\nquick, expressive gestures, but his voice was slow and soft, pitched in\r\na deep musical key, but with all its softness unmistakably\r\nauthoritative. He pointed with outstretched, steady hand to something\r\nbeyond her line of vision, and as he turned to enter the tent he\r\nlaughed softly, and she shivered involuntarily. Then he swept in, and\r\nshe drew back from him with lowered eyes. She would not look at him;\r\nshe would not meet his look. His presence was an offence, she was\r\nscorched with shame. Every fibre of her being cried out in protest at\r\nhis proximity. She wished with passionate fierceness that she could\r\ndie. She shook feverishly and caught her quivering lip between her\r\nteeth to keep it still, and the red-gold curls lay wet against her\r\nforehead. Her breast heaved stormily with the rapid beating of her\r\nheart, but she held herself proudly erect. He crossed the tent with a\r\nlong noiseless stride.\r\n\r\n"I hope that Gaston took care of you properly and gave you everything\r\nthat you wanted?" he said easily, stooping to a little table to light a\r\ncigarette. The coolness of his words and manner were like a dash of\r\ncold water. She had been prepared for anything but this calm\r\nnonchalance in a situation that was intolerable. His tone conveyed the\r\nperfunctory regret of a host for an unavoidable absence. Her fear gave\r\nway to rage, her body stiffened, her hands clenched.\r\n\r\n"Is it not time that this ended? Haven\'t you done enough?" she burst\r\nout passionately. "Why have you committed this outrage?"\r\n\r\nA thin thread of smoke drifted towards her, as if the hand holding the\r\ncigarette had moved in her direction in one of the gestures that she\r\nhad noticed outside, but there was no answer. His silence infuriated\r\nher and she grew utterly reckless.\r\n\r\n"Do you think that you can keep me here, you fool? That I can vanish\r\ninto the desert and no notice be taken of my disappearance--that no\r\ninquiries will be made?"\r\n\r\n"There will be no inquiries," he answered calmly.\r\n\r\nShe ground the heel of her boot into the soft carpet. "There\r\n_will_ be inquiries," she choked furiously. "I am not such a\r\nnonentity that nothing will be done when I am missed. The English\r\nauthorities will make the French Government find out who is\r\nresponsible, and you will have to pay for what you have done."\r\n\r\nHe laughed--the little amused laugh that sent the same cold feeling of\r\ndread through her that she had felt the day before.\r\n\r\n"The French Government has no jurisdiction over me. I am not subject to\r\nit. I am an independent chief, my own master. I recognise no\r\ngovernment. My tribe obey me and only me."\r\n\r\nHer shaking fingers found the handkerchief for which they were groping,\r\nand she wiped the moisture that had gathered on the palms of her hands.\r\n\r\n"When I am missed----" she began desperately, trying to keep a bold\r\nfront, but her assurance was leaving her.\r\n\r\n"You will not be missed for so long that it will be too late," he\r\nreplied drily.\r\n\r\n"Too late! What do you mean?" she gasped.\r\n\r\n"Your own plans will stop any possibility of inquiry for some time to\r\ncome." He paused, and behind her, Diana heard him strike another match.\r\nThe banal little incident nearly snapped her nerves that were stretched\r\nto breaking-point. She put her hands to her head to try and stop the\r\nthrobbing in her temples.\r\n\r\n"You engaged a caravan in charge of Mustafa Ali," he went on evenly,\r\n"to travel in the desert for a month. You set out from Biskra, but your\r\nintention was at the end of the time to travel northward to Oran and\r\nthere dismiss the caravan. From there you were to cross to Marseilles,\r\nthen to Cherbourg, where you would embark for America to follow your\r\nbrother, who has already started."\r\n\r\nShe listened breathlessly with an ever-increasing fear growing in her\r\neyes. The slow, casual voice detailing her itinerary with the quiet\r\ncertainty of perfect knowledge filled her with a terror that made her\r\nwant to scream. She swayed a little as she stood, her eyes fixed on the\r\nendless strip of desert and gold-flecked sky visible through the\r\nopening of the tent, but she saw nothing of the undulating sand, nor\r\nthe red glory of the setting sun.\r\n\r\n"How do you know--all--this?" she whispered with dry lips that\r\ntrembled.\r\n\r\n"I wished to know. It was quite simple." The answer was given\r\ncarelessly, and again the thin thread of smoke drifted across her face.\r\n\r\nHer anger flamed up again. "Is it money that you want? Are you holding\r\nme for ransom?" But her scornful voice faltered and died away on the\r\nlast word, and it did not need his silence to convince her that it was\r\nno question of ransom. She had only spoken to try and stifle the inner\r\nconviction that grew despite her efforts to crush it. Her hands were\r\nlocked together tightly, her eyes still staring out unseeing at the\r\nwonderful sunset. She felt dazed, hopeless, like a fugitive who has\r\nturned into a cul-de-sac, hemmed in on every side; there seemed no way\r\nout, no loophole of escape. She wrung her hands convulsively and a\r\ngreat shudder shook her. Then in her despair a faint ray of hope came.\r\n\r\n"Mustafa Ali, or one of the caravan men may have given the alarm\r\nalready in Biskra--if you have not--murdered them all," she whispered\r\njerkily.\r\n\r\n"I have not murdered them all," he rejoined shortly, "but Mustafa Ali\r\nwill not give any alarm in Biskra."\r\n\r\n"Why?" She tried to keep silent, but the question was forced from her,\r\nand she waited tense for his answer. Tales of ruthless Arab cruelty\r\nsurged through her mind. What had been the fate of the unfortunate\r\ncaravan leader? Her eyes closed and her throat grew dry.\r\n\r\n"There was no need for any murder," he continued sarcastically. "When\r\nyou come to know me better you will realise that I do not leave too\r\nmuch to chance. \'All things are with Allah, blessed be his name.\' Good!\r\nBut it is well to remember that Allah does not always concern himself\r\nwith the affairs of men, and arrange accordingly. If I had left this\r\naffair to chance there might very easily have been, as you suggest,\r\nmurder done--though we do not call it murder in the desert. It was very\r\nsimple. _Voyons_! You paid Mustafa Ali well to guide you in the\r\ndesert. I paid him better to lead you to me. I paid him well enough to\r\nmake him content to remove himself from Biskra, where awkward questions\r\nmight be asked, to another sphere of usefulness where he is not known,\r\nand where he can build up for himself a new reputation as a caravan\r\nleader."\r\n\r\nThere was another silence and her hands went groping to her throat. It\r\nhad been no chance affair then--no accidental meeting that the Arab\r\nchief had turned to his own account, but an organised outrage that had\r\nbeen carefully planned from the beginning. From the very outset she had\r\nbeen a dupe. She ground her teeth with rage. Her suave, subservient\r\nguide had been leading her the whole time, not in the direction that\r\nhad been mapped out in Biskra, but towards the man who had bought him\r\nto betray his trust. Mustafa Ali\'s shifting eyes, his desire to hurry\r\nher from the oasis where they had rested at mid-day, his tone were all\r\nexplained. He had acted well. The last touch--the imaginary wound that\r\nhad toppled him slowly out of his saddle had been a masterpiece, she\r\nreflected bitterly. Nothing had been omitted to make the attempt a\r\nsuccess. The horse that had been given her to ride was the Sheik\'s\r\nbeyond all doubt, trained to his whistle. Even her revolver had been\r\ntampered with. She had not missed, as she had thought. She remembered\r\nthe noise, the fleeting vision she had had in the hotel at Biskra. It\r\nhad been some one in her room, Mustafa Ali himself, or one of his men,\r\nwho had stolen in and substituted the blank cartridges. The possibility\r\nof Aubrey changing his mind and accompanying her must also have been\r\nthought of, for the Sheik had provided against the resistance that\r\nwould certainly have then been made by the number of followers he had\r\nbrought with him--a large enough force to frustrate easily any\r\nattempted opposition to the attack.\r\n\r\nThe net that she had felt closing round her earlier in the afternoon\r\nseemed wrapped round her now inextricably, drawing tighter and tighter,\r\nsmothering her. She gasped for breath. The sinking sun seemed suddenly\r\nto leap up wildly into the heavens; then she pulled herself together\r\nwith a tremendous effort. "Why have you done this?" she murmured\r\nfaintly.\r\n\r\nThen for a moment her heart stood still, her eyes dilating. He had come\r\nclose behind her, and she waited in an agony, until he caught her to\r\nhim, crushing her against him, forcing her head back on his arm.\r\n\r\n"Because I wanted you. Because one day in Biskra, four weeks ago, I saw\r\nyou for a few moments, long enough to know that I wanted you. And what\r\nI want I take. You played into my hands. You arranged a tour in the\r\ndesert. The rest was easy."\r\n\r\nHer eyes were shut, the long dark lashes quivering on her pale cheeks\r\nso that she could not see his face, but she felt him draw her closer to\r\nhim and then his fierce kisses on her mouth. She struggled frantically,\r\nbut she was helpless, and he laughed softly as he kissed her lips, her\r\nhair, her eyes passionately. He stood quite still, but she felt the\r\nheavy beating of his heart under her cheek, and understood dimly the\r\npassion that she had aroused in him. She had experienced his tremendous\r\nstrength. She realised from what he had told her that he recognised no\r\nlaw beyond his own wishes, and was prepared to go to any lengths to\r\nfulfil them. She knew that her life was in his hands, that he could\r\nbreak her with his lean brown fingers like a toy is broken, and all at\r\nonce she felt pitifully weak and frightened. She was utterly in his\r\npower and at his mercy--the mercy of an Arab who was merciless.\r\n\r\nShe gave in suddenly, lying quiet in his arms. She had touched the\r\nlowest depths of degradation; he could do nothing more to her than he\r\nhad done. For the moment she could fight no further, she was worn out\r\nand utterly weary. A numb feeling of despair came over her and with it\r\na sense of unreality, as if it were a hideous nightmare from which she\r\nwould wake, for the truth seemed too impossible, the setting too\r\ntheatrical. The man himself was a mystery. She could not reconcile him\r\nand the barbaric display in which he lived with the evidences of\r\nrefinement and education that the well-worn books in the tent evinced.\r\nThe fastidious ordering of his appointments puzzled her; it was strange\r\nto find in such a place. A dozen incongruities that she had noticed\r\nduring the day crowded into her recollection until her head reeled. She\r\nturned from them wearily; she was too tired to think, too spent in mind\r\nand body. And with the despair a kind of indifference stole over her.\r\nShe had suffered so much that nothing more mattered.\r\n\r\nThe strong arms around her tightened slowly. "Look at me," he said in\r\nthe soft slow voice that seemed habitual to him, and which contrasted\r\noddly with the neat, clipping French that he spoke. She shivered and\r\nher dark lashes flickered for a moment. "Look at me." His voice was\r\njust as slow, just as soft, but into it had crept an inflection that\r\nwas unmistakable.\r\n\r\nTwenty-four hours ago Diana Mayo had not known the meaning of the word\r\nfear, and had never in all her life obeyed any one against her\r\ninclination, but in twenty-four hours she had lived through years of\r\nemotions. For the first time she had pitted her will against a will\r\nthat was stronger than her own, for the first time she had met an\r\narrogance that was greater and a determination that was firmer than\r\nhers. For the first time she had met a man who had failed to bow to her\r\nwishes, whom a look had been powerless to transform into a willing\r\nslave. In a few hours that had elapsed she had learned fear, a terrible\r\nfear that left her sick with apprehension, and she was learning\r\nobedience. Obedient now, she forced herself to lift her eyes to his,\r\nand the shamed blood surged slowly into her cheeks. His dark,\r\npassionate eyes burnt into her like a hot flame. His encircling arms\r\nwere like bands of fire, scorching her. His touch was torture.\r\nHelpless, like a trapped wild thing, she lay against him, panting,\r\ntrembling, her wide eyes fixed on him, held against their will.\r\nFascinated she could not turn them away, and the image of the brown,\r\nhandsome face with its flashing eyes, straight, cruel mouth and strong\r\nchin seemed searing into her brain. The faint indefinite scent of an\r\nuncommon Turkish tobacco clung about him, enveloping her. She had been\r\nconscious of the same scent the previous day when he had held her in\r\nhis arms during the wild ride across the desert.\r\n\r\nHe smiled down at her suddenly. "_Bon Dieu_! Do you know how\r\nbeautiful you are?" he murmured. But the sound of his voice seemed to\r\nbreak a spell that had kept her dumb. She struggled again to free\r\nherself.\r\n\r\n"Let me go!" she cried piteously, and it was her complete immunity from\r\nhim that she prayed for, but he chose wilfully to misunderstand her.\r\nThe passion faded from his eyes, giving place to a gleam of mockery.\r\n\r\n"There is plenty of time. Gaston is the most discreet servant. We shall\r\nhear him when he comes," he said with a low laugh.\r\n\r\nBut she persisted with the courage of desperation. "When will you let\r\nme go?"\r\n\r\nWith an exclamation of impatience he put her from him roughly, and\r\ngoing to the divan flung himself down on the cushions, lit another\r\ncigarette and picked up a magazine that was lying on an inlaid stool\r\nbeside him.\r\n\r\nShe bit her lips to keep back the hysterical sobs that rose in her\r\nthroat, nerving herself with clenched hands, and followed him. "You\r\n_must_ tell me. I _must_ know. When will you let me go?"\r\n\r\nHe turned a page with deliberation, and flicked the ash from his\r\ncigarette before looking up. A heavy scowl gathered on his face, and\r\nhis eyes swept her from head to foot with a slow scrutiny that made her\r\nshrink. "When I am tired of you," he said coldly.\r\n\r\nShe shuddered violently and turned away with a little moan, stumbling\r\nblindly towards the inner room, but as she reached the curtains his\r\nvoice arrested her. He had thrown aside the magazine and was lying back\r\non the divan, his long limbs stretched out indolently, his hands\r\nclasped behind his head.\r\n\r\n"You make a very charming boy," he said lightly, with a faint smile,\r\n"but it was not a boy that I saw in Biskra. You understand?"\r\n\r\nBeyond the curtains she stood a moment, shaking all over, her face\r\nhidden in her hands, able to relax a little the hold she was keeping on\r\nherself. Yes! She understood, plainly enough. The understanding had\r\nalready been forced upon her. It was an order from one who was prepared\r\nto compel his commands, to make herself more attractive with all that\r\nit implied in the eyes of the man who held her in his power and who\r\nlooked at her as no other man had ever dared to look, with appraising\r\ncriticism that made her acutely conscious of her sex, that made her\r\nfeel like a slave exposed for sale in a public market.\r\n\r\nShe must take off the boyish clothes that somehow seemed to lend her\r\ncourage and substitute, to gratify the whim of the savage in the next\r\nroom, the womanly dress that revealed more intimately the slender lines\r\nof her figure and intensified the uncommon beauty of her face.\r\n\r\nShe went to the dressing table with lagging feet and stared resentfully\r\nat the white face and haggard eyes that looked back at her from the\r\nmirror. It was like the face of a stranger. Aubrey\'s words came back to\r\nher with an irony that was horrible. To-night she did not dress to\r\nplease herself. Her face was set, her eyes almost black with rage, but\r\nbehind the rage there was lurking apprehension. She started at every\r\nsound that came from the adjoining room. Her fingers, wet with\r\nperspiration, seemed almost unable to fulfil their task. She hated him,\r\nshe hated herself, she hated her beauty that had brought this horror\r\nupon her. She would have rebelled if she had dared, but instinctively\r\nshe hurried--fear had already driven her so far. But when she was ready\r\nshe did not move from the table beside which she stood. Fear had forced\r\nher to haste, but her still struggling pride would not permit her to\r\nobey her fear any further. She raised her eyes to the glass again,\r\nglowering angrily at the pale reflection, and the old obstinacy mingled\r\nwith the new pain that filled them. Must she endure his mocking glance\r\nwith chalk-like cheeks and eyes like a beaten hound? Had she not even\r\ncourage enough left to hide the fear that filled her with\r\nself-contempt? The wave of anger that went through her rushed the\r\ncolour into her face and she leaned nearer the glass with a little\r\nmurmur of satisfaction that stopped abruptly as her fingers gripped the\r\nedge of the table, and she continued staring into the mirror not at her\r\nown face, but at the white robes that appeared behind her head,\r\nblotting out the limited view she had had of the room.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik was standing behind her. He had come with the peculiar\r\nnoiseless tread that she had noticed before. He swung her round to look\r\nat her and she writhed under his eyes of admiration, straining from him\r\nas far as his grip allowed. Holding her with one hand he took her chin\r\nin the other and tilted her face up to his with a little smile. "Don\'t\r\nlook so frightened. I don\'t want anything more deadly than some soap\r\nand water. Surely even an Arab may be allowed to wash his hands?"\r\n\r\nHis mocking voice and his taunt of fear stung her, but she would not\r\nanswer and, with a laugh and a shrug, he lot her go, picking up a razor\r\nfrom the table and lounging into the bathroom.\r\n\r\nWith crimson cheeks Diana fled into the outer room, His manner could\r\nnot have been more casual if she had been his wife a dozen years. She\r\nwaited for him in a tumult of emotions, but with the advent of Gaston\r\nand dinner he returned to the attitude of dispassionate, courteous host\r\nthat he had assumed when he first came in. He was a few minutes late,\r\nand apologised gravely as he sat down opposite her. He maintained the\r\nattitude throughout dinner, and conscious of the watching manservant\r\nDiana made herself reply to his easy conversation.\r\n\r\nHe talked mainly of the desert and the sport that it offered, as if he\r\nhad studied her tastes and chosen the topic to please her. He spoke\r\nwell; what he said was interesting, and showed complete knowledge of\r\nthe subject, and at any other time Diana would have listened fascinated\r\nand absorbed, but now the soft, slow, cultured voice only seemed to add\r\nto the incongruity of the situation. The role of willing guest that he\r\nwas forcing upon her was almost more than she could play, and the\r\nnecessity of sitting still and responding was taxing her endurance to\r\nthe utmost. And all the time she was aware acutely of his constant\r\nsurveillance. Reluctantly her own furtive glance was drawn frequently\r\nto his face, and always his dark fierce eyes were watching her with a\r\nsteadiness that racked her nerves, till she was reminded irresistibly\r\nof an exhibition that she had seen in a circus in Vienna, where a lion\r\ntamer had concluded an unusually daring performance by dining in the\r\nlions\' cage, surrounded by savage snarling brutes very different from\r\nthe sleepy half-drugged creatures ordinarily shown. Interested in the\r\nanimals, she had gone behind with Aubrey after the performance, and\r\nwhile fondling some tiny lion cubs that had been brought for her to see\r\nhad chatted with the tamer, a girl little older than herself. She had\r\nbeen somewhat unapproachable until she had realised from Diana\'s\r\nfriendly manner that her questions were prompted by real interest and\r\nnot mere curiosity, and had unbent with surprising swiftness, accepting\r\nDiana\'s proffered cigarettes and taking her to see her special lions,\r\nwho were boxed for the night. Diana had wandered up and down before the\r\nnarrow cages, looking at the big brutes still restless from the show,\r\nrubbing her cheek on the soft little round head of the cub she was\r\nholding in her arms, smiling at its sleepy rasping purr.\r\n\r\n"Are you ever afraid?" she had asked suddenly--"not of the ordinary\r\nperformance, but of that last act, when you dine all alone with them?"\r\n\r\nThe girl shrugged her shoulders, blowing a little cloud of smoke into\r\nthe cub\'s face, and her eyes had met Diana\'s slowly over his little\r\nyellow body. "One does not taste very much," she had said drily.\r\n\r\nAnd it was so with Diana. She had eaten mechanically everything that\r\nhad been put before her, but she had tasted nothing. She had one\r\nthought in her mind that excluded everything else--to hide from the\r\nprobing eyes that watched her ceaselessly the overmastering fear that\r\naugmented every moment. One thing she had noticed during the meal. For\r\nher only the servant poured out the light French wine that he had\r\nbrought. Her eyes wandered to the Sheik\'s empty glass, and meeting her\r\nglance he smiled, with a little inclination.\r\n\r\n"Excuse me. I do not drink wine. It is my only virtue," he added, with\r\na sudden gleam leaping into his eyes that drove the blood into her\r\ncheeks and her own eyes on to her plate.\r\n\r\nShe had forgotten that he was an Arab.\r\n\r\nThe dinner seemed interminable, and yet she wished that it would never\r\nend. While the servant was in the room she was safe; the thought of his\r\ngoing sent a cold shudder through her. With the coffee came a huge\r\nPersian hound, almost upsetting the Frenchman in the entrance in his\r\nfrantic endeavour to precede him through the doorway. He flung his long\r\ngrey body across the Sheik\'s knees with a whine of pleasure and then\r\nturned his head to growl at Diana. But the growl died away quickly, and\r\nhe lumbered down and came to her side curiously, eyeing her for a\r\nmoment and then thrusting his big head against her.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik laughed. "You are honoured. Kopec makes few friends."\r\n\r\nShe did not answer. The natural reply was almost certain to provoke a\r\nretort that she did not desire, so she remained silent, smoothing the\r\nhound\'s rough coat. With her heart turning slowly to lead she lingered\r\nover her coffee until there was no further possible pretext for\r\nremaining at the table, then rose with a short, sharp sigh.\r\n\r\nFor some minutes the Sheik had sat silent, his own coffee long since\r\nfinished. He made no comment when she got up, and went himself to the\r\nbig divan, followed by the hound, who had gone back to him as soon as\r\nhe moved.\r\n\r\nDiana turned to the little bookcase, snatching at the opportunity it\r\noffered for further silence, and took a book at random. She did not\r\nknow what she was looking at, she did not care. She only prayed\r\nfervently that she might be left alone, that the sudden silent fit that\r\nhad come over him might continue.\r\n\r\nNear her Gaston was clearing away the table and as he finished he\r\npaused to speak to his master. Diana heard the words "le petit Sheik,"\r\nbut the rest was in Arabic and unintelligible to her. The Sheik frowned\r\nwith a gesture of annoyance, then nodded, and the servant left the\r\ntent.\r\n\r\nA few moments after a voice that she had not heard before made her look\r\nup.\r\n\r\nThe young Arab who had ridden in with the Sheik was standing beside the\r\ndivan. The fierce eyes that were watching her every movement met hers,\r\nand his cigarette was waved towards the young man. "My lieutenant,\r\nYusef, a son of the desert with the soul of a _flaneur._ His body\r\nis here with me, but his heart is on the _trottoirs_ of Algiers."\r\n\r\nThe tall lad laughed and salaamed profoundly, then straightened\r\nhimself, posing magnificently until a curt word from the Sheik recalled\r\nhim to his errand and his swagger changed swiftly to a deference of\r\nwhich the significance was not lost on Diana. The Arab might unbend to\r\nhis people if it so pleased him, but he kept them well in hand. She\r\nlooked at the lieutenant as he stood before his chief. He was tall and\r\nslender as a girl, with an air of languid indolence that was obviously\r\na pose, for it was slipping from him now fast as he talked. His face\r\nwas strikingly handsome, only saved from effeminacy by a firm chin. He\r\nwas patently aware of his good looks. But he was also patently in awe\r\nof his chief, and the news that he brought was apparently not welcome.\r\n\r\nThrough her thick lashes Diana watched them intently. The younger man\r\nvoluble, gesticulating, at times almost cringing. The Sheik silent,\r\nexcept for an occasional word, the heavy scowl back on his face,\r\ngrowing blacker every moment. At last with a shrug of impatience he got\r\nup and they went out together, the hound following them. Diana subsided\r\non to the thick rug beside the bookcase. For a moment again she was\r\nalone, free of the watching eyes that seemed to be burning into her all\r\nthe time, free of the hated proximity. She dropped her head on her\r\nknees with a little whimper of weariness. For a moment she need not\r\ncheck the tide of misery that rushed over her. She was tired in mind\r\nand body, exhausted with the emotion that had shaken her until she knew\r\nthat no matter what happened in the future the Diana of yesterday was\r\ndead, and her new self was strange and unfamiliar. She did not trust\r\nit; she feared its capacity for maintaining the struggle she had\r\nresolved upon. The old courageous self had never failed her, this new\r\nshrinking fearful personality filled her with distrust. Her confidence\r\nin herself was gone. Her contempt of herself was unutterable. The\r\nstrength that remained was not sufficient to conquer the fear that had\r\ntaken so strong a hold upon her. She could only hope to hide it, to\r\ndeny him at least that much satisfaction. She had grovelled at his feet\r\nonce and it had amused him. He had laughed! She would die rather than\r\nafford him a similar amusement. She could never wipe out the\r\nrecollection of her cowardice; he would remember always, and so would\r\nshe; but she could atone for it if her strength held. And she prayed\r\nthat it might hold, until a sob broke from her and her hands cramped\r\naround her knees. She pushed her hair off her forehead with a heavy\r\nsigh, and she looked back over her shoulder at the empty room. It had\r\nchanged since this morning in the indefinable way a strange room does\r\nchange after a few hours\' association. If she could leave it now and\r\nnever see it again in all her life no single detail of it would ever be\r\nforgotten. Its characteristics had been stamped upon her as familiarly\r\nas if the hours passed in it had been years. And yesterday was years\r\nago, when the poor silly fool that had been Diana Mayo had ridden\r\nblindly into the trap from which her boasted independence had not been\r\nable to save her. She had paid heavily for the determination to ignore\r\nthe restrictions of her sex laid upon her and the payment was not yet\r\nover. Her tired body shrank from the struggle that must recommence so\r\nsoon. If he would only spare her until this numbing weariness that made\r\nher so powerless should lessen. She heard his voice at the door and her\r\nicy fingers grasped at the book that had slipped to the ground. The\r\nthick rugs deadened the sound of his movements, but she knew\r\ninstinctively that he had come in and gone back to the divan where he\r\nhad been sitting before. She knew that he was looking at her. She could\r\nfeel his eyes fixed on her and she quivered with the consciousness of\r\nhis stare. She waited, shivering, for him to speak or move. His methods\r\nof torture were diverse, she thought with dreary bitterness. Behind the\r\ntent in the men\'s lines a tom-tom was beating, and the irregular rhythm\r\nseemed hammering inside her own head. She could have shrieked with the\r\nagony of it.\r\n\r\n"Come here--Diane."\r\n\r\nShe started, for a moment hardly recognising the Gallic rendering of\r\nher name, and then flushed angrily without answering or moving. It was\r\na very little thing to stir her after all that had been done, but the\r\nuse of her name flamed the anger that had been almost swamped in fear.\r\nThe proprietory tone in his voice roused all her inherent obstinacy.\r\nShe was not his to go at his call. What he wanted he must take--she\r\nwould never give voluntarily. She sat with her hands gripped tightly in\r\nher lap, breathing rapidly, her eyes dark with apprehension.\r\n\r\n"Come here," he repeated sharply.\r\n\r\nStill she took no notice, but the face that he could not see was\r\ngrowing very white.\r\n\r\n"I am not accustomed to having my orders disobeyed," he said at last,\r\nvery slowly.\r\n\r\n"And I am not accustomed to obeying orders," she retorted fiercely,\r\nthough her lips were trembling.\r\n\r\n"You will learn." The sinister accent of his voice almost shattered her\r\nremaining courage.\r\n\r\nShe crouched, gasping, on the ground, the same horrible terror that had\r\ncome to her last night stealing over her irresistibly, paralysing her.\r\nWaiting, listening, agonising, the tom-tom growing louder and\r\nlouder--or was it only the throbbing in her own head? With a choking\r\ncry she leaped to her feet suddenly and fled from him, back till the\r\nside of the tent stopped her and she stood, with wide-flung arms,\r\ngripping the black and silver hangings until he reached her.\r\n\r\nStooping he disengaged her clinging fingers from the heavy drapery and\r\ndrew her hands slowly together up to his breast with a little smile.\r\n"Come," he whispered, his passionate eyes devouring her.\r\n\r\nShe fought against the fascination with which they dominated her,\r\nresisting him dumbly with tight-locked lips till he held her\r\npalpitating in his arms.\r\n\r\n"Little fool," he said with a deepening smile. "Better me than my men."\r\n\r\nThe gibe broke her silence.\r\n\r\n"Oh, you brute! You brute!" she wailed, until his kisses silenced her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\n"A month! Thirty-one days! Oh, God! Only thirty-one days. It seems a\r\nlifetime. Only a month since I left Biskra. A month! A month!"\r\n\r\nDiana flung herself on to her face, burying her head deeply into the\r\ncushions of the divan, shutting out from her sight the barbaric luxury\r\nof her surroundings, shuddering convulsively. She did not cry. The\r\ncomplete breakdown of the first night had never been repeated. Tears of\r\nshame and anger had risen in her eyes often, but she would not let them\r\nfall. She would not give her captor the satisfaction of knowing that he\r\ncould make her weep. Her pride was dying hard. Her mind travelled back\r\nslowly over the days and nights of anguished revolt, the perpetual\r\nclash of will against will, the enforced obedience that had made up\r\nthis month of horror. A month of experience of such bitterness that she\r\nwondered dully how she still had the courage to rebel. For the first\r\ntime in her life she had had to obey. For the first time in her life\r\nshe was of no account. For the first time she had been made conscious\r\nof the inferiority of her sex. The training of years had broken down\r\nunder the experience. The hypothetical status in which she had stood\r\nwith regard to Aubrey and his friends was not tolerated here, where\r\nevery moment she was made to feel acutely that she was a woman, forced\r\nto submit to everything to which her womanhood exposed her, forced to\r\nendure everything that he might put upon her--a chattel, a slave to do\r\nhis bidding, to bear his pleasure and his displeasure, shaken to the\r\nvery foundation of her being with the upheaval of her convictions and\r\nthe ruthless violence done to her cold, sexless temperament. The\r\nhumiliation of it seared her proud heart. He was pitiless in his\r\narrogance, pitiless in his Oriental disregard of the woman subjugated.\r\nHe was an Arab, to whom the feelings of a woman were non-existent. He\r\nhad taken her to please himself and he kept her to please himself, to\r\namuse him in his moments of relaxation.\r\n\r\nTo Diana before she had come to Africa the life of an Arab Sheik in his\r\nnative desert had been a very visionary affair. The term sheik itself\r\nwas elastic. She had been shown Sheiks in Biskra who drove hard\r\nbargains to hire out mangy camels and sore-covered donkeys for trips\r\ninto the interior. Her own faithless caravan-leader had called himself\r\n"Sheik." But she had heard also of other and different Sheiks who lived\r\nfar away across the shimmering sand, powerful chiefs with large\r\nfollowings, who seemed more like the Arabs of her imaginings, and of\r\nwhose lives she had the haziest idea. When not engaged in killing their\r\nneighbours she visualized them drowsing away whole days under the\r\ninfluence of narcotics, lethargic with sensual indulgence. The pictures\r\nshe had seen had been mostly of fat old men sitting cross-legged in the\r\nentrance of their tents, waited on by hordes of retainers, and looking\r\nlanguidly, with an air of utter boredom, at some miserable slave being\r\nbeaten to death.\r\n\r\nShe had not been prepared for the ceaseless activity of the man whose\r\nprisoner she was. His life was hard, strenuous and occupied. His days\r\nwere full, partly with the magnificent horses that he bred, and partly\r\nwith tribal affairs that took him from the camp for hours at a time.\r\nUpon one or two occasions he had been away for the whole night and had\r\ncome back at daybreak with all the evidences of hard riding. Some days\r\nshe rode with him, but when he had not the time or the inclination, the\r\nFrench valet went with her.
