A beautiful grey thoroughbred called Silver\r\nStar was kept for her use, and sometimes on his back she was able to\r\nforget for a little time. So the moments of relaxation were less\r\nfrequent than they might have been, and it was only in the evenings\r\nwhen Gaston had come and gone for the last time and she was alone with\r\nthe Sheik that an icy hand seemed to close down over her heart. And,\r\naccording to his mood, he noticed or ignored her. He demanded implicit\r\nobedience to his lightest whim with the unconscious tyranny of one who\r\nhad always been accustomed to command. He ruled his unruly followers\r\ndespotically, and it was obvious that while they loved him they feared\r\nhim equally. She had even seen Yusef, his lieutenant, cringe from the\r\nheavy scowl that she had, herself, learned to dread.\r\n\r\n"You treat them like dogs," she said to him once. "Are you not afraid\r\nthat one day they will rise against you and murder you?"\r\n\r\nAnd he had only shrugged his shoulders and laughed, the same low laugh\r\nof amusement that never failed to make her shiver.\r\n\r\nThe only person whose devotion seemed untinged by any conflicting\r\nsentiment was the French valet, Gaston.\r\n\r\nIt was the Sheik\'s complete indifference to everything beyond his own\r\nwill, his Oriental egoism, that stung her most. He treated her\r\nsupplications and invectives with a like unconcern. The paroxysms of\r\nwild rage that filled her periodically made no impression on him. He\r\naccorded them a shrug of ennui or watched her with cold curiosity, his\r\nlips parted in a little cruel smile, as if the dissection of her\r\nlacerated feelings amused him, until his patience was exhausted, and\r\nthen, with one of the lithe, quick movements that she could never\r\nevade, his hands would grip and hold her and he would look at her. Only\r\nthat, but in the grasp of his lean, brown fingers and under the stare\r\nof his dark, fierce eyes her own would drop, and the frantic words die\r\nfrom her lips. She was physically afraid of him, and she hated him and\r\nloathed herself for the fear he inspired. And her fear was legitimate.\r\nHis strength was abnormal, and behind it was the lawlessness and\r\nabsolutism that allowed free rein to his savage impulses. He held life\r\nand death in his hand.\r\n\r\nA few days after he had taken her she had seen him chastise a servant.\r\nShe did not know what the man\'s fault had been, but the punishment\r\nseemed out of all proportion to anything that could be imagined, and\r\nshe had watched fascinated with horror, until he had tossed away the\r\nmurderous whip, and without a second glance at the limp, blood-stained\r\nheap that huddled on the ground with suggestive stillness had strolled\r\nback unconcerned to the tent. The sight had sickened her and haunted\r\nher perpetually. His callousness horrified her even more than his\r\ncruelty. She hated him with all the strength of her proud, passionate\r\nnature. His personal beauty even was an additional cause of offence.\r\nShe hated him the more for his handsome face and graceful, muscular\r\nbody. His only redeeming virtue in her eyes was his total lack of\r\nvanity, which she grudgingly admitted. He was as unconscious of himself\r\nas was the wild animal with which she compared him.\r\n\r\n"He is like a tiger," she murmured deep into the cushions, with a\r\nshiver, "a graceful, cruel, merciless beast." She remembered a tiger\r\nshe had shot the previous winter in India. After hours of weary,\r\ncramped waiting in the machan the beautiful creature had slipped\r\nnoiselessly through the undergrowth and emerged into the clearing. He\r\nhad advanced midway towards the tree where she was perched and had\r\nstopped to listen, and the long, free stride, the haughty poise of the\r\nthrown-back head, the cruel curl of the lips and the glint in the\r\nferocious eyes flashing in the moonlight, were identical with the\r\nexpression and carriage of the man who was her master. Then it had been\r\nadmiration without fear, and she had hesitated at wantonly destroying\r\nso perfect a thing, until the quick pressure of her shikari\'s fingers\r\non her arm brought her back to facts and reminded her that the "perfect\r\nthing" was reported to have eaten a woman the previous week. And now it\r\nwas fear with a reluctant admiration that she despised herself for\r\naccording.\r\n\r\nA hand on her shoulder made her start up with a cry. Usually her nerves\r\nwere in better control, but the thick rugs deadened every sound, and\r\nshe had not expected him so soon. He had been out since dawn and had\r\ncome in much past his usual time, and had been having a belated siesta\r\nin the adjoining room.\r\n\r\nAngry with herself she bit her lip and pushed the tumbled hair off her\r\nforehead. He dropped on to the divan beside her and lit the inevitable\r\ncigarette; he smoked continuously every moment he was not in the\r\nsaddle. She glanced at him covertly. He was lying with his head thrown\r\nback against the cushions, idly blowing smoke-rings and watching them\r\ndrift towards the open door-way. And as she looked he yawned and turned\r\nto her.\r\n\r\n"Zilah is careless. Insist that she puts away your boots, and does not\r\nleave your clothes lying on the floor. There was a scorpion in the\r\nbathroom to-day," he said lazily, stretching out his long legs.\r\n\r\nShe flushed hotly, as she always did when he made any casual reference\r\nto the intimacy of their life. It was his casualness that frightened\r\nher, the carelessly implied continuance of a state that scorched her\r\nwith shame. His attitude invariably suggested a duration of their\r\nrelations that left her numb with a kind of helpless despair. He was so\r\nsure of himself, so sure of his possession of her.\r\n\r\nShe felt the warm blood pouring over her face now, up to the roots of\r\nher bright hair and dyeing her slender neck, and she put her hands up\r\nto her head, her fingers thrust through her loose curls, to shield her\r\nface from his eyes.\r\n\r\nShe gave a sigh of relief when Gaston came in bringing a little tray\r\nwith two filigree-cased cups of coffee.\r\n\r\n"I have brought coffee; Madame\'s tea is finished," he murmured in tones\r\nof deepest distress, and with a gesture that conveyed a national\r\ncalamity.\r\n\r\nThere had been just enough tea taken on the tour to last a month. It\r\nwas another pin-prick, another reminder. She set her teeth, moving her\r\nhead angrily, and found herself looking into a pair of mocking eyes,\r\nand, as always, her own dropped.\r\n\r\nGaston said a few words in Arabic to his master, and the Sheik\r\nswallowed the boiling coffee and went out hastily. The valet moved\r\nabout the tent with his usual deft noiselessness, gathering up\r\ncigarette ends and spent matches, and tidying the room with an\r\nassiduous orderliness that was peculiarly his own. Diana watched him\r\nalmost peevishly. Was it the influence of the desert that made all\r\nthese men cat-like in their movements, or was the servant consciously\r\nor unconsciously copying his master? With a sudden fit of childish\r\nirritability she longed to smash something, and, with an impetuous\r\nhand, sent the little inlaid table with the tray and coffee-cups\r\nflying. She was ashamed of the impulse even before the crash came, and\r\nlooked at Gaston clearing up the debris with anxious eyes. What was the\r\nmatter with her? The even temper on which she prided herself and the\r\nnerves that had been her boast had vanished, gone by the board in the\r\nlast month. If her nerve failed her utterly what would become of her?\r\nWhat would she do?\r\n\r\nGaston had gone, and she looked around the tent with a hunted\r\nexpression. There seemed no escape possible from the misery that was\r\nalmost more than she could bear.\r\n\r\nThere was a way out that had been in her mind often, and she had\r\nsearched frequently in the hope that she might find the means. But the\r\nSheik had also thought and had taken precautions. One day it seemed as\r\nif her desperate wish might be fulfilled, and she had had only a\r\nmoment\'s hesitation as she stretched out her hand to take the revolver\r\nthat had been left lying on a table, but as her fingers closed on the\r\nbutt a muscular hand closed over hers. He had come in with his usual\r\nsilent step and was close to her without her knowing. He had taken the\r\nweapon from her quietly, holding her eyes with his own, and had jerked\r\nit open, showing the empty magazine. "Do you think that I am quite a\r\nfool?" he had asked without a trace of expression in his voice.\r\n\r\nAnd since then she had been under a ceaseless, unobtrusive surveillance\r\nthat had left her no chance of carrying out her terrible resolve. She\r\nburied her face in her hands. "Oh, my God! Is it never going to end? Am\r\nI never going to get away from him?"\r\n\r\nShe sprang to her feet and walked restlessly round the tent, her hands\r\nclasped behind her back, her head thrown up, and her lips pressed close\r\ntogether. She panted as if she had been running, and her eyes had a\r\nfar-away, unseeing look. Gradually she got command of herself again and\r\nthe nervous excitement died down, leaving her weary and very desolate.\r\nThe solitude seemed suddenly horrible. Anything would be better than\r\nthe silent emptiness of the great tent. A noise outside attracted her,\r\nand she wandered to the doorway and out under the awning. Near her the\r\nSheik with Gaston and Yusef stood watching a mad, ramping colt that was\r\nbeing held with difficulty by two or three men, who clung to him\r\ntenaciously in spite of his efforts to break away, and beyond was a\r\nsemi-circle of Arabs, some mounted and some on foot, leaving a wide,\r\nopen space between them and the tent. They were intensely excited,\r\ntalking and gesticulating, the mounted men riding round the outer ring\r\nthat they formed. Diana leaned against one of the lances that supported\r\nthe awning and watched the scene with growing interest. This camp was\r\nmany miles to the south of the one to which she had first been brought,\r\nand which had been broken up a few days after her capture. The setting\r\nwas wonderful, the far-off hills dusky in the afternoon light, the\r\nclustering palms behind the tents, the crowd of barbaric figures in\r\npicturesque, white robes, the horsemen moving continuously up and down,\r\nand in the midst of everything the beautiful, wild creature, frenzied\r\nby the noise, kicking and biting at the men holding him. After a moment\r\nthe Sheik held up his hand, and a man detached himself from the\r\nchattering crowd and came to him salaaming. The Sheik said a few words,\r\nand with another salaam and a gleam of white teeth, the man turned and\r\napproached the struggling group in the centre of the ring.\r\n\r\nDiana straightened up with interest. The frantic colt was going to be\r\nbroken. It was already saddled. Several additional men ran forward, and\r\nbetween them the horse was forcibly held for a moment--only for a\r\nmoment, but it was long enough for the man who leaped like a flash on\r\nto his back. The others fell away, racing from the reach of the\r\nterrible lashing heels. Amazed for the moment at the sudden\r\nunaccustomed weight, the colt paused, and then reared straight up, till\r\nit seemed to Diana that he must fall backward and crush the man who was\r\nclinging to him. But he came down at last, and for a few moments it was\r\nalmost impossible to follow his spasmodic movements as he strove to rid\r\nhimself of his rider. The end came quickly. With a twisting heave of\r\nhis whole body he shot the Arab over his head, who landed with a dull\r\nthud and lay still, while the men who had been holding the colt dashed\r\nin and secured him before he was aware of his liberty. Diana looked\r\ntowards the fallen man; a little crowd were gathered around him, and\r\nher heart beat faster as she thought that he was dead. Dead so quickly,\r\nand only a moment before he had been so full of life and strength.\r\nDeath meant nothing to these savages, she thought bitterly, as she\r\nwatched the limp body being carried away by three or four men, who\r\nargued violently over their burden. She glanced at the Sheik. He seemed\r\nperfectly unconcerned and did not even look in the direction of the man\r\nwho had fallen. On the contrary, he laughed, and, turning to Yusef, put\r\nhis hand en his shoulder and nodded towards the colt. Diana gave a\r\ngasp. He spared no one. He was going to make the young man take his\r\nchance as the rough-rider had taken his. She knew that the lieutenant\r\nrode well, as did all Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s followers, and that his\r\nlanguid manner was only a pose, but he looked so young and boyish, and\r\nthe risk seemed enormous. She had seen colts broken before many times,\r\nbut never a colt so madly savage as this one. But to Yusef the chance\r\nwas evidently welcome. With an answering laugh, he swaggered out into\r\nthe arena, where the men greeted him with shouts. There was the same\r\nprocedure as before, and Yusef bounded up lightly into the saddle. This\r\ntime, instead of rearing, the frightened beast dashed forward in a wild\r\neffort to escape, but the mounted men, closing up, headed him into the\r\nmiddle of the ring again, and he went back to his first tactics with a\r\nrapidity that was too much for the handsome lad on his back, and in a\r\nfew moments he was thrown heavily. With a shrill scream the colt turned\r\non him open-mouthed, and Yusef flung up one arm to save his face. But\r\nthe men reached him in time, dragging the colt from him by main force.\r\nHe rose to his feet unsteadily and limped to the tents behind. Diana\r\ncould not see him easily for the throng around him.\r\n\r\nAgain she looked at the Sheik and ground her teeth. He was stooping to\r\nlight a cigarette from a match that Gaston was holding, and then they\r\nwalked together nearer to the colt. The animal was now thoroughly\r\nmaddened, and it was increasingly difficult to hold him. They went up\r\nclose to the struggling, yelling grooms, and the next minute Diana saw\r\nGaston sitting firmly in the empty saddle. The little man rode\r\nmagnificently, and put up a longer fight than the others had done, but\r\nat last his turn came, and he went flying over the colt\'s head. He came\r\ndown lightly on his hands and knees, and scrambled to his feet in an\r\ninstant amidst a storm of shouts and laughter. Laughing himself he came\r\nback to the Sheik with a shrug of the shoulders and outspread, eloquent\r\nhands. They spoke together for a moment, too low for Diana to hear, and\r\nthen Ahmed Ben Hassan went again into the middle of the ring. Diana\'s\r\nbreath came more quickly. She guessed his intention before he reached\r\nthe colt, and she moved forward from under the awning and joined\r\nGaston, who was wrapping his handkerchief round a torn hand.\r\n\r\n"Monseigneur will try?" she asked a little breathlessly.\r\n\r\nGaston looked at her quickly. "Try, Madame?" he repeated in a queer\r\nvoice. "Yes, he will try."\r\n\r\nAgain the empty saddle was filled, and a curious hush came over the\r\nwatching crowd. Diana looked on with bright, hard eyes, her heart\r\nbeating heavily. She longed passionately that the colt might kill him,\r\nand, at the same time, illogically, she wanted to see him master the\r\ninfuriated animal. The sporting instinct in her acknowledged and\r\nresponded to the fight that was going on before her eyes. She hated him\r\nand she hoped that he might die, but she was forced to admire the\r\nwonderful horsemanship that she was watching. The Sheik sat like a\r\nrock, and every effort made to unseat him was unsuccessful. The colt\r\nplunged wildly, making furious blind dashes backward and forward,\r\nstopping dead in the hope of dislodging his rider, twirling round\r\nsuddenly until it seemed impossible that he could keep his feet. Then\r\nhe started rearing, straight up, his forelegs beating the air, higher\r\nand higher, and then down, to commence again without a moment\'s\r\nbreathing-space.\r\n\r\nDiana heard Gaston\'s breath whistle through his teeth. "Look, Madame!"\r\nhe cried sharply, and Diana saw the Sheik give a quick glance behind\r\nhim, and, as the colt shot up again, almost perpendicular, with a jerk\r\nhe pulled him deliberately over backwards, leaping clear with a\r\ntremendous effort as the horse crashed to the ground. He was in the\r\nsaddle again almost before the dazed creature had struggled to its\r\nfeet. And then began a scene that Diana never forgot. It was the final\r\nstruggle that was to end in defeat for either man or horse, and the\r\nSheik had decided that it was not to be for the man. It was a\r\npunishment of which the untamed animal was never to lose remembrance.\r\nThe savagery and determination of the man against the mad determination\r\nof the horse. It was a hideous exhibition of brute strength and\r\nmerciless cruelty. Diana was almost sick with horror from the\r\nbeginning; she longed to turn away, but her eyes clung fascinated to\r\nthe battle that was going on. The hush that had fallen on the crowd had\r\ngiven way to roars of excitement, and the men pressed forward eagerly,\r\nto give back precipitately when the still-fighting animal\'s heels\r\nflashed too near.\r\n\r\nDiana was shaking all over and her hands were clenching and unclenching\r\nas she stared at the man, who seemed a part of the horse he was sitting\r\nso closely. Would it never end? She did not care now which killed the\r\nother so that it would only stop. The man\'s endurance seemed mere\r\nbravado. She clutched Gaston\'s arms with a hand that was wringing wet.\r\n"It is horrible," she gasped with an accent of loathing.\r\n\r\n"It is necessary," he replied quietly.\r\n\r\n"Nothing can justify that," she cried passionately.\r\n\r\n"Your pardon, Madame. He must learn. He killed a man this morning,\r\nthrew him, and what you call in English \'savaged\' him."\r\n\r\nDiana hid her face in her hands. "I can\'t bear it," she said pitifully.\r\n\r\nA few minutes later Gaston clicked his tongue against his teeth. "See,\r\nMadame. It is over," he said gently.\r\n\r\nShe looked up fearfully. The Sheik was standing on the ground beside\r\nthe colt, who was swaying slowly from side to side with heaving sides\r\nand head held low to the earth, dripping blood and foam. And as she\r\nlooked he tottered and collapsed exhausted. There was a rush from all\r\nsides, and Gaston went towards his master, who towered above the crowd\r\naround him.\r\n\r\nDiana turned away with an exclamation of disgust. It was enough to have\r\nseen a display of such brutality; it was too much to stand by while his\r\nfellow-savages acclaimed him for his cruelty.\r\n\r\nShe went slowly back into the tent, shaken with what she had seen, and\r\nstood in undecided hesitation beside the divan. The helpless feeling\r\nthat she so often experienced swept over her with renewed force. There\r\nwas nowhere that she could get away from him, no privacy, no respite.\r\nDay and night she must endure his presence with no hope of escape. She\r\nclosed her eyes in a sudden agony, and then stiffened at the sound of\r\nhis voice outside.\r\n\r\nHe came in laughing, a cigarette dangling from one blood-stained hand,\r\nwhile with the other he wiped the perspiration from his forehead,\r\nleaving a dull red smear. She shrank from him, looking at him with\r\nblazing eyes. "You are a brute, a beast, a devil! I hate you!" she\r\nchoked furiously.\r\n\r\nFor a moment an ugly look crossed his face, and then he laughed again.\r\n"Hate me by all means, _ma belle_, but let your hatred be\r\nthorough. I detest mediocrity," he said lightly, as he passed on into\r\nthe other room.\r\n\r\nShe sank down on to the couch. She had never felt so desperate, so\r\npowerless. She stared straight before her, shivering, as she went over\r\nthe scene she had just witnessed, her fingers picking nervously at the\r\njade-green silk of her dress. She longed for some power that would\r\ndeaden her feelings and blunt her capacity for suffering. She looked at\r\nGaston with hard eyes when he came in. He had approved of what the\r\nSheik had done, would have done it himself if he had been able. They\r\nwere all alike.\r\n\r\n"The man who was hurt first," she asked abruptly, with a touch of her\r\nold hauteur in her voice, "is he dead?"\r\n\r\n"Oh no, Madame. He has concussion but he will be all right. They have\r\nhard heads, these Arabs."\r\n\r\n"And Yusef?"\r\n\r\nGaston grinned. "_Le petit_ Sheik has a broken collar-bone. It is\r\nnothing. A few days\' holiday to be petted in his harem, _et\r\nvoila_!"\r\n\r\n"His harem?" echoed Diana in surprise. "Is he married?"\r\n\r\n"_Mais oui_, Madame. He has two wives."\r\n\r\nAt Diana\'s exclamation he shrugged deprecatingly. "_Que\r\nvoulez-vous?_ It is the custom of the country," he said tolerantly,\r\nwith the air of conceding a melancholy fact with the best grace\r\npossible.\r\n\r\nThe customs of the country was dangerous ground, and Diana changed the\r\nsubject hastily. "Where did you learn to ride, Gaston?"\r\n\r\n"In a racing-stable at Auteuil, Madame, when I was a boy. Then I was\r\nfive years in the French cavalry. After that I came to Monseigneur."\r\n\r\n"And you have been with him--how long?"\r\n\r\n"Fifteen years, Madame."\r\n\r\n"Fifteen years," she repeated wonderingly. "Fifteen years here, in the\r\ndesert?"\r\n\r\n"Here and elsewhere, Madame," he answered rather more shortly than\r\nusual, and with a murmur of excuse left the tent.\r\n\r\nDiana leaned back against the cushions with a little sigh. Gaston need\r\nnot have been afraid that she was trying to learn his master\'s secrets\r\nfrom him. She had not fallen as low as that. The mystery of the man\r\nwhose path had crossed hers so terribly seemed to augment instead of\r\nlessen as the time went on. What was the power in him that compelled\r\nthe devotion of his wild followers and the little French ex-cavalryman?\r\nShe knit her forehead in perplexity and was still puzzling over it when\r\nhe came back. Immaculate and well-groomed he was very different from\r\nthe dishevelled, bloodstained savage of half-an-hour before. She shot a\r\nnervous glance at him, remembering her outburst, but he was not angry.\r\nHe looked grave, but his gravity seemed centred in himself as he passed\r\nhis lean fingers tenderly over his smooth chin. She had seen Aubrey do\r\nsimilarly hundreds of times. Occidental or Oriental, men seemed very\r\nalike. She waited for him to speak and waited vainly. One of the\r\ntaciturn fits to which she had grown accustomed had come over\r\nhim--hours sometimes in which he simply ignored her altogether. The\r\nevening meal was silent. He spoke once to Gaston, but he spoke in\r\nArabic, and the servant replied only with a nod of compliance. And\r\nafter Gaston was gone he did not speak for a long time, but sat on the\r\ndivan, apparently absorbed in his thoughts.\r\n\r\nRestless, Diana moved about the tent, listlessly examining objects that\r\nshe knew by heart, and flirting over the pages of the French magazines\r\nshe had read a dozen times. Usually she was thankful for his silent\r\nmoods. To-night with a woman\'s perversity she wanted him to speak. She\r\nwas unstrung, and the utter silence oppressed her. She glanced over her\r\nshoulder at him once or twice, but his back looked unapproachable. Yet\r\nwhen he called her, with a swift revulsion of feeling, she wished he\r\nhad kept silent. She went to him slowly. She was too unnerved to-night\r\nto struggle against him. What would be the use? she thought wearily; it\r\nwould only end in defeat as it always did. He pulled her down on the\r\ndivan beside him, and before she realised what he was doing slipped a\r\nlong jade necklace over her head. For a moment she looked stupidly at\r\nthe wonderful thing, almost unique in the purity of its colour and the\r\nmarvellous carving on the uniform square pieces of which it was\r\ncomposed, and then with a low cry she tore it off and flung it on the\r\nground.\r\n\r\n"How dare you?" she gasped.\r\n\r\n"You don\'t like it?" he asked in his low, unruffled voice, his eyebrows\r\nraised in real or assumed surprise. "Yet it matches your dress," and\r\nlightly his long fingers touched the folds of green silk swathed across\r\nthe youthful curve of her breast. He glanced at an open box filled with\r\nshimmering stones on a low stool beside him.\r\n\r\n"Pearls are too cold and diamonds too banal for you," he said slowly.\r\n"You should wear nothing but jade. It is the colour of the evening sky\r\nagainst the sunset of your hair."\r\n\r\nHe had never spoken like that to her before, or used that tone of\r\nvoice. His methods had been more fierce than tender. She glanced up\r\nswiftly at his face, but it baffled her. There was no love in his eyes\r\nor even desire, nothing but an unusual gentleness. "Perhaps you would\r\nprefer the diamonds and the pearls," he went on, pointing disdainfully\r\nat the box.\r\n\r\n"No, no. I hate them! I hate them all! I will not wear your jewels. You\r\nhave no right to think that I am that kind of woman," she cried\r\nhysterically.\r\n\r\n"You do not like them? _Bon Dieu!_ None of the other women ever\r\nrefused them. On the contrary, they could never get enough," he said\r\nwith a laugh.\r\n\r\nDiana looked up with a startled glance, a look of horror dawning in her\r\neyes. "Other women?" she repeated blankly.\r\n\r\n"You didn\'t suppose you were the first, did you?" he asked with brutal\r\ncandour. "Don\'t look at me like that. They were not like you, they came\r\nto me willingly enough--too willingly. Allah! How they bored me! I\r\ntired of them before they tired of me."\r\n\r\nShe flung her arm across her eyes with a dry sob, straining away from\r\nhim. She had never thought of that. In the purity of her mind it had\r\nnever occurred to her. She was only one of many, one of a succession of\r\nmistresses, taken and discarded at his whim. She writhed with the shame\r\nthat filled her. "Oh, you hurt me!" she whispered very low, and then\r\nanger killed all other feeling. He had loosened his arm about her and\r\nshe wrenched herself free and sprang to her feet. "I hate you, do you\r\nunderstand? I hate you! I hate you!"\r\n\r\nHe lit a cigarette leisurely before answering and moved into a more\r\ncomfortable position on the divan. "So you have already told me this\r\nafternoon," he said at length coolly, "and with reiteration your remark\r\nbecomes less convincing, _ma cherie_."\r\n\r\nHer anger ebbed away. She was too tired to be angry. She was humiliated\r\nand hurt, and the man before her had it in his power to hurt her more,\r\nbut she was at his mercy and to-night she could not fight. She pushed\r\nthe hair off her forehead with a heavy sigh and looked at the Sheik\'s\r\nlong length stretched out on the couch, the steely strength of his\r\nlimbs patent even in the indolent attitude in which he was lying, at\r\nhis brown handsome face, inscrutable as it always was to her, and the\r\nfeeling of helplessness came back with renewed force and with it the\r\nsense of her own pitiful weakness against his force, compelling her to\r\nspeak. "Have you never felt pity for a thing that was weaker than\r\nyourself? Have you never spared anything or any one in all your life?\r\nHave you nothing in your nature but cruelty? Are all Arabs hard like\r\nyou?" she said shakily. "Has love never even made you merciful?"\r\n\r\nHe glanced up at her with a harsh laugh, and shook his head. "Love?\r\n_Connais pas!_ Yes, I do," he added with swift mockery, "I love my\r\nhorses."\r\n\r\n"When you don\'t kill them," she retorted.\r\n\r\n"I am corrected. When I don\'t kill them."\r\n\r\nThere was something in his voice that made her reckless, that made her\r\nwant to hurt him. "If you give no love to the--the women whom you bring\r\nhere, do you give love to the women of your harem? You have a harem, I\r\nsuppose, somewhere?" she braved him with curling lip and scornful\r\nvoice, but as she spoke she knew that she had only hurt herself and her\r\nvoice faltered.\r\n\r\nHis hand reached out suddenly and he dragged her down into his arms\r\nagain with a laugh. "And if I have, are you jealous? What if the nights\r\nI spent away from you were passed in my harem--what then?"\r\n\r\n"Then may Allah put it into the heart of one of your wives to poison\r\nyou so that you never come back," she said fiercely.\r\n\r\n"Allah! So beautiful and so bloodthirsty," he said in bantering\r\nreproof. Then he turned her face up to his, smiling into her angry eyes\r\nwith amusement. "I have no harem and, thanks be to Allah, no wives,\r\n_cherie._ Does that please you?"\r\n\r\n"Why should I care? It is nothing to me," she replied sharply, with a\r\nvivid blush.\r\n\r\nHe held her closer, looking deeply into her eyes, holding them as he\r\ncould when he liked, in spite of her efforts to turn them away--a\r\nmesmerism she could not resist.\r\n\r\n"Shall I make you care? Shall I make you love me? I can make women love\r\nme when I choose."\r\n\r\nShe went very white and her eyes flickered. She knew that he was only\r\namusing himself, that he was utterly indifferent to her feelings, that\r\nhe did not care if she hated or loved him, but it was a new form of\r\ntorture that was more detestable than anything that had gone before it.\r\nIt infuriated her that he could even suggest that she could come to\r\ncare for him, that she could ever look on him as anything but a brutal\r\nsavage who had committed a hideous outrage, that she could ever have\r\nany feeling for him except hatred and loathing. That he should class\r\nher with the other women he spoke of revolted her, she felt degraded,\r\nsoiled as she had never done before, and she had thought that she had\r\nfelt the utmost humiliation of her position.\r\n\r\nThe colour rushed back into her face. "I would rather you killed me,"\r\nshe cried passionately.\r\n\r\n"So would I," he said drily, "for if you loved me you would bore me and\r\nI should have to let you go. While as it is"--he laughed softly--"as it\r\nis I do not regret the chance that took me into Biskra that day."\r\n\r\nHe let her go and got up with a yawn, watching her approvingly as she\r\ncrossed the tent. The easy swing of her boyish figure and the defiant\r\ncarriage of her head reminded him of one of his own thoroughbred\r\nhorses. She was as beautiful and as wild as they were. And as he broke\r\nthem so would he break her. She was nearly tamed now, but not quite,\r\nand by Allah! it should be quite! As he turned his foot struck against\r\nthe jade necklace lying on the rug where she had thrown it. He picked\r\nit up and called her back. She came reluctantly, slowly, with mutinous\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nHe held out the necklace silently, and silently she stared not at it\r\nbut at him. Her heart began to beat faster, and the colour slowly left\r\nher face. "Take it. I wish it," he said quietly.\r\n\r\n"No." It was little more than a gasp.\r\n\r\n"You will wear it to please me," he went on in the same soft voice, and\r\nthe old hateful mockery crept into his eyes, "to please my artistic\r\nsoul. I have an artistic soul even though I am only an Arab."\r\n\r\n"I will not!"\r\n\r\nThe mockery was wiped out of his eyes in a flash, giving place to the\r\nusual ferocity, and his forehead knit in the dreaded heavy scowl.\r\n"Diane, obey me!"\r\n\r\nShe clenched her teeth on her lower lip until a rim of blood stained\r\ntheir whiteness. If he would only shout or bluster like the average\r\nangry man she felt that she could brave him longer, but the cold quiet\r\nrage that characterised him always was infinitely more sinister, and\r\nparalysed her with its silent force. She had never heard him raise his\r\nvoice in anger or quicken his usual slow, soft tone, but there was an\r\ninflection that came into his voice and a look that came into his eyes\r\nthat was more terrible than any outburst. She had seen his men shrink\r\nwhen, standing near him, she had barely been able to hear what he had\r\nsaid. She had seen a look from him silence a clamorous quarrel that had\r\nbroken out among his followers too close to his own tent for his\r\npleasure. And that inflection was in his voice and that look was in his\r\neyes now. It was no longer use to resist. The fear of him was an agony.\r\nShe would have to obey, as in the end he always forced her to obey. She\r\nwrenched her eyes away from his compelling stare, her bosom heaving\r\nunder the soft silk, her chin quivering, and reached out blindly and\r\ntook it from him. But the sudden chill of it against her bare breast\r\nseemed to revive the courage that was not yet dead in her. She flung up\r\nher head, the transient colour flaming into her cheeks, and her lips\r\nsprang open, but he drew her to him swiftly, and laid his hand over her\r\nmouth. "I know, I know," he said coldly. "I am a brute and a beast and\r\na devil. You need not tell me again. It commences to grow tedious." His\r\nhand slipped to her shoulder, his fingers gripping the delicate,\r\nrounded arm. "How much longer are you going to fight? Would it not be\r\nwiser after what you have seen to-day to recognise that I am master?"\r\n\r\n"You mean that you will treat me as you treated the colt this\r\nafternoon?" she whispered, her eyes drawn back irresistibly to his in\r\nspite of all her efforts.\r\n\r\n"I mean that you must realise that my will is law."\r\n\r\n"And if I do not?" He guessed rather than heard the words.\r\n\r\n"Then I will teach you, and I think that you will learn--soon."\r\n\r\nShe quivered in his hands. It was a threat, but how much of it he meant\r\nto be taken literally she did not know. Again every ghastly detail of\r\nthe afternoon passed with lightning speed through her mind. When he\r\npunished he punished mercilessly. To what lengths would he go? The Arab\r\nstandards were not those of the men amongst whom she had lived. The\r\nposition of a woman in the desert was a very precarious one. There were\r\ntimes when she forgot altogether that he was an Arab until some chance,\r\nas now, drove the hard fact home indisputably. He was an Arab, and as a\r\nwoman she need expect no mercy at his hands. His hands! She looked down\r\nfor a second sideways at the fingers gripping her shoulder and she saw\r\nthem again stained with blood, saw them clenched round the dripping\r\nthong. She knew already by bitter experience the iron grip of his lean\r\nfingers and the compelling strength of his arms. Her quick imagination\r\nleaped ahead. What she had already suffered would be nothing compared\r\nwith what would be. The remembrance of the stained, huddled figure of\r\nthe servant he had chastised rose before her. And as she battled with\r\nherself, still torn in her passionate desire to make her strong will\r\nand courageous spirit triumph over her coward woman\'s body that shrank\r\ninstinctively from physical torture, his arm tightened around her and\r\nshe felt the hard muscles pressing against her shoulders and soft, bare\r\nneck, a suggestion of the force lying dormant beside her. She looked up\r\nat him slowly.\r\n\r\nHis expression was unchanged, his forehead was still drawn together in\r\nthe heavy frown and there was no softening in his eyes. The cruel lines\r\nabout his mouth were accentuated and the tiger-look in his face was\r\nmore marked than ever. He was not threatening idly; he meant what he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n"You had better kill me," she said drearily.\r\n\r\n"That would be to admit my own defeat," he replied coolly. "I do not\r\nkill a horse until I have proved beyond all possible doubt that I\r\ncannot tame it. With you I have no such proof. I can tame you and I\r\nwill. But it is for you to choose and to choose to-night if you will\r\nobey me willingly or if I must make you. I have been very patient--for\r\nme," he added, with an odd smile flitting across his face, "but my\r\npatience is exhausted. Choose quickly." Insensibly he drew her closer\r\nto him till his arm felt like an inflexible steel band about her, and\r\nshe thought with a shudder of the coils of a great serpent closing\r\nround its victim. She made a final effort to conquer herself, but\r\nbetween her and the broad chest so close to her she seemed to see a\r\nhorse\'s head held low in agony, blood and foam dripping from his\r\nlacerated mouth, and a horse\'s flanks heaving piteously, torn with the\r\ncruel punishment he had undergone. A sudden nausea came over her,\r\neverything seemed to swim before her eyes, and she swayed against the\r\nman who was holding her. Her bodily fear overruled her mind. She could\r\nnot bear any more.\r\n\r\n"I will obey you," she whispered heavily.\r\n\r\nHe took her chin in his fingers and jerked her head up sharply, staring\r\nat her intently until she felt he was looking into her very soul. The\r\nheavy scowl smoothed away but the fierceness lingered in his eyes.\r\n"Good!" he said at length briefly. "You are wise," he added\r\nsignificantly. He tilted her head further back, bending his own down\r\nuntil his lips were nearly touching hers. She shivered involuntarily,\r\nan anguished appeal leaping into her eyes. He smiled ironically. "Do\r\nyou hate them so much, my kisses?"\r\n\r\nShe swallowed convulsively.\r\n\r\n"You are at least candid if you are not complimentary;" and with that\r\nhe released her and turned away.\r\n\r\nShe reached the curtain that divided the two rooms, her heart beating\r\nwildly, giddy with the strain that she had gone through. She paused a\r\nmoment and looked back at him, amazed at her own temerity. He had\r\nunbuttoned the flap of the tent and was standing in the entrance\r\nlooking out into the night. The scent of the peculiar tobacco he used\r\ndrifted to her with the draught from the open door. Her eyes grew\r\npuzzled. Would she ever understand him? To-night he had given her a\r\nchoice instead of simply enforcing his will, he had made her choose to\r\nsave herself, he had proved his determination and his mastery over her.\r\nAnd with his last words the unexpected gentleness had come into his\r\nvoice again and the cruel lines about his mouth had relaxed in a smile\r\nof amusement. It was the swift transition from ferocity to gentleness\r\nthat she could never fathom. His complex nature was beyond her\r\nunderstanding. She would not try to understand him; she could never\r\nknow the depths of his baffling personality. She only knew that for\r\nsome reason of his own he had spared her, and she feared him more than\r\never.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\n\r\nUnder the awning of the tent Diana was waiting for Gaston and the\r\nhorses, pulling on her thick riding-gloves nervously. She was wrought\r\nup to the utmost pitch of excitement. Ahmed Ben Hassan had been away\r\nsince the previous day and it was uncertain if he would return that\r\nnight or the next. He had been vague as to how long he would be\r\nabsent. There had been a constant coming and going amongst his\r\nfollowers--messengers arriving on exhausted horses at all hours of the\r\nday and night, and the Sheik himself had seemed unusually preoccupied.\r\nHe had not condescended to give any reason for the special activity of\r\nhis people and she had not asked him.\r\n\r\nIn the four weeks that had elapsed since she had promised him her\r\nobedience she had been very silent. The fear and hatred of him grew\r\ndaily. She had learned to stifle the wild fits of rage and the angry\r\nwords that leaped to her lips. She had learned to obey--a reluctant\r\nobedience given with compressed lips and defiant eyes, but given, and\r\nwith a silence that surprised even herself. Day after day she had\r\nfollowed the usual routine, dumb unless he spoke to her; and with his\r\nown attention occupied with matters beyond the four walls of his tent\r\nhe had not noticed or did not trouble to heed her silence. Lately he\r\nhad left her very much alone; she had ridden with him almost daily\r\nuntil the last week, when he had announced curtly that in the meantime\r\nthe length of her rides must be curtailed and that Gaston would\r\naccompany her. He had not offered any explanation, and she had not\r\nsought one. She had chosen to see in it merely another act of tyranny\r\nimposed on her by the man whose arbitrary exercise of power over her\r\nand whose tacit possession of her galled her continually. And under the\r\nsullen submission a wild fury of revolt was raging. She searched\r\nfeverishly for means of flight, and now the Sheik\'s absence seemed to\r\nhave given her the chance she had been waiting for. In the solitude of\r\nthe previous night she had tossed impatiently from side to side of the\r\nbig couch, vainly trying to find some means of taking advantage of her\r\ncomparative freedom to effect her escape. Surely she could find some\r\nway of avoiding Gaston\'s vigilance. Excitement had kept her awake half\r\nthe night, and in the morning she had had hard work to keep her\r\nagitation hidden and to appear as usual. She had even been afraid to\r\norder the horses any earlier in her nervous terror lest the valet\r\nshould suspect there was any reason behind the simple request. After\r\nher _petit dejeuner_ she had paced the tent, unable to sit still,\r\ndreading lest any moment might bring the return of the Sheik and\r\nfrustrate her hopes. She looked back into the room with a shudder as\r\nher eyes travelled over the luxurious appointments and different\r\nobjects that had become so curiously familiar in the last two months.\r\nThe unexpected equipments and the man\'s own baffling personality would\r\nremain in her recollection always as an enigma that she would never be\r\nable to solve. So much had been so inexplicable in himself and in his\r\nmode of life. She drew a long breath and went out hastily into the\r\nsunshine.\r\n\r\nThe horses were waiting, and Gaston was standing ready to hold her\r\nstirrup. She fondled the beautiful grey horse\'s soft nose and patted\r\nhis satiny neck with a hand that trembled a little. She loved the horse\r\nand to-day he should be the means of saving her. He responded to her\r\ncaresses, gentling her with slobbering mouth and whinnying softly. With\r\none last look at the big double tent and the rest of the camp behind it\r\nshe mounted and rode away without another backward glance. She had to\r\nexercise a rigid control over herself. She longed to put Silver Star\r\ninto a hand gallop at once and shake off Gaston, but she was still too\r\nnear the camp. She must be patient and put a certain number of miles\r\nbetween herself and the possibility of pursuit before she attempted\r\nanything. Too early an endeavour would only bring the whole horde in\r\nwild chase at her heels. The thought of the promise she had given to\r\nthe man from whom she was flying came back to her. She had promised\r\nobedience, but she had not promised that she would not try to escape,\r\nand, if she had, no promise wrung from her by fear was valid in her\r\nopinion.\r\n\r\nShe rode steadily forward at a slow, swinging canter, instinctively\r\nsaving her horse, plan after plan passing through her brain to be\r\nrejected as impracticable. Silver Star fretted continually at the\r\nmoderate pace, tossing his head and catching at his bit. She took no\r\nheed of the time beyond the fact that it was passing quickly, and that\r\nif anything was to be done it must be done as soon as possible. But\r\nGaston, riding a few paces behind her, was very much alive to the hour\r\nand had looked several times at his watch. He ranged alongside of her\r\nnow with a murmured apology. "Pardon, Madame. It grows late," and\r\nsubmitted his wrist watch for her inspection.\r\n\r\nDiana glanced mechanically at her own wrist and then remembered that\r\nshe had broken her watch the day before. She pulled up, and tilting her\r\nhelmet back mopped her hot forehead, and, as she did so, a sharp breeze\r\nsprang up, the curious wind that comes and goes so rapidly in the\r\ndesert. An idea flashed into her mind. It was a poor chance, but it\r\nmight succeed. She shot a glance at Gaston. He was looking in the\r\nopposite direction, and, raising her hand, she fluttered her\r\nhandkerchief a moment in the breeze and then let it go. The wind\r\ncarried it some distance away. She gave a little cry and caught at the\r\nbridle of the valet\'s horse.\r\n\r\n"Oh, Gaston, my handkerchief!" and pointed to where the morsel of\r\ncambric lay white against a rock. With a comical exclamation of dismay\r\nhe slipped to the ground and started to run across the sand.\r\n\r\nShe waited until he had got well on his way, sitting tense with shining\r\neyes and thumping heart, then, snatching off her helmet, she brought it\r\ndown with a resounding smack on the hindquarters of the servant\'s\r\nhorse, stampeding it in the direction of the camp, and, wheeling Silver\r\nStar, headed for the north, deaf to Gaston\'s cries.\r\n\r\nWild with excitement and free to go his own pace at last her mount\r\ngalloped swiftly and the wind whistled past Diana\'s ears. To the\r\npossible fate of the little Frenchman left on foot so far from the\r\nencampment she gave no heed. For the moment she did not even think of\r\nhim, she had no thought for anybody but herself. Her ruse by its very\r\nsimplicity had succeeded. She was free and she did not care about\r\nanything else. She had no plans or ideas what she should do or where\r\nshe should go beyond the fact that she would keep riding northward. She\r\nhad vague hopes that she might fall in with friendly Arabs who, for a\r\npromised reward, would guide her to civilisation. Most of them could\r\nspeak a little French, and for the rest her small stock of Arabic must\r\ndo. She knew that she was mad to attempt to ride across the desert\r\nalone, but she did not mind. She was free. She was too excited to think\r\ncoherently. She laughed and shouted like a mad thing and her madness\r\ncommunicated itself to the grey, who was going at racing speed. Diana\r\nknew that he was out of control, that she could not stop him if she\r\ntried, but she did not want to try, the faster the better. In time he\r\nwould tire himself, but until then let him go as he pleased. She was\r\nfast putting miles between herself and the camp that had been a prison,\r\nbetween herself and the brute who had dared to do what he had done. At\r\nthe thought of the Sheik a sick feeling of fear ran through her. If\r\nanything should happen? If he should catch her again? She shuddered,\r\nand a cry burst from her lips, but she gripped herself at once. She was\r\nidiotic, contemptible; it was impossible. It would be hours, perhaps\r\neven the next day, before the alarm was given; he would not know in\r\nwhat direction she had gone. She would have miles of start on one of\r\nthe fleetest of his horses. She tried to put him out of her mind. She\r\nhad escaped from him and his cruelty, it was a nightmare that was over.\r\nThe effects would remain with her always, nothing would ever be the\r\nsame again, but the daily dread, the daily contamination would be gone,\r\nthe helpless tortured feeling, the shame of submission that had filled\r\nher with an acute self-loathing that was as intense as her passionate\r\nhatred of the man who had forced her to endure his will. The memory of\r\nit would live with her for ever. He had made her a vile thing. Her\r\ncheeks scorched with the thought and she shivered at the remembrance of\r\nall that she had gone through. She had been down into the depths and\r\nshe would carry the scars all her life. The girl who had started out so\r\ntriumphantly from Biskra had become a woman through bitter knowledge\r\nand humiliating experience.\r\n\r\nThe pace was less killing now. Silver Star had settled down into the\r\nsteady tireless gallop for which Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s horses were famous.\r\nThe little breeze had died away as quickly as it had sprung up, and it\r\nwas very hot. Diana looked about her with glowing eyes. Everything\r\nseemed different. From the first she had loved the desert, but back of\r\neverything and mingled with everything had been the feeling of fear,\r\nthe continual restraint, the perpetual subservience to the whims of her\r\ncaptor which had dominated everything. But now the whole aspect was\r\nchanged. She loved the endless, undulating expanse stretching out\r\nbefore her, and as the grey topped each rise her interest grew keener.\r\nWhat might not be behind the next one? For an hour or more the ground\r\nrose and fell in monotonous succession, and then the desert grew level\r\nagain and quite suddenly she could see for miles. About two miles away\r\na few palm trees showed clustering together, and Diana turned in their\r\ndirection. They probably meant a well, and it was time she rested her\r\nhorse and herself. It was the tiniest little oasis, and she drew rein\r\nand dismounted with fears for the well she had hoped to find. But there\r\nwas one, very much silted up, and she set to work to clear it as well\r\nas she could to procure enough for herself and Silver Star, who was\r\nfrantically trying to get to the water. It was exhausting work, but she\r\nmanaged to satisfy the grey, and, having unloosed his girths, she flung\r\nherself down on the ground in a small patch of shade. She lit a\r\ncigarette and lay flat on her back with her helmet over her eyes.\r\n\r\nFor the first time since she had shaken off Gaston she began to think\r\nseriously. What she had done was madness. She had no food for herself\r\nor her horse, no water, and Heaven alone knew where the next well might\r\nbe. She was alone in an uncivilised country among a savage people with\r\nno protection of any kind. She might fall in with friendly Arabs or she\r\nmight not. She might come across an encampment, or she might wander for\r\ndays and see no one, in which case death from hunger and thirst stared\r\nher in the face. What would she do when night came? With a sharp cry\r\nshe leaped to her feet. What was she to do? She looked all around the\r\nlittle oasis with startled eyes, at the few palm trees and clumps of\r\ncamel thorn, the broken well and the grey horse still snuffing about\r\nits mouth. She felt frightened for the first time; she was alone and\r\nabout her was unending space, and she felt an atom, insignificant, the\r\nleast of all things. She looked up into the clear sky and the blue\r\nvastness appalled her.\r\n\r\nThen the sudden panic to which she had given way subsided and her\r\ncourage rose with a bound. It was only midday, anything might happen\r\nbetween then and nightfall. Of one thing only she was sure, she did not\r\nrepent of what she had done. Behind her was Ahmed Ben Hassan and before\r\nher was possibly death, and death was preferable. She was quite calm\r\nagain and lay down in the patch of shade once more with a resolute\r\ndetermination to mind. Time to think of them when they came. For the\r\nnext hour or two she must rest and escape the intense heat. She rolled\r\nover on her face with her head in her arms and tried to sleep, but she\r\nwas too excited, and soon gave up the attempt. And in any case, she\r\nargued with herself, she might sleep too long and lose precious time.\r\nShe stretched luxuriously on the soft ground, thankful for the shade\r\nfrom the burning sun. The grey, tired of nosing round the well and\r\nblowing disdainfully at the thorn bushes, wandered over to her side and\r\nnuzzled her gently. She caught at his velvety nose and drew it down\r\nbeside her face. He was a very affectionate beast and gentler than most\r\nof the other horses, and he pressed close up to her, whinnying softly\r\nand looking at her with large expressive eyes. "I haven\'t anything to\r\ngive you, poor old boy," she said regretfully, kissing his muzzle and\r\nthen pushing him away from her. She looked up again into the sky, a\r\ndark speck sailed overhead, the slow heavy flight of a vulture. In a\r\nfew hours he might be picking her bones! Merciful Heavens! Why did such\r\nthoughts come into her head? Had she nothing left of the courage that\r\nhad once been second nature? If she let her nerves get the upper hand\r\nshe might as well make no further effort, but lie down and die at once.\r\nWith shaking fingers she took another cigarette; smoking would soothe\r\nher. Yet she hesitated before she lit it; there were only a few left\r\nand her need might still be greater. But with a reckless laugh she\r\nsnapped the thin case to, and carefully scraped the evil-smelling\r\nsulphur match torn from a flat wood strip. She settled herself\r\ncomfortably again full length. All around her were the innumerable tiny\r\nnoises of the desert, the hum of countless insect life, the rustling of\r\nthe sand and the occasional dry crackle of the camel thorns made by the\r\nslipping of a twig or the displacing of a branch, sounds that would\r\nhave been incomprehensible some weeks before. For a few minutes a sand\r\nspider attracted her attention and she watched his hurried painstaking\r\noperations with wondering interest. Gradually a drowsy feeling stole\r\nover her and she realised suddenly that the air was impregnated with\r\nthe scent of the tobacco that was always associated with the Sheik. It\r\nwas one of his cigarettes that she was smoking. She had always been\r\npowerfully affected by the influence of smell, which induced\r\nrecollection with her to an extraordinary degree, and now the uncommon\r\npenetrating odour of the Arab\'s cigarettes brought back all that she\r\nhad been trying to put out of her mind. With a groan she flung it away\r\nand buried her face in her arms. The past rose up, and rushed,\r\nuncontrolled, through her brain. Incidents crowded into her\r\nrecollection, memories of headlong gallops across the desert riding\r\nbeside the man who, while she hated him, compelled her admiration,\r\nmemories of him schooling the horses that he loved, sitting them like a\r\ncentaur, memories of him amongst his men, memories more intimately\r\nconnected with herself, of his varying moods, his swift changes from\r\nsavage cruelty to amazing gentleness, from brutal intolerance to sudden\r\nconsideration. There had even been times when he had interested her\r\ndespite herself, and she had forgotten the relationship in which they\r\nstood towards each other in listening to his deep, slow voice, till a\r\nword or a gesture brought back the fact vividly. Memories of moments\r\nwhen she had struggled against his caresses, and he had mocked her\r\nhelplessness with his great strength, when she had lain in his arms\r\npanting and exhausted, cold with fear and shrinking from his fierce\r\nkisses. She had feared him as she had never believed it possible to\r\nfear. His face rose before her clearly with all the expressions she had\r\nlearned to know and dread. She tried to banish it, striving with all\r\nher might to put him from her mind, twisting this way and that,\r\nwrithing on the soft sand as she struggled with the obsession that held\r\nher. She saw him all the time plainly, as though he were there before\r\nher. Would he pursue her always, phantom-like? Would the recollection\r\nof the handsome brown face haunt her for ever with its fierce eyes and\r\ncruel mouth? She buried her head deeper in her arms, but the vision\r\npersisted until with a scream she started up with heaving chest and\r\nwild eyes, standing rigid, staring towards the south with a desperate\r\nfixedness that made her eyeballs ache. The sense of his presence had\r\nbeen terribly real. She dropped on to the ground again with an\r\nhysterical laugh, and pushed the thick hair off her forehead wearily.\r\nSilver Star laying his muzzle suddenly on her shoulder made her start\r\nagain violently with heavy, beating heart. A frightened look went\r\nacross her face. "I\'m nervous," she muttered, looking round with a\r\nlittle shiver. "I shall go mad if I stay here much longer." The little\r\noasis that she had hailed so joyfully had become utterly repugnant and\r\nshe was impatient to get away from it. She climbed eagerly into the\r\nsaddle, and, with the rapid motion, she regained her calm and her\r\nspirits rose quickly.\r\n\r\nShe shook off the feeling of apprehension that had taken hold of her\r\nand her nervous fears died away. A reckless feeling, like the\r\nexcitement of the morning, came over her, and she urged the grey on\r\nwith coaxing words, and responding to her voice, and hardly feeling her\r\nlight weight, he raced on untiringly. All around was silence and a\r\nsolitude that was stupendous. The vast emptiness was awe-inspiring. The\r\nafternoon was wearing away; already it was growing cooler. Diana had\r\nseen no sign of human life since she had left Gaston hours before and a\r\nlittle feeling of anxiety stirred faintly deep down in her heart.\r\nTraces of caravans she passed several times, and from the whitening\r\nbones of dead camels she turned her head in aversion--they were too\r\nintimately suggestive. She had seen a few jackals, and once a hyena\r\nlumbered away clumsily among some rocks as she passed. She had got away\r\nfrom the level desert, and was threading her way in and out of some low\r\nhills, which she felt were taking her out of her right course. She was\r\nsteering by the setting sun, which had turned the sky into a glory of\r\ngolden crimson, but the intricate turnings amongst the rocky hills were\r\nbewildering. The low, narrow defile seemed hemming her in, menacing her\r\non all sides, and she was beginning to despair of finding her way out\r\nof the labyrinth, when, on rounding a particularly sharp turn, the\r\nrocks fell away suddenly and she rode out into open country. She\r\nbreathed a sigh of relief and called out cheerily to the grey, but, as\r\nshe looked ahead, her voice died away, and she reined him in sharply\r\nwith a quickening heart-beat. Across the desert about a mile away she\r\nsaw a party of Arabs coming towards her. There were about fifty of\r\nthem, the leader riding a big, black horse some little distance in\r\nfront of his followers. In the clear atmosphere they seemed nearer than\r\nthey were. It was not what she wished. She had hoped for an encampment,\r\nwhere there would be women or a caravan of traders whose constant\r\ncommunication with the towns would make them realise the importance of\r\nguiding her to civilisation unharmed. This band of fighting men, for\r\nshe could see their rifles clearly, and their close and orderly\r\nformation was anything but peaceful, filled her with the greatest\r\nmisgivings. Only the worst might be expected from the wild, lawless\r\ntribesmen towards a woman alone amongst them. She had fled from one\r\nhideousness to another which would be ten times more horrible. Her face\r\nblanched and she set her teeth in desperation. The human beings she had\r\nprayed for were now a deadly menace, and she prayed as fervently that\r\nthey might pass on and not notice her. Perhaps it was not too late,\r\nperhaps they had not yet seen her and she might still slip away and\r\nhide in the twisting turnings of the defile. She backed Silver Star\r\nfurther into the shadow of the rock, but as she did so she saw that she\r\nhad been seen. The leader turned in his saddle and raised his hand high\r\nabove his head, and with a wild shout and a great cloud of dust and\r\nsand his men checked their horses, dragging them back on to their\r\nhaunches, while he galloped towards her alone. And at the same moment\r\nan icy hand clutched at Diana\'s heart and a moan burst from her lips.\r\nThere was no mistaking him or the big black horse he rode. For a moment\r\nshe reeled with a sudden faintness, and then with a tremendous effort\r\nshe pulled herself together, dragging her horse\'s head round and urged\r\nhim back along the track which she had just left, and behind her raced\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan, spurring the great, black stallion as he had never\r\ndone before. With ashy face and wild, hunted eyes Diana crouched\r\nforward on the grey\'s neck, saving him all she could and riding as she\r\nhad never ridden in her life. Utterly reckless, she urged the horse to\r\nhis utmost pace, regardless of the rough, dangerous track. Perhaps she\r\ncould still shake off her pursuer among the tortuous paths of the\r\nhills. Nothing mattered but that. Better even an ugly toss and a broken\r\nneck than that he should take her again. Panic-stricken she wanted to\r\nshriek and clenched her teeth on her lips to keep back the scream that\r\nrose in her throat. She dared not look behind, but straight ahead\r\nbefore her, riding with all her skill, hauling the grey round perilous\r\ncorners and bending lower and lower in the saddle to aid him. In her\r\nterror she had forgotten what a little distance the hills stretched\r\nfrom where she had entered them, and blindly she turned into the track\r\nby which she had come, leaving the main hills on her right hand and\r\nemerging on to the open desert on the south side of the range. There\r\nwas nothing now but the sheer speed of her horse to save her, and how\r\nlong could she count on it? Then with a little glimmer of hope she\r\nremembered that the Sheik was riding The Hawk, own brother to the grey,\r\nand she knew that neither had ever outpaced the other. She had ridden\r\nhard all day, but it was probable that Ahmed Ben Hassan had ridden\r\nharder; he never spared his horses, and his weight was considerably\r\ngreater than hers. Would it not be possible for Silver Star, carrying\r\nthe lighter burden, to outdistance The Hawk? It was a chance. She would\r\ntake it, but she would never give in. The perspiration was rolling down\r\nher face and her breath was coming laboriously. Suddenly, a few minutes\r\nafter she had left the hills behind, the Sheik\'s deep voice came\r\nclearly across the space between them.\r\n\r\n"If you do not stop I will shoot your horse. I give you one minute."\r\n\r\nShe swayed a little in the saddle, clutching the grey\'s neck to steady\r\nherself and for a moment she closed her eyes, but she did not falter\r\nfor an instant. She would not stop; nothing on earth should make her\r\nstop now. Only, because she knew the man, she kicked her feet clear of\r\nthe stirrups. He had said he would shoot and he would shoot, and if the\r\ngrey shied or swerved a hair\'s breadth she would probably receive the\r\nbullet that was meant for him. Better that! Yes, even better that!\r\n\r\nSilver Star tore on headlong and the minute seemed a lifetime. Then\r\nbefore even she heard the report he bounded in the air and fell with a\r\ncrash. Diana was flung far forward and landed on some soft sand. For a\r\nmoment she was stunned by the fall, then she staggered dizzily to her\r\nfeet and stumbled back to the prostrate horse. He was lashing out\r\nwildly with his heels, making desperate efforts to rise. And as she\r\nreached him the black horse dashed up alongside, stopping suddenly, and\r\nrearing straight up. The Sheik leaped to the ground and ran towards\r\nher. He caught her wrist and flung her out of his way, and she lay\r\nwhere she had fallen, every nerve in her body quivering. She was beaten\r\nand with the extinguishing of her last hope all her courage failed her.\r\nShe gave way to sheer, overwhelming terror, utterly cowed. Every\r\nfaculty was suspended, swallowed up in the one dominating force, the\r\ndread of his voice and the dread of the touch of his hands. She heard a\r\nsecond report and knew that he had put Silver Star out of his misery,\r\nand then, in a few seconds, his voice beside her. She got up\r\nunsteadily, shrinking from him.\r\n\r\n"Why are you here, and where is Gaston?"\r\n\r\nIn a stifled voice she told him everything. What did it matter? If she\r\ntried to be silent he would force her to speak.\r\n\r\nHe made no comment, and bringing The Hawk nearer tossed her up roughly\r\ninto the saddle and swung up behind her, the black breaking at once\r\ninto the usual headlong gallop. She made no kind of resistance, a\r\ncomplete apathy seemed to have come over her. She did not look at the\r\nbody of Silver Star, she looked at nothing, clinging to the front of\r\nthe saddle, and staring ahead of her unseeingly. She had dropped her\r\nhelmet when she fell and she had left it, thankful to be relieved of\r\nthe pressure on her aching head. Her mental collapse had affected her\r\nphysically, and it needed a real effort of will-power to enable her to\r\nsit up right. Very soon they would join the horsemen, who were waiting\r\nfor them, and for her pride\'s sake she must concentrate all her energy\r\nto avoid betraying her weakness.\r\n\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan did not go back through the defile, he turned into a\r\nlittle path that Diana had overlooked and which skirted the hills. In\r\nabout half-an-hour the troop met them, riding slowly from the opposite\r\ndirection. She did not raise her eyes as they approached, but she heard\r\nYusef\'s clear tenor voice calling out to the Sheik, who answered\r\nshortly as the men fell in behind him. Back over the ground that she\r\nhad traversed so differently. She knew that it had been madness from\r\nthe first. She should have known that it could never succeed, that she\r\ncould never reach civilisation alone. She had been a fool ever to\r\nimagine that she could win through. The chance that had thrown her\r\nagain into the Sheik\'s power might just as easily have thrown her into\r\nthe hands of any other Arab. Luck had helped Ahmed Ben Hassan even as\r\nshe herself had unknowingly played into his hands when he had captured\r\nher first. Fate was with him. It was useless to try and struggle\r\nagainst him any more. Her brain was a confused medley of thoughts that\r\nshe was too tired to unravel, strange, conflicting ideas chasing wildly\r\nthrough her mind. She did not understand them, she did not try. The\r\neffort of thinking made her head ache agonisingly. She was conscious of\r\na great unrest, a dull aching in her heart and a terrible depression\r\nthat was altogether apart from the fear she felt of the Sheik. She gave\r\nup trying to think; she was concerned only with trying to keep her\r\nbalance.\r\n\r\nShe lifted her head for the first time and looked at the magnificent\r\nsky. The sun had almost set, going down in a ball of molten fire, and\r\nthe heavens on either side were a riot of gold and crimson and palest\r\ngreen, shading off into vivid blue that grew blacker and blacker as the\r\nglory of the sunset died away. The scattered palm trees and the far-off\r\nhills stood out in strong relief. It was a country of marvellous\r\nbeauty, and Diana\'s heart gave a sudden throb as she realised that she\r\nwas going back to it all. She was drooping wearily, unable to sit\r\nupright any longer, and once or twice she jolted heavily against the\r\nman who rode behind her. His nearness had ceased to revolt her; she\r\nthought of it with a dull feeling of wonder. She had even a sense of\r\nrelief at the thought of the strength so close to her. Her eyes rested\r\non his hands, showing brown and muscular under the folds of his white\r\nrobes. She knew the power of the long, lean fingers that could, when he\r\nliked, be gentle enough. Her eyes filled with sudden tears, but she\r\nblinked them back before they fell. She wanted desperately to cry. A\r\nwave of terrible loneliness went over her, a feeling of desolation, and\r\na strange, incomprehensible yearning for what she did not know. As the\r\nsunset faded and it grew rapidly dusk a chill wind sprang up and she\r\nshivered from time to time, drooping more and more with fatigue, at\r\ntimes only half conscious. She had drifted into complete oblivion, when\r\nshe was awakened with a jerk that threw her back violently against the\r\nSheik, but she was too tired to more than barely understand that they\r\nhad stopped for something, and that there were palm trees near her. She\r\nfelt herself lifted down and a cloak wrapped round her, and then she\r\nremembered nothing more. She awoke slowly, shaking off a persistent\r\ndrowsiness by degrees. She was still tired, but the desperate weariness\r\nwas gone, and she was conscious of a feeling of well-being and\r\nsecurity. The cool, night air blew in her face, dissipating her\r\nsleepiness. She became aware that night had fallen, and that they were\r\nstill steadily galloping southward. In a few moments she was wide\r\nawake, and found that she was lying across the saddle in front of the\r\nSheik, and that he was holding her in the crook of his arm. Her head\r\nwas resting just over his heart, and she could feel the regular beat\r\nbeneath her cheek. Wrapped warmly in the cloak and held securely by his\r\nstrong arm at first she was content to give way only to the sensation\r\nof bodily rest. It was enough for the moment to lie with relaxed\r\nmuscles, to have to make no effort of any kind, to feel the soothing\r\nrush of the wind against her face, and the swift, easy gallop of The\r\nHawk as he carried them on through the night. Them! With a start of\r\nrecollection she realised fully whose arm was round her, and whose\r\nbreast her head was resting on. Her heart beat with sudden violence.\r\nWhat was the matter with her? Why did she not shrink from the pressure\r\nof his arm and the contact of his warm, strong body? What had happened\r\nto her? Quite suddenly she knew--knew that she loved him, that she had\r\nloved him for a long time, even when she thought she hated him and when\r\nshe had fled from him. She knew now why his face had haunted her in the\r\nlittle oasis at midday--that it was love calling to her subconsciously.\r\nAll the confusion of mind that had assailed her when they started on\r\nthe homeward journey, the conflicting thoughts and contrary emotions,\r\nwere explained. But she knew herself at last and knew the love that\r\nfilled her, an overwhelming, passionate love that almost frightened her\r\nwith its immensity and with the sudden hold it had laid upon her. Love\r\nhad come to her at last who had scorned it so fiercely. The men who had\r\nloved her had not had the power to touch her, she had given love to no\r\none, she had thought that she could not love, that she was devoid of\r\nall natural affection and that she would never know what love meant.\r\nBut she knew now--a love of such complete surrender that she had never\r\nconceived. Her heart was given for all time to the fierce desert man\r\nwho was so different from all other men whom she had met, a lawless\r\nsavage who had taken her to satisfy a passing fancy and who had treated\r\nher with merciless cruelty. He was a brute, but she loved him, loved\r\nhim for his very brutality and superb animal strength. And he was an\r\nArab! A man of different race and colour, a native; Aubrey would\r\nindiscriminately class him as a "damned nigger." She did not care. It\r\nmade no difference. A year ago, a few weeks even, she would have\r\nshuddered with repulsion at the bare idea, the thought that a native\r\ncould even touch her had been revolting, but all that was swept away\r\nand was nothing in the face of the love that filled her heart so\r\ncompletely. She did not care if he was an Arab, she did not care what\r\nhe was, he was the man she loved. She was deliriously, insanely happy.\r\nShe was lying against his heart, and the clasp of his arm was joy\r\nunspeakable. She was utterly content; for the moment all life narrowed\r\ndown to the immediate surroundings, and she wished childishly that they\r\ncould ride so for ever through eternity. The night was brilliant. The\r\nstars blazed against the inky blackness of the sky, and the light of\r\nthe full moon was startlingly clear and white. The discordant yelling\r\nof a pack of hunting jackals came from a little distance, breaking the\r\nperfect stillness. The men were riding in unusual silence, though a low\r\nexclamation or the subdued jingle of accoutrements was heard\r\noccasionally, once some one fired at a night creature that bounded out\r\nfrom almost under his horse\'s feet. But the Sheik flung a word of\r\nsavage command over his shoulder and there were no more shots. Diana\r\nstirred slightly, moving her head so that she could see his face\r\nshowing clearly in the bright moonlight, which threw some features into\r\nhigh relief and left the rest in dark shadow. She looked at him with\r\nquickening breath. He was peering intently ahead, his eyes flashing in\r\nthe cold light, his brows drawn together in the characteristic heavy\r\nscowl, and the firm chin, so near her face, was pushed out more\r\ndoggedly than usual.\r\n\r\nHe felt her move and glanced down. For a moment she looked straight\r\ninto his eyes, and then with a low, inarticulate murmur she hid her\r\nface against him. He did not speak, but he shifted her weight a little,\r\ndrawing her closer into the curve of his arm.\r\n\r\nIt was very late when they reached the camp. Lights flashed up in the\r\nbig tent and on all sides, and they were surrounded by a crowd of\r\nexcited tribesmen and servants. In spite of the hard day\'s work The\r\nHawk started plunging and rearing, his invariable habit on stopping,\r\nwhich nothing could break, and at a word from the Sheik two men leaped\r\nto his head while he transferred Diana to Yusef\'s outstretched arms.\r\nShe was stiff and giddy, and the young man helped her to the door of\r\nthe tent, and then vanished again into the throng of men and horses.\r\n\r\nDiana sank wearily on to the divan and covered her face with her hands.\r\nShe was trembling with fatigue and apprehension. What would he do to\r\nher? She asked herself the question over and over again, with shaking,\r\nsoundless lips, praying for courage, nerving herself to meet him. At\r\nlast she heard his voice and, looking up, saw him standing in the\r\ndoorway. His back was turned, and he was giving orders to a number of\r\nmen who stood near him, for she could hear their several voices; and\r\nshortly afterwards half-a-dozen small bands of men rode quickly away in\r\ndifferent directions. For a few moments he stood talking to Yusef and\r\nthen came in. At the sight of him Diana shrank back among the soft\r\ncushions, but he took no notice of her, and, lighting a cigarette,\r\nbegan walking up and down the tent. She dared not speak to him, the\r\nexpression on his face was terrible.\r\n\r\nTwo soft-footed Arab servants brought a hastily prepared supper. It was\r\na ghastly meal. He never spoke or showed in any way that he was\r\nconscious of her presence. She had had nothing to eat all day, but the\r\nfood nearly choked her and she could hardly swallow it, but she forced\r\nherself to eat a little. It seemed interminable until the servants\r\nfinally withdrew, after bringing two little gold-cased cups of native\r\ncoffee. She gulped it down with difficulty. The Sheik had resumed his\r\nrestless pacing, smoking cigarette after cigarette in endless\r\nsuccession. The monotonous tramp to and fro worked on Diana\'s nerves\r\nuntil she winced each time he passed her, and, huddled on the divan,\r\nshe watched him continually, fascinated, fearful.\r\n\r\nHe never looked at her. From time to time he glanced at the watch on\r\nhis wrist and each time his face grew blacker. If he would only speak!\r\nHis silence was worse than anything he could say. What was he going to\r\ndo? He was capable of doing anything. The suspense was torture. Her\r\nhands grew clammy and she wrenched at the soft open collar of her\r\nriding-shirt with a feeling of suffocation.\r\n\r\nTwice Yusef came to report, and the second time the Sheik came back\r\nslowly from the door where he had been speaking to him and stopped in\r\nfront of Diana, looking at her strangely.\r\n\r\nShe flung out her hands instinctively, shrinking further back among the\r\ncushions, her eyes wavering under his. "What are you going to do to\r\nme?" she whispered involuntarily, with dry lips.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her without answering for a while, as if to prolong the\r\ntorture she was enduring, and a cruel look crept into his eyes. "That\r\ndepends on what happens to Gaston," he said at length slowly.\r\n\r\n"Gaston?" she repeated stupidly. She had forgotten the valet, in all\r\nthat had occurred since the morning she had forgotten his very\r\nexistence.\r\n\r\n"Yes--Gaston," he said sternly. "You do not seem to have thought of\r\nwhat might happen to him."\r\n\r\nShe sat up slowly, a puzzled look coming into her face. "What could\r\nhappen to him?" she asked wonderingly.\r\n\r\nHe dragged back the flap of the tent and pointed out into the darkness.\r\n"Over there in the south-west, there is an old Sheik whose name is\r\nIbraheim Omair. His tribe and mine have been at feud for generations.\r\nLately I have learned that he has been venturing nearer than he has\r\never before dared. He hates me. To capture my personal servant would be\r\nmore luck than he could have hoped for."\r\n\r\nHe dropped the flap and began walking up and down again. There was a\r\nsinister tone in his voice that made Diana suddenly comprehend the\r\nlittle Frenchman\'s peril. Ahmed Ben Hassan was not the man to be easily\r\nalarmed on any one\'s behalf. That he was anxious about Gaston was\r\napparent, and with her knowledge of him she understood his anxiety\r\nargued a very real danger. She had heard tales before she left Biskra,\r\nand since then she had been living in an Arab camp, and she knew\r\nsomething of the fiendish cruelty and callous indifference to suffering\r\nof the Arabs. Ghastly mental pictures with appalling details crowded\r\nnow into her mind. She shuddered.\r\n\r\n"What would they do to him?" she asked shakily, with a look of horror.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik paused beside her. He looked at her curiously and the cruelty\r\ndeepened in his eyes. "Shall I tell you what they would do to him?" he\r\nsaid meaningly, with a terrible smile.\r\n\r\nShe gave a cry and flung her arms over her head, hiding her face. "Oh,\r\ndo not! Do not!" she wailed.\r\n\r\nHe jerked the ash from his cigarette. "Bah!" he said contemptuously.\r\n"You are squeamish."\r\n\r\nShe felt sick with the realisation of what could result to Gaston from\r\nher action. She had had no personal feeling with regard to him. On the\r\ncontrary, she liked him--she had not thought of him, the man, when she\r\nhad stampeded his horse and left him on foot so far from camp. She had\r\nlooked upon him only as a jailer, his master\'s deputy.\r\n\r\nThe near presence of this hostile Sheik explained many things she had\r\nnot understood: Gaston\'s evident desire daring their ride not to go\r\nbeyond a certain distance, the special activity that had prevailed of\r\nlate amongst the Sheik\'s immediate followers, and the speed and silence\r\nthat had been maintained during the headlong gallop across the desert\r\nthat evening. She had known all along the Arab\'s obvious affection for\r\nhis French servant, and it was confirmed now by the anxiety that he did\r\nnot take the trouble to conceal--so unlike his usual complete\r\nindifference to suffering or danger.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him thoughtfully. There were still depths that she had\r\nnot fathomed in his strange character. Would she ever arrive at even a\r\ndistant understanding of his complex nature? There was a misty yearning\r\nin her eyes as they followed his tall figure up and down the tent.
