His\r\nfeet made no sound on the thick rugs, and he moved with the long,\r\ngraceful stride that always reminded her of the walk of a wild animal.\r\nHer new-found love longed for expression as she watched him. If she\r\ncould only tell him! If she had only the right to go to him and in his\r\narms to kiss away the cruel lines from his mouth! But she had not. She\r\nmust wait until she was called, until he should choose to notice the\r\nwoman whom he had taken for his pleasure, until the baser part of him\r\nhad need of her again. He was an Arab, and to him a woman was a slave,\r\nand as a slave she must give everything and ask for nothing.\r\n\r\nAnd when he did turn to her again the joy she would feel in his embrace\r\nwould be an agony for the love that was not there. His careless kisses\r\nwould scorch her and the strength of his arms would be a mockery. But\r\nwould he ever turn to her again? If anything happened to Gaston--if\r\nwhat he had suggested became a fact and the servant fell a victim to\r\nthe blood feud between the two tribes? She knew he would be terribly\r\navenged, and what would her part be? She wondered dully if he would\r\nkill her, and how. If the long, brown fingers with their steely\r\nstrength would choke the life out of her. Her hands went up to her\r\nthroat mechanically. He stopped near her to light a fresh cigarette,\r\nand she was trying to summon up courage to speak to him of Gaston when\r\nthe covering of the doorway was flung open and Gaston himself stood in\r\nthe entrance.\r\n\r\n"Monseigneur--" he stammered, and with his two hands outstretched, palm\r\nuppermost, he made an appealing gesture.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik\'s hand shot out and gripped the man\'s shoulder. "Gaston!\r\n_Enfin, mon ami!_" he said slowly, but there was a ring in his low\r\nvoice that Diana had never heard before.\r\n\r\nFor a moment the two men stared at each other, and then Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan gave a little laugh of great relief. "Praise be to Allah, the\r\nMerciful, the Compassionate," he murmured.\r\n\r\n"To his name praise!" rejoined Gaston softly, then his eyes roved\r\naround the tent towards Diana, and there was no resentment in them, but\r\nonly anxiety.\r\n\r\n"Madame is----" he hesitated, but the Sheik cut him short.\r\n\r\n"Madame is quite safe," he said dryly, and pushed him gently towards\r\nthe door with a few words in rapid Arabic. He stood some time after\r\nGaston had gone to his own quarters looking out into the night, and\r\nwhen he came in, lingered unusually over closing the flap. Diana stood\r\nhesitating. She was worn out and her long riding-boots felt like lead.\r\nShe was afraid to go and afraid to stay. He seemed purposely ignoring\r\nher. The relief of Gaston\'s return was enormous, but she had still to\r\nreckon with him for her attempted flight. That he said no word about it\r\nat the moment meant nothing; she knew him too well for that. And there\r\nwas Silver Star, the finest of all his magnificent horses--she had yet\r\nto pay for his death. The strain that she had gone through since the\r\nmorning was tremendous, she could not bear much more. His silence\r\naggravated her breaking nerves until she felt that her nerves would go.\r\nHe had moved over to the writing-table and was tearing the wrapping off\r\na box of cartridges preparatory to refilling the magazine of his\r\nrevolver. The little operation seemed to take centuries. She started at\r\neach separate click. She gripped her hands and passed her tongue over\r\nher dry lips. If he would not speak she must, she could endure it no\r\nlonger.\r\n\r\n"I am sorry about Silver Star," she faltered, and even to herself her\r\nvoice sounded hoarse and strange. He did not answer, but only shrugged\r\nhis shoulders as he dropped the last cartridge into its place.\r\n\r\nThe gesture and his uncompromising attitude exasperated her. "You had\r\nbetter have shot me," she said bitterly.\r\n\r\n"Perhaps. You would have been easier replaced. There are plenty of\r\nwomen, but Silver Star was almost unique," he retorted quickly, and she\r\nwinced at the cold brutality of his tone.\r\n\r\nA little sad smile curved her lips. "Yet you shot your horse to get me\r\nback," she said in a barely audible voice.\r\n\r\nHe flung round with an oath. "You little fool! Do you know so little of\r\nme yet? Do you think that I will let anything stand between me and what\r\nI want? Do you think that by running away from me you will make me want\r\nyou less? By Allah! I would have found you if you had got as far as\r\nFrance. What I have I keep, until I tire of it--and I have not tired of\r\nyou yet." He jerked her to him, staring down at her passionately, and\r\nfor a moment his face was the face of a devil. "How shall I punish\r\nyou?" He felt the shudder he expected go through her and laughed as she\r\nshrank in his arms and hid her face. He forced her head up with\r\nmerciless fingers. "What do you hate most?--my kisses?" and with\r\nanother mocking laugh he crushed his mouth to hers in a long\r\nsuffocating embrace.\r\n\r\nThen he let her go suddenly, and, blind and dizzy, she reeled from him\r\nand staggered. He caught her as she swayed and swept her into his arms.\r\nHer head fell back against his shoulder and his face changed at the\r\nsight of her quivering features. He carried her into the adjoining room\r\nand laid her on the couch, his hands lingering as he drew them from\r\nher. For a moment he stood looking down with smouldering eyes on the\r\nslight, boyish figure lying on the bed, the ferocity dying out of his\r\nface. "Take care you do not wake the devil in me again, _ma\r\nbelle,"_ he said sombrely.\r\n\r\nAlone Diana turned her face into the pillows with a moan of anguish.\r\nBack in the desert a few hours ago, under the shining stars, when the\r\ntruth had first come to her, she had thought that she was happy, but\r\nshe knew now that without his love she would never be happy. She had\r\ntasted the bitterness of his loveless kisses and she knew that a worse\r\nbitterness was to come, and she writhed at the thought of what her life\r\nwith him would be.\r\n\r\n"I love him! I love him! And I want his love more than anything in\r\nHeaven and earth."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\n\r\n\r\nDiana was sitting on the divan in the living-room of the tent lingering\r\nover her _petit dejeuner_, a cup of coffee poised in one hand and\r\nher bright head bent over a magazine on her knee. It was a French\r\nperiodical of fairly recent date, left a few days before by a Dutchman\r\nwho was touring through the desert, and who had asked a night\'s\r\nhospitality. Diana had not seen him, and it was not until the traveller\r\nhad been served with dinner in his own tent that the Sheik had sent the\r\nusual flowery message conveying what, though wrapped in honeyed words,\r\namounted practically to a command that he should come to drink coffee\r\nand let himself be seen. Only native servants had been in attendance,\r\nand it was an Arab untinged by any Western influence who had received\r\nhim, talking only Arabic, which the Dutchman spoke fluently, and\r\nplacing at his disposal himself, his servants and all his belongings\r\nwith the perfunctory Oriental insincerity which the traveller knew\r\nmeant nothing and accepted at its own value, returning to the usual set\r\nphrases the customary answers that were expected of him. Once or twice\r\nas they talked a woman\'s subdued voice had reached the Dutchman\'s ears\r\nfrom behind the thick curtains, but he knew too much to let any\r\nexpression betray him, and he smiled grimly to himself at the thought\r\nof the change that an indiscreet question would bring to the stern face\r\nof his grave and impassive host. He was an elderly man with a tender\r\nheart, and he wondered speculatively what the girl in the next room\r\nwould have to pay for her own indiscretion in allowing her voice to be\r\nheard. He left the next morning early without seeing the Sheik again,\r\nescorted for some little distance by Yusef and a few men.\r\n\r\nDiana read eagerly. Anything fresh to read was precious. She looked\r\nlike a slender boy in the soft riding-shirt and smart-cut breeches, one\r\nslim foot in a long brown boot drawn up under her, and the other\r\nswinging idly against the side of the divan. She finished her coffee\r\nhastily, and, lighting a cigarette, leaned back with a sigh of content\r\nover the magazine.\r\n\r\nTwo months had slipped away since her mad flight, since her dash for\r\nfreedom that had ended in tragedy for the beautiful Silver Star and so\r\nunexpectedly for herself. Weeks of vivid happiness that had been mixed\r\nwith poignant suffering, for the perfect joy of being with him was\r\nmarred by the passionate longing for his love. Even her surroundings\r\nhad taken on a new aspect, her happiness coloured everything. The\r\nEastern luxury of the tent and its appointments no longer seemed\r\ntheatrical, but the natural setting of the magnificent specimen of\r\nmanhood who surrounded himself by all the display dear to the heart of\r\nthe native. How much was for his own pleasure and how much was for the\r\nsake of his followers she had never been able to determine. The\r\nbeauties and attractions of the desert had multiplied a hundred times.\r\nThe wild tribesmen, with their primitive ways and savagery, had ceased\r\nto disgust her, and the free life with its constant exercise and simple\r\nroutine was becoming indefinitely dear to her. The camp had been moved\r\nseveral times--always towards the south--and each change had been a\r\nsource of greater interest.\r\n\r\nAnd since the night that he had carried her back in triumph he had been\r\nkind to her--kind beyond anything that she had expected. He had never\r\nmade any reference to her fight or to the death of the horse that he\r\nhad valued so highly; in that he had been generous. The episode over,\r\nhe wished no further allusion to it. But there was nothing beyond\r\nkindness. The passion that smouldered in his dark eyes often was not\r\nthe love she craved, it was only the desire that her uncommon type and\r\nher utter dissimilarity from all the other women who had passed through\r\nhis hands had awakened in him. The perpetual remembrance of those other\r\nwoman brought her a constant burning shame that grew stronger every\r\nday, a shame that was only less strong than her ardent love, and a wild\r\njealousy that tortured her with doubts and fears, an ever-present demon\r\nof suggestion reminding her of the past when it was not she who lay in\r\nhis arms, nor her lips that received his kisses. The knowledge that the\r\nembraces she panted for had been shared by _les autres_ was an\r\nopen wound that would not heal. She tried to shut her mind to the past.\r\nShe knew that she was a fool to expect the abstinence of a monk in the\r\nstrong, virile desert man. And she was afraid for the future. She\r\nwanted him for herself alone, wanted his undivided love, and that he\r\nwas an Arab with Oriental instincts filled her with continual dread,\r\ndread of the real future about which she never dared to think, dread of\r\nthe passing of his transient desire. She loved him so passionately, so\r\ncompletely, that beyond him was nothing. He was all the world. She gave\r\nherself to him gladly, triumphantly, as she would give her life for him\r\nif need be. But she had schooled herself to hide her love, to yield\r\napathetically to his caresses, and to conceal the longing that\r\npossessed her. She was afraid that the knowledge that she loved him\r\nwould bring about the disaster she dreaded. The words that he had once\r\nused remained continually in her mind: "If you loved me you would bore\r\nme, and I should have to let you go." And she hid her love closely in\r\nher heart. It was difficult, and it hurt her to hide it from him and to\r\nassume indifference. It was difficult to remember that she must make a\r\nshow of reluctance when she was longing to give unreservedly. She\r\ndropped the end of the cigarette hissing into the dregs of the coffee\r\nand turned a page, and, as she did so, she looked up suddenly, the\r\nmagazine dropping unheeded on the floor. Close outside the tent the\r\nsame low, vibrating baritone was singing the Kashmiri love song that\r\nshe had heard last the night before she left Biskra. She sat tense, her\r\neyes growing puzzled.\r\n\r\n_"Pale hands I loved beside the Shalimar. Where are you now? Who lies\r\nbeneath your spell?"_\r\n\r\nThe voice came nearer and he swept in, still singing, and came to her.\r\n_"Pale hands, pink tipped,"_ he sang, stopping in front of her and\r\ncatching her fingers in his up to his lips, but she tore them away\r\nbefore he kissed them.\r\n\r\n"You do know English?" she cried sharply, her eyes searching his.\r\n\r\nHe flung himself on the divan beside her with a laugh. "Because I sing\r\nan English song?" he replied in French. "_La! la!_ I heard a\r\nSpanish boy singing in \'Carmen\' once in Paris who did not know a word\r\nof French beside the score. He learned it parrot-like, as I learn your\r\nEnglish songs," he added, smiling.\r\n\r\nShe watched him light a cigarette, and her forehead wrinkled\r\nthoughtfully. "It was you who sang outside the hotel in Biskra that\r\nnight?" she asked at last, more statement than question.\r\n\r\n"One is mad sometimes, especially when the moon is high," he replied\r\nteasingly.\r\n\r\n"And was it you who came into my bedroom and put the blank cartridges\r\nin my revolver?"\r\n\r\nHis arm stole round her, drawing her to him, and he lifted her head up\r\nso that he could look into her eyes. "Do you think that--I would have\r\nallowed anybody else to go to your room at night?--I, an Arab, when I\r\nmeant you for myself?"\r\n\r\n"You were so sure?"\r\n\r\nHe laughed softly, as if the suggestion that any plan of his could be\r\nliable to miscarriage amused him infinitely, and the smouldering\r\npassion flamed up in his dark eyes. He strained her to him hungrily, as\r\nif her slim body lying against his had awakened the sleeping fires\r\nwithin him. She struggled against the pressure of his arm, averting her\r\nhead.\r\n\r\n"Always cold?" he chided. "Kiss me, little piece of ice."\r\n\r\nShe longed to, and it almost broke her heart to persevere in her\r\nefforts to repulse him. A wild desire seized her to tell him that she\r\nloved him, to make an end once and for all of the misery of doubt and\r\nfear that was sapping her strength from her, and abide by the issue.\r\nBut the spark of hope that lived in her heart gave her courage, and she\r\nfought down the burning words that sought utterance, forcing\r\nindifference into her eyes and a mutinous pout to her lips.\r\n\r\nHis black brows drew together slowly. "Still disobedient? You said you\r\nwould obey me. I loathe the English, but I thought their word----"\r\n\r\nShe interrupted him with a quick gesture, and, turning her face to his,\r\nfor the first time kissed him voluntarily, brushing his tanned cheek\r\nwith swift, cold lips.\r\n\r\nHe laughed disdainfully. "_Bon Dieu_! Has the hot sun of the\r\ndesert taught you no better than that? Have you learned so little from\r\nme? Has the vile climate of your detestable country frozen you so\r\nthoroughly that nothing can melt you? Or is there some man in England\r\nwho has the power to turn you from a statue to a woman?" he added, with\r\nan angry snarl.\r\n\r\nShe clenched her hands with the pain of his words. "There is no one,"\r\nshe muttered, "but I--I don\'t feel like that."\r\n\r\n"You had better learn," he said thickly. "I am tired of holding an\r\nicicle in my arms," and sweeping her completely into his masterful\r\ngrasp he covered her face with fierce, burning kisses.\r\n\r\nAnd for the first time she surrendered to him wholly, clinging to him\r\npassionately, and giving him kiss for kiss with an absolute abandon of\r\nall resistance. At last he let her go, panting and breathless, and\r\nleaped up, drawing his hand across his eyes.\r\n\r\n"You go to my head, Diane," he said, with a laugh that was half anger,\r\nand shrugging his shoulders moved across the tent to the chest where\r\nthe spare arms were kept, and unlocking it took out a revolver and\r\nbegan to clean it.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him bewildered. What had he meant? How could she\r\nreconcile what he said with the advice that he had given her before?\r\nWas he totally inconsistent? Did he, after all, want the satisfaction\r\nof knowing that he had made her love him--of flattering himself on the\r\npower he exercised over her? Did he care that he was able to torture\r\nher heart with a refinement of cruelty that took all and gave nothing?\r\nDid he wish her to crawl abjectly to his feet to give him the pleasure\r\nof spurning her contemptuously, or was it only that he wanted her\r\nsenses merely to respond to his ardent, Eastern temperament? Her face\r\ngrew hot and shamed. She knew the fiery nature that was hidden under\r\nhis impassive exterior and knew the control he exercised over himself,\r\nknew, too, that the strain he put upon himself was liable to be broken\r\nwith unexpected suddenness. It was an easy thing to rule his wild\r\nfollowers, and she guessed that the relaxation that he looked for in\r\nthe privacy of his own tent meant more to him than he would ever have\r\nadmitted, than perhaps he even know. The hatred and defiance with which\r\nshe had repelled him had provoked and amused him, but it had also at\r\ntimes angered him.\r\n\r\nHe was very human, and there must have been moments when he wanted a\r\nwilling mate rather than a rebellious prisoner. She gave a quick sigh\r\nas she looked at him. He was so strong, so vigorous, so intensely\r\nalive. It was going to be very difficult to anticipate his moods and be\r\nsubservient to his temper. She sighed again wearily. If she could but\r\nmake him and keep him happy. She ruffled her loose curls, tugging them\r\nwith a puzzled frown, a trick that was a survival of her nursery days,\r\nwhen she clutched frantically at her red-gold mop to help her settle\r\nany childish difficulty.\r\n\r\nShe knelt up suddenly on the cushions of the divan. "Why do you hate\r\nthe English so bitterly, Monseigneur?" She had dropped almost\r\nunconsciously into Gaston\'s mode of address for some time; it was often\r\nawkward to give him no name, and she shrank from using his own; and the\r\ntitle fitted him.\r\n\r\nHe looked up from his work, and, gathering the materials together,\r\nbrought them over to the divan. "Light me a cigarette, _cherie_,\r\nmy hands are busy," he replied irrelevantly.\r\n\r\nShe complied with a little laugh. "You haven\'t answered my question."\r\n\r\nHe polished the gleaming little weapon in his hand for some time\r\nwithout speaking. "_Ma petite_ Diane, your lips are of an adorable\r\nredness and your voice is music in my ears, but--I detest questions.\r\nThey bore me to a point of exasperation," he said at last lightly, and\r\nstarted humming the Kashmiri song again.\r\n\r\nShe knew him well enough to know that all questions did not bore him,\r\nbut that she must have touched some point connected with the past of\r\nwhich she was ignorant that affected him, and to prove her knowledge\r\nshe asked another question. "Why do you sing? You have never sung\r\nbefore."\r\n\r\nHe looked at her with a smile of amusement at her pertinacity.\r\n"Inquisitive one! I sing because I am glad. Because my friend is\r\ncoming."\r\n\r\n"Your friend?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, by Allah! The best friend a man ever had. Raoul de Saint Hubert."\r\n\r\nShe flashed a look at the bookcase with a jerk of her head, and he\r\nnodded. "Coming here?" she queried, and the dismay she felt sounded in\r\nher voice.\r\n\r\nHe frowned in quick annoyance at her tone. "Why not?" he said\r\nhaughtily.\r\n\r\n"No reason," she murmured, sinking down among the cushions again and\r\npicking up the magazine from the floor. The advent of a stranger--a\r\nEuropean--was a shock, but she felt that the Sheik\'s eyes were on her\r\nand she determined to show no feeling in his presence. "What time will\r\nyou be ready to ride?" she asked indifferently, with a simulated yawn,\r\nflirting over the pages.\r\n\r\n"I can\'t ride with you to-day. I am going to meet Saint Hubert. His\r\ncourier only came an hour ago. It is two years since I have seen him."\r\n\r\nDiana slipped off the couch and went to the open doorway. A detachment\r\nof men were already waiting for him, and, close by the tent, Shaitan of\r\nthe ugly temper was biting and fidgeting in the hands of the grooms.\r\nShe scowled at the beautiful, wicked creature\'s flat-laid ears and\r\nrolling eyes. She would have backed him fearlessly herself if the Sheik\r\nhad let her, but she was nervous for him every time he rode the vicious\r\nbeast. No one but the Sheik could manage him, and though she knew that\r\nhe had perfect mastery over the horse, she never lost the feeling of\r\nnervousness, a sensation the old Diana had never, never experienced,\r\nand she wished to-day that it had been any other horse but Shaitan\r\nwaiting for him.\r\n\r\nShe went back to him slowly. "It makes my head ache, to stay in all\r\nday. May Gaston not ride with me?" she asked diffidently, her eyes\r\nanywhere but on his face. He had not allowed her to ride with any one\r\nexcept himself since her attempted escape, and to her tentative\r\nsuggestions that the rides with the valet might be resumed he had given\r\na prompt refusal. He hesitated now, and she was afraid he was going to\r\nrefuse again, and she looked up wistfully. "Please, Monseigneur," she\r\nwhispered humbly.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her for a moment with his chin squarer than usual. "Are\r\nyou going to run away again?" he asked bluntly.\r\n\r\nHer eyes filled slowly with tears, and she turned her head away to hide\r\nthem. "No, I am not going to run away again," she said very low.\r\n\r\n"Very well, I will tell him. He will be delighted, _le bon_\r\nGaston. He is your very willing slave in spite of the trick you played\r\nhim. He has a beautiful nature, _le pauvre diable_. He is not an\r\nArab, eh, little Diane?" The mocking smile was back in his eyes as he\r\nturned her face up to his in the usual peremptory way. Then he held out\r\nthe revolver he had been cleaning with sudden seriousness. "I want you\r\nto carry this always now when you ride. Ibraheim Omair is still in the\r\nneighbourhood."\r\n\r\nShe looked at it blankly.\r\n\r\n"But----" she stammered.\r\n\r\nHe knew what was in her mind, and he stooped and kissed her lightly. "I\r\ntrust you," he said quietly, and went out.\r\n\r\nShe followed him to the door, the revolver dangling from her hand, and\r\nwatched him mount and ride away. His horsemanship was superb and her\r\neyes glowed as they followed him. She went back into the tent and\r\nslipped the revolver into the holster he had left lying on a stool,\r\nand, tucking it and Saint Hubert\'s novel, which she took from the\r\nbookcase, under her arm, went into the bed-room and, calling to Zilah\r\nto pull off her riding-boots, threw herself on the bed to laze away the\r\nmorning, and to try and picture the author from the book he had\r\nwritten.\r\n\r\nShe hated him in advance; she was jealous of him and of his coming. The\r\nSheik\'s sudden new tenderness had given rise to a hope she hardly dared\r\nallow herself to dwell upon. Might not the power that she had exercised\r\nover other men be still extended to him in spite of the months that he\r\nhad been indifferent to anything except the mere physical attraction\r\nshe had for him? Was it not possible that out of that attraction might\r\ndevelop something finer and better than the primitive desire she had\r\naroused? Oriental though he was, might he not be capable of a deep and\r\nlasting affection? He might have loved her if no outside influence had\r\ncome to interrupt the routine that had become so intimately a part of\r\nhis life. Those other episodes to which he had referred so lightly had\r\nbeen a matter of days or weeks, not months, as in her case. He might\r\nhave cared but for the coming of this Frenchman. She hurled Saint\r\nHubert\'s book across the room in a fit of girlish rage and buried her\r\nhead in her arms. He would be odious--a smirking, conceited egotist!\r\nShe had met several French writers and she visualised him\r\ncontemptuously. His books were undoubtedly clever. So much the worse;\r\nhe would be correspondingly inflated. His novel revealed a passionate,\r\nemotional temperament that promised to complicate the situation if he\r\nshould be pleased to cast an eye of favour on her. She writhed at the\r\nvery thought. And that he was to see her was evident; the Sheik had\r\nleft no orders to the contrary. It was not to be the case of the Dutch\r\ntraveller, when the fact that she belonged to an Arab had been brought\r\nhome to her effectually by Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s peremptory commands, and\r\nshe had experienced for the first time the sensation of a woman kept in\r\nseclusion.\r\n\r\nThe emotions of the morning and the disappointment of the intended\r\nride, together with the dismay produced by the unexpected visitor, all\r\ncombined to agitate her powerfully, and she worked herself up into a\r\nfever of self-torture and unhappiness. She ended by falling asleep and\r\nslept heavily for some hours. Zilah waked her with a shy hand on her\r\narm and a soft announcement of lunch, and Diana sat up, rubbing her\r\neyes, flushed and drowsy. She stared uncomprehendingly for a moment at\r\nthe Arab girl, and then waved her away imperiously and buried her head\r\nin the pillows again. Lunch, when her heart was breaking!\r\n\r\nMindful of her lord\'s deputy, who was waiting in the next room, and\r\nwhom she regarded with awe, Zilah held her ground with a timid\r\ninsistence until Diana started up wrathfully and bade her go in tones\r\nthat she had never used before to the little waiting-girl. Zilah fled\r\nprecipitately, and, thoroughly awakened, Diana swung her heels to the\r\nground and with her elbows on her knees rested her hot head in her\r\nhands. She felt giddy, her head ached and her mouth was parched and\r\ndry. She got up languidly, and going to the table studied her face in\r\nthe mirror intently. She frowned at the reflection. She had never been\r\nproud of her own beauty; she had lived with it always and it had seemed\r\nto her a thing of no consequence, and now that it had failed to arouse\r\nthe love she wanted in Ahmed Ben Hassan she almost hated it.\r\n\r\n"Are you going to have fever or are you merely bad-tempered?" she asked\r\nout loud, and the sound of her own voice made her laugh in spite of her\r\nheavy heart. She went into the bathroom and soused her head in cold\r\nwater. When she came back a frightened Zilah was putting a small tray\r\non the brass-topped table by the bed.\r\n\r\n"M\'sieur Gaston," she stammered, almost crying.\r\n\r\nDiana looked at the tray, arranged with all the dainty neatness dear to\r\nthe valet\'s heart, and then at the travelling clock on the table beside\r\nit, and realised that it was an hour past her usual lunch-time and that\r\nshe was extremely hungry, after all. A little piece of paper on the\r\ntray caught her eye, and, picking it up, she read in Gaston\'s clear\r\nthough minute handwriting, "At what hour does Madame desire to ride?"\r\n\r\nThe servant clearly had no intention of giving up the programme for the\r\nafternoon without a struggle. She smiled as she added a figure to the\r\nend of the note, and went to the curtains that divided the rooms.\r\n"Gaston!"\r\n\r\n"Madame!"\r\n\r\nShe passed the paper silently through the curtains and went back to her\r\nlunch. When she sent Zilah away with the empty tray she rescued the\r\nVicomte de Saint Hubert\'s book from the floor where she had thrown it\r\nand tried to read it dispassionately. She turned to the title-page and\r\nstudied the pencilled scrawl "Souvenir de Raoul" closely. It did not\r\nlook like the handwriting of a small-minded man, but handwriting was\r\nnothing to go by, she argued obstinately. Aubrey, who was the essence\r\nof selfishness, wrote beautifully, and had once been told by an expert\r\nthat his writing denoted a generous love of his fellow-men, which\r\ndeduction had aroused no enthusiasm in the baronet, and had given his\r\nsister over to helpless mirth. She turned the pages, dipping here and\r\nthere, finally forgetting the author altogether in the book. It was a\r\nwonderful story of a man\'s love and faithfulness, and Diana pushed it\r\naside at last with a very bitter sigh. Things happened so in books. In\r\nreal life they happened very differently. She looked round the room\r\nwith pain-filled eyes, at the medley of her own and the Sheik\'s\r\nbelongings, her ivory toilet appointments jostling indiscriminately\r\namong his brushes and his razors on the dressing-table, and then at the\r\npillow beside her where his head rested every night. She stooped and\r\nkissed it with a little quivering breath. "Ahmed. Oh, Monseigneur!" she\r\nmurmured longingly. Then, with an impatient jerk of the head, she\r\nsprang up and dragged on her boots. She pulled a soft felt hat down\r\nover her eyes and picked up the revolver the Sheik had given her. She\r\npaused a moment, looking at it with an odd smile before buckling it\r\nround her slim waist. Gaston\'s face lit up with genuine pleasure when\r\nshe came out to the horses. She had felt a momentary embarrassment\r\nbefore she left the tent, thinking of the last time he had ridden with\r\nher, but she had known from the moment he came back that night that he\r\nbore no malice, and the look on his face and his stammered words to the\r\nSheik had indicated that the fear he felt for her was not for what\r\nmight have happened in the desert, but for what might yet happen to her\r\nat the hands of his master and hers.\r\n\r\nThe horse that she rode always now was pure white, not so fast as\r\nSilver Star and very tricky, called The Dancer, from a nervous habit of\r\ndancing on his hind-legs at starting and stopping, like a circus-horse.\r\nHe was difficult to mount, and edged away shyly as Diana tried to get\r\nher foot into the stirrup. But she swung up at last, and by the time\r\nThe Dancer had finished his display of _haute ecole_ Gaston was\r\nmounted. "After riding The Dancer I feel confident to enter for the\r\n_Concours Hippique_," she laughed over her shoulder, and touched\r\nthe horse with her heel.\r\n\r\nShe wanted exercise primarily, hard physical exercise that would tire\r\nher out and keep her mind occupied and prevent her from thinking, and\r\nthe horse she rode supplied both needs. He required watching all the\r\ntime. She let him out to his full pace for his own sake and hers, and\r\nthe air and the movement banished her headache, and a kind of\r\nexhilaration came over her, making her almost happy. After a while she\r\nreined in her horse and waved to Gaston to come alongside. "Tell me of\r\nthis Vicomte de Saint Hubert who is coming. You know him, I suppose, as\r\nyou have been so long with Monseigneur?"\r\n\r\nGaston smiled. "I knew him before Monseigneur did. I was born on the\r\nestate of Monsieur le Comte de Saint Hubert, the father of Monsieur le\r\nVicomte. I and my twin brother Henri. We both went into Monsieur\'s le\r\nComte\'s training stables, and then after our time in the Cavalry Henri\r\nbecame valet to Monsieur le Vicomte, and I came to Monseigneur."\r\n\r\nDiana took off her hat and rubbed her forehead thoughtfully. Fifteen\r\nyears ago Ahmed must have been about twenty. Why should an Arab chief\r\nof that age, or any age, indulge in such an anomaly as a French valet,\r\nor for that matter why should a French valet attach himself to an Arab\r\nSheik and exile himself in the wilds of the desert? Whichever way she\r\nturned, the mystery of the man she loved seemed to crop up. She started\r\narguing with herself in a circle--why should the Sheik have a European\r\nservant or why should he not, until she gave it up in hopeless\r\nconfusion.\r\n\r\nShe turned to Gaston with the intention of asking further of the coming\r\nvisitor, and, keeping The Dancer as still as she could, sat looking at\r\nthe valet with great, questioning eyes, fanning her hot face with her\r\nhat. Gaston, whose own horse stood like a rock, was frankly mopping his\r\nforehead. Dianna decided against any more questions. Gaston would\r\nnaturally be hopelessly biased, having been born and brought up in the\r\nshadow of the family, and after all she would rather judge for herself.\r\nOne inquiry only she permitted herself: "The family of Saint Hubert,\r\nare they of the old or the new _noblesse?"_\r\n\r\n"Of the old, Madame," replied Gaston quickly.\r\n\r\nDiana coaxed her nervous mount close beside his steadier companion,\r\nand, thrusting his bridle and her hat into Gaston\'s hands, slipped to\r\nthe ground and walked away a little distance to the top of a small\r\nmound. She sat down on the summit with her back to the horses and her\r\narms clasped round her knees. All that the coming of this strange man\r\nmeant to her rushed suddenly over her. He was a man, obviously, who\r\nmoved in the world, her world, since he apparently travelled\r\nextensively and his father was wealthy enough to run a racing stable as\r\na hobby and was a member of the dwindling class of _ancienne\r\nnoblesse_. It was characteristic of her that she put first what she\r\ndid. How could she bear to meet one of her own order in the position in\r\nwhich she was? She who had been proud Diana Mayo and now--the mistress\r\nof an Arab Sheik? She laid her face on her knees with a shudder. The\r\nordeal before her cut like a knife into her heart. The pride that Ahmed\r\nBen Hassan had not yet killed flamed up and racked her with humiliation\r\nand shame, the shame that still seared her soul like a hot iron, so\r\nthat there were moments she could not bear even the presence of the man\r\nwho had made her what she was, in spite of the love she bore him, and,\r\npleading fever, prayed to be alone. Not that he ever granted her\r\nprayer, for he knew fever when he saw it, but would pull her down\r\nbeside him with a mocking laugh that still had the power to hurt so\r\nmuch. The thought of what it would be to her to meet his friend had\r\npresumably never entered his mind, or if it had it had made no\r\nimpression and been dismissed as negligible. It was the point of view,\r\nshe supposed drearily; the standpoint from which he looked at things\r\nwas fundamentally different from her own--racially and temperamentally\r\nthey were poles apart. To him she was only the woman held in bondage, a\r\nthing of no account. She sat very still for a while with her face\r\nhidden, until a discreet cough from Gaston warned her that time was\r\nflying. She went back to the horses slowly with white face and\r\ncompressed lips. There was the usual trouble in mounting, and her\r\nstrained nerves made her impatient of The Dancer\'s idiosyncrasies, and\r\nshe checked him sharply, making him rear dangerously.\r\n\r\n"Careful, Madame," cried Gaston warningly.\r\n\r\n"For whom--me or Monseigneur\'s horse?" she retorted bitterly, and\r\nignoring her hat, which Gaston held out to her with reproachful eyes,\r\nshe spurred the horse viciously, making him break into a headlong\r\ngallop. It had got to be gone through, so get it over as soon as\r\npossible. And behind her, Gaston, for the first time in all his long\r\nservice, cursed the master he would cheerfully have died for.\r\n\r\nThe horse\'s nerves, like her own, were on edge, and he pulled badly,\r\nhis smooth satiny neck growing dark and seamed with sweat; Diana needed\r\nall her knowledge to control him, and she began to wonder if when they\r\ncame to the camp she would be able to stop him. She topped an\r\nundulation that was some little distance from the tents with\r\nmisgivings, and wrapped the reins round her hands to prevent them\r\nslipping through her fingers. As they neared she saw the Sheik standing\r\noutside his tent, with a tall, thin man beside him. She had only a\r\nglimpse of dark, unruly hair and a close-cut beard as she shot past,\r\nunable to pull up The Dancer. But just beyond the tent, with the reins\r\ncutting into her hands, she managed to haul him round and bring him\r\nback. A couple of grooms jumped to his head, but, owing to his peculiar\r\ntactics, landed short, and he pranced to his own satisfaction and\r\nDiana\'s rage, until the amusement of it passed and he let himself be\r\ncaught. Diana had done nothing to stop him once she had managed to turn\r\nhim. If the horse chose to behave like a fool she was not going to be\r\nmade to look foolish by fighting him when she knew that it was useless.\r\nIn the hands of the men he sidled and snorted, and, dropping the reins,\r\nDiana pulled off her gloves and sat for a moment rubbing her sore\r\nhands. Then the Sheik came forward and she slid down. Before looking at\r\nhim she turned and, catching at The Dancer\'s head, struck him angrily\r\nover the nose with her thick riding-gloves and watched him led away,\r\nplunging and protesting, pulling the gloves through her fingers\r\nnervously, until Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s voice made her turn.\r\n\r\n"Diane, the Vicomte de Saint Hubert waits to be presented to you."\r\n\r\nShe drew herself up and the colour that had come into her face drained\r\nout of it again. Slowly she glanced up at the man standing before her,\r\nand looked straight into the most sympathetic eyes that her own sad,\r\ndefiant ones had ever seen. Only for a moment, then he bowed with a\r\nconventional murmur that was barely audible.\r\n\r\nHis lack of words gave her courage. "Monsieur," she said coldly in\r\nresponse to his greeting, then turned to the Sheik without looking at\r\nhim. "The Dancer has behaved abominably. Gaston, my hat, please!\r\nThanks." And vanished into the tent without a further look at any one.\r\n\r\nIt was late, but she lingered over her bath and changed with slow\r\nreluctance into the green dress that the Sheik preferred--a concession\r\nthat she despised herself for making. She had taken up the jade\r\nnecklace when he joined her.\r\n\r\nHe turned her to him roughly, with his hands on her shoulders, and the\r\nmerciless pressure of his fingers was indication enough without the\r\nblack scowl on his face that he was angry. "You are not very cordial to\r\nmy guest."\r\n\r\n"Is it required of a slave to be cordial towards her master\'s friends?"\r\nshe replied in a stifled voice.\r\n\r\n"What is required is obedience to my wishes," he said harshly.\r\n\r\n"And is it your wish that I should please this Frenchman?"\r\n\r\n"It is my wish."\r\n\r\n"If I were a woman of your own race----" she began bitterly, but he\r\ninterrupted her.\r\n\r\n"If you were a woman of my own race there would be no question of it,"\r\nhe said coldly. "You would be for the eyes of no other man than me. But\r\nsince you are not----" He broke off with an enigmatical jerk of the\r\nhead.\r\n\r\n"Since I am not you are less merciful than if I was," she cried\r\nmiserably. "I could wish that I was an Arab woman."\r\n\r\n"I doubt it," he said grimly. "The life of an Arab woman would hardly\r\nbe to your taste. We teach our women obedience with a whip."\r\n\r\n"Why have you changed so since this morning," she whispered, "when you\r\ntold me that you trusted no one to climb to my balcony in the hotel but\r\nyourself? Are you not an Arab now as then? Have I become of so little\r\nvalue to you that you are not even jealous any more?"\r\n\r\n"I can trust my friend, and--I do not propose to share you with him,"\r\nhe said brutally.\r\n\r\nShe winced as if he had struck her, and hid her face in her hands with\r\na low cry.\r\n\r\nHis fingers gripped her shoulder cruelly. "You will do as I wish?" The\r\nwords were a question, but the intonation was a command.\r\n\r\n"I have no choice," she murmured faintly.\r\n\r\nHis hands dropped to his sides and he turned to leave the room, but she\r\ncaught his arm. "Monseigneur! Have you no pity? Will you not spare me\r\nthis ordeal?"\r\n\r\nHe made a gesture of refusal. "You exaggerate," he said impatiently,\r\nbrushing her hand from his arm.\r\n\r\n"If you will be merciful this once----." she pleaded breathlessly, but\r\nhe cut her short with a fierce oath. "If?" he echoed. "Do you make\r\nbargains with me? Have you so much yet to learn?"\r\n\r\nShe looked at him with a little weary sigh. The changing mood that she\r\nhad set herself to watch for had come upon him suddenly and found her\r\nunprepared. The gentleness of the morning had vanished and he had\r\nreverted to the tyrannical, arbitrary despot of two months ago. She\r\nknew that it was her own fault. She knew him well enough to know that\r\nhe was intolerant of any interference with his wishes. She had learned\r\nthe futility of setting her determination against his. There was one\r\nmaster in his camp, whose orders, however difficult, must be obeyed.\r\n\r\nHis attention had concentrated on a broken fingernail, and he turned to\r\nthe dressing-table for a knife. She followed him with her eyes and\r\nwatched him carefully trimming the nail. She had often, amongst the\r\nmany things that puzzled her, wondered at the fastidious care he took\r\nof his well-manicured hands. The light of the lamp fell full on his\r\nface, and there was a dull ache in her heart as she looked at him. He\r\ndemanded implicit obedience, and only a few hours before she had made\r\nup her mind to unreserved submission, and she had broken down at the\r\nfirst test. The proof of her obedience was a hard one, from which she\r\nshrank, but it was harder far to see the look of anger she had provoked\r\non the face of the man she loved. For two months of wild happiness it\r\nhad been absent, the black scowl she had learned to dread had not been\r\ndirected at her, and the fierce eyes had looked at her with only\r\nkindness or amusement shining in their dark depths. Anything could be\r\nborne but a continuance of his displeasure. No sacrifice was too great\r\nto gain his forgiveness. She could not bear his anger. She longed so\r\ndesperately for happiness, and she loved him so passionately, so\r\nutterly, that she was content to give up everything to his will. If she\r\ncould only get back the man of the last few weeks, if she had not\r\nangered him too far. She was at his feet, tamed thoroughly at last, all\r\nher proud, angry self-will swamped in the love that was consuming her\r\nwith an intensity that was an agony. Love was a bitter pain, a torment\r\nthat was almost unendurable, a happiness that mocked her with its\r\nhollowness, a misery that tortured her with visions of what might have\r\nbeen. She went to him slowly, and he turned to her abruptly.\r\n\r\n"Well?" His voice was hard and uncompromising, and the flash of his\r\neyes was like the tiger\'s in the Indian jungle.\r\n\r\nShe set her teeth to keep down the old paralysing fear.\r\n\r\n"I will do what you want. I will do anything you want, only be kind to\r\nme, Ahmed," she whispered unsteadily. She had never called him by his\r\nname before; she did not even know that she had done so now, but at the\r\nsound of it a curious look crossed his face, and he drew her into his\r\narms with hands that were as gentle as they had been cruel before. She\r\nlet him lift her face to his, and met his searching gaze bravely.\r\nHolding her look with the mesmerism that he could exert when he chose,\r\nhe read in her face her final surrender, and knew that while it pleased\r\nhim to keep her he had broken her utterly to his hand. A strange\r\nexpression grew in his eyes as they travelled slowly over her. She was\r\nlike a fragile reed in his strong grasp that he could crush without an\r\neffort, and yet for four months she had fought him, matching his\r\ndetermination with a courage that had won his admiration even while it\r\nhad exasperated him. He knew she feared him, he had seen terror leap\r\ninto her flickering eyes when she had defied him most. Her defiance and\r\nher hatred, which had piqued him by contrast with the fawning adulation\r\nto which he had been accustomed and which had wearied him infinitely,\r\nhad provoked in him a fixed resolve to master her. Before he tired of\r\nher she must yield her will to him absolutely. And to-night he knew\r\nthat the last struggle had been made, that she would never oppose him\r\nagain, that she was clay in his hands to do with as he would. And the\r\nknowledge that he had won gave him no feeling of exultation, instead a\r\nvague, indefinite sense of irritation swept over him and made him swear\r\nsoftly under his breath. The satisfaction he had expected in his\r\ntriumph was lacking and the unaccountable dissatisfaction that filled\r\nhim seemed inexplicable. He did not understand himself, and he looked\r\ndown at her again with a touch of impatience. She was very lovely, he\r\nthought, with a strange new appreciation of the beauty he had\r\nappropriated, and very womanly in the soft, clinging green dress. The\r\nslim, boyish figure that rode with him had a charm all its own, but it\r\nwas the woman in her that sent the hot blood racing through his veins\r\nand made his heart beat as it was beating now. His eyes lingered a\r\nmoment on her bright curls, on her dark-fringed, pleading eyes and on\r\nher bare neck, startlingly white against the jade green of her gown,\r\nthen he put her from him.\r\n\r\n"_Va_," he said gently, "_depeche-toi_."\r\n\r\nShe looked after him as he went through the curtains with a long,\r\nsobbing sigh. She was paying a heavy price for her happiness, but she\r\nwould have paid a heavier one willingly. Nothing mattered now that he\r\nwas not angry any more. She knew what her total submission meant: it\r\nwas an end to all individualism, a complete self-abnegation, an\r\nabsolute surrender to his wishes, his moods and his temper. And she was\r\ncontent that it should be so, her love was prepared to endure whatever\r\nhe might put upon her. Nothing that he could do could alter that, and\r\nnothing should make her own her love. She had hidden it from him, and\r\nshe would hide it from him--cost what it might. Though he did not love\r\nher he wanted her still; she had read that in his eyes five minutes\r\nago, and she was happy even for that.\r\n\r\nShe turned to the glass suddenly and wrenched the silk folds off her\r\nshoulder. She looked at the marks of his fingers on the delicate skin\r\nwith a twist of the lips, then shut her eyes with a little gasp and hid\r\nher bruised arm hastily, her mouth quivering. But she did not blame\r\nhim, she had brought it on herself; she knew his mood, and he did not\r\nknow his own strength.\r\n\r\n"If he killed me he could not kill my love," she murmured, with a\r\nlittle pitiful smile.\r\n\r\nThe men were waiting for her, and with a murmured apology for her\r\nlateness she took her place. The Sheik and his guest resumed the\r\nconversation that her entrance had interrupted. Diana\'s thoughts were\r\nin confusion. She felt as if she were in some wild, improbable dream.\r\nAn Arab Sheik, a French explorer, and herself playing the conventional\r\nhostess in the midst of lawless unconventionalism. She looked around\r\nthe tent that had become so familiar, so dear. It seemed different\r\nto-night, as if the advent of the stranger had introduced a foreign\r\natmosphere. She had grown so accustomed to the routine that had been\r\nimposed upon her that even the Vicomte\'s servant standing behind his\r\nmaster seemed strange. The man\'s likeness to his twin brother was\r\nstriking, the only difference being that while Gaston\'s face was\r\nclean-shaven, Henri\'s upper lip was hidden by a neat, dark moustache.\r\nThe service was, as always, perfect, silent and quick.\r\n\r\nShe glanced at the Sheik covertly. There was a look on his face that\r\nshe had never seen and a ring in his voice that was different even from\r\nthe tone she had heard when Gaston had come back on the night of her\r\nflight. That had been relief and the affection of a man for a valued\r\nservant, this was the deep affection of a man for the one chosen\r\nfriend, the love passing the love of women. And the jealousy she had\r\nfelt in the morning welled up uncontrollably. She looked from the Sheik\r\nto the man who was absorbing all his attention, but in his pale, clever\r\nface, half hidden by the close beard, she saw no trace of the\r\nconceited, smirking egotist she had imagined, and his voice, as low as\r\nthe Sheik\'s, but more animated, was not the voice of a man unduly\r\nelated or conscious of himself. And as she looked her eyes met his. A\r\nsmile that was extraordinarily sweet and half-sad lit up his face.\r\n\r\n"Is it permitted to admire Madame\'s horsemanship?" he asked, with a\r\nlittle bow.\r\n\r\nDiana coloured faintly and twisted the jade necklace round her fingers\r\nnervously. "It is nothing," she said, with a shy smile that his\r\nsympathetic personality evoked in spite of herself. "With The Dancer it\r\nis all foolishness and not vice. One has to hold on very tightly. It\r\nwould have been humiliating to precipitate myself at the feet of a\r\nstranger. Monseigneur would not have approved of the concession to The\r\nDancer\'s peculiarities. It is an education to ride his horses,\r\nMonsieur."\r\n\r\n"It is a strain to the nerves to ride _beside_ some of them,"\r\nreplied the Vicomte pointedly.\r\n\r\nDiana laughed with pure amusement. The man whose coming she had loathed\r\nwas making the dreadful ordeal very easy for her. "I sympathise,\r\nMonsieur. Was Shaitan very vile?"\r\n\r\n"If Monsieur de Saint Hubert is trying to suggest to you that he\r\nsuffers from nerves, Diane," broke in the Sheik, with a laugh,\r\n"disabuse yourself at once. He has none."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert turned to him with a quick smile. _"Et toi,_ Ahmed,\r\neh? Do you remember----?" and he plunged into a flood of reminiscences\r\nthat lasted until the end of dinner.\r\n\r\nThe Vicomte had brought with him a pile of newspapers and magazines,\r\nand Diana curled up on the divan with an armful, hungry for news, but,\r\nsomehow, as she dipped into the batch of papers her interest waned.\r\nAfter four months of complete isolation it was difficult to pick up the\r\nthreads of current events, allusions were incomprehensible, and\r\ncontroversies seemed pointless. The happenings of the world appeared\r\ntame beside the great adventure that was carrying her on irresistibly\r\nand whose end she could not see and dared not think of. She pushed them\r\naside carelessly and kept only on her knee a magazine that served as a\r\npretext for her silence.\r\n\r\nWhen Gaston brought coffee the Vicomte hailed him with a gay laugh.\r\n"_Enfin,_ Gaston, after two years the nectar of the gods again!\r\nThere is a new machine for you amongst my things, _mon ami,_\r\nproviding it has survived Henri\'s packing."\r\n\r\nHe brought a cup to Diana and set it on a stool beside her. "Ahmed\r\nflatters himself I come to see him, Madame. I do not. I come to drink\r\nGaston\'s coffee. It has become proverbial, the coffee of Gaston. I\r\npropitiate him every time I come with a new apparatus for making it.\r\nThe last is a marvel of ingenuity. Excuse me, I go to drink it with the\r\nreverence it inspires. It is a rite, Madame, not a gastronomic\r\nindulgence."\r\n\r\nOnce more the sympathetic eyes looked straight into hers, and the quick\r\nblood rushed into her face as she bent her head again hurriedly over\r\nthe magazine. She knew instinctively that he was trying to help her,\r\ntalking nonsense with a tact that ignored her equivocal position. She\r\nwas grateful to him, but even his chivalry hurt. She watched him under\r\nher thick lashes as he went back to the Sheik and sat down beside him,\r\nrefusing his host\'s proffered cigarettes with a wry face of disgust and\r\na laughing reference to a "perverted palate," as he searched for his\r\nown. The hatred she had been prepared to give him had died away during\r\ndinner--only the jealousy remained, and even that had changed from its\r\nfirst intensity to an envy that brought a sob into her throat. She\r\nenvied him the light that shone in the Arab\'s dark eyes, she envied him\r\nthe intonation of the soft slow voice she loved. Her eyes turned to the\r\nSheik. He was leaning back with his hands clasped behind his head,\r\ntalking with a cigarette between his teeth. His attitude towards his\r\nEuropean friend was that of an equal, the haughty, peremptory accent\r\nthat was noticeable when he spoke to his followers was gone, and a flat\r\ncontradiction from Saint Hubert provoked only a laugh and a gesture of\r\nacceptance.\r\n\r\nAs they sat talking the contrast between the two men was strongly\r\nmarked. Beside the Frenchman\'s thin, spare frame and pale face, which\r\ngave him an air of delicacy, the Sheik looked like a magnificent animal\r\nin superb condition, and his quiet repose accentuated the Vicomte\'s\r\nquick, nervous manner. Under the screen of her thick lashes Diana\r\nwatched them unheeded. Their voices rose and fell continuously; they\r\nseemed to have a great deal to say to each other, and they talked\r\nindiscriminately French and Arabic so that much that they said was\r\nincomprehensible to her. She was glad that it should be so, she did not\r\nwant to know what they were saying. It seemed as if they had forgotten\r\nher presence with the accumulated conversation of two years. She was\r\nthankful to be left alone, happy for the rare chance of studying the\r\nbeloved face unnoticed. It was seldom she had the opportunity, for when\r\nthey were alone she was afraid to look at him much lest her secret\r\nshould be betrayed in her eyes. But she looked at him now unobserved,\r\nwith passionate longing. She was so intent that she did not notice\r\nGaston come in until he seemed suddenly to appear from nowhere beside\r\nhis master. He murmured something softly and the Sheik got up. He\r\nturned to Saint Hubert.\r\n\r\n"Trouble with one of the horses. Will you come? It may interest you."\r\n\r\nThey went out together, leaving her alone, and she slipped away to the\r\ninner room. In half-an-hour they came back, and for a few minutes\r\nlonger stayed chatting, then the Vicomte yawned and held out his watch\r\nwith a laugh. The Sheik went with him to his tent and sat down on the\r\nside of his guest\'s camp-bed. Saint Hubert dismissed the waiting Henri\r\nwith a nod and started to undress silently. The flow of talk and ready\r\nlaugh seemed to have deserted him, and he frowned as he wrenched his\r\nthings off with nervous irritability.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik watched him for a while, and then took the cigarette out of\r\nhis mouth with a faint smile. "_Eh, bien!_ Raoul, say it," he said\r\nquietly.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert swung round. "You might have spared her," he cried.\r\n\r\n"What?"\r\n\r\n"What? Good God, man! Me!"\r\n\r\nThe Sheik flicked the ash from his cigarette with a gesture of\r\nindifference. "Your courier was delayed, he only came this morning. It\r\nwas too late then to make other arrangements."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert took a hasty turn up and down the tent and stopped in\r\nfront of the Sheik with his hands thrust deep in his pockets and his\r\nshoulders hunched up about his ears. "It is abominable," he burst out.\r\n"You go too far, Ahmed."\r\n\r\nThe Sheik laughed cynically. "What do you expect of a savage? When an\r\nArab sees a woman that he wants he takes her. I only follow the customs\r\nof my people."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert clicked his tongue impatiently. "Your people!--which\r\npeople?" he asked in a low voice.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik sprang to his feet with flashing eyes, his hand dropping\r\nheavily on Saint Hubert\'s shoulder.\r\n\r\n"Stop, Raoul! Not even from you----!" he cried passionately, and then\r\nbroke off abruptly, and the anger died out of his face. He sat down\r\nagain quietly, with a little amused laugh. "Why this sudden access of\r\nmorality, _mon ami?_ You know me and the life I lead. You have\r\nseen women in my camp before now."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert dismissed the remark with a contemptuous wave of the hand.\r\n"There is to comparison. You know it as well as I," he said succinctly.\r\nHe moved over slowly to the camp table, where his toilet things had\r\nbeen laid out, and began removing the links from the cuffs of his\r\nshirt. "She is English, surely that is reason enough," he flung over\r\nhis shoulder.\r\n\r\n"You ask me, _me_ to spare a woman because she is English? My good\r\nRaoul, you amuse me," replied the Sheik, with an ugly sneer.\r\n\r\n"Where did you see her?" asked Saint Hubert curiously.\r\n\r\n"In the streets of Biskra, for five minutes, four months ago."\r\n\r\nThe Vicomte turned quickly. "You love her?" he shot out, with all the\r\nsuddenness of an American third degree.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik exhaled a long, thin cloud of blue smoke and watched it\r\neddying towards the top of the tent. "Have I ever loved a woman? And\r\nthis woman is English," he said in a voice as hard as steel.\r\n\r\n"If you loved her you would not care for her nationality."\r\n\r\nThe Sheik spat the end of his cigarette on to the floor contemptuously.\r\n"By Allah! Her cursed race sticks in my throat. But for that----" He\r\nshrugged his shoulders impatiently and got up from the bed on which he\r\nwas sitting.\r\n\r\n"Let her go then," said Saint Hubert quickly. "I can take her back to\r\nBiskra."\r\n\r\nThe Sheik turned to him slowly, a sudden flame of fierce jealousy\r\nleaping into his eyes. "Has she bewitched you, too? Do you want her for\r\nyourself, Raoul?" His voice was as low as ever, but there was a\r\ndangerous ring in it.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert flung his hands out in a gesture of despair. "Ahmed! Are\r\nyou mad? Are you going to quarrel with me after all these years on such\r\na pretext? _Bon Dieu!_ What do you take me for? There has been too\r\nmuch in our lives together ever to let a woman come between us. What is\r\na woman or any one to me where you are concerned? It is for quite a\r\ndifferent reason that I ask you, that I beg you to let this girl go."\r\n\r\n"Forgive me, Raoul. You know my devilish temper," muttered the Sheik,\r\nand for a moment his hand rested on Saint Hubert\'s arm.\r\n\r\n"You have not answered me, Ahmed."\r\n\r\nThe Sheik turned away. "She is content," he said evasively.\r\n\r\n"She has courage," amended the Vicomte significantly.\r\n\r\n"As you say, she has courage," agreed the Sheik, without a particle of\r\nexpression in his voice.\r\n\r\n_"Bon sang----"_ quoted Saint Hubert softly.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik swung round quickly. "How do you know she has good blood in\r\nher?"\r\n\r\n"It is very evident," replied Saint Hubert drily.\r\n\r\n"That is not what you mean. What do you know?"\r\n\r\nThe Vicomte shrugged his shoulders, and, going to his suit-case, took\r\nfrom it an English illustrated paper, and opening it at the central\r\npage handed it to the Sheik silently.\r\n\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan moved closer to the hanging lamp so that the light\r\nfell directly on the paper in his hands. There were two large\r\nfull-length photographs of Diana, one in evening dress and the other as\r\nthe Vicomte had first seen her, in riding breeches and short jacket,\r\nher hat and whip lying at her feet, and the bridle of the horse that\r\nwas standing beside her over her arm.\r\n\r\nUnder the photographs was written: "Miss Diana Mayo, whose protracted\r\njourney in the desert is causing anxiety to a large circle of friends.\r\nMiss Mayo left Biskra under the guidance of a reputable caravan-leader\r\nfour months ago, with the intention of journeying for four weeks in the\r\ndesert and returning to Oran. Since the first camp nothing has been\r\nheard of Miss Mayo or her caravan. Further anxiety is occasioned by the\r\nfact that considerable unrest is reported amongst the tribes in the\r\nlocality towards which Miss Mayo was travelling. Her brother, Sir\r\nAubrey Mayo, who is detained in America as the result of an accident,\r\nis in constant cable communication with the French authorities. Miss\r\nMayo is a well-known sports-woman and has travelled widely."\r\n\r\nFor a long time the Sheik studied the photographs silently, then with\r\nslow deliberation he tore the page out of the paper and rolled it up.\r\n"With your permission," he said coolly, and held it over the flame of\r\nthe little lamp by the bedside. He held it until the burning paper\r\ncharred to nothing in his hand and then flicked the ashes from his long\r\nfingers. "Henri has seen this?"\r\n\r\n"Unquestionably. Henri reads all my papers," replied Saint Hubert, with\r\na touch of impatience.\r\n\r\n"Then Henri can hold his tongue," said the Sheik nonchalantly,\r\nsearching in the folds of his waist-cloth for his case and lighting\r\nanother cigarette with elaborate carelessness.\r\n\r\n"What are you going to do?" asked Saint Hubert pointedly.\r\n\r\n"I? Nothing! The French authorities have too many affairs on hand and\r\ntoo high an appreciation of Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s horses to prosecute\r\ninquiries in my direction. Besides, they are not responsible.\r\nMademoiselle Mayo was warned of the risks she ran before she left\r\nBiskra. She chose to take the risks, _et voila!"_\r\n\r\n"Will nothing make you change your mind?"\r\n\r\n"I am not given to changing my mind. You know that. And, besides, why\r\nshould I? As I told you before, she is content."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert looked him full in the face. "Content! Cowed is the better\r\nword, Ahmed."\r\n\r\nThe Sheik laughed softly. "You flatter me, Raoul. Do not let us speak\r\nany more about it. It is an unfortunate contretemps, and I regret that\r\nit distresses you," he said lightly; then with a sudden change of\r\nmanner he laid his hands on the Vicomte\'s shoulders. "But this can make\r\nno difference to our friendship, _mon ami;_ that is too big a\r\nthing to break down over a difference of opinion. You are a French\r\nnobleman, and I----!" He gave a little bitter laugh. "I am an\r\nuncivilised Arab. We cannot see things in the same way."\r\n\r\n"You could, but you will not, Ahmed," replied the Vicomte, with an\r\naccent of regret. "It is not worthy of you." He paused and then looked\r\nup again with a little crooked smile and a shrug of defeat. "Nothing\r\ncan ever make any difference with us, Ahmed. I can disagree with you,\r\nbut I can\'t wipe out the recollection of the last twenty years."\r\n\r\nA few minutes later the Sheik left him and went out into the night. He\r\ntraversed the short distance between the tents slowly, stopping to\r\nspeak to a sentry, and then pausing outside his own tent to look up at\r\nthe stars. The Persian hound that always slept across the entrance\r\nuncurled himself and got up, thrusting a wet nose into his hand. The\r\nSheik fondled the huge creature absently, stroking the dog\'s shaggy\r\nhead mechanically, hardly conscious of what he was doing. A great\r\nrestlessness that was utterly foreign to his nature had taken\r\npossession of him. He had been aware of it growing within him for some\r\ntime, becoming stronger daily, and now the coming of Raoul de Saint\r\nHubert seemed to have put the crowning touch to a state of mind that he\r\nwas unable to understand. He had never been given to thinking of\r\nhimself, or criticising or analysing his passing whims and fancies. All\r\nhis life he had taken what he wanted; nothing on which he had ever laid\r\neyes of desire had been denied him. His wealth had brought him\r\neverything he had ever wished. His passionate temper had been\r\ncharacteristic even when he was a child, but these strange fits of\r\nunreasonable irritability were new, and he searched for a cause vainly.\r\nHis keen eyes looked through the darkness towards the south. Was it the\r\nnearness of his hereditary enemy, who had presumed to come closer than\r\nhe had ever done before to the border of the country that Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan regarded as his own, that was causing this great unrest? He\r\nlaughed contemptuously. Nothing would give him greater pleasure than\r\ncoming into actual collision with the man whom he had been trained from\r\nboyhood to hate. As long as Ibraheim Omair remained within his own\r\nterritory Ahmed Ben Hassan held his hand and kept in check his fierce\r\nfollowers, whose eyes were turned longingly towards the debatable land,\r\nbut once let the robber Sheik step an inch over the border, and it was\r\nwar, and war until one or both of the chiefs were dead. And if he died\r\nwho had no son to succeed him; the huge tribe would split up in\r\nnumerous little families for want of a leader to keep them together,\r\nand it would be left to the French Government to take over, if they\r\ncould, the vast district that he had governed despotically. And at the\r\nthought he laughed again. No, it was not Ibraheim Omair who was\r\ntroubling him. He pushed the hound aside and went into the tent. The\r\ndivan where Diana had been sitting was strewn with magazines and\r\npapers, the imprint of her slender body still showed in the soft,\r\nheaped-up cushions, and a tiny, lace-edged handkerchief peeped out\r\nunder one of them. He picked it up and looked at it curiously, and his\r\nforehead contracted slowly in the heavy black scowl. He turned his\r\nburning eyes toward the curtains that divided the rooms. Saint Hubert\'s\r\nwords rang in his ears. "English!" he muttered with a terrible oath.\r\n"And I have made her suffer as I swore any of that damned race should\r\nif they fell into my hands. Merciful Allah! Why does it give me so\r\nlittle pleasure?"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\n\r\nDiana came into the living-room one morning about a week after the\r\narrival of the Vicomte de Saint Hubert. She had expected to find the\r\nroom empty, for the Sheik had risen at dawn and ridden away on one of\r\nthe distant expeditions that had become so frequent, and she thought\r\nhis friend had accompanied him, but as she parted the curtains between\r\nthe two rooms she saw the Frenchman sitting at the little writing-table\r\nsurrounded by papers and writing quickly, loose sheets of manuscript\r\nlittering the floor around him. It was the first time that they had\r\nchanced to be alone, and she hesitated with a sudden shyness. But Saint\r\nHubert had heard the rustle of the curtain, and he sprang to his feet\r\nwith the courteous bow that proclaimed his nationality.\r\n\r\n"Your pardon, Madame. Do I disturb you? Tell me if I am in the way. I\r\nam afraid I have been very untidy," he added, laughing apologetically,\r\nand looking at the heap of closely-written sheets strewing the rug.\r\n\r\nDiana came forward slowly, a faint colour rising in her face. "I\r\nthought you had gone with Monseigneur."\r\n\r\n"I had some work to do--some notes that I wanted to transcribe before I\r\nforgot myself what they meant; I write vilely. I have had a hard week,\r\ntoo, so I begged a day off. I may stay? You are sure I do not disturb\r\nyou?"\r\n\r\nHis sympathetic eyes and the deference in his voice brought an\r\nunexpected lump into her throat. She signed to him to resume his work\r\nand passed out under the awning. Behind the tent the usual camp hubbub\r\nfilled the air. A knot of Arabs at a little distance were watching one\r\nof the rough-riders schooling a young horse, noisily critical and\r\noffering advice freely, undeterred by the indifference with which it\r\nwas received. Others lounged past engaged on the various duties\r\nconnected with the camp, with the Eastern disregard for time that\r\nrelegated till to-morrow everything that could possibly be neglected\r\nto-day. Near her one of the older men, more rigid in his observances\r\nthan the generality of Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s followers, was placidly\r\nabsorbed in his devotions, prostrating himself and fulfilling his\r\nritual with the sublime lack of self-consciousness of the Mohammedan\r\ndevotee.\r\n\r\nOutside his own tent the valet and Henri were sitting in the sun,\r\nGaston on an upturned bucket, cleaning a rifle, and his brother\r\nstretched full length on the ground, idly flapping at the flies with\r\nthe duster with which he had been polishing the Vicomte\'s riding-boots.\r\nBoth men were talking rapidly with frequent little bursts of gay\r\nlaughter. The Persian hound was lying at their feet. He raised his head\r\nas Diana appeared, and, rising, went to her slowly, rearing up against\r\nher with a paw on each shoulder, making clumsy efforts to lick her\r\nface, and she pushed him down with difficulty, stooping to kiss his\r\nshaggy head.\r\n\r\nShe looked away across the desert beyond the last palms of the oasis. A\r\nhaze hung round about, shimmering in the heat and blurring the outline\r\nof the distant hills. A tiny breeze brought the acrid smell of camels\r\ncloser to her, and the creaking whine of the tackling over the well\r\nsounded not very far away. Diana gave a little sigh. It had all grown\r\nso familiar. She seemed to have lived no other life beside this nomad\r\nexistence. The years that had gone before faded into a kind of dim\r\nremembrance, the time when she had travelled ceaselessly round the\r\nworld with her brother seemed very remote. She had existed then,\r\nfilling her life with sport, unconscious of the something that was\r\nlacking in her nature, and now she was alive at last, and the heart\r\nwhose existence she had doubted was burning and throbbing with a\r\npassion that was consuming her. Her eyes swept lingeringly around the\r\ncamp with a very tender light in them. Everything she saw was connected\r\nwith and bound up in the man who was lord of it all. She was very proud\r\nof him, proud of his magnificent physical abilities, proud of his hold\r\nover his wild turbulent followers, proud with the pride of primeval\r\nwoman in the dominant man ruling his fellow-men by force and fear.\r\n\r\nThe old Arab had finished his prayers and rose leisurely from his\r\nknees, salaaming with a broad smile. All the tribesmen smiled on her,\r\nand would go out of their way to win a nod of recognition from her. She\r\nfaltered a few words in stumbling Arabic in reply to his long, flowery\r\nspeech, and with a little laugh beat a hasty retreat into the tent.\r\n\r\nShe paused beside the Vicomte. "Is it another novel?" she asked shyly,\r\nindicating the steadily increasing pile of manuscript.\r\n\r\nHe turned on his chair, resting his arms on the rail, twirling a\r\nfountain pen between his fingers, and smiled at her as she curled up on\r\nthe divan with Kopec, who had followed her into the tent. "No, Madame,\r\nSomething more serious this time. It is a history of this very curious\r\ntribe of Ahmed\'s. They are different in so many ways from ordinary\r\nArabs. They have been a race apart for generations. They have beliefs\r\nand customs peculiarly their own. You may, for instance, have noticed\r\nthe singular absence among them of the strict religious practices that\r\nhold among other Mohammedans. Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s tribe worship first\r\nand foremost their Sheik, then the famous horses for which they are\r\nrenowned, and then and then only--Allah."\r\n\r\n"Is Monseigneur a Mohammedan?"\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert shrugged. "He believes in a God," he said evasively,\r\nturning back to his writing.\r\n\r\nDiana studied him curiously as he bent over his work. She smiled\r\nwhen she thought of the mental picture she had drawn of Saint Hubert\r\nbefore he came, and contrasted it with the real man under her eyes.\r\nDuring the week that he had been in the camp he had forced her\r\nliking and compelled her confidence by the sympathetic charm of his\r\nmanner. He had carried off a difficult position with a delicacy and\r\n_savoir-faire_ that had earned him her gratitude. He had saved her\r\na hundred humiliations with a tact that had been as spontaneous as it\r\nhad been unobtrusive. And they had the bond between them of the common\r\nlove they had for this strange leader of a strange tribe. What had been\r\nthe origin of the friendship between these utterly dissimilar men--a\r\nfriendship that seemed to go back to the days of their boyhood? The\r\nquestion intrigued her and she pondered over it, lying quietly on the\r\ndivan, smoothing the hound\'s huge head resting on her knee.\r\n\r\nThe Vicomte wrote rapidly for some time and then flung down his pen\r\nwith an exclamation of relief, gathered up the loose sheets from the\r\nfloor and, stacking them in an orderly heap on the table, swung round\r\non his chair again. He looked at the girl\'s slender little figure lying\r\nwith the unconsciously graceful attitude of a child against the\r\nheaped-up cushions, her face bent over the dog\'s rough, grey head, and\r\nhe felt an unwonted emotion stirring in him. The quick sympathy that\r\nshe had aroused from the first moment of seeing her had given place to\r\na deeper feeling that moved him profoundly, and with it a chivalrous\r\ndesire to protect, a longing to stand between her and the irremediable\r\ndisaster that loomed inevitably ahead of her.\r\n\r\nShe felt his concentrated gaze and looked up. "You have done your\r\nwork?"\r\n\r\n"All I can do at the moment. Henri must unravel the rest; he has a\r\npassion for hieroglyphics. He is an invaluable person; I could never\r\nget on without him. He bullied me when we were boys together--at least\r\nthat is what I called it. He called it \'amusing Monsieur le Vicomte,\'\r\nand for the last fifteen years he has tyrannised over me\r\nwholeheartedly." He laughed and snapped his fingers at Kopec, who\r\nwhined and rolled his eyes in his direction, but did not lift his head\r\nfrom Diana\'s knee.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause, and Diana continued fondling the hound absently. "I\r\nhave read your books, Monsieur--all that Monseigneur has here," she\r\nsaid at last, looking up gravely.\r\n\r\nHe gave a little bow with a few murmured words that she did not catch.\r\n\r\n"Your novel interested me," she went on, still stroking the hound, as\r\nif the nearness of the great beast helped her.\r\n\r\n"As a rule novels bore me, the subjects they deal with have been of no\r\ninterest to me, but this one gripped me. It is unusual, it is\r\nwonderful, but--is it real?" She had spoken dispassionately with the\r\nboyish candour that was characteristic, not complimenting an author on\r\na masterpiece, but stating a fact simply, as it appeared to her.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert leaned forward over the back of his chair. "In what\r\nway--real?" he asked.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him squarely. "Do you think there really exists such a\r\nman as you have drawn--a man who could be as tender, as unselfish, as\r\nfaithful as your hero?"\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert looked away, and, picking up his pen, stabbed idly at the\r\nblotting-pad, drawing meaningless circles and dots, with a slow shrug.\r\nThe scorn in her voice and the sudden pain in her eyes hurt him.\r\n\r\n"Do you know such a man, Monsieur, or is he wholly a creature of your\r\nimagination?" she persisted.\r\n\r\nHe completed a complicated diagram on the sheet of blotting-paper\r\nbefore answering. "I do know a man who, given certain circumstances,\r\nhas the ability to develop into such a character," he said eventually\r\nin a low voice.\r\n\r\nShe laughed bitterly. "Then you are luckier than I. I am not very old,\r\nbut during the last five years I have met many men of many\r\nnationalities, and I have never known one who in any degree resembles\r\nthe _preux chevalier_ of your book. The men who have most\r\nintimately touched my life have not known the meaning of the word\r\ntenderness, and have never had a thought for any one beyond themselves.\r\nYou have been more fortunate in your acquaintances, Monsieur."\r\n\r\nA dull red crept into the Vicomte\'s face, and he continued looking at\r\nthe pen in his fingers. "Beautiful women, Madame," he said slowly,\r\n"unfortunately provoke in some men all that is basest and vilest in\r\ntheir natures. No man knows to what depths of infamy he may stoop under\r\nthe stress of a sudden temptation."\r\n\r\n"And the woman pays," cried Diana vehemently. "Pays for the beauty God\r\ncurses her with--the beauty she may hate herself; pays until the beauty\r\nfades. How much----" She pulled herself up short, biting her lips.\r\nMoved by the sense of the sympathy that had unconsciously been\r\ninfluencing her during the past week and which had shaken the\r\nself-suppression that she had imposed upon herself, her tongue had run\r\naway with her. She was afraid of the confidence that his manner was\r\nalmost demanding of her. Her pride restrained her from the compassion\r\nthat her loneliness had nearly yielded to.\r\n\r\n"Excuse me," she said coldly, "my ideas cannot possibly interest you."\r\n\r\n"On the contrary, you interest me profoundly," he corrected quickly.\r\n\r\nShe noticed the slight difference in his words and laughed more\r\nbitterly than before. "As what?--a subject for vivisection? Get on your\r\noperating coat and bring your instruments without delay. The victim is\r\nall ready for you. It will be \'copy\' for your next book!"\r\n\r\n"Madame!"\r\n\r\nHe had sprung to his feet, and she looked up at him miserably, her hand\r\nheld out in swift contrition. "Oh, forgive me! I shouldn\'t have said\r\nthat. You haven\'t deserved it. You have been--kind. I am grateful.\r\nForgive me and my rudeness. It must be the heat, it makes one very\r\nirritable, don\'t you think?"\r\n\r\nHe ignored her pitiful little subterfuge and raised her outstretched,\r\nquivering fingers to his lips. "If you will honour me with your\r\nfriendship," he said, with a touch of the old-world chivalry that was\r\noften noticeable in him, "my life is at your service."\r\n\r\nBut as he spoke his voice changed. The touch of her cold fingers sent a\r\nrush of feeling through him that for an instant overpowered him.\r\n\r\nShe let her hand lie in his, and for a few moments she avoided his eyes\r\nand looked down at the rough head in her lap. Then she met his gaze\r\nfrankly. "Your offer is too rare a thing to put on one side. If you\r\nwill be my friend, as you are Monseigneur\'s friend----" she faltered,\r\nturning her head away, and her fingers lying in his trembled slightly.\r\n\r\nHe started and crushed the hand he was holding unknowingly, as the\r\nthought was forced on him. Monseigneur\'s friend! He realized that in\r\nthe last few moments he had forgotten the Sheik, had forgotten\r\neverything, swept off his feet by an intense emotion that staggered him\r\nwith its unexpectedness, except the loveliness and helplessness of the\r\ngirl beside him. His head was reeling; his calmness, his loyalty, his\r\nearlier feelings of dispassionate pity had given way to an extreme\r\nagitation that was rushing him headlong and threatening to overwhelm\r\nhim. His heart beat furiously and he clenched his teeth, fighting to\r\nregain his usual _sang-froid_. The emotional temperament that\r\nDiana had divined from his novel had sprung uppermost with a bound,\r\noverthrowing the rigid repression of years. The blood beat in his ears\r\nas he strove to master himself, to crush the madness that had come upon\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nHe had closed his eyes with the shock of self-revelation, he opened\r\nthem now and looked down at her hesitatingly, almost fearfully,\r\nclasping her hand closer in his and leaning nearer to her, drawn\r\nirresistibly by the intoxication of her nearness.
