He saw her through a\r\nmist that cleared gradually, saw that she was ignorant of the emotion\r\nshe had awakened in him, and, conscious only of his sympathy, had left\r\nher hand in his as she would have left it in her brother\'s. She was\r\nbent low over the hound, her face almost touching his big head, and as\r\nSaint Hubert looked a glistening tear dropped on Kopec\'s rough, grey\r\nneck. She had forgotten him, forgotten even that he was standing beside\r\nher, in the one predominant thought that filled her mind. With an\r\nimmense effort he got command of himself. Somehow he must conquer this\r\nsudden insanity. The loyalty that had hung trembling in the balance\r\nreasserted itself and a self-disgust seized him. He had been within an\r\nace of betraying the man who had been for twenty years nearer to him\r\nthan a brother. She belonged to his friend, and now he had not even the\r\nright to question the ethics of the Sheik\'s possession of her. The calm\r\nthat he had lost came back to him. The wound would heal though it might\r\nalways throb, but he was strong enough to hide its existence even from\r\nthe jealous eyes that had watched him ceaselessly since his outburst on\r\nthe night of his arrival. He had been conscious of them daily. Even\r\nthis morning the Sheik had made every effort short of a direct command\r\nto induce him to go with him on the expedition that had taken him away\r\nso early. Sure of himself now, he lifted her fingers to his lips again\r\nreverently with a kind of renunciation in his kiss, and laid her hand\r\ndown gently. He turned away with a smothered sigh and a little pang at\r\nher complete absorption, and, as he did so, Henri came in quickly.\r\n\r\n"Monsieur le Vicomte! Will you come? There has been an accident."\r\n\r\nWith a cry that Saint Hubert never forgot Diana leaped to her feet, her\r\nface colourless, and her lips framed the word "Ahmed," though no sound\r\ncame from them. She was shaking all over, and the Vicomte put his arm\r\nround her instinctively. She clung to him, and he knew with a bitter\r\ncertainty that the support of a table or a chair would have meant no\r\nless to her.\r\n\r\n"What is it, Henri?" he said sharply, with a slight movement that\r\ninterposed himself between Diana and his servant.\r\n\r\n"One of the men, Monsieur le Vicomte. His gun burst, and his hand is\r\nshattered."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert nodded curtly towards the door and turned his attention to\r\nDiana. She sank down on the divan and, gathering the hound\'s head in\r\nher arm, buried her face in his neck. "Forgive me," she murmured, her\r\nvoice muffled in the rough, grey hair. "It is stupid of me, but he is\r\nriding that brute Shaitan to-day. I am always nervous. Please go. I\r\nwill come in a minute."\r\n\r\nHe went without a word. "I am always nervous." The tales he had heard\r\nof Diana Mayo as he passed through Biskra did not include nerves. His\r\nface was set as he ran hurriedly across the camp.\r\n\r\nDiana sat quite still after he had gone until the nervous shuddering\r\nceased, until Kopec twisted his head free of her arms and licked her\r\nface with an uneasy whine. She brushed her hand across her eyes with a\r\ngasp of relief, and went out into the bright sunlight with the hound at\r\nher heels.\r\n\r\nThe noisy clamour of excited voices guided her to the scene of the\r\naccident, and the surrounding crowd opened to let her pass through. The\r\nwounded man was sitting holding up his hand stoically for Saint\r\nHubert\'s ministrations with a look of mild interest on his face. In\r\nresponse to Diana\'s smile and cheery word he grinned sheepishly with a\r\nroll of his fine eyes. Saint Hubert looked up quickly. "It is not a\r\npleasant sight," he said doubtfully.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t mind. Let me hold that," she said quietly, rolling up her\r\nsleeves and taking a crimson-spattered basin from Henri. Saint Hubert\r\nflashed another look at her, marvelling at her steady voice and even\r\ncolour when he thought of the white-faced girl who had clung trembling\r\nto him ten minutes earlier. Outside of Ahmed Ben Hassan she still\r\nretained the fearless courage that she had always had; it was only when\r\nanything touched him nearly that the new Diana, with the coward anxiety\r\nof love, rose paramount.\r\n\r\nShe watched the Vicomte\'s skilful treatment of the maimed hand with\r\ninterest. There was a precision in his movement and a deft touch that\r\nindicated both knowledge and practise. "You are a doctor?"\r\n\r\n"Yes," he said, without looking up from his work, "I studied when I was\r\na young man and passed all the necessary examinations. It is\r\nindispensable when one travels as I do. I have found it invaluable."\r\n\r\nHe took up some dressing that Henri held ready for him, and Diana\r\nhanded the now unwanted bowl to Gaston. She looked again at the Arab,\r\nwhose impassive face showed no sign of any feeling. "Does he feel it\r\nvery much, do you think?" she asked the valet.\r\n\r\nHe laughed and shrugged his shoulders. "Less than I should, Madame.\r\nWhat is really troubling him is the thought of what Monseigneur will\r\nsay when he hears that Selim was fool enough to buy a worthless gun\r\nfrom one of the servants of the Dutchman who passed here last week,"\r\nand he added a few teasing words in Arabic which made Selim look up\r\nwith a grimace.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert finished adjusting the bandages and stood up, wiping the\r\nperspiration from his forehead.\r\n\r\n"Will he do all right now?" asked Diana anxiously.\r\n\r\n"I think so. The thumb is gone, as you saw, but I think I can save the\r\nrest of the hand. I will watch him carefully, but these men of Ahmed\'s\r\nare in such excellent condition that I do not think there will be any\r\ntrouble."\r\n\r\n"I am going to ride," said Diana, turning away. "It is rather late, but\r\nthere is just time. Will you come?"\r\n\r\nIt was a temptation and he hesitated, gathering together the\r\ninstruments he had been using, but prudence prevailed.\r\n\r\n"I should like to, but I ought to keep an eye on Selim," he said\r\nquietly, snatching at the plausible excuse that offered. He found her\r\nlater before the big tent as she was ready to start, and waited while\r\nshe mounted.\r\n\r\n"If I am late don\'t wait for me. Tell Henri to give you your lunch,"\r\nshe called out between The Dancer\'s idiotic prancings.\r\n\r\nHe watched her ride away, with Gaston a few paces behind and followed\r\nby the escort of six men that the Sheik had lately insisted upon. The\r\ncontinual presence of these six men riding at her heels irked her\r\nconsiderably. The wild, free gallops that she had loved became quite\r\ndifferent with the thought of the armed guard behind her. They seemed\r\nto hamper her and put a period to her enjoyment. The loneliness of her\r\nrides had been to her half their charm; she had grown accustomed to and\r\noblivious of Gaston, but she was acutely conscious of the six pairs of\r\neyes watching her every movement. She did not see the necessity for\r\nthem. She had never been aware of anything any time when she was riding\r\nthat seemed to justify the Sheik\'s order. The oasis was not on a\r\ncaravan route, and if she ever saw Arabs at any distance from the camp\r\nthey always proved to be Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s own men. She had thought of\r\nremonstrating with him, but her courage had failed her. His mood, since\r\nthe coming of Saint Hubert, had been of the coldest--almost repellant.\r\nThe weeks of happiness that had gone before had developed the intimacy\r\nbetween them almost into a feeling of camaraderie. He had been more\r\nhumane, more Western, more considerate than he had ever been, and the\r\nfear that she had of him had lain quiescent. She could have asked him\r\nthen. But since the morning of Raoul\'s arrival, when the unexpected\r\nfervour of his embrace had given new birth to the hope that had almost\r\ndied within her, he had changed completely into a cold reserve that\r\nchilled her. His caresses had been careless and infrequent, and his\r\nindifference so great that she had wondered miserably if the flame of\r\nhis passion for her was burning out and if this was the end. And yet\r\nthroughout his indifference she had been conscious, like Saint Hubert,\r\nof the surveillance of constant jealous eyes that watched them both\r\nwith a fierce scrutiny that was felt rather than actually seen. But the\r\nspark of hope that the knowledge of this jealousy still fanned was not\r\ngreat enough to overcome the barrier that his new mood had raised\r\nbetween them. She dared ask no favour of him now. Her heart tightened\r\nat the thought of his indifference. It hurt so. This morning he had\r\nleft her without a word when he had gone out into the early dawn, and\r\nshe was hungry for the kisses he withheld. She was used to his taciturn\r\nfits, but her starved heart ached perpetually for tangible recognition.\r\nLove, the capacity for which she had so long denied, had become a force\r\nthat, predominating everything, held her irresistibly. The accumulated\r\naffection that, for want of an outlet, had been stemmed within her, had\r\nburst all restraint, and the love that she gave to the man to whom she\r\nhad surrendered her proud heart was immeasurable--a love of infinite\r\ntenderness and complete unselfishness, a love that had made her\r\nstrangely humble. She had yielded up everything to him, he dominated\r\nher wholly. Her imperious will had bent before his greater\r\ndetermination, and his mastery over her had provoked a love that craved\r\nfor recompense. She only lived for him and for the hope of his love,\r\nengulfed in the passion that enthralled her. Her surrender had been no\r\ncommon one. The feminine weakness that she had despised and fought\r\nagainst had triumphed over her unexpectedly without humiliating\r\nthoroughness. Sex had supervened to overthrow all her preconceived\r\nnotions. The womanly instincts that under Aubrey\'s training had been\r\nsuppressed and undeveloped had, in contact with the Sheik\'s vivid\r\nmasculinity and compelling personality, risen to the surface with\r\nstartling completeness.\r\n\r\nTo-day she was almost desperate. His callousness of the morning had\r\nwounded her deeply, and a wave of rebellion welled up in her. She would\r\nnot be thrown aside without making any effort to fight for his love.\r\nShe would use every art that her beauty and her woman\'s instinct gave\r\nher. Her cheek burned as she thought of the role she was setting\r\nherself. She would be no better than "those others" whose remembrance\r\nstill made her shiver. But she crushed down the repugnant feeling\r\nresolutely, flinging up her head with the old haughty gesture and\r\ndrawing herself straighter in the saddle with compressed lips. She had\r\nendured so much already that she could even bear this further outrage\r\nto her feelings. At no matter what cost she must make him care for her.\r\nThough she loathed the means she would make him love her. But even as\r\nshe planned the doubt of her ability to succeed crept into her mind,\r\ntorturing her with insidious recollections.\r\n\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan was no ordinary man to succumb to the fascinations of\r\na woman. She had experienced his obstinacy, and knew the inflexibility\r\nof his nature. His determination was a rock against which she had been\r\nbroken too many times not to know its strength. For a moment she\r\ndespaired, then courage came to her again, thrusting away the doubts\r\nthat crowded in upon her and leaving the hope that still lingered in\r\nher heart. A faint tremulous smile curved her lips, and she looked up,\r\nforcing her thoughts back to the present with an effort.\r\n\r\nAt the beginning of the ride they had passed several vedettes sitting\r\nmotionless on their impatient horses. The men had swung their rifles\r\nhigh in the air in salute as she passed, and once or twice Gaston had\r\nshouted a question as he galloped after her. But for the last hour they\r\nhad seen no one. The desert was undulating here, rising and falling in\r\nshort, sharp declivities that made a wide outlook impossible.\r\n\r\nGaston spurred to Diana\'s side. "Will Madame please to turn?" he said\r\nrespectfully. "It is late, and it is not safe riding amongst these\r\nslopes. One cannot see what is coming and I am afraid."\r\n\r\n"Afraid, Gaston?" she rallied laughingly.\r\n\r\n"For you, Madame," he answered gravely.\r\n\r\nShe reined in The Dancer as she spoke; but it was too late. Even as she\r\nturned her horse\'s head innumerable Arabs seemed to spring up on all\r\nsides of them. Before she realised what was happening her escort\r\nflashed past and wheeled in behind her, shooting steadily at the horde\r\nof men who poured in upon them, and, with a groan, Gaston seized her\r\nbridle and urged the horses back in the direction from which they had\r\ncome. The noise was deafening, the raucous shouting of the Arabs and\r\nthe continuous sharp crack of the rifles. Bullets began to whizz past\r\nher.\r\n\r\nGaston tucked his reins under his knee, and with one hand grasping The\r\nDancer\'s bridle and his revolver in the other, rode looking back over\r\nhis shoulder. Diana, too, glanced behind her, and mechanically her\r\nfingers closed over the shining little weapon that the Sheik had given\r\nher the previous week. She saw with a sudden sickening the six men who\r\nhad formed her escort beaten back by the superior numbers that enclosed\r\nthem on every side. Already two were down and the rest were on foot,\r\nand, as she watched, they were swallowed up in the mass of men that\r\npoured over them, and, at the same time, a party of about twenty\r\nhorsemen detached themselves from the main body and galloped towards\r\nher and Gaston.\r\n\r\nShe seized his arm. "Can\'t we do something? Can\'t we help them? We\r\ncan\'t leave them like that," she gasped, wrenching the revolver from\r\nthe holster at her waist.\r\n\r\n"No, no, Madame, it is impossible. It is a hundred to six. You must\r\nthink of yourself. Go on, Madame. For God\'s sake, ride on. We may have\r\na chance." He loosed her bridle and dropped behind her, interposing\r\nhimself between her and the pursuing Arabs. A fierce yelling and a hail\r\nof bullets that went wide made Diana turn her head as she crouched low\r\nin the saddle. She realised the meaning of Gaston\'s tactics and checked\r\nher horse deliberately.\r\n\r\n"I won\'t go first. You must ride with me," she cried, wincing as a\r\nbullet went close by her.\r\n\r\n"_Mon Dieu!_ What are you stopping for? Do you think I can face\r\nMonseigneur if anything happens to you, Madame?" replied Gaston\r\nfiercely. "Do as I tell you. Go on!" Deference was gone in the fear\r\nthat roughened his voice.\r\n\r\nHe looked back and his face grew grey. For himself he had no fear, but\r\nfor the girl beside him he dared not even think. They were Ibraheim\r\nOmair\'s men who had trapped them, and he cursed his folly in allowing\r\nDiana to come so far. Yet it had seemed safe enough. The scout\'s\r\nreports had lately proved that the robber Sheik had up to now respected\r\nthe boundary line between the two territories. This must be a sudden\r\ntentative raid which had met with unlooked-for success. The bait would\r\nbe too tempting to allow of any slackening on the part of the raiders.\r\nThe white woman, who was Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s latest toy, and his\r\nservant, whom he was known to value so highly, would be a prize that\r\nwould not be lightly let go. For himself it would be probably torture,\r\ncertainly death, and for her----! He set his teeth as he looked at her\r\nand the perspiration poured down his face. He would kill her himself\r\nbefore it came to that. And as he looked she turned her head, and met\r\nhis agonised eyes for a moment, smiling bravely. He had refrained up\r\ntill now from shooting, trying to reserve his ammunition for a last\r\nresource, but he saw that he must delay no longer. He fired slowly and\r\nsteadily, picking his men with careful precision. It was a forlorn\r\nhope, but by checking the leaders even for a few moments he might gain\r\ntime. The accuracy of his aim, that every time proved effectual, might\r\nkeep back the onrush until they got clear of the undulating country,\r\nuntil they got out into the open where the sounds of the firing might\r\nreach some of the outpost sentinels, until they got too near to the\r\nSheik\'s camp for pursuit to be possible. The bullets pattered\r\ncontinuously round them, but the men who fired them were not Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan\'s carefully trained marksmen. But still Gaston knew that their\r\nposition was almost hopeless. Any moment a bullet might reach one of\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nTheir pursuers, too, seemed to guess his thoughts and opened out into\r\nan irregular, extended line, swerving and manoeuvring continually,\r\nmaking accurate shooting impossible, while they urged their horses to a\r\nterrific pace trying to outflank them. Diana was shooting now. The\r\nthought of her escort\'s annihilation and her own and Gaston\'s peril had\r\novercome the reluctance she had had at first, and she had even a moment\r\nto wonder at her coolness. She did not feel afraid, the death of\r\nAhmed\'s men had made her angry, a fierce revengeful anger that made her\r\nsee red and filled her with a desire to retaliate in kind. She fired\r\nrapidly, emptying her revolver, and she had just reloaded with steady\r\nfingers when The Dancer stumbled, recovering himself for a few steps,\r\nand then lurched slowly over on to his side, blood pouring from his\r\nmouth. Diana sprang clear, and in a moment Gaston was beside her,\r\nthrusting her behind him, shielding her with his own body, and firing\r\nsteadily at the oncoming Arabs.\r\n\r\nThe same feeling of unreality that she had experienced once before the\r\nfirst day in the Sheik\'s camp came over her. The intense stillness--for\r\nthe Arabs had ceased shouting--the hot, dry sand with the shimmering\r\nheat haze rising like mist from its whispering surface, the cloudless\r\ndeep blue sky overhead, the band of menacing horsemen circling nearer\r\nand nearer, the dead Dancer, with Gaston\'s horse standing quietly\r\nbeside his prostrate companion, and lastly, the man beside her, brave\r\nand devoted to the end, all seemed fantastic and unreal. She viewed it\r\ndispassionately, as if she were a spectator rather than a participant\r\nin the scene. But for a moment only, then the reality of the situation\r\ncame clearly to her again. Any minute might mean death for one or other\r\nor both of them, and with an instinctive movement she pressed closer to\r\nGaston. They were both silent, there seemed nothing to say. The valet\'s\r\nleft hand clenched over hers at the involuntary appeal for\r\ncompanionship that she made, and she felt it contract as a bullet\r\ngashed his forehead, blinding him for a moment with the blood that\r\ndripped into his eyes. He let go her hand to brush his arm across his\r\nface, and as he did so the Arabs with suddenly renewed shouting bore\r\ndown upon them.\r\n\r\nGaston turned sharply and Diana read his purpose in the horror in his\r\neyes. She held up her head with a little nod and the same brave smile\r\non her white lips. "Please," she whispered, "quickly!" A spasm crossed\r\nhis face, "Turn your head," he muttered desperately. "I cannot do it if\r\nyou----"\r\n\r\nThere was a rattle of shots, and with a gasp he crumpled up against\r\nher. For a moment it was pandemonium. Standing over Gaston\'s body she\r\nfired her last shot and flung the empty revolver in the face of a man\r\nwho sprang forward to seize her. She turned with a desperate hope of\r\nreaching Gaston\'s horse, but she was hemmed in, and for a second she\r\nstood at bay, hands clenched and teeth set, braving the wild faces that\r\nsurrounded her, and were closing in upon her, with flashing defiant\r\neyes. Then she was conscious of a crashing blow on her head, the ground\r\nheaved up under her feet, everything went black before her eyes, and\r\nwithout a sound she fell senseless.\r\n\r\nLate in the afternoon Saint Hubert was still writing in the big tent.\r\nHenri had deciphered the notes that had baffled his master in the\r\nmorning, and the Vicomte had taken advantage of the solitude to do some\r\nlong-neglected work. He had forgotten the time, forgotten to be\r\nsurprised at Diana\'s continued absence, immersed in the interesting\r\nsubject he was dealing with, and not realising the significance of her\r\ndelayed return. Ahmed had spoken of the proximity of his hereditary\r\nenemy, but Saint Hubert had not grasped how near the robber Sheik had\r\nventured.\r\n\r\nHe was too engrossed to notice the usual noise in the camp that\r\nheralded the Sheik\'s arrival, and he looked up with a start when Ahmed\r\nBen Hassan swept in. The Sheik\'s dark eyes glanced sombrely around the\r\ntent and without a word he went through into the inner room. In a\r\nmoment he came hack.\r\n\r\n"Where is Diane?"\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert got up, puzzled at his tone. He looked at his watch. "She\r\nwent for a ride this morning. _Dieu!_ I had no idea it was so\r\nlate."\r\n\r\n"This morning!--and not back yet?" repeated the Sheik slowly. "What\r\ntime this morning?"\r\n\r\n"About ten, I think," replied Saint Hubert uneasily. "I\'m not sure. I\r\ndidn\'t look. There was an accident, and she delayed to watch me tie up\r\none of your foolish children who had been playing with a worthless\r\ngun."\r\n\r\nThe Sheik moved over to the doorway. "She had an escort?" he asked\r\ncurtly.\r\n\r\n"Yes."\r\n\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan\'s face hardened and the heavy scowl contracted his\r\nblack brows. Had she all these weeks been tricking him--feigning a\r\ncontent she did not feel, lulling his suspicions to enable her to seize\r\nanother opportunity to attempt to get away? For a moment his face grew\r\ndark, then he put the thought from him. He trusted her. Only a week\r\nbefore she had given him her word, and he knew she would not lie to\r\nhim. And, besides, the thing was impossible. Gaston would never be\r\ncaught napping a second time, and there were also the six men who\r\nformed her guard. She would never be able to escape the vigilance of\r\nseven men. But it was the trust he had in her that weighed most with\r\nhim. He had never trusted a woman before, but this woman had been\r\ndifferent. The others who had come and gone so lightly had not even\r\nleft a recollection behind them; they had faded into one concrete cause\r\nof utter boredom. There had never been any reason to trust or mistrust\r\nthem, or to care if they came or went. Satiety had come with possession\r\nand with it indifference. But the emotion that this girl\'s uncommon\r\nbeauty and slender boyishness had aroused in him had not diminished\r\nduring the months she had been living in his camp. Her varying moods,\r\nher antagonism, her fits of furious rage, and, lastly, her unexpected\r\nsurrender, had kept his interest alive. He had grown accustomed to her.\r\nHe had come to looking forward with a vague, indefinite pleasure, on\r\nreturning from his long expeditions, to seeing the dainty little figure\r\ncurled up among the cushions on the big divan. Her presence seemed to\r\npervade the atmosphere of the whole tent, changing it utterly. She had\r\nbecome necessary to him as he had never believed it possible that a\r\nwoman could be. And with the change that she had made in his camp there\r\nhad come a change in himself also.\r\n\r\nFor the first time a shadow had risen between him and the man whose\r\nfriendship had meant everything to him since, as a lad of fifteen, he\r\nhad come under the influence of the young Frenchman, who was three\r\nyears his senior. He realized that since the night of Raoul\'s arrival\r\nhe had been seething with insensate jealousy. He had relied on the\r\nWestern tendencies that prompted him to carry off the difficult\r\nsituation, but his ingrained Orientalism had broken through the\r\nsuperficial veneer. He was jealous of every word, every look she gave\r\nSaint Hubert. Pride had prevented an open rupture with the Vicomte this\r\nmorning, but he had ridden away filled with a cold rage that had\r\naugmented every hour and finally driven him back earlier than he had\r\nintended, riding with a recklessness that had been apparent even to his\r\nmen. The sight of Raoul sitting alone absorbed in his work had in part\r\nallayed his suspicions, and he had gone on into the other room with a\r\nfeeling of new expectancy that had changed to a sudden chill at its\r\nemptiness. The vacant room had brought home to him abruptly all that\r\nthe girl meant to him. A latent anxiety crept into his eyes.\r\n\r\nHe went out under the awning and clapped his hands, and a servant\r\nanswered the summons almost immediately. He gave an order and waited,\r\nhis hands thrust into the folds of his waist-cloth and his teeth\r\nclenched on a cigarette that he had forgotten to light.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert joined him. "What do you think?" he asked, with a touch of\r\ndiffidence.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know what to think," replied the Sheik shortly.\r\n\r\n"But is there any real danger?"\r\n\r\n"There is always danger in the desert, particularly when that devil is\r\nabroad." He motioned to the south with an impatient jerk of his head.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert\'s breath whistled sharply through his teeth. "My God! You\r\ndon\'t imagine----"\r\n\r\nBut the Sheik only shrugged his shoulders and turned to Yusef, who had\r\ncome up with half-a-dozen men. There was a rapid interchange of\r\nquestions and answers, some brief orders, and the men hurried away in\r\ndifferent directions, while Ahmed Ben Hassan turned again to Saint\r\nHubert.\r\n\r\n"They were seen by three of the southern patrols this morning, but of\r\ncourse it was nobody\'s business to find out if they had come back or\r\nnot. I will start at once--in about ten minutes. You will come with me?\r\nGood! I have sent for reinforcements, who are to follow us if we are\r\nnot back in twelve hours." His voice was expressionless, and only Raoul\r\nde Saint Hubert, who had known him since boyhood, could and did\r\nappreciate the significance of a fleeting look that crossed his face as\r\nhe went back into the tent.\r\n\r\nFor a moment the Vicomte hesitated, but he knew that not even he was\r\nwanted inside that empty tent, and a half-bitter, half-sad feeling that\r\nthe perfect friendship and confidence that had existed between them for\r\ntwenty years would never again be the same came to them, the regretful\r\nsense of inevitable change, the consciousness of personal relegation.\r\nThen fear for Diana drove out every other consideration, and he went to\r\nhis own quarters with a heavy heart.\r\n\r\nWhen he came back in a few minutes with Henri following him the camp\r\nhad undergone a transformation. With the promptness of perfect\r\ndiscipline the hundred men who had been chosen to go on the expedition\r\nwere already waiting, each man standing by his horse, and the Sheik,\r\nquiet and impassive as usual, was superintending the distribution of\r\nextra ammunition. A groom was walking The Hawk slowly up and down, and\r\nYusef, whose gloomy eyes had been fixed reproachfully on his chief,\r\nchafing against the order to remain behind to take command of the\r\nreinforcements should they be needed, went to him and took the horse\'s\r\nbridle from him and brought him to the Sheik. Even as he held the\r\nstirrup Saint Hubert could see that he was expostulating with an\r\nunusual insistence, begging for permission to accompany them. But the\r\nSheik shook his head, and the young man stood sullenly aside to avoid\r\nThe Hawk\'s hoofs as he reared impatiently.\r\n\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan motioned Saint Hubert to his side and in silence the\r\ncavalcade started at the usual swift gallop. The silence impressed\r\nRaoul, who was accustomed to the Arab\'s usual clamour. It affected his\r\nsensitive temperaments, filling him with a sinister foreboding. The\r\nsilent band of stern-faced horsemen riding in close and orderly\r\nformation behind them suggested something more than a mere relief\r\nparty. The tradition of reckless courage and organised fighting\r\nefficiency that had made the tribe known and feared for generations had\r\nbeen always maintained, and under the leadership of the last two\r\nholders of the hereditary name to so high a degree that the respect in\r\nwhich it was held was such that no other tribe had ventured to dispute\r\nits supremacy, and for many years its serious fighting capacities had\r\nnot been tested.\r\n\r\nEven Ibraheim Omair had inherited a feud that was largely traditional.\r\nOnly once during the lifetime of the last Ahmed Ben Hassan had he dared\r\nto come into open conflict, and the memory of it had lasted until now.\r\nSkirmishes there had been and would always be inevitably sufficient to\r\nkeep the tribesmen in a state of perpetual expectancy, and for this\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan preserved the rigid discipline that prevailed in his\r\ntribe, insisting on the high standard that had kept them famous. The\r\nlife-work that his predecessor had taken over from his father the\r\npresent Ahmed Ben Hassan had carried on and developed with autocratic\r\nperseverance. The inborn love of fighting had been carefully fostered\r\nin the tribe, the weapons with which they were armed were of the newest\r\npattern. Raoul knew with perfect certainty that to the picked men\r\nfollowing them this hasty expedition meant only one thing--war, the war\r\nthat they had looked forward to all their lives, precipitated now by an\r\naccident that gave to a handful of them the chance that hundreds of\r\ntheir fellow-tribesmen were longing for, a chance that sent them\r\njoyfully behind their chief, careless whether the reinforcements that\r\nhad been sent for arrived in time or not. The smallness of their\r\nnumbers was a source of pleasure rather than otherwise; if they won\r\nthrough to them would be the glory of victory; if they were annihilated\r\nwith them would rest the honour of dying with the leader whom they\r\nworshipped, for not one of them doubted that Ahmed Ben Hassan would not\r\nsurvive his bodyguard, the flower of his tribe, the carefully chosen\r\nmen from whose ranks his personal escort was always drawn. With them he\r\nwould crush his hereditary enemy or with them he would die.\r\n\r\nThe short twilight had gone and a brilliant moon shone high in the\r\nheavens, illuminating the surrounding country with a clear white light.\r\nAt any other time the beauty of the scene, the glamour of the Eastern\r\nnight, the head-long gallop in company with this band of fierce\r\nfighting men would have stirred Saint Hubert profoundly. His artistic\r\ntemperament and his own absolute fearlessness and love of adventure\r\nwould have combined to make the expedition an exciting experience that\r\nhe would not willingly have foregone. But the reason for it all, the\r\nperil of the girl whom he loved so unexpectedly, changed the whole\r\ncolour of the affair, tinging it with a gravity and a suspense that\r\nleft a cold fear in his heart. And if to him, what then to the man\r\nbeside him? The question that Ahmed Ben Hassan had negatived so\r\nscornfully a week before had been answered differently in the swift\r\nlook that had crossed his face this evening. He had not spoken since\r\nthey started, and Saint Hubert had not felt able to break the silence.\r\nThey had left the level country and were in amongst the long,\r\nsuccessive ranges of undulating ground, the summits standing out silver\r\nwhite in the gleaming moonlight, the hollows filled with dark shadow,\r\nlike black pools of deep, still water. And at the bottom of one of the\r\nslopes the Sheik pulled up suddenly with a low, hissing exclamation. A\r\nwhite shape was lying face downwards, spread-eagled on the sand, almost\r\nunder The Hawk\'s feet, and at their approach two lean, slinking forms\r\ncantered away into the night. The Sheik and Henri reached the still\r\nfigure simultaneously and Saint Hubert almost as quickly. He made a\r\nhurried examination. The bullet that had stunned Gaston had glanced\r\noff, leaving an ugly cut, and others that had hit him at the same time\r\nhad ploughed through his shoulder, breaking the bone and causing\r\nbesides wounds that had bled freely. He had staggered more than a mile\r\nbefore he had fainted again from loss of blood. He came to under Saint\r\nHubert\'s handling, and lifted his heavy eyes to the Sheik, who was\r\nkneeling beside him.\r\n\r\n"Monseigneur--Madame--Ibraheim Omair," he whispered weakly, and\r\nrelapsed into unconsciousness.\r\n\r\nFor a moment the Sheik\'s eyes met Raoul\'s across his body, and then\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan rose to his feet. "Be as quick as you can," he said,\r\nand went back to his horse. He leaned against The Hawk, his fingers\r\nmechanically searching for and lighting a cigarette, his eyes fixed\r\nunseeingly on the group around Gaston. The valet\'s broken words had\r\nconfirmed the fear that he had striven to crush since he discovered\r\nDiana\'s absence.\r\n\r\nHe had only seen Ibraheim Omair once when, ten years before, he had\r\ngone with the elder Ahmed Ben Hassan to a meeting of the more powerful\r\nchiefs at Algiers, arranged under the auspices of the French\r\nGovernment, to confer on a complicated boundary question that had\r\nthreatened an upheaval amongst the tribes which the nominal protectors\r\nof the country were afraid would be prejudicial to their own prestige,\r\nas it would have been beyond their power to quell. He had chafed at\r\nhaving to meet his hereditary enemy on equal terms, and only the\r\nrestraining influence of the old Sheik, who exacted an unquestioning\r\nobedience that extended even to his heir, had prevented a catastrophe\r\nthat might have nullified the meeting and caused infinitely more\r\ncomplications than the original boundary dispute. But the memory of the\r\nrobber Sheik remained with him always, and the recollection of his\r\nbloated, vicious face and gross, unwieldy body rose clearly before him\r\nnow.\r\n\r\nIbraheim Omair and the slender daintiness that he had prized so\r\nlightly. Diane! His teeth met through the cigarette in his mouth. His\r\nsenseless jealousy and the rage provoked by Raoul\'s outspoken criticism\r\nhad recoiled on the innocent cause. She, not Saint Hubert, had felt the\r\nbrunt of his anger. In the innate cruelty of his nature it had given\r\nhim a subtle pleasure to watch the bewilderment, alternating with\r\nflickering fear, that had come back into the deep blue eyes that for\r\ntwo months had looked into his with frank confidence. He had made her\r\nacutely conscious of his displeasure. Only last night, when his lack of\r\nconsideration and his unwonted irritability had made her wince several\r\ntimes during the evening and after Saint Hubert had gone to his own\r\ntent, he, had looked up to find her eyes fixed on him with an\r\nexpression that, in his dangerous mood, had excited all the brutality\r\nof which he was capable, and had filled him with a desire to torture\r\nher. The dumb reproach in her eyes had exasperated him, rousing the\r\nfiendish temper that had been hardly kept in check all the previous\r\nweek. And yet, when he held her helpless in his arms, quivering and\r\nshrinking from the embrace that was no caress, but merely the medium of\r\nhis anger, and the reproach in her wavering eyes changed to mute\r\nentreaty, the pleasure he had anticipated in her fear had failed him as\r\nit had before, and had irritated him further. The wild beating of her\r\nheart, the sobbing intake of her breath, the knowledge of his power\r\nover her, gave him no gratification, and he had flung her from him\r\ncursing her savagely, till she had fled into the other room with her\r\nhands over her ears to shut out the sound of his slow, deliberate\r\nvoice. And this morning he had left her without a sign of any kind, no\r\nword or gesture that might have effaced the memory of the previous\r\nnight. He had not meant to, he had intended to go back to her before he\r\nfinally rode away, but Saint Hubert\'s refusal to accompany him had\r\nkilled the softer feelings that prompted him, and his rage had flamed\r\nup again.\r\n\r\nAnd now? The longing to hold her in his arms, to kiss the tears from\r\nher eyes and the colour into her pale lips, was almost unbearable. He\r\nwould give his life to keep even a shadow from her path, and she was in\r\nthe hands of Ibraheim Omair! The thought and all that it implied was\r\ntorture, but no sign escaped him of the hell he was enduring. The\r\nunavoidable delay seemed interminable, and he swung into the saddle,\r\nhoping that the waiting would seem less with The Hawk\'s restless,\r\nnervous body gripped between his knees, for though the horse would\r\nstand quietly with his master beside him, he fretted continually at\r\nwaiting once the Sheik was mounted, and the necessity for soothing him\r\nwas preferable to complete inaction.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert rose to his feet at last, and, leaving behind Henri and\r\ntwo Arabs, who were detailed to take the wounded man back to the camp,\r\nthe swift gallop southward was resumed. On, over the rising and falling\r\nground along which Gaston had stumbled, blind and faint with loss of\r\nblood and the pain of his wounds, past the dead body of The Dancer,\r\nghostly white in the moonlight, lying a little apart from the\r\nsemicircle of Arabs that proved the efficiency of Gaston\'s shooting\r\nwhere Diana and he had made their last stand. The Sheik made no sign\r\nand did not check the headlong gallop, but continued on, The Hawk\r\ntaking the fallen bodies that lay in his path in his stride, with only\r\na quiver of repugnance and a snort of disgust. Still on, past the\r\nhuddled bundles of tumbled draperies that marked the way significantly,\r\navoiding them where the moonlight illuminated brightly, and riding over\r\nthem in the deep hollows, where once Raoul\'s horse stumbled badly and\r\nnearly fell, recovering himself with a wild scramble, and the Vicomte\r\nheard the dead man\'s skull crack under the horse\'s slipping hoof.\r\n\r\nThe distant howling of jackals came closer and closer until, topping\r\none long rise and descending into a hollow that was long enough and\r\nwide enough to be fully lit by the moon, they came to the place where\r\nthe ambush had been laid. Instinctively Ahmed Ben Hassan knew that\r\namongst the jostling heaps of corpses and dead horses lay the bodies of\r\nhis own men. Perhaps amongst the still forms from which the jackals,\r\nwhose hideous yelling they had heard, had slunk away, there might be\r\none left with life enough to give some news. One of his own men who\r\nwould speak willingly, or one of Ibraheim Omair\'s who would be made to\r\nspeak. His lips curled back from his white teeth in a grin of pure\r\ncruelty.\r\n\r\nThe silence that had prevailed amongst his men broke suddenly as they\r\nsearched quickly among the dead. The Sheik waited impassively, silent\r\namidst the muttered imprecations and threats of vengeance of his\r\nfollowers as they laid beside him the six remains of what had been\r\nDiana\'s escort, slashed and mutilated almost beyond recognition. But it\r\nwas he who noticed that the last terrible figure stirred slightly as it\r\nwas laid down, and it was into his face, grown suddenly strangely\r\ngentle, that the dying Arab looked with fast-filming eyes. The man\r\nsmiled, the happy smile of a child that had obtained an unexpected\r\nreward, and raised his hand painfully in salute, then pointed mutely to\r\nthe south.\r\n\r\nThe Sheik caught his follower\'s nerveless fingers as they fell in his\r\nown strong grasp, and with a last effort the Arab drew his chief\'s hand\r\nto his forehead and fell back dead.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\nSlowly and painfully, through waves of deadly nausea and with the\r\nsurging of deep waters in her ears, Diana struggled back to\r\nconsciousness. The agony in her head was excruciating, and her limbs\r\nfelt cramped and bruised. Recollection was dulled in bodily pain, and,\r\nat first, thought was merged in physical suffering. But gradually the\r\nfog cleared from her brain and memory supervened hesitatingly. She\r\nremembered fragmentary incidents of what had gone before the oblivion\r\nfrom which she had just emerged. Gaston, and the horror and resolution\r\nin his eyes, the convulsive working of his mouth as he faced her at the\r\nlast moment. Her own dread--not of the death that was imminent, but\r\nlest the mercy it offered should be snatched from her. Then before the\r\nvalet could effect his supreme devotion had come the hail of bullets,\r\nand he had fallen against her, the blood that poured from his wounds\r\nsaturating her linen coat, and rolled over across her feet. She\r\nremembered vaguely the wild figures hemming her in, but nothing more.\r\n\r\nHer eyes were still shut; a leaden weight seemed fixed on them, and the\r\neffort to open them was beyond her strength. "Gaston," she whispered\r\nfeebly, and stretched out her hand. But instead of his body or the dry\r\nhot sand her fingers had expected to encounter they closed over soft\r\ncushions, and with the shock she sat up with a jerk, her eyes staring\r\nwide, but, sick and faint, she fell back again, her arm flung across\r\nher face, shielding the light that pierced like daggers through her\r\nthrobbing eye-balls. For a while she lay still, fighting against the\r\nweakness that overpowered her, and by degrees the horrible nausea\r\npassed and the agony in her head abated, leaving only a dull ache. The\r\ndesire to know where she was and what had happened made her forget her\r\nbruised body. She moved her arm slightly from before her eyes so that\r\nshe could see, and looked cautiously from under thick lashes, screened\r\nby the sleeve of her coat. She was lying on a pile of cushions in one\r\ncorner of a small-tented apartment which was otherwise bare, except for\r\nthe rug that covered the floor. In the opposite corner of the tent an\r\nArab woman crouched over a little brazier, and the smell of native\r\ncoffee was heavy in the air. She closed her eyes again with a shudder.\r\nThe attempted devotion of Gaston had been useless. This must be the\r\ncamp of the robber Sheik, Ibraheim Omair.\r\n\r\nShe lay still, pressing closely down amongst the cushions, and\r\nclenching the sleeve of her jacket between her teeth to stifle the\r\ngroan that rose to her lips. A lump came into her throat as she thought\r\nof Gaston. In those last moments all inequality of rank had been swept\r\naway in their common peril--they had been only a white man and a white\r\nwoman together in their extremity. She remembered how, when she had\r\npressed close to him, his hand had sought and gripped hers, conveying\r\ncourage and sympathy. All that he could do he had done, he had shielded\r\nher body with his own, it must have been over his lifeless body that\r\nthey had taken her. He had proved his faithfulness, sacrificing his\r\nlife for his master\'s play-thing. Gaston was in all probability dead,\r\nbut she was alive, and she must husband her strength for her own needs.\r\nShe forced the threatening emotion down, and, with an effort,\r\ncontrolled the violent shivering in her limbs, and sat up slowly,\r\nlooking at the Arab woman, who, hearing her move, turned to gaze at\r\nher. Instantly Diana realised that there was no help or compassion to\r\nbe expected from her. She was a handsome woman, who must have been\r\npretty as a girl, but there was no sign of softness in her sullen face\r\nand vindictive eyes. Instinctively Diana felt that the glowing menace\r\nof the woman\'s expression was inspired by personal hatred, and that her\r\npresence in the lent was objectionable to her. And the feeling gave a\r\nnecessary spur to the courage that was fast coming back to her. She\r\nstared with all the haughtiness she could summon to her aid; she had\r\nlearned her own power among the natives of India the previous year, and\r\nhere in the desert there was only one Arab whose eyes did not fall\r\nbeneath hers, and presently with a muttered word the woman turned back\r\nto her coffee-making.\r\n\r\nDiana\'s muscles relaxed and she sat back easily on the cushions, the\r\nlittle passage of wills had restored her confidence in herself. She\r\nmoved her hand and it brushed against her jacket, coming away stained\r\nand sticky, and she noticed for the first time that all one side and\r\nsleeve were soaked with blood. She ripped it off with a shudder and\r\nflung it from her, rubbing the red smear from her hands with a kind of\r\nhorror.\r\n\r\nThe little tent was intensely hot, and there was a close, pungent smell\r\nthat was eminently _native_ that she never experienced in the cool\r\nairiness and scrupulous cleanliness of Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s tents. Her\r\nsensitive lip curled with disgust, all her innate fastidiousness in\r\nrevolt. The heat aggravated a burning thirst that was parching her\r\nthroat. She got up on to her feet slowly, and with infinite caution, to\r\nprevent any jar that might start again the throbbing in her head; but\r\nthe effects of the blow were wearing off, and, though her head\r\ncontinued to ache, it did no more than that, and the sick, giddy\r\nfeeling had gone completely. She crossed the tent to the side of the\r\nArab woman.\r\n\r\n"Give me some water," she said in French, but the woman shook her head\r\nwithout looking up. Diana repeated the request in Arabic, one of the\r\nfew sentences she knew without stumbling. This time the woman rose up\r\nhastily and held out a cup of the coffee she had been making.\r\n\r\nDiana hated the sweet, thick stuff, but it would do until she could get\r\nthe water she wanted, and she put out her hand to take the little cup.\r\nBut her eyes met the other\'s fixed on her, and something in their\r\nmalignant stare made her pause. A sudden suspicion shot through her\r\nmind. The coffee was drugged. What beyond the woman\'s expression made\r\nher think so she did not know, but she was sure of it. She put the cup\r\naside impatiently.\r\n\r\n"No. Not coffee. Water," she said firmly.\r\n\r\nBefore she realised what was happening the woman thrust a strong arm\r\nround her and forced the cup to her lips. That confirmed Diana\'s\r\nsuspicions and rage lent her additional strength. The woman was strong,\r\nbut Diana was stronger, younger and more active. She dashed the cup to\r\nthe floor, spilling its contents, and, with an effort, tore the\r\nclinging hands from her and sent the woman crashing on to the ground,\r\nrolling against the brazier, oversetting it, and scattering brass pots\r\nand cups over the rug. The woman scrambled to her knees and beat out\r\nthe glowing embers, uttering scream after scream in a shrill, piercing\r\nvoice. And, in answer to her cries, a curtain at the side of the tent,\r\nthat Diana had not noticed, slid aside and a gigantic Nubian came in.\r\nWith outstretched hand shaking with rage, pointing at Diana, she burst\r\ninto voluble abuse, punctuating every few words with the shrieks that\r\nhad brought the negro.\r\n\r\nDiana could understand nothing of what she said, but her expressive\r\ngestures told the story of the struggle plainly enough. The Nubian\r\nlistened with white teeth flashing in a broad grin, and shook his head\r\nin response to some request urged with denunciatory fist. He picked up\r\nthe last remaining embers that had scattered on the rug, rubbing the\r\nsmouldering patches till they were extinguished, and then turned to\r\nleave the room. But Diana called him back. She went a step forward, her\r\nhead high, and looked him straight in the face.\r\n\r\n"Fetch me water!" she said imperiously. He pointed to the coffee that\r\nthe woman had recommenced to make, her back turned to them, but Diana\r\nstamped her foot. "Water! Bring me water!" she said again, more\r\nimperiously than before. With a wider grin the negro made a gesture of\r\nacquiescence and went out, returning in a few moments with a\r\nwater-skin.\r\n\r\nThe thought of its condition made her hesitate for a moment, but only\r\nfor a moment. Her thirst was too great to allow niceties to interfere\r\nwith it. She picked up one of the clean coffee-cups that had rolled to\r\nher feet, rinsed it several times, and then drank. The water was warm\r\nand slightly brackish, but she needed it too much to mind. In spite of\r\nbeing tepid it relieved the dry, suffocating feeling in her throat and\r\nrefreshed her. The Nubian went away again, leaving the woman still\r\ncrouching over the brazier.\r\n\r\nDiana walked back to the cushions and dropped down on to them gladly.\r\nThe events of the last few moments had tried her more than she\r\nrealised, her legs were shaking under her, and she was thankful to sit\r\ndown. But her courage had risen with a bound; the fact that she was\r\nphysically stronger than the woman who had been put to guard her, and\r\nalso that she had gained her point with the burly negro, had a great\r\nmoral effect on her, further restoring her confidence in herself.\r\n\r\nHer position was an appalling one, but hope was strong within her. The\r\nfact that since she had regained consciousness she had seen only the\r\nwoman and the Nubian seemed to argue that Ibraheim Omair must be absent\r\nfrom his camp; the thought that he might purposely be delaying the\r\nmoment of inspecting his captive with a view to prolonging her mental\r\ntorture she put from her as improbable. She did not credit him with so\r\nmuch acumen. And from his absence her courage gained strength. If it\r\ncould only be prolonged until Ahmed reached her. That the Sheik would\r\ncome she knew, her faith in him was unbounded. If he only came in time!\r\nHours had passed since the ambuscade had surprised them. It had been\r\nearly afternoon then. Now the lighted lamp told her it was night. How\r\nlate she did not know. Her watch had been broken some months before,\r\nand she had no means of even guessing the hour, but it must be well on\r\nin the evening. By now the absence of herself and Gaston and their\r\nescort would be discovered. He would know her peril and he would come\r\nto her. Of that she had no doubt. Although he had changed so strangely\r\nin the last few days, though the wonderful gentleness of the last two\r\nmonths had merged again into indifference and cruelty, still she never\r\ndoubted. Even if desire had passed and indifference had become so great\r\nthat she was no longer necessary to him, still the Oriental jealousy\r\nwith which he was so deeply imbued would never allow him to let her\r\npass so lightly from his keeping. He might discard her at his own\r\npleasure, but no one would take her from him with impunity. Her woman\'s\r\nintuition had sensed the jealousy that had actuated him during the\r\nunhappy days since Saint Hubert had come. An inconsistent jealousy that\r\nhad been unprovoked and unjustified, but for which she had suffered.\r\nShe had known last night, when she winced under his sarcastic tongue,\r\nand later, when Saint Hubert had left them and his temper had suddenly\r\nboiled over, that she was paying for the unaccustomed strain that he\r\nwas putting on his own feelings. His curses had eaten into her heart,\r\nand she had fled from him to stifle the coward instinct that urged her\r\nto confess her love and beg his mercy. She had lain awake with\r\nshivering apprehension waiting for him, but when, after nearly two\r\nhours, he had sauntered in, the usual cigarette between his lips,\r\nindifference had taken the place of rage, and he had ignored her, as\r\nshe had grown used to being ignored. And long after she knew from his\r\neven breathing that he was asleep she had lain wide-eyed beside him,\r\ngrasping at what happiness she could, living for the moment as she had\r\nschooled herself to live, trying to be content with just the fact of\r\nhis nearness. And the indifference of the night had been maintained\r\nwhen he had left her at dawn, his persistent silence pointing the\r\ncontinuance of his displeasure. But he would come, if for no other\r\nreason than the same jealousy which held him in its inexorable grip. He\r\nwould come! He would come! She whispered it over to herself as if\r\nmerely the sound of the words gave her courage. He would not let\r\nanything happen to her. Every moment that Ibraheim Omair stayed away\r\nwas so much gained, every moment he would be coming nearer. The\r\nreversal of the role he played in her life brought a quivering smile to\r\nher lips. For the advent of the man who a few weeks before she had\r\nloathed for his brutal abduction of herself she now prayed with the\r\ndesperation of despair. He represented safety, salvation, everything\r\nthat made life worth living.\r\n\r\nA sudden noise and men\'s voices in the adjoining room sent her to her\r\nfeet with heaving breast and clenched hands. But the sharp, guttural\r\nvoice predominating over the other voices killed the wild hope that had\r\nsprung up in her by its utter dissimilarity to the soft low tones for\r\nwhich she longed. Ibraheim Omair! He had come first! She set her teeth\r\nwith a long, shuddering breath, bracing herself to meet what was\r\ncoming.\r\n\r\nThe Arab woman turned to look at her again with a sneering smile that\r\nwas full of significance, but beyond a fleeting glance of disdain Diana\r\npaid no attention to her. She stood rigid, one foot beating nervously\r\ninto the soft rug. She noticed irrelevantly at the moment that both her\r\nspurs and the empty holster had been removed whilst she was\r\nunconscious, and with the odd detachment that transfers a train of\r\nthought from the centre of importance even at a supreme moment, she\r\nwondered, with an annoyance that seemed curiously futile, why it had\r\nbeen done.\r\n\r\nThe voices in the next room continued, until Diana almost prayed for\r\nthe moment she was waiting for would come; suspense was worse than the\r\nordeal for which she was nerving herself, It came at last. The curtain\r\nslid aside again, and the same huge negro she had seen before entered.\r\nHe came towards her, and her breath hissed in suddenly between her set\r\nteeth, but before he reached her the Arab woman intercepted him,\r\nblocking his way, and with wild eyes and passionate gestures poured out\r\na stream of low, frenzied words. The Nubian turned on her impatiently\r\nand thrust her roughly out of his way, and, coming to Diana, put out\r\nhis hand as if to grasp her arm, but she stepped back with flashing\r\neyes and a gesture that he obeyed.\r\n\r\nHer heart was pounding, but she had herself under control. Only her\r\nhands twitched, her long fingers curling and uncurling spasmodically,\r\nand she buried them deep in her breeches\' pockets to hide them. She\r\nwalked slowly to the curtain and nodded to the Nubian to draw it aside,\r\nand slower still she passed into the other room. Only a little larger\r\nthan the one she had left, almost as bare, but her mind took in these\r\nthings uncomprehendingly, for all her attention was focussed on the\r\ncentral figure in the room.\r\n\r\nIbraheim Omair, the robber Sheik, lolling his great bulk on a pile of\r\ncushions, a little inlaid stool with coffee beside him, and behind him,\r\nstanding motionless as if formed of bronze, two other negroes, so like\r\nthe one that had summoned her that they seemed like statues that had\r\nbeen cast from one mould.\r\n\r\nDiana paused for a moment framed in the entrance, then, with head\r\nthrown back and swaggering, boyish stride, she moved across the thick\r\nrugs leisurely and halted in front of the chief, looking straight at\r\nhim with haughty, curling lips and insolent, half-closed eyes. The hold\r\nshe was exercising over herself was tremendous, her body was rigid with\r\nthe effort, and her hands deep down in her pockets clenched till the\r\nnails bit into the palms. Every instinct was rebelling against the calm\r\nshe forced upon herself. She longed to scream and make a dash for the\r\nopening that she guessed was behind her, and to take her chance in the\r\ndarkness outside. But she knew that such a chance was impossible; if\r\nshe ever reached the open air she would never be allowed to get more\r\nthan a few steps from the tent. Her only course lay in the bravado that\r\nalone kept her from collapse. She must convey the impression of\r\nfearlessness, though cold terror was knocking at her heart. Masked with\r\nindifference her veiled eyes were watching the robber chief closely.\r\nThis was, indeed, the Arab of her imaginings, this gross, unwieldy\r\nfigure lying among the tawdry cushions, his swollen, ferocious face\r\nseamed and lined with every mark of vice, his full, sensual lips parted\r\nand showing broken, blackened teeth, his deep-set, bloodshot eyes with\r\na look in them that it took all her resolution to sustain, a look of\r\nsuch bestial evilness that the horror of it bathed her in perspiration.\r\nHis appearance was slovenly, his robes, originally rich, were stained\r\nand tumbled, the fat hands lying spread out on his knees were engrained\r\nwith dirt, showing even against his dark skin. His heavy face lit up\r\nwith a gleam of malicious satisfaction as Diana came towards him, his\r\nloose mouth broadened in a wicked smile. He leaned forward a little,\r\nweighing heavily on the hands that were on his knees, his eyes roving\r\nslowly over her till they rested on her face again.\r\n\r\n"So! the white woman of my brother Ahmed Ben Hassan," he said slowly,\r\nin villainous French, with a sudden, snarling intonation as he uttered\r\nhis enemy\'s name. "Ahmed Ben Hassan! May Allah burn his soul in hell!"\r\nhe added with relish, and spat contemptuously.\r\n\r\nHe leaned back on the cushions with a grunt, and drank some coffee\r\nnoisily.\r\n\r\nDiana kept her eyes fixed on him, and under their unwavering stare he\r\nseemed to be uneasy, his own inflamed eyes wandering ceaselessly over\r\nher, one hand fumbling at the curved hilt of a knife stuck in his belt,\r\nand at last he grew exasperated, hitching himself forward once more and\r\nbeckoning her to come nearer to him. She hesitated, and as she paused\r\nuncertainly, there was a flutter of draperies behind her, and the Arab\r\nwoman from the inner room, evading the negro who stepped forward to\r\nstop her, flung herself at the feet of Ibraheim Omair, clinging to his\r\nknees with a low wailing cry. In a flash Diana realised the meaning of\r\nthe hatred that had gleamed in the woman\'s eyes earlier in the evening.\r\nTo her she was a rival, whose coming to share the favours of her lord\r\nhad aroused all the jealousy of the reigning favourite. A wave of\r\ndisgust mingled with the fear that was torturing her. She jerked her\r\nhead angrily, fighting against the terror that was growing on her, and\r\nfor a moment her lashes drooped and hid her eyes. When she looked up\r\nagain the woman was still crouched at the old Arab\'s feet, imploring\r\nand distraught.\r\n\r\nIbraheim Omair looked down on her curiously, his lips drawn back from\r\nhis blackened teeth in an evil grin, and then shook her off violently\r\nwith a swift blow in the mouth, but the woman clung closer, with\r\nupturned, desperate face, a thin trickle of blood oozing from her lips,\r\nand with a hoarse growl that was like the dull roar of a savage beast\r\nthe robber chief caught her by the throat and held her for a moment,\r\nher frantic, clutching hands powerless against his strong grasp, then\r\nslowly drew the long knife from the ample folds of his waist-cloth, and\r\nas slowly drove it home into the strangling woman\'s breast. With savage\r\ncallousness, before he released his hold of her, he wiped the stained\r\nknife carefully on her clothing and replaced it, and then flung the\r\ndead body from him. It rolled over on the rug midway between him and\r\nDiana.\r\n\r\nThere was a momentary silence in the room, and Diana became conscious\r\nof a muffled, rhythmical beat near her, like the ticking of a great\r\nclock, and realised with dull wonder that it was her own heart beating.\r\nShe seemed turned to stone, petrified with the horror of the last few\r\nmoments. Her eyes were glued to the still figure on the rug before her\r\nwith the gaping wound in the breast, from which the blood was welling,\r\nstaining the dark draperies of the woman\'s clothes, and creeping slowly\r\ndown to the rug on which the body lay. She was dazed, and odd thoughts\r\nflitted through her mind. It was a pity, she thought stupidly, that the\r\nblood should spoil the rug. It was a lovely rug. She wondered what it\r\nwould have cost in Biskra--less, probably, than it would in London.\r\nThen she forgot the rug as her eyes travelled upward to the woman\'s\r\nface. The mouth was open and the streak of blood was drying, but it was\r\nthe eyes, protruding, agonised, that brought Diana abruptly to herself.\r\nShe seemed to wake suddenly to the full realisation of what had\r\nhappened and to her own peril. She felt physically sick for a moment,\r\nbut she fought it down. Very slowly she raised her head, and, meeting\r\nIbraheim Omair\'s eyes fixed on her, she looked full at him across the\r\ndead woman\'s body and laughed! It was that or shriek. The curls were\r\nclinging drenched on her forehead, and she wondered if her clenched\r\nhands would ever unclose. She must make no sign, she must not scream or\r\nfaint, she must keep her nerve until Ahmed came. Oh, dear God, send him\r\nquickly! The laugh wavered hysterically, and she caught her lip between\r\nher teeth. She must do something to distract her attention from that\r\nawful still shape at her feet. Almost unconsciously she grasped the\r\ncigarette case in her pocket and took it out, dragging her eyes from\r\nthe horrible sight on which they were fixed, and chose and lit a\r\ncigarette with slow care, flicking the still-burning match on to the\r\ncarpet between the feet of the negro who stood near her. He had not\r\nmoved since he had failed to stop the woman\'s entrance, and the two\r\nstationed behind the pile of cushions had stood motionless, their eyes\r\nhardly following the tragedy enacted before them. At a nod from the\r\nchief they came now and carried away the body of the woman. One\r\nreturned in a moment, bringing fresh coffee, and then vanished\r\nnoiselessly.\r\n\r\nThen Ibraheim Omair leaned forward with a horrible leer and beckoned to\r\nDiana, patting the cushions beside him. Mastering the loathing that\r\nfilled her she sat down with all the unconcern she could assume. The\r\nproximity of the man nauseated her. He reeked of sweat and grease and\r\nill-kept horses, the pungent stench of the native. Her thoughts went\r\nback to the other Arab, of whose habits she had been forced into such\r\nan intimate knowledge. Remembering all that she had heard of the desert\r\npeople she had been surprised at the fastidious care he took of\r\nhimself, the frequent bathing, the spotless cleanliness of his robes,\r\nthe fresh wholesomeness that clung about him, the faint, clean smell of\r\nshaving-soap mingling with the perfume of the Turkish tobacco that was\r\nalways associated with him.\r\n\r\nThe contrast was hideous.\r\n\r\nShe refused the coffee he offered her with a shake of her head, paying\r\nno attention to his growl of protest, not even understanding it, for he\r\nspoke in Arabic. As she laid down the end of her cigarette with almost\r\nthe feeling of letting go a sheet anchor--for it had at least kept her\r\nlips from trembling--his fat hand closed about her wrist and he jerked\r\nher towards him.\r\n\r\n"How many rifles did the Frenchman bring to that son of darkness?" he\r\nsaid harshly.\r\n\r\nShe turned her head, surprised at the question, and met his bloodshot\r\neyes fixed on hers, half-menacing, half-admiring, and looked away again\r\nhastily. "I do not know."\r\n\r\nHis fingers tightened on her wrist. "How many men had Ahmed Ben Hassan\r\nin the camp in which he kept you?"\r\n\r\n"I do not know."\r\n\r\n"I do not know! I do not know!" he echoed with a sudden savage laugh.\r\n"You will know when I have done with you." He crushed her wrist until\r\nshe winced with pain, and turned her head away further that she might\r\nnot see his face. Question after question relating to the Sheik and his\r\ntribe followed in rapid succession, but to all of them Diana remained\r\nsilent, with averted head and compressed lips. He should not learn\r\nanything from her that might injure the man she loved, though he\r\ntortured her, though her life paid the price of her silence, as it\r\nprobably would. She shivered involuntarily. "Shall I tell you what they\r\nwould do to him?" She could hear the Sheik\'s voice plainly as on the\r\nnight when she had asked him what Gaston\'s fate would be at the hands\r\nof Ibraheim Omair. She could hear the horrible meaning he had put into\r\nthe words, she could see the terrible smile that had accompanied them.\r\nHer breath came faster, but her courage still held. She clung\r\ndesperately to the hope that was sustaining her. Ahmed must come in\r\ntime. She forced down the torturing doubts that whispered that he might\r\nnever find her, that he might come too late, that when he came she\r\nmight be beyond a man\'s desire.\r\n\r\nIbraheim Omair ceased his questioning. "Later you will speak," he said\r\nsignificantly, and drank more coffee. And his words revived the\r\nagonising thoughts she had crushed down. Her vivid imagination conjured\r\nup the same ghastly mental pictures that had appalled her when she had\r\napplied them to Gaston, but now it was herself who was the central\r\nfigure in all the horrors she imagined, until the shuddering she tried\r\nto suppress shook her from head to foot, and she clenched her teeth to\r\nstop them chattering.\r\n\r\nIbraheim Omair kept his hold upon her, and presently, with a horrible\r\nloathing, she felt his hand passing over her arm, her neck, and down\r\nthe soft curves of her slim young body, then with a muttered\r\nejaculation he forced her to face him.\r\n\r\n"What are you listening for? You think that Ahmed Ben Hassan will come?\r\nLittle fool! He has forgotten you already. There are plenty more white\r\nwomen in Algiers and Oran that he can buy with his gold and his devil\r\nface. The loves of Ahmed Ben Hassan are as the stars in number. They\r\ncome and go like the swift wind in the desert, a hot breath--and it\'s\r\nfinished. He will not come, and if he does, he will not find you, for\r\nin an hour we shall be gone."\r\n\r\nDiana writhed in his grasp. The hateful words in the guttural voice,\r\npronounced in vile French, the leering, vicious face with the light of\r\nadmiration growing in the bloodshot eyes, were all a ghastly nightmare.\r\nWith a sudden desperate wrench she freed herself and fled across the\r\ntent--panic-stricken at last. But in her blind rush she tripped, and\r\nwith a swiftness that seemed incompatible with his unwieldiness\r\nIbraheim Omair followed her and caught her in his arms. Struggling he\r\ncarried her to the divan. For a moment he paused, and instinctively\r\nDiana lay still, reserving her strength for the final struggle.\r\n\r\n"One hour, my little gazelle, one hour----" he said hoarsely, and bent\r\nhis face to hers.\r\n\r\nWith a cry Diana flung her head aside and strained away from him,\r\nfighting with the strength of madness. She fought like a boy with a\r\nswift thought of gratitude for Aubrey\'s training, and twisting and\r\nwrithing she managed to slip through his grasp until her feet rested on\r\nthe ground. But his grip on her never relaxed; he dragged her back to\r\nhim, resisting fiercely, ripping the thin shirt from her shoulders,\r\nbaring her white, heaving bosom. Gasping, she struggled, until, little\r\nby little, his arms closed round her again. She braced her hands\r\nagainst his chest, fending him from her till she felt the muscles in\r\nher arms must crack, but the crushing force of his whole weight was\r\nbearing her steadily backwards, and downwards on to the soft cushions\r\nbeside them. His hot breath was on her face, the sickening reek of his\r\nclothes was in her nostrils. She felt her resistance growing weaker,\r\nher heart was labouring, beating with wild bounds that suffocated her,\r\nthe strength was going from her arms, only a moment more and her force\r\nwould be exhausted. Her brain was growing numbed, as it had been when\r\nthe man who held her had murdered the woman before her eyes. If he\r\nwould only kill her now. Death would be easy compared with this. The\r\nfaint hope that still lingered was almost extinguished. Ahmed had not\r\ncome, and in her agony the thought of him was a further torture. The\r\nsneering words of Ibraheim Omair had not shaken her faith. He would\r\ncome, but he would come too late. He would never know now that she\r\nloved him. Oh, God! How she loved him! Ahmed! Ahmed! And with the\r\nsoundless cry the last remnant of her strength went all at once, and\r\nshe fell weakly against the chief. He forced her to her knees, and,\r\nwith his hand twined brutally in her curls, thrust her head back. There\r\nwas a mad light in his eyes and a foam on his lips as he dragged the\r\nknife from his waistbelt and laid the keen edge against her throat. She\r\ndid not flinch, and after a moment he dropped it with a horrible laugh.\r\n\r\n"No, afterwards," he said, and picked her up unresistingly. He flung\r\nher on the cushions and for one awful moment she felt his hands on her.\r\nThen from outside came a sudden uproar and the sharp crack of rifles.\r\nThen in a lull in the firing the Sheik\'s powerful voice: "Diane!\r\nDiane!"\r\n\r\nHis voice and the knowledge of his nearness gave her new strength. She\r\nleaped up in spite of Ibraheim Omair\'s gripping hands. "Ahmed!" she\r\nscreamed once, then the chief\'s hand dashed against her mouth, but,\r\nfrantic, she caught it in her teeth, biting it to the bone, and as he\r\nwrenched it away, shrieked again, "Ahmed! Ahmed!"\r\n\r\nBut it seemed impossible that her voice could be heard above the\r\ndemoniacal noise outside the tent, and she could not call again, for,\r\nwith a snarl of rage, the chief caught her by the throat as he had\r\ncaught the Arab woman. And like the Arab woman her hands tore at his\r\ngripping fingers vainly. Choking, stifling with the agony in her\r\nthroat, her lungs seemed bursting, the blood was beating in her ears\r\nlike the deafening roar of waves, and the room was darkening with the\r\nfilm that was creeping over her eyes. Her hands fell powerless to her\r\nsides and her knees gave way limply. He was holding her upright only by\r\nthe clutch on her throat. The drumming in her ears grew louder, the\r\ntent was fading away into blackness. Dimly, with no kind of emotion,\r\nshe realised that he was squeezing the life out of her and she heard\r\nhis voice coming, as it were, from a great distance: "You will not\r\nlanguish long in Hawiyat without your lover. I will send him quickly to\r\nyou."\r\n\r\nShe was almost unconscious, but she heard the sneering voice break\r\nsuddenly and the deadly pressure on her throat relaxed as the chief\'s\r\nhands rapidly transferred their grip to her aching shoulders, swinging\r\nher away from him and in front of him. To lift her head was agony, and\r\nthe effort brought back the black mist that had lessened with the\r\nslackening of Ibraheim Omair\'s fingers round her neck, but it cleared\r\nagain sufficiently for her to see, through a blurring haze, the outline\r\nof the tall figure that was facing her, standing by the ripped-back\r\ndoorway.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause, a silence that contrasted oddly with the tumult\r\noutside, and Diana wondered numbly why the Sheik did nothing, why he\r\ndid not use the revolver that was clenched in his hand Then slowly she\r\nunderstood that he dared not fire, that the chief was holding her, a\r\nliving shield, before him, sheltering himself behind the only thing\r\nthat would deter Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s unerring shots. Cautiously Ibraheim\r\nOmair moved backward, still holding her before him, hoping to gain the\r\ninner room. But in the shock of his enemy\'s sudden appearance he\r\nmiscalculated the position of the divan and stumbled against it, losing\r\nhis balance for only a moment, but long enough to give the man whose\r\nrevolver covered him the chance he wanted. With the cold ring of steel\r\npressing against his forehead the robber chief\'s hands dropped from\r\nDiana, and she slid weak and trembling on to the rug, clasping her\r\npulsating throat, moaning with the effort that it was to breathe.\r\n\r\nFor a moment the two men looked into each other\'s eyes and the\r\nknowledge of death leaped into Ibraheim Omair\'s. With the fatalism of\r\nhis creed he made no resistance, as, with a slow, terrible smile, the\r\nSheik\'s left hand reached out and fastened on his throat. It would be\r\nquicker to shoot, but as Diana had suffered so should her torturer die.\r\nAll the savagery in his nature rose uppermost. Beside the pitiful,\r\ngasping little figure on the rug at his feet there was the memory of\r\nsix mutilated bodies, his faithful followers, men of his own age who\r\nhad grown to manhood with him, picked men of his personal bodyguard who\r\nhad been intimately connected with him all his life, and who had served\r\nhim with devotion and unwavering obedience. They and others who had\r\nfrom time to time fallen victims to Ibraheim Omair\'s hatred of his more\r\npowerful enemy. The man who was responsible for their deaths was in his\r\npower at last, the man whose existence was a menace and whose life was\r\nan offence, of whose subtleties he had been trained from a boy to\r\nbeware by the elder Ahmed Ben Hassan, who had bequeathed to him the\r\ntribal hatred of the race of whom Ibraheim Omair was head, and whose\r\ndying words had been the wish that his successor might himself\r\nexterminate the hereditary enemy. But far beyond the feelings inspired\r\nby tribal hatred or the remembrance of the vow made five years ago\r\nbeside the old Sheik\'s deathbed, or even the death of his own\r\nfollowers, was the desire to kill, with his bare hands, the man who had\r\ntortured the woman he loved. The knowledge of her peril, that had\r\ndriven him headlong through the night to her aid, the sight of her\r\nhelpless, agonised, in the robber chief\'s hands, had filled him with a\r\nmadness that only the fierce joy of killing would cure. Before he could\r\nlisten to the clamouring of the new love in his heart, before he could\r\ngather up into his arms the beloved little body that he was yearning\r\nfor, he had to destroy the man whose murders were countless and who had\r\nat last fallen into his hands.\r\n\r\nThe smile on his face deepened and his fingers tightened slowly on\r\ntheir hold. But with the strangling clasp of Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s hands\r\nupon him the love of life waked again in Ibraheim Omair and he\r\nstruggled fiercely. Crouched on the floor Diana watched the two big\r\nfigures swaying in mortal combat with wide, fearful eyes, her hands\r\nstill holding her aching throat. Ibraheim Omair wrestled for his life,\r\nconscious of his own strength, but conscious also of the greater\r\nstrength that was opposed to him. The Sheik let go the hold upon his\r\nthroat and with both arms locked about him manoeuvred to get the\r\nposition he required, back to the divan. Then, with a wrestler\'s trick,\r\nhe swept Ibraheim\'s feet from under him and sent his huge body\r\nsprawling on to the cushions, his knee on his enemy\'s chest, his hands\r\non his throat. With all his weight crushing into the chief\'s breast,\r\nwith the terrible smile always on his lips, he choked him slowly to\r\ndeath, till the dying man\'s body arched and writhed in his last agony,\r\ntill the blood burst from his nose and mouth, pouring over the hands\r\nthat held him like a vice.\r\n\r\nDiana\'s eyes never left the Sheik\'s face, she felt the old paralysing\r\nfear of him rushing over her, irresistibly drowning for the moment even\r\nthe love she had for him. She had seen him in cruel, even savage moods,\r\nbut nothing that had ever approached the look of horrible pleasure that\r\nwas on his face now. It was a revelation of the real man with the thin\r\nlayer of civilisation stripped from him, leaving only the primitive\r\nsavage drunk with the lust of blood. And she was afraid, with a\r\nshuddering horror, of the merciless, crimson-stained hands that would\r\ntouch her, of the smiling, cruel mouth that would be pressed on hers,\r\nand of the murderous light shining in his fierce eyes. But for the\r\ndying wretch expiating his crimes so hideously she felt no pity, he was\r\nbeyond all sympathy. She had seen him murder wantonly, and she knew\r\nwhat her own fate would have been if Ahmed Ben Hassan had not come. And\r\nthe retribution was swift. The Sheik was being more merciful to him\r\nthan the robber chief had been to many, a few moments of agony instead\r\nof hours of lingering torture.\r\n\r\nThe noise outside the tent was growing louder as the fighting rolled\r\nback in its direction, and once or twice a bullet ripped through the\r\nhangings. One that came closer than the others made Diana turn her head\r\nand she saw what Ahmed Ben Hassan, absorbed in the fulfilment of his\r\nhorrible task, had not even thought of--the three big negroes and a\r\ndozen Arabs who had stolen in silently from the inner room. For once,\r\nin the intoxication of the moment, the Sheik was careless and caught\r\noff his guard. Agony leaped into her eyes. The fear of him was wiped\r\nout in the fear for him. She tried to warn him, but no sound would come\r\nfrom her throbbing throat, and she crawled nearer to him and touched\r\nhim. He dropped the dead chief back into the tumbled cushions and\r\nlooked up swiftly, and at the same moment Ibraheim Omair\'s men made a\r\nrush. Without a word he thrust her behind the divan and turned to meet\r\nthem. Before his revolver they gave way for a moment, but the burly\r\nNubians behind swept the Arabs forward. Three times he fired and one of\r\nthe negroes and two Arabs fell, but the rest hurled themselves on him,\r\nand Diana saw him surrounded. His strength was abnormal, and for some\r\nminutes the struggling mass of men strained and heaved about him. Diana\r\nwas on her feet, swaying giddily, powerless to help him, cold with\r\ndread. Then above the clamour that was raging inside and out she heard\r\nSaint Hubert\'s voice shouting, and with a shriek that seemed to rip her\r\ntortured throat she called to him. The Sheik, too, heard, and with a\r\ndesperate effort for a moment won clear, but one of the Nubians was\r\nbehind him, and, as Saint Hubert and a crowd of the Sheik\'s own men\r\npoured in through the opening, he brought down a heavy club with\r\ncrashing force on Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s head, and as he fell another drove\r\na broad knife deep into his back. For a few minutes more the tramping\r\nfeet surged backward and forward over the Sheik\'s prostrate body. Diana\r\ntried to get to him, faint and stumbling, flung here and there by the\r\nfighting, struggling men, until a strong hand caught her and drew her\r\naside.
