She strained against the detaining arm, but it was one of\r\nAhmed\'s men, and she gave in as a growing faintness came over her.\r\nMistily she saw Saint Hubert clear a way to his friend\'s side, and then\r\nshe fainted, but only for a few moments. Saint Hubert was still on his\r\nknees beside the Sheik when she opened her eyes, and the tent was quite\r\nquiet, filled with tribesmen waiting in stoical silence. The camp of\r\nIbraheim Omair had been wiped out, but Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s men looked\r\nonly at the unconscious figure of their leader.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert glanced up hastily as Diana came to his side. "You are all\r\nright?" he asked anxiously, but she did not answer. What did it matter\r\nabout her?\r\n\r\n"Is he going to die?" she said huskily, for speaking still hurt\r\nhorribly.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know--but we must get away from here. I need more appliances\r\nthan I have with me, and we are too few to stay and risk a possible\r\nattack if there are others of Ibraheim Omair\'s men in the\r\nneighbourhood."\r\n\r\nDiana looked down on the wounded man fearfully. "But the ride--the\r\njolting," she gasped.\r\n\r\n"It has got to be risked," replied Saint Hubert abruptly.\r\n\r\nOf the long, terrible journey back to Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s camp Diana\r\nnever remembered very much. It was an agony of dread and apprehension,\r\nof momentary waiting for some word or exclamation from the powerful\r\nArab who was holding him, or from Saint Hubert, who was riding beside\r\nhim, that would mean his death, and of momentary respites from fear and\r\nfaint glimmerings of hope as the minutes dragged past and the word she\r\nwas dreading did not come. Once a sudden halt seemed to stop her heart\r\nbeating, but it was only to give a moment\'s rest to the Arab whose\r\nstrength was taxed to the uttermost with the Sheik\'s inert weight, but\r\nwho refused to surrender his privilege to any other. Moments of\r\nsemi-unconsciousness, when she swayed against the arm of the watchful\r\ntribesman riding beside her, and his muttered ejaculation of "Allah!\r\nAllah!" sent a whispered supplication to her own lips to the God they\r\nboth worshipped so differently. He must not die. God would not be so\r\ncruel.\r\n\r\nFrom time to time Saint Hubert spoke to her, and the quiet courage of\r\nhis voice steadied her breaking nerves. As they passed the scene of the\r\nambuscade he told her of Gaston. It was there that the first band of\r\nwaiting men met them, warned already of their coming by a couple of\r\nArabs whom the Vicomte had sent on in advance with the news.\r\n\r\nThe dawn was breaking when they reached the camp. Diana had a glimpse\r\nof rows of unusually silent men grouped beside the tent, but all her\r\nmind was concentrated on the long, limp figure that was being carefully\r\nlifted down from the sweating horse. They carried him into the tent and\r\nlaid him on the divan, beside which Henri had already put out all the\r\nimplements that his master would need.\r\n\r\nWhile Saint Hubert, with difficulty, cleared the tent of the Sheik\'s\r\nmen Diana stood beside the divan and looked at him. He was soaked in\r\nblood that had burst through the temporary bandages, and his whole body\r\nbore evidence of the terrible struggle that had gone before the blow\r\nthat had felled him. One blood-covered hand hung down almost touching\r\nthe rug. Diana lifted it in her own, and the touch of the nerveless\r\nfingers sent a sob into her throat. She caught her lip between her\r\nteeth to stop it trembling as she laid his hand down on the cushions.\r\nSaint Hubert came to her, rolling up his shirt-sleeves significantly.\r\n\r\n"Diane, you have been through enough," he said gently. "Go and rest\r\nwhile I do what I can for Ahmed. I will come and tell you as soon as I\r\nam finished."\r\n\r\nShe looked up fiercely. "It\'s no good telling me to go away, because I\r\nwon\'t. I must help you. I can help you. I shall go mad if you don\'t let\r\nme do something. See! My hands are quite steady." She held them out as\r\nshe spoke, and Saint Hubert gave in without opposition.\r\n\r\nThe weakness that had sent her trembling into his arms the day before\r\nhad been the fear of danger to the man she loved, but in the face of\r\nactual need the courage that was so much a part of her nature did not\r\nfail her. He made no more remonstrances, but set about his work\r\nquickly. And all through the horrible time that followed she did not\r\nfalter. Her face was deadly pale, and dark lines showed below her eyes,\r\nbut her hands did not shake, and her voice was low and even. She\r\nsuffered horribly. The terrible wound that the Nubian\'s knife had made\r\nwas like a wound in her own heart. She winced as if the hurt had been\r\nher own when Saint Hubert\'s gentle, dexterous fingers touched the\r\nSheik\'s bruised head. And when it was over and Raoul had turned aside\r\nto wash his hands, she slipped on to her knees beside him. Would he\r\nlive? The courage that had kept her up so far had not extended to\r\nasking Saint Hubert again, and a few muttered words from Henri, to\r\nwhich the Vicomte had responded with only a shrug, had killed the words\r\nthat were hovering on her lips. She looked at him with anguished eyes.\r\n\r\nOnly a few hours before he had come to her in all the magnificence of\r\nhis strength. She looked at the long limbs lying now so still, so\r\nterribly, suggestively still, and her lips trembled again, but her\r\npain-filled eyes were dry. She could not cry, only her throat ached and\r\nthrobbed perpetually. She leaned over him whispering his name, and a\r\nsudden hunger came to her to touch him, to convince herself that he was\r\nnot dead. She glanced back over her shoulder at Saint Hubert, but he\r\nhad gone to the open doorway to speak to Yusef, and was standing out\r\nunder the awning. She bent lower over the unconscious man; his lips\r\nwere parted slightly, and the usual sternness of his mouth was relaxed.\r\n\r\n"Ahmed, oh, my dear!" she whispered unsteadily, and kissed him with\r\nlips that quivered against the stillness of his. Then for a moment she\r\ndropped her bright head beside the bandaged one on the pillow, but when\r\nthe Vicomte came back she was kneeling where he had left her, her hands\r\nclasped over one of the Sheik\'s and her face hidden against the\r\ncushions.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert put his hand on her shoulder. "Diane, you are torturing\r\nyourself unnecessarily. We cannot know for some time how it will go\r\nwith him. Try and get some sleep for a few hours. You can do no good by\r\nstaying here. Henri and I will watch. I will call you if there is any\r\nchange, my word of honour."\r\n\r\nShe shook her head without looking up. "I can\'t go. I couldn\'t sleep."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert did not press it. "Very well," he said quietly, "but if\r\nyou are going to stay you must take off your riding-boots and put on\r\nsomething more comfortable than those clothes."\r\n\r\nShe realised the sense of what he was saying, and obeyed him without a\r\nword. She even had to admit to herself a certain sensation of relief\r\nafter she had bathed her aching head and throat, and substituted a\r\nthin, silk wrap for the torn, stained riding-suit.\r\n\r\nHenri was pouring out coffee when she came back, and Saint Hubert\r\nturned to her with a cup in his outstretched hand. "Please take it. It\r\nwill do you good," he said, with a little smile that was not reflected\r\nin his anxious eyes.\r\n\r\nShe took it unheeding, and, swallowing it hastily, went to the side of\r\nthe divan again. She slid down on to the rug where she had knelt\r\nbefore. The Sheik was lying as she had left him. For a few moments she\r\nlooked at him, then drowsily her eyes closed and her head fell forward\r\non the cushions, and with a half-sad smile of satisfaction Saint Hubert\r\ngathered her up into his arms.\r\n\r\nHe carried her into the bedroom, hesitating beside the couch before he\r\nput her down. Surely one moment out of a lifetime might be granted to\r\nhim. He would never have the torturing happiness of holding her in his\r\narms again, would never again clasp her against the heart that was\r\ncrying out for her with the same mad passion that had swept over him\r\nyesterday. He looked down longingly on the pale face lying against his\r\narm, and his features contracted at the sight of the cruel marks\r\nmarring the whiteness of her delicate throat. The love that all his\r\nlife he had longed for, that he had sought vainly through many\r\ncountries, had come to him at last, and it had come too late. The\r\nhelpless loveliness lying in his arms was not for him. It was Ahmed\r\nwhom she loved, Ahmed who had waked to such a tardy recognition of the\r\npriceless gift that she had given him, Ahmed whom he must wrest from\r\nthe grim spectre that was hovering near him lest the light that shone\r\nin her violet eyes should go out in the blackness of despair. And yet\r\nas he looked at her with eyes filled with hopeless misery a demon of\r\nsuggestion whispered within him, tempting him. He knew his friend as no\r\none else did. What chance of happiness had any woman with a man like\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan, at the mercy of his savage nature and passionate\r\nchangeable moods? What reason to suppose that the love that had flamed\r\nup so suddenly at the thought that he had lost her would survive the\r\nknowledge of repossession? To him, all his life, a thing desired had\r\nupon possession become valueless. With the fulfilment of acquisition\r\nhad come always disinterest. The pleasure of pursuit faded with\r\nownership. Would this hapless girl who had poured out such a wealth of\r\nlove at the feet of the man who had treated her brutally fare any\r\nbetter at his hands? Her chance was slight, if any. Ahmed in the full\r\npower of his strength again would be the man he had always been,\r\nimplacable, cruel, merciless. Saint Hubert\'s own longing, his\r\npassionate, Gallic temperament, were driving him as they had driven him\r\nthe day before. The longing to save her from misery was acute, that,\r\nand his own love, prompted by the urging of the desire within him. Then\r\nhe trembled, and a great fear of himself came over him. Ahmed was his\r\nfriend. Who was he that he should judge him? He could at least be\r\nhonest with himself, he could own the truth. He coveted what was not\r\nhis, and masked his envy with a hypocrisy that now appeared\r\ncontemptible. The clasp of his arms around her seemed suddenly a\r\nprofanation, and he laid her down very gently on the low couch, drawing\r\nthe thin coverlet over her, and went back slowly to the other room.\r\n\r\nHe sent Henri away and sat down beside the divan to watch with a\r\nfeeling of weariness that was not bodily. The great tent was very\r\nstill, a pregnant silence seemed to hang in the air, a brooding hush\r\nthat strained Saint Hubert\'s already overstrained nerves. He had need\r\nof all his calm, and he gripped himself resolutely. For a time Ahmed\r\nBen Hassan lay motionless, and then, as the day crept on and the early\r\nrays of the warm sun filled the tent, he moved uneasily, and began to\r\nmutter feverishly in confused Arabic and French. At first the words\r\nthat came were almost unintelligible, pouring out with rapid\r\nindistinctness, then by degrees his voice slowed, and hesitating,\r\ninterrupted sentences came clearly from his lips. And beside him, with\r\nhis face buried in his hands, Raoul de Saint Hubert thanked God\r\nfervently that he had saved Diana the added torture of listening to the\r\nrevelations of the past four months.\r\n\r\nThe first words were in Arabic, then the slow, soft voice lapsed into\r\nFrench, pure as the Vicomte\'s own.\r\n\r\n"Two hours south of the oasis with the three broken palm trees by the\r\nwell.... Lie still, you little fool, it is useless to struggle. You\r\ncannot get away, I shall not let you go.... Why have I brought you\r\nhere? You ask me why? _Mon Dieu!_ Are you not woman enough to\r\nknow? No! I will not spare you. Give me what I want willingly and I\r\nwill be kind to you, but fight me, and by Allah! you shall pay the\r\ncost!... I know you hate me, you have told me so already. Shall I make\r\nyou love me?... Still disobedient? When will you learn that I am\r\nmaster?... I have not tired of you yet, you lovely little wild\r\nthing, _garcon manque...._ You say she is cowed; I say she is\r\ncontent--content to give me everything I ask of her.... For four months\r\nshe has fought me. Why does it give me no pleasure to have broken her\r\nat last? Why do I want her still? She is English and I have made her\r\npay for my hatred of her cursed race. I have tortured her to keep my\r\nvow, and still I want her.... Diane, Diane, how beautiful you are!...\r\nWhat devil makes me hate Raoul after twenty years? Last night she only\r\nspoke to him, and when he went I cursed her till I saw the terror in\r\nher eyes. She fears me. Why should I care if she loves him.... I knew\r\nshe was not asleep when I went to her. I felt her quivering beside\r\nme.... I wanted to kill Raoul when he would not come with me, but for\r\nthat I would have gone back to her.... Allah! how long the day has\r\nbeen.... Has it been long to her? Will she smile or tremble when I\r\ncome?... Where is Diane?... Diane, Diane, how could I know how much you\r\nmeant to me? How could I know that I should love you?... Diane, Diane,\r\nmy sunshine. The tent is cold and dark without you.... Ibraheim Omair!\r\nThat devil and Diane! Oh, Allah! Grant me time to get to her.... How\r\nthe jackals are howling.... See, Raoul, there are the tents.... Diane,\r\nwhere are you?... Grand Dieu! He has been torturing her!... You knew\r\nthat I would come, _ma bien aimee_, only a few moments while I\r\nkill him, then I can hold you in my arms. _Dieu!_ If you knew how\r\nmuch I loved you.... Diane, Diane, it is all black. I cannot see you,\r\nDiane, Diane...."\r\n\r\nAnd hour after hour with weary hopelessness the tired voice went\r\non--"Diane, Diane...."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was evening when Diana opened drowsy and heavy eyes, a bitter taste\r\nin her mouth from the effects of the drug that Saint Hubert had given\r\nher. Everything had been laid out in readiness for her waking with the\r\nlittle touches that were characteristic of Zilah\'s handiwork, but the\r\nArab girl herself was not visible. The lamp was lighted, and Diana\r\nturned her head languidly, still half confused, to look at the clock\r\nbeside her. The tiny chime sounded seven times, and with a rush of\r\nrecollection she leaped up. More than twelve hours since she had knelt\r\nbeside him after drinking the coffee that Raoul had given her. She\r\nguessed what he had done and tried to be grateful, but the thought of\r\nwhat might have happened during the twelve hours she had lain like a\r\nlog was horrible. She dressed with feverish haste and went into the\r\nouter room. It was filled with Arabs, many of whom she did not\r\nrecognise, and she knew that they must belong to the reinforcements\r\nthat Ahmed Ben Hassan had sent for. Two, who seemed from their\r\nappearance to be petty chiefs, were talking in low tones to Saint\r\nHubert, who looked worn and tired. The rest were grouped silently about\r\nthe divan, looking at the still-unconscious Sheik. The restlessness and\r\ndelirium of the morning had passed and been succeeded by a death-like\r\nstupor. Nearest to him stood Yusef, his usual swaggering self-assurance\r\nchanged into an attitude of deepest dejection, and his eyes, that were\r\nfixed on Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s face, were like those of a whipped dog.\r\n\r\nGradually the tent emptied until only Yusef was left, and at last,\r\nreluctantly, he too went, stopping at the entrance to speak to Saint\r\nHubert, who had just taken leave of the two headmen.\r\n\r\nThe Vicomte came back, bringing a chair for Diana, and put her into it\r\nwith gentle masterfulness. "Sit down," he said almost gruffly. "You\r\nlook like a ghost."\r\n\r\nShe looked up at him reproachfully. "You drugged that coffee, Raoul. If\r\nhe had died to-day while I was asleep I don\'t think I could ever have\r\nforgiven you."\r\n\r\n"My dear child," he said gravely, "you don\'t know how near you were to\r\ncollapse. If I had not made you sleep I should have had three patients\r\non my hands instead of two."\r\n\r\n"I am very ungrateful," she murmured, with a tremulous little smile.\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert brought a chair for himself and dropped into it wearily.\r\nHe felt very tired, the strain of the past twenty-four hours had been\r\ntremendous. He had a very real fear that was fast growing into a\r\nconviction that his skill was going to prove unequal to save his\r\nfriend\'s life, and beside that anxiety and his physical fatigue he had\r\nfought a bitter fight with himself all day, tearing out of his heart\r\nthe envy and jealousy that filled it, and locking away his love as a\r\nsecret treasure to be hidden for always. His devotion to Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan had survived the greatest test that could be imposed upon it,\r\nand had emerged from the trial strengthened and refined, with every\r\ntrace of self obliterated. It had been the hardest struggle of his\r\nlife, but it was over now, and all the bitterness had passed, leaving\r\nonly a passionate desire for Diana\'s happiness that outweighed every\r\nother thought. One spark of comfort remained. He would not be quite\r\nuseless. His help and sympathy would be necessary to her, and even for\r\nthat he was grateful.\r\n\r\nHe looked across the divan at her, and the change that the last few\r\nhours had made in her struck him painfully. The alert, vigorous\r\nboyishness that had been so characteristic was gone. Her slim figure\r\ndrooping listlessly in the big chair, her white face with the new marks\r\nof suffering on it, and her wide eyes burning with dumb misery, were\r\nall purely womanly. And yet though he resented the change he wished it\r\ncould have gone further. The restraint she was putting on herself was\r\nunnatural. She asked no questions and she shed no tears. He could have\r\nborne them both easier than the silent anguish of her face. He feared\r\nthe results of the emotion she was repressing so rigidly.\r\n\r\nThere was a long silence.\r\n\r\nHenri came in once and Diana roused herself to ask for Gaston, and then\r\nrelapsed into silent watchfulness again. She sighed once, a long\r\nquivering sigh that nearly broke Saint Hubert\'s heart. He rose and bent\r\nover the Sheik with his fingers on his wrist, and as he laid the\r\nnerveless hand down again she leaned nearer and covered it with her\r\nown.\r\n\r\n"His hand is so big for an Arab\'s," she said softly, like a thought\r\nspoken aloud unconsciously.\r\n\r\n"He is not an Arab," replied Saint Hubert with sudden, impatient\r\nvehemence. "He is English."\r\n\r\nDiana looked up at him swiftly with utter bewilderment in her startled\r\neyes. "I don\'t understand," she faltered. "He hates the English."\r\n\r\n"_Quand-meme_, he is the son of one of your English peers. His\r\nmother was a Spanish lady; many of the old noble Spanish families have\r\nMoorish blood in their veins, the characteristics crop up even after\r\ncenturies. It is so with Ahmed, and his life in the desert has\r\naccentuated it. Has he never told you anything about himself?"\r\n\r\nShe shook her head. "Sometimes I have wondered----" she said\r\nreflectively. "He seemed different from the others, and there has been\r\nso much that I could never understand. But then again there were times\r\nwhen he seemed pure Arab," she added in a lower voice and with an\r\ninvoluntary shiver.\r\n\r\n"You ought to know," said Saint Hubert. "Yes!" he went on firmly, as\r\nshe tried to interrupt him. "It is due to you. It will explain so many\r\nthings. I will take the responsibility. His father is the Earl of\r\nGlencaryll."\r\n\r\n"But I know him," said Diana wonderingly. "He was a friend of my\r\nfather. I saw him only a few months ago when Aubrey and I passed\r\nthrough Paris. He is such a magnificent-looking old man, so fierce and\r\nsad. Oh, now I know why that awful frown of Ahmed\'s has always seemed\r\nso familiar. Lord Glencaryll frowns like that. It is the famous Caryll\r\nscowl. But I still don\'t understand." She looked from Saint Hubert to\r\nthe unconscious man on the divan and back to Saint Hubert with a new\r\ntrouble growing in her eyes.\r\n\r\n"I had better tell you the whole story," said Raoul, dropping back into\r\nhis chair.\r\n\r\n"Thirty-six years ago my father, who was as great a wanderer as I am,\r\nwas staying here in the desert with his friend the Sheik Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan. A chance acquaintance some years before over the purchase of\r\nsome horses had ripened into a very intimate friendship that was\r\nunusual between a Frenchman and an Arab. The Sheik was a wonderful man,\r\nvery enlightened, with strong European tendencies. As a matter of pure\r\nfact he was not too much in sympathy with the French form of\r\nadministration as carried on in Algeria, but he was not affected\r\nsufficiently by it to make any real difficulty. The territory that he\r\nregarded as his own lay too much to the south, and he kept his large\r\nand scattered tribe in too good order for any interference to be\r\npossible. He was unmarried, and the women of his own race seemed to\r\nhave no attraction for him. He was wrapped up in his tribe and his\r\nhorses. My father had come for a stay of some months. My mother had\r\nrecently died and he wanted to get away from everything that reminded\r\nhim of her. One evening, shortly after his arrival at the camp, a party\r\nof the Sheik\'s men who had been absent for some days in the north on\r\nthe chief\'s affairs arrived, bringing with them a woman whom they had\r\nfound wandering in the desert. How she had got there, or from what\r\ndirection she had come, they did not know. They were nearer\r\ncivilisation than Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s camp at the time, but with true\r\nnative tendency to avoid responsibility they thought that the disposal\r\nof her was a matter more for their Sheik than themselves. She could\r\ngive no account of herself, as, owing to the effects of the sun or\r\nother causes, she was temporarily out of her mind. Arabs are very\r\ngentle with any one who is mad--\'Allah has touched them!\' She was taken\r\nto the tent of one of the headmen, whose wife looked after her. For\r\nsome days it was doubtful whether she would recover, and her condition\r\nwas aggravated by the fact that she was shortly to become a mother. She\r\ndid regain her senses after a time, however, but nothing could make her\r\nsay anything about herself, and questions reduced her to terrible fits\r\nof hysterical crying which were prejudicial in her state of health. She\r\nseemed calmest when she was left quite alone, but even then she started\r\nat the slightest sound, and the headman\'s wife reported that she would\r\nlie for hours on her bed crying quietly to herself. She was quite\r\nyoung--seemingly not more than nineteen or twenty. From her accents my\r\nfather decided that she was Spanish, but she would admit nothing, not\r\neven her nationality. In due course of time the child was born, a boy."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert paused a moment and nodded towards the Sheik. "Even after\r\nthe child\'s birth she refused to give any account of herself. In that\r\nshe was as firm as a rock; in everything else she was the frailest,\r\ngentlest little creature imaginable. She was very small and slender,\r\nwith quantities of soft dark hair and beautiful great dark eyes that\r\nlooked like a frightened fawn\'s. I have heard my father describe her\r\nmany times, and I have seen the water-colour sketch he made of her--he\r\nwas quite an amateur. Ahmed has it locked away somewhere. She nearly\r\ndied when the baby was born, and she never recovered her strength. She\r\nmade no complaint and never spoke of herself, and seemed quite content\r\nas long as the child was with her. She was a child herself in a great\r\nnumber of ways. It never seemed to occur to her that there was anything\r\nodd in her continued residence in the Sheik\'s camp. She had a tent and\r\nservants of her own, and the headman\'s wife was devoted to her. So were\r\nthe rest of the camp for that matter. There was an element of the\r\nmysterious in her advent that had taken hold of the superstitious\r\nArabs, and the baby was looked upon as something more than human and\r\nwas adored by all the tribe. The Sheik himself, who had never looked\r\ntwice at a woman before in his life, became passionately attached to\r\nher. My father says that he has never seen a man so madly in love as\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan was with the strange white girl who had come so oddly\r\ninto his life. He repeatedly implored her to marry him, and even my\r\nfather, who has a horror of mixed marriages, was impelled to admit that\r\nany woman might have been happy with Ahmed Ben Hassan. She would not\r\nconsent, though she would give no reason for her refusal, and the\r\nmystery that surrounded her remained as insolvable during the two years\r\nthat she lived after the baby\'s birth as it had been on the day of her\r\narrival. And her refusal made no difference with the Sheik. His\r\ndevotion was wonderful. When she died my father was again visiting the\r\ncamp. She knew that she was dying, and a few days before the end she\r\ntold them her pitiful little history. She was the only daughter of one\r\nof the oldest noble houses in Spain, as poor as they were noble, and\r\nshe had been married when she was seventeen to Lord Glencaryll, who had\r\nseen her with her parents in Nice. She had been married without any\r\nregard to her own wishes, and though she grew to love her husband she\r\nwas always afraid of him. He had a terrible temper that was very easily\r\nroused, and, in those days, he also periodically drank a great deal\r\nmore than was good for him, and when under the influence of drink\r\nbehaved more like a devil than a man. She was very young and\r\n_gauche_, failing often to do what was required of her from mere\r\nnervousness. He was exigent and made no allowance for her youth and\r\ninexperience, and her life was one long torture. And yet in spite of it\r\nall she loved him. Even in speaking of it she insisted that the fault\r\nwas hers, that the trouble was due to her stupidity, glossing over his\r\nbrutality; in fact, it was not from her, but from inquiries that he\r\nmade after her death, that my father learned most of what her life had\r\nbeen. It seems that Lord Glencaryll had taken her to Algiers and had\r\nwished to make a trip into the desert. He had been drinking heavily,\r\nand she did not dare to upset his plans by refusing to go with him or\r\neven by telling him how soon her child was going to be born. So she\r\nwent with him, and one night something happened--what she would not\r\nsay, but my father says he has never seen such a look of terror on any\r\nwoman\'s face as she hurried over that part of her story. Whatever it\r\nwas she waited until the camp was asleep and then slipped out into the\r\ndesert, mad with fear, with no thought beyond a blind instinct of\r\nflight that drove her panic-stricken to face any danger rather than\r\nremain and undergo the misery she was flying from. She remembered\r\nhurrying onward, terrified by every sound and every shadow, frightened\r\neven by the blazing stars that seemed to be watching her and pointing\r\nout the way she had taken, until her mind was numb from utter weariness\r\nand she remembered nothing more until she awoke in the headman\'s tent.\r\nShe had been afraid to say who she was lest she should be sent back to\r\nher husband. And with the birth of the child she became more than ever\r\ndetermined to preserve her secret. The boy should be spared the\r\nsuffering she had herself endured, he should not be allowed to fall\r\ninto the hands of his father to be at his mercy when the periodical\r\ndrinking fits made him a very fiend of cruelty. She made my father and\r\nthe Sheik swear that not until the boy grew to manhood should Lord\r\nGlencaryll be told of his existence. She wrote a letter for her husband\r\nwhich she gave into my father\'s keeping, together with her wedding\r\nring, which had an inscription inside of it, and a miniature of\r\nGlencaryll which she had worn always hidden away from sight. She was\r\nvery contrite with the Sheik, begging his forgiveness for the sorrow\r\nshe had caused him and for keeping from his knowledge the fact that she\r\nwas not free. She loved her husband loyally to the end, but the last\r\nfew days that she lived the Sheik\'s devotion seemed to wake an\r\nanswering tenderness in her heart. She was happiest when he was with\r\nher, and she died in his arms with his kisses on her lips. She left her\r\nboy in his keeping, and Ahmed Ben Hassan adopted him formally and made\r\nhim his heir, giving him his own name--the hereditary name that the\r\nSheik of the tribe has borne for generations. His word was law amongst\r\nhis people, and there was no thought of any opposition to his wishes;\r\nfurther, the child was considered lucky, and his choice of successor\r\nwas received with unanimous delight. All the passionate love that the\r\nSheik had for the mother was transferred to the son. He idolised him,\r\nand the boy grew up believing that Ahmed Ben Hassan was his own father.\r\nWith the traits he had inherited from his mother\'s people and with his\r\ndesert upbringing he looked, as he does now, pure Arab. When he was\r\nfifteen my father induced the Sheik to send him to Paris to be\r\neducated. With his own European tendencies the Sheik had wished it\r\nalso, but he could not bring himself to part with the boy before, and\r\nit was a tremendous wrench to let him go when he did. It was then that\r\nI first saw him. I was eighteen at the time, and had just begun my\r\nmilitary training, but as my regiment was stationed in Paris I was able\r\nto be at home a good deal. He was such a handsome, high-spirited lad.\r\nMen mature very young in the desert and in many ways he was a great\r\ndeal older than I was, in spite of my three years\' seniority. But, of\r\ncourse, in other ways he was a perfect child. He had a fiendish temper\r\nand resented any check on his natural lawless inclinations. He loathed\r\nthe restrictions that had to be put upon him and he hated the restraint\r\nof town life. He had been accustomed to having his own way in nearly\r\neverything, and to the constant adulation of the tribesmen, and he was\r\nnot prepared to give to anybody else the obedience that he gave\r\nwillingly to the Sheik. There were some very stormy times, and I never\r\nadmired my father in anything so much as his handling of that young\r\nsavage. His escapades were nerve-racking and his _beaux yeux_ led\r\nhim into endless scrapes. The only threat that reduced him to order was\r\nthat of sending him home to the Sheik in disgrace. He would promise\r\namendment and take himself off to the Bois to work off his superfluous\r\nenergy on my father\'s horses--until he broke out again. But in spite of\r\nhis temper and his _diableries_ he was very lovable and everybody\r\nliked him.\r\n\r\n"After a year with us in Paris my father, always mindful of his real\r\nnationality, sent him for two years to a tutor in England, where I had\r\nmyself been. The tutor was an exceptional man, used to dealing with\r\nexceptional boys, and Ahmed did very well with him. I don\'t mean that\r\nhe did much work--that he evaded skilfully and spent most of his time\r\nhunting and shooting. The only thing that he studied at all seriously\r\nwas veterinary surgery, which he knew would be useful to him with his\r\nown horses, and in which his tutor was level-headed enough to encourage\r\nhim. Then at the end of two years he came back to us for another year.\r\nHe had gone to the desert every summer for his holidays, and on each\r\noccasion the Sheik let him return with greater reluctance. He was\r\nalways afraid that the call of civilisation would be too much for his\r\nadopted son, especially as he grew older, but although Ahmed had\r\nchanged very much from the wild desert lad who had first come to us,\r\nand had developed into a polished man of the world, speaking French and\r\nEnglish as fluently as Arabic, with plenty of means to amuse himself in\r\nany way that he wished--for the Sheik was very rich and kept him\r\nlavishly supplied with money--and though in that last year he was with\r\nus he was courted and feted in a way that would have turned most\r\npeople\'s heads, he was always secretly longing for the time when\r\nhe would go back to the desert. It was the desert, not civilisation\r\nthat called loudest to him. He loved the life and he adored the man\r\nwhom he thought was his father. To be the son and heir of Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan seemed to him to be the highest pinnacle that any man\'s ambition\r\ncould reach. He was perfectly indifferent to the flattery and attention\r\nthat his money and his good looks brought him. My father entertained\r\nvery largely and Ahmed became the fashion--\'_Le bel Arabe_\' he\r\nwas called, and he enjoyed a _succes fou_ which bored him to\r\nextinction--and at the end of the year, having written to the Sheik for\r\npermission to go home, he shook the dust of Paris off his feet and went\r\nback to the desert. I went with him. It was my first visit and the\r\nfirst time that I had experienced Ahmed _en prince_. I had never\r\nseen him in anything but European clothes, and I got quite a shock when\r\nI came up on deck the morning that we arrived at Oran and found an Arab\r\nof the Arabs waiting for me. The robes and a complete change of\r\ncarriage and expression that seemed to go with them altered him\r\ncuriously and I hardly recognised him. Some of his men were waiting for\r\nhim on the quay and their excitement was extraordinary. I realised from\r\nthe deference and attention that the French officials paid to Ahmed the\r\nposition that the old Sheik had made for himself and the high esteem in\r\nwhich he was held. We spent the rest of the day in arranging for the\r\nconsiderable baggage that he had brought with him to be forwarded by\r\nthe camel caravan that had been sent for the purpose, and also in\r\nbusiness for the Sheik in Oran. We spent the night in a villa on the\r\noutskirts of the town belonging to an old Arab who entertained us\r\nlavishly, and who spent the evening congratulating Ahmed heartily on\r\nhaving escaped from the clutches of the odious French, by no means\r\nabashed when Ahmed pointed out that there was an odious Frenchman\r\npresent, for he dismissed me with a gesture that conveyed that my\r\nnationality was my misfortune and not my fault, and in impressing on\r\nhim the necessity of immediately acquiring a wife or two and settling\r\ndown for the good of the tribe--all this in the intervals of drinking\r\ncoffee, listening to the most monotonous native music and watching\r\nbarbaric dances. There was one particularly well-made dancing girl that\r\nthe old man tried to induce Ahmed to buy, and he made a show of\r\nbargaining for her--not from any real interest he took in her, but\r\nmerely to see the effect that it would have on me. But I refused to be\r\ndrawn, and as my head was reeling with the atmosphere I escaped to bed\r\nand left him still bargaining. We started early next morning, and were\r\njoined a few miles out of the town by a big detachment of followers.\r\nThe excitement of the day before was repeated on a very much larger\r\nscale. It was a novel experience for me, and I can hardly describe my\r\nfeelings in the midst of that yelling horde of men, galloping wildly\r\nround us and firing their rifles until it seemed hardly possible that\r\nsome accident would not happen. It was Ahmed\'s attitude that impressed\r\nme most. He took it all quietly as his due, and when he had had enough\r\nof it stopped it with a peremptory authority that was instantly obeyed,\r\nand apologised for the exuberant behaviour of his children. It was a\r\nnew Ahmed to me; the boy I had known for four years seemed suddenly\r\ntransformed into a man who made me feel very young. In France I had\r\nnaturally always rather played elder brother, but here Ahmed was on his\r\nown ground and the roles seemed likely to be reversed. The arrival at\r\nthe Sheik\'s camp was everything that the most lavish scenic producer\r\ncould have wished. Though I had heard of it both from my father and\r\nAhmed I was not quite prepared for the splendour with which the Sheik\r\nsurrounded himself. With Eastern luxury was mingled many European\r\nadjuncts that added much to the comfort of camp life. The meeting\r\nbetween the Sheik and Ahmed was most touching. I had a very happy time\r\nand left with regret. The charm of the desert took hold of me then and\r\nhas never left me since. But I had to return to my medical studies. I\r\nleft Ahmed absorbed in his life and happier than I had ever seen him in\r\nParis. He was nineteen then, and when he was twenty-one my father had\r\nthe unpleasant task of carrying out Lady Glencaryll\'s dying wishes. He\r\nwrote to Lord Glencaryll asking him to come to Paris on business\r\nconnected with his late wife, and, during the course of a very painful\r\ninterview, put the whole facts before him. With the letter that the\r\npoor girl had written to her husband, with the wedding-ring and the\r\nlocket, together with the sketch that my father had made of her, the\r\nproofs of the genuineness of the whole affair were conclusive.\r\nGlencaryll broke down completely. He admitted that his wife had every\r\njustification for leaving him, he spared himself nothing. He referred\r\nquite frankly to the curse of which he had been the slave and which had\r\nmade him irresponsible for his actions when he was under its influence.\r\nHe had never known himself what had happened that terrible night, but\r\nthe tragedy of his wife\'s disappearance had cured him. He had made\r\nevery effort to find her and it was many years before he gave up all\r\nhope. He mourned her bitterly, and worshipped her memory. It was\r\nimpossible not to pity him, for he had expiated his fault with agony\r\nthat few men can have experienced. The thought that he had a son and\r\nthat son her child almost overwhelmed him. He had ardently desired an\r\nheir, and, thinking himself childless, the fact that his title and his\r\nold name, of which he was very proud, would die with him had been a\r\ngreat grief. His happiness in the knowledge of Ahmed\'s existence was\r\npathetic, he was consumed with impatience for his son\'s arrival.\r\nNothing had been said to Ahmed in case Lord Glencaryll should prove\r\ndifficult to convince and thereby complicate matters, but his ready\r\nacceptance of the affair and his eagerness to see his son made further\r\ndelay unnecessary, and my father sent for Ahmed. The old Sheik let him\r\ngo in ignorance of what was coming. He had always dreaded the time when\r\nhis adopted son would have to be told of his real parentage, fearful of\r\nlosing him, jealous of sharing his affection and resenting anybody\'s\r\nclaim to him over his own. And so, with the only instance he ever gave\r\nof want of moral courage, he sent Ahmed to Paris with no explanation,\r\nand left to my father the task of breaking to him the news. I shall\r\nnever forget that day. It had been arranged that Ahmed should be told\r\nfirst and that afterwards father and son should meet. Ahmed arrived in\r\nthe morning in time for _dejeuner_, and afterwards we went to my\r\nfather\'s study, and there he told him the whole story as gently and as\r\ncarefully as he could. Ahmed was standing by the window. He never said\r\na word the whole time my father was speaking, and when he finished he\r\nstood quite still for a few moments, his face almost grey under the\r\ndeep tan, his eyes fixed passionately on my father\'s--and then his\r\nfiendish temper broke out suddenly. It was a terrible scene. He cursed\r\nhis father in a steady stream of mingled Arabic and French blasphemy\r\nthat made one\'s blood run cold. He cursed all English people\r\nimpartially. He cursed my father because he had dared to send him to\r\nEngland. He cursed me because I had been a party to the affair. The\r\nonly person whom he spared was the Sheik; who after all was as much\r\nimplicated as we were, but he never once mentioned him. He refused to\r\nsee his father, refused to recognise that he was his father, and he\r\nleft the house that afternoon and Paris that night, going straight back\r\nto the desert, taking with him Gaston, who had arranged some time\r\nbefore to enter his service as soon as his time in the cavalry was up.\r\nA letter that Lord Glencaryll wrote to him, addressed to Viscount\r\nCaryll, which is, of course, his courtesy title, begging for at least\r\nan interview, and which he gave to us to forward, was returned\r\nunopened, and scrawled across the envelope: _\'Inconnu._ Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan.\' And since that day his hatred of the English had been a\r\nmonomania, and he has never spoken a word of English. Later on, when we\r\nused to travel together, his obvious avoidance of English people was at\r\ntimes both awkward and embarrassing, and I have often had to go through\r\nthe farce of translating into French or Arabic remarks made to him by\r\nEnglish fellow-travellers, that is, when he condescended to notice the\r\nremarks, which was not often. From the day he learned the truth about\r\nhimself for two years we saw nothing of him. Then the old Sheik asked\r\nus to visit him. We went with some misgivings as to what Ahmed\'s\r\nreception of us would be, but he met us as if nothing had happened. He\r\nignored the whole episode and has never referred to it. It is a closed\r\nincident. The Sheik warned us that Ahmed had told him that any\r\nreference to it would mean the breaking off of all relations with us.\r\nBut Ahmed himself had changed indescribably. All the lovable qualities\r\nthat had made him so popular in Paris were gone, and he had become the\r\ncruel, merciless man he has been ever since. The only love left in him\r\nwas given to his adopted father, whom he worshipped. Later I was\r\nallowed back on the old footing, and he has always been good to Gaston,\r\nbut with those three exceptions he has spared nobody and nothing. He is\r\nmy friend, I love him, and I am not telling you more than you know\r\nalready."\r\n\r\nSaint Hubert broke off and looked anxiously at Diana, but she did not\r\nmove or meet his gaze. She was sitting with her hand still clasped over\r\nthe Sheik\'s and the other one shading her face, and the Vicomte went on\r\nspeaking: "It is so easy to judge, so difficult to understand another\r\nperson\'s temptations. Ahmed\'s position has always been a curious one.\r\nHe has had unique temptations with always the means of gratifying\r\nthem."\r\n\r\nThere was a longer pause, but still Diana did not move or speak.\r\n\r\n"The curse of Ishmael had taken hold of me by then and I wandered\r\ncontinually. Sometimes Ahmed came with me; we have shot big game\r\ntogether in most parts of the globe. A few times he stayed with us in\r\nParis, but never for long; he always wearied to get back to the desert.\r\nFive years ago the old Sheik died; he was an exceptionally strong man,\r\nand should have lived for years but for an accident which crippled him\r\nhopelessly and from which he died a few months afterwards. Ahmed\'s\r\ndevotion during his illness was wonderful. He never left him, and since\r\nhe succeeded to the leadership of the tribe he has lived continuously\r\namongst his people, absorbed in them and his horses, carrying on the\r\ntraditions handed down to him by his predecessor and devoting his life\r\nto the tribe. They are like children, excitable, passionate and\r\nheadstrong, and he has never dared to risk leaving them alone too long,\r\nparticularly with the menace of Ibraheim Omair always in the\r\nbackground. He has never been able to seek relaxation further afield\r\nthan Algiers or Oran----" Saint Hubert stopped abruptly, cursing himself\r\nfor a tactless fool. She could not fail to realise the significance of\r\nthose visits to the gay, vicious little towns. The inference was\r\nobvious. His thoughtless words would only add to her misery. Her\r\nsensitive mind would shrink from the contamination they implied. If\r\nAhmed was going to die, she would be desolate enough without forcing on\r\nher knowledge the unworthiness of the man she loved. He pushed his\r\nchair back impatiently and went to the open doorway. He felt that she\r\nwanted to be alone. She watched him go, then slipped to her knees\r\nbeside the couch.\r\n\r\nShe had realised the meaning of Raoul\'s carelessly uttered words and\r\nthey had hurt her poignantly, but it was no new sorrow. He had told her\r\nhimself months ago, callously, brutally, sparing her nothing,\r\nextenuating nothing. She pressed her cheek against the hand she was\r\nholding. She did not blame him, she could only love him, no matter what\r\nhis life had been. It was Ahmed as he was she loved, his faults, his\r\nvices were as much a part of him as his superb physique and the\r\nalternating moods that had been so hard to meet. She had never known\r\nhim otherwise. He seemed to stand alone, outside the prescribed\r\nconventions that applied to ordinary men. The standards of common usage\r\ndid not appear compatible with the wild desert man who was his own law\r\nand followed only his own precedent, defiant of social essentials and\r\nscornful of criticism. The proud, fierce nature and passionate temper\r\nthat he had inherited, the position of despotic leadership in which he\r\nhad been reared, the adulation of his followers and the savage life in\r\nthe desert, free from all restraint, had combined to produce the\r\nhaughty unconventionalism that would not submit to the ordinary rules\r\nof life. She could not think of him as an Englishman. The mere accident\r\nof his parentage was a factor that weighed nothing. He was and always\r\nwould be an Arab of the wilderness. If he lived! He _must_ live!\r\nHe could not go out like that, his magnificent strength and fearless\r\ncourage extinguished by a treacherous blow that had not dared to meet\r\nhim face to face--in spite of the overwhelming numbers--but had struck\r\nhim down from behind, a coward stroke. He must live, even if his life\r\nmeant death to her hopes of happiness; that was nothing compared with\r\nhis life. She loved him well enough to sacrifice anything for him. If\r\nhe only lived she could bear even to be put out of his life. It was\r\nonly he that mattered, his life was everything. He was so young, so\r\nstrong, so made to live. He had so much to live for. He was essential\r\nto his people. They needed him. If she could only die for him. In the\r\ndays when the world was young the gods were kind, they listened to the\r\nprayers of hapless lovers and accepted the life that was offered in\r\nplace of the beloved whose life was claimed. If God would but listen to\r\nher now. If He would but accept her life in exchange for his. If----!\r\nif----!\r\n\r\nHer fingers crept up lightly across his breast, fearful lest even their\r\ntender touch should injure his battered body, and she looked long and\r\nearnestly at him. His crisp brown hair was hidden by the bandages that,\r\ndead white against his tanned face, swathed his bruised head. His\r\nclosed eyes with the thick dark lashes curling on his cheek, hiding the\r\nusual fierce expression that gleamed in them, and the relaxation of the\r\nhard lines of his face made him look singularly young. That youthful\r\nlook had been noticeable often when he was asleep, and she had watched\r\nit wondering what Ahmed the boy had been like before he grew into the\r\nmerciless man at whose hands she had suffered so much.\r\n\r\nAnd now the knowledge of his boyhood seemed to make him even dearer\r\nthan he had been before. What sort of man would he have been if the\r\nlittle dark-eyed mother had lived to sway him with her gentleness? Poor\r\nlittle mother, helpless and fragile!--yet strong enough to save her boy\r\nfrom the danger that she feared for him, but paying the price of that\r\nstrength with her life, content that her child was safe.\r\n\r\nDiana thought of her own mother dying in the arms of a husband who\r\nadored her, and then of the little Spanish girl slipping away from\r\nlife, a stranger in a strange land, her heart crying out for the\r\nhusband whom she still loved, turning in ignorance of his love for\r\nconsolation in the agony of death to the lover she had denied, and\r\nseeking comfort in his arms. A sudden jealousy of the two dead women\r\nshook her. They had been loved. Why could not she be loved? Wherein did\r\nshe fail that he would not love her? Other men had loved her, and his\r\nlove was all she longed for in the world. To feel his arms around her\r\nonly once with love in their touch, to see his passionate eyes kindle\r\nonly once with the light she prayed for. She drew a long sobbing\r\nbreath. "Ahmed, _mon bel Arabe_," she murmured yearningly.\r\n\r\nShe rose to her feet. She was afraid of breaking down, of giving way to\r\nthe fear and anxiety that racked her. She turned instinctively to the\r\nhelp and sympathy that offered and went to Saint Hubert, joining him\r\nunder the awning. Usually at night the vicinity of the Sheik\'s tent was\r\navoided by the tribesmen, even the sentry on guard was posted at some\r\nlittle distance. Kopec curled up outside the doorway kept ample watch.\r\nBut to-night the open space was swarming with men, some squatting on\r\nthe ground in circles, others clustered together in earnest\r\nconversation, and far off through the palm trees she caught an\r\noccasional glimpse of mounted men. Yusef and the headmen acting under\r\nhim were taking no risks, there was to be no chance of a surprise\r\nattack.\r\n\r\n"You must be very tired, Raoul," she said, slipping her hand through\r\nhis arm, for her need was almost as much for physical as mental\r\nsupport. The frank touch of her hand sent a quiver through him, but he\r\nsuppressed it, and laid his own hand over her cold fingers.\r\n\r\n"I must not think of that yet. Later on, perhaps, I can rest a little.\r\nHenri can watch; he is almost as good a doctor as I am, the\r\nincomparable Henri! Ahmed and I have always quarrelled over the\r\nrespective merits of our servants."\r\n\r\nHe felt her hand tighten on his arm at the mention of the Sheik\'s name\r\nand heard the smothered sigh that she choked back. They stood in\r\nsilence for a while watching the shifting groups of tribesmen. A little\r\nknot of low-voiced men near them opened up, and one of their number\r\ncame to Saint Hubert with an inquiry.\r\n\r\n"The men are restless." Raoul said when the Arab had gone back to his\r\nfellows with all the consolation the Vicomte could give him. "Their\r\ndevotion is very strong. Ahmed is a god to them. Their anxiety takes\r\nthem in a variety of ways. Yusef, who has been occupied with his duties\r\nmost of the day, has turned to religion for the first time in his life,\r\nhe has gone to say his prayers with the pious Abdul, as he thinks that\r\nAllah is more likely to listen if his petitions go heavenward in\r\ncompany with the holy man\'s."\r\n\r\nDiana\'s thoughts strayed back to the story that Saint Hubert had told\r\nher. "Does Lord Glencaryll know that you see Ahmed?" she asked.\r\n\r\n"Oh yes. He and my father became great friends. He often stays with us\r\nin Paris. We are a link between him and Ahmed. He is always hungry for\r\nany news of him, and still clings to the hope that one day he will\r\nrelent. He has never made any further effort to open up relations with\r\nhim because he knows it would be useless. If there is to be any\r\n_rapprochement_ between them it must come from Ahmed. They have\r\nalmost met accidentally once or twice, and Glencaryll has once seen\r\nhim. It was at the opera. He was staying in Paris for some months and\r\nhad a box. I had gone across from our own box on the other side of the\r\nhouse to speak to him. There were several people with him. I was\r\nstanding beside him, talking. Ahmed had just come into our box opposite\r\nand was standing right in the front looking over the theatre. Something\r\nhad annoyed him and he was scowling. The likeness was unmistakable.\r\nGlencaryll gave a kind of groan and staggered back against me. \'Good\r\nGod! Who is that?\' he said, and I don\'t think he knew he was speaking\r\nout loud.\r\n\r\n"A man next him looked in the direction he was looking and laughed.\r\n\'That\'s the Saint Huberts\' wild man of the desert. Looks fierce,\r\ndoesn\'t he? The women call him "_le bel Arabe_." He certainly\r\nwears European clothes with better grace than most natives. He is said\r\nto have a peculiar hatred of the English, so you\'d better give him a\r\nwide berth, Glencaryll, if you don\'t want to be bow-stringed or have\r\nyour throat cut, or whatever fancy form of death the fellow cultivates\r\nin his native habitat. Raoul can tell you all about him.\'\r\n\r\n"There was not any need for me to tell him. Fortunately the opera began\r\nand the lights went down, and I persuaded him to go away before the\r\nthing was over."\r\n\r\nDiana gave a little shiver. She felt a great sympathy coming over her\r\nfor the lonely old man, hoping against hope for the impossible, that\r\nshe had not felt earlier in the evening. He, too, was wearing his heart\r\nout against the inflexible will of Ahmed Ben Hassan.\r\n\r\nShe shivered again and turned back into the tent with Saint Hubert.\r\nThey halted by the couch and stood for a long time in silence. Then\r\nDiana slowly raised her head and looked up into Raoul\'s face, and he\r\nread the agonised question in her eyes.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t know," he said gently. "All things are with Allah."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\n\r\nThe night grew hotter and the atmosphere more oppressive. Wrapped in a\r\nthin silk kimono Diana lay very still on the outside of the wide couch\r\nin the inner room, propped high with pillows that the shaded light of\r\nthe little reading-lamp beside her might fall on the book she held, but\r\nshe was not reading.\r\n\r\nIt was Raoul\'s latest book, that he had brought with him, but she could\r\nnot concentrate her mind on it, and it lay idle on her knee--while her\r\nthoughts were far away. It was three months since the night that Saint\r\nHubert had almost given up hope of being able to save the Sheik\'s\r\nlife--a night that had been followed by days of suspense that had\r\nreduced Diana to a weary-eyed shadow of her former vigorous self, and\r\nhad left marks on Raoul that would never be effaced. But thanks to his\r\ngreat strength and splendid constitution the Sheik had rallied and\r\nafter the first few weeks convalescence had been rapid. When the\r\nterrible fear that he might die was past it had been a wonderful\r\nhappiness to wait on him. With the determination to live for the\r\nmoment, to which she had forced herself, she had banished everything\r\nfrom her mind but the joy of being near him and of being necessary to\r\nhim. It had been a very silent service, for he would lie for hours with\r\nclosed eyes without speaking, and something that she could not master\r\nkept her tongue-tied in his presence when they were alone. Only once he\r\nhad referred to the raid. As she bent over him to do some small office\r\nhis fingers closed feebly round her wrist and his eyes, with a\r\nsearching apprehension in them, looked into hers for the first time\r\nsince the night when she had fled from his curses.\r\n\r\n"Was it--in time?" he whispered slowly, and as she nodded with crimson\r\ncheeks and lowered eyes he turned his head away without another word,\r\nbut a shudder that he was too weak to control shook him.\r\n\r\nBut the happiness of ministering to him passed very swiftly. As he grew\r\nstronger he managed so that she was rarely alone with him, and he\r\ninsisted on her riding twice every day, sometimes with Saint Hubert,\r\nsometimes with Henri, coolly avowing a preference for his own society\r\nor that of Gaston, who was beginning to get about again. Later, too, he\r\nwas much occupied with headmen who came in from the different camps,\r\nand as the days passed she found herself more and more excluded from\r\nthe intimacy that had been so precious. She was thrown much into the\r\nsociety of Raoul de Saint Hubert. All that they had gone through\r\ntogether had drawn them very closely to each other, and Diana often\r\nwondered what her girlhood would have been like if it had been spent\r\nunder his guardianship instead of that of Sir Aubrey Mayo. The sisterly\r\naffection she had never given her own brother she gave to him, and,\r\nwith the firm hold over himself that he had never again slackened, the\r\nVicomte accepted the role of elder brother which she unconsciously\r\nimposed on him.\r\n\r\nIt was hard work sometimes, and there were days when he dreaded the\r\ndaily rides, when the strain seemed almost more than he could bear, and\r\nhe began to make tentative suggestions about resuming his wanderings,\r\nbut always the Sheik pressed him to stay.\r\n\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan\'s final recovery was quick, and the camp soon settled\r\ndown into normal conditions. The reinforcements were gone back to the\r\ndifferent camps from which they had been drawn. There was no further\r\nneed of them. Ibraheim Omair\'s tribe, with their leader dead, had\r\nbroken up and scattered far to the south; there was no chief to keep\r\nthem together and no headman strong enough to draw them round a new\r\nchieftain, for Ibraheim had allowed no member of his tribe to attain\r\nany degree of wealth or power that might prove him a rival; so they had\r\nsplit up into numerous small bands lacking cohesion. In fulfilling the\r\nvow made to his predecessor Ahmed Ben Hassan had cleared the desert of\r\na menace that had hung over it for many years.\r\n\r\nThe relations between the Sheik and Saint Hubert had gone back to what\r\nthey had been the night of Raoul\'s arrival, before his candid criticism\r\nhad roused the Sheik\'s temper and fired his jealousy. The recollection\r\nof the miserable week that had preceded the raid had been wiped out in\r\nall that had followed it. No shadow could ever come between them again\r\nsince Raoul had voluntarily stood on one side and sacrificed his own\r\nchance of happiness for his friend\'s.\r\n\r\nAnd with the Sheik\'s complete recovery his attitude towards Diana had\r\nreverted to the cold reserve that had chilled her before--a reserve\r\nthat was as courteous as it was indifferent. He had avoided her as much\r\nas had been possible, and the continual presence of Saint Hubert had\r\nbeen a barrier between them. Unostensibly but effectually he had\r\ncontrived that Raoul should never leave them alone. Though he included\r\nher in the general conversation he rarely spoke to her directly, and\r\noften she found him looking at her with his fierce eyes filled with an\r\nexpression that baffled her, and as each time the quick blood rushed\r\ninto her face his forehead drew together in the heavy frown that was so\r\ncharacteristic. During meals it was Raoul that kept the conversation\r\nfrom lapsing with ready tact and an eloquent flow of words, ranging\r\nover many subjects. In the evening the men became immersed in the\r\nprojection of Saint Hubert\'s new book, for details of which he was\r\ndrawing on the Sheik\'s knowledge, and long after Diana left them she\r\ncould hear the two voices, both deep and musical, but Raoul\'s quicker\r\nand more emphatic, continuously rising and falling, till at last Raoul\r\nwould go to his own tent and Gaston would come--noiseless and\r\nsoft-toned as his master. Ordinarily the Sheik dispensed with him at\r\nnight, but since his wound, the valet, as soon as he had himself\r\nrecovered, had always been in attendance. Some nights he lingered\r\ntalking, and others the Sheik dismissed him in a few minutes with only\r\na curt word or two, and then there would be silence, and Diana would\r\nbury her face in her pillow and writhe in her desperate loneliness,\r\nsick with longing for the strong arms she had once dreaded and the\r\nkisses she had once loathed. He had slept in the outer room since his\r\nillness, and tossing feverishly on the soft cushions of the big empty\r\nbed in which she lay alone Diana had suffered the greatest humiliation\r\nshe had yet experienced. He had never loved her, but now he did not\r\neven want her. She was useless to him. She was less than nothing to\r\nhim. He had no need of her. She would lie awake listening wearily to\r\nthe tiny chimes of the little clock with the bitter sense of her\r\nneedlessness crushing her. She was humbled to the very dust by his\r\nindifference. The hours of loneliness in the room that was redolent\r\nwith associations of him were filled with memories that tortured her.\r\nIn her fitful sleep her dreams were agonies from which she awakened\r\nwith shaking limbs and shuddering breath, and waking, her hand would\r\nstretch out groping to him till remembrance came with cruel vividness.\r\n\r\nIn the daytime, too, she had been much alone, for as soon as the Sheik\r\nwas strong enough to sit in the saddle the two men had ridden far\r\nafield every day, visiting the outlying camps and drawing into Ahmed\r\nBen Hassan\'s own hands again the affairs that had had to be relegated\r\nto the headmen.\r\n\r\nAt last Raoul had announced that his visit could be protracted no\r\nlonger and that he must resume his journey to Morocco. He was going up\r\nto Oran and from there to Tangier by coasting steamer, collecting at\r\nTangier a caravan for his expedition through Morocco. His decision once\r\nmade he had speeded every means of getting away with a despatch that\r\nhad almost suggested flight.\r\n\r\nTo Diana his going meant the hastening of a crisis that could not be\r\nput off much longer. The situation was becoming impossible. She had\r\nsaid good-bye to him the night before. She had never guessed the love\r\nshe had inspired in him, and she wondered at the sadness in his eyes\r\nand his unaccustomed lack of words. He had wanted to say so much and he\r\nhad said so little. She must never guess and Ahmed must never guess, so\r\nhe played the game to the end. Only that night after she had left them\r\nthe voices sounded in the adjoining room for a very short time. And\r\nthis morning he and Ahmed Ben Hassan had ridden away at daybreak. She\r\nhad not been asleep; she had heard them go, and almost she wished Raoul\r\nback, for with his presence the vague fear that assailed her seemed\r\nfurther away. The camp had seemed very lonely and the day very long.\r\n\r\nShe had ridden with Gaston, and hurried over her solitary dinner, and\r\nsince then she had been waiting for the Sheik to come back. In what\r\nmood would he come? Since Raoul\'s announcement of his departure he had\r\nbeen more than usually taciturn and reserved. The book she held slipped\r\nat length on to the floor, and she let it he unheeded. The usual\r\nstillness of the desert seemed to-night unusually still-sinister\r\neven--and the silence was so intense that the sudden squeal of a\r\nstallion a little distance away made her start with madly racing heart\r\nEarlier in the evening a tom-tom had been going persistently in the\r\nmen\'s lines, and later a native pipe had shrilled thinly in monotonous\r\ncadence; but she had grown accustomed to these sounds; they were of\r\nnightly occurrence and they soothed rather than irritated her, and when\r\nthey stopped the quiet had become intensified to such a degree that she\r\nwould have welcomed any sound. To-night her nerves were on edge. She was\r\nrestless and excited, and her thoughts were chaos.\r\n\r\nShe was alone again at his mercy. What would his attitude be? Her hands\r\nclenched on her knees. At times she lay almost without breathing,\r\nstraining to hear the faintest sound that would mean his return, and\r\nthen again lest she should hear what she listened for. She longed for\r\nhim passionately, and at the same time she was afraid, He had changed\r\nso much that there were moments when she had the curious feeling that\r\nit was a stranger who was coming back to her, and she both dreaded his\r\ncoming and yearned for it with a singular combination of emotions. She\r\nlooked round the room where she had at once suffered so much and been\r\nso happy with troubled eyes. She had never been nervous before, but\r\nto-night her imagination ran riot. There was electricity in the air\r\nwhich acted on her overstrung nerves. The little shaded lamp threw a\r\ncircle of light round the bed, but left the rest of the room dim, and\r\nthe dusky corners seemed full of odd new shadows that came and went\r\nillusively. Hangings and objects that were commonly familiar to her\r\ntook on fantastic shapes that she watched nervously, till at last she\r\nbrushed her hand across her eyes with a laugh of angry impatience. Was\r\nthe love that had changed her so completely also making her a coward?\r\nHad even her common-sense been lost in the one great emotion that held\r\nher? She understood perfectly the change that had taken place in her.\r\nShe had never had any illusions about herself, and had never attempted\r\nto curb the obstinate self-will and haughty pride that had\r\ncharacterized her. She thought of it curiously, her mind going back\r\nover the last few months that had changed her whole life. The last mad\r\nfreak for which she had paid so dearly had been the outcome of an\r\narrogant determination to have her own way in the face of all protests\r\nand advice. And with a greater arrogance and a determination stronger\r\nthan her own Ahmed Ben Hassan had tamed her as he tamed the magnificent\r\nhorses that he rode. He had been brutal and merciless, using no half\r\nmeasures, forcing her to obedience by sheer strength of will and\r\ncompelling a complete submission. She thought of how she had feared and\r\nhated him with passionate intensity, until the hatred had been swamped\r\nby love as passionate and as intense. She did not know why she loved\r\nhim, she had never been able to analyse the passion that held her so\r\nstrongly, but she knew deep down in her heart that it went now far past\r\nhis mere physical beauty and superb animal strength. She loved him\r\nblindly with a love that had killed her pride and brought her to his\r\nfeet humbly obedient. All the love that had lain dormant in her heart\r\nfor years was given to him. Body and soul she belonged to him. And the\r\nchange within her was patent in her face, the haughty expression in her\r\neyes had turned to a tender wistfulness, with a curious gleam of\r\nexpectancy that flickered in them perpetually; the little mutinous\r\nmouth had lost the scornful curve. And with the complete change in her\r\nexpression she was far more beautiful now than she had ever been. But\r\nwith her love was the fear of him that she had learned during the first\r\nhours of her captivity, the physical fear that she had never lost, even\r\nduring the happy weeks that had preceded the coming of Saint Hubert,\r\nand the greater fear that was with her always, and that at times drove\r\nher, with wide-stricken eyes, wildly to pace the tent as if to escape\r\nthe shadow that hung over her--the fear of the time when he should tire\r\nof her. The thought racked her, and now, as always, she tried to put it\r\nfrom her, but it continued, persistently haunting her like a grim\r\nspectre. Always the same thought tortured her--he had not taken her for\r\nlove. No higher motive than a passing fancy had stirred him. He had\r\nseen her, had wished for her and had taken her, and once in his power\r\nit had amused him to break her to his hand. She realised all that. And\r\nhe had been honest, he had never pretended to love her. Often when the\r\nhumour took him he could be gentle, as in those last few weeks, but\r\ngentleness was not love, and she had never seen the light that she\r\nlonged for kindle in his eyes. His caresses had been passionate or\r\ncareless with his mood. She did not know that he loved her. She had not\r\nbeen with him during the long hours of his delirium and she had not\r\nheard what Raoul de Saint Hubert had heard. And since his recovery his\r\nattitude of aloofness had augmented her fear. There seemed only one\r\nconstruction to put on his silence, and his studied and obvious\r\navoidance of her. The passing fancy had passed. It was as if the\r\nfleeting passion he had had for her had been drained from him with the\r\nblood that flowed from the terrible wound he had received. He was tired\r\nof her and seeking for a means to disembarrass himself of her. Vaguely\r\nshe felt that she had known this for weeks, but to-night was the first\r\ntime that she had had courage to be frank with herself. It must be so.\r\nEverything pointed to it; the curious expression she had seen in his\r\neyes and his constant heavy frown all confirmed it. She flung her arm\r\nacross her eyes with a little moan. He was tired of her and the bottom\r\nhad fallen out of her world. The instinct to fight for his love that\r\nhad been so strong in her the day that Ibraheim Omair had captured her\r\nhad died with the death of all her hopes. Her spirit was broken. She\r\nknew that her will was helpless against his, and with a fatalism that\r\nshe had learned in the desert she accepted the inevitable with a\r\ncrushed feeling of hopelessness.\r\n\r\nShe wondered numbly what would become of her. It did not seem to matter\r\nmuch. Nothing mattered now that he did not want her any more. The old\r\nlife was far away, in another world. She could never go back to it. She\r\ndid not care. It was nothing to her. It was only here in the desert, in\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan\'s arms, that she had become alive, that she had\r\nlearned what life really meant, that she had waked both to happiness\r\nand sorrow.\r\n\r\nThe future stretched out blank and menacing before her, but she turned\r\nfrom it with a great sob of despair. It was on him that her thoughts\r\nwere fixed. How would life be endurable without him? Dully she wondered\r\nwhy she did not hate him for having done to her what he had done, for\r\nhaving made her what she was. But nothing that he could do could kill\r\nthe love now that he had inspired. And she would never regret. She\r\nwould always have the memory of the fleeting happiness that had been\r\nhers--in after years that memory would be all that she would have to\r\nlive for. Even in her heart she did not reproach him, there was no\r\nbitterness in her misery. She had always known that it would come,\r\nthough she had fenced with it, shutting it out of her mind resolutely.\r\nHe had never led her to expect anything else. There was no link to\r\nbring them closer together, no bond between them. If she could have had\r\nthe promise of a child. Alone though she was the sensitive colour\r\nflamed into her cheeks, and she hid her face in the pillows with a\r\nquivering sob. A child that would be his and hers, a child--a boy with\r\nthe same passionate dark eyes, the same crisp brown hair, the same\r\ngraceful body, who would grow up as tall and strong, as brave and\r\nfearless as his father. Surely he must love her then. Surely the memory\r\nof his own mother\'s tragic history would make him merciful to the\r\nmother of his son. But she had no hope of that mercy. She lay shaking\r\nwith passionate yearning and the storm of bitter tears that swept over\r\nher, hungry for the clasp of his arms, faint with longing. The pent-up\r\nmisery of weeks that she had crushed down surged over. There was nobody\r\nto hear the agonising sobs that shook her from head to foot. She could\r\nrelax the control that she had put upon herself and which had seemed to\r\nbe slowly turning her to stone. She could give way to the emotion that,\r\nsuppressed, had welled up choking in her throat and gripped her\r\nforehead like red-hot bands eating into her brain. Tears were not easy\r\nto her. She had not wept since that first night when, with the fear of\r\nworse than death, she had grovelled at his feet, moaning for mercy. She\r\nhad not wept during the terrible hours she was in the power of Ibraheim\r\nOmair, nor during the days that Raoul de Saint Hubert had fought for\r\nhis friend\'s life. But to-night the tears that all her life she had\r\ndespised would not be denied. Tortured with conflicting emotions,\r\nunsatisfied love, fear and uncertainty, utterly unnerved, she gave\r\nherself up at last to the feelings she could no longer restrain. Prone\r\non the wide bed, her face buried in the pillows, her hands clutching\r\nconvulsively at the silken coverings, she wept until she had no more\r\ntears, until the anguished, sobs died away into silence and she lay\r\nquiet, exhausted.\r\n\r\nShe wrestled with herself. The weakness that she had given way to must\r\nbe conquered. She knew that, without any possibility of doubt, his\r\ncoming would seal her fate--whatever it was to be. She must wait until\r\nthen. A long, shuddering sigh ran through her. "Ahmed! Ahmed Ben\r\nHassan," she murmured slowly, lingering with wistful tenderness on the\r\nwords. She pressed her face closer into the cushions, clasping her\r\nhands over her head, and for a long time lay very still. The heat was\r\nintense and every moment the tent seemed to grow more airless. The room\r\nwas stifling, and, with a little groan, Diana sat up, pushing the heavy\r\nhair oft her damp forehead, and covered her flushed face with her\r\nhands. A cicada began its shrill note close by, chirping with maddening\r\npersistency. Quite suddenly her mind was filled with thoughts of her\r\nown people, the old home in England, the family for whose honour her\r\nancestors had been so proudly jealous. Even Aubrey, lazy and\r\nself-indulgent as he was, prized the family honour as he prized nothing\r\nelse on earth; and now she, proud Diana Mayo, who had the history of\r\nher race at her fingers\' ends, who had gloried in the long line of\r\nupright men and chaste women, had no thankfulness in her heart that in\r\nher degradation she had been spared a crowning shame. Beside her love\r\neverything dwindled into nothingness. He was her life, he filled her\r\nhorizon. Honour itself was lost in the absorbing passion of her love.\r\nHe had stripped it from her and she was content that it should lie at\r\nhis feet. He had made her nothing, she was his toy, his plaything,\r\nwaiting to be thrown aside. She shuddered again and looked around the\r\ntent that she had shared with him with a bitter smile and sad, hunted\r\neyes.... After her--who? The cruel thought persisted. She was torn with\r\na mad, primitive jealousy, a longing to kill the unknown woman who\r\nwould inevitably succeed her, a desire that grew until a horror of her\r\nown feelings seized her, and she shrank down, clasping her hands over\r\nher ears to shut out the insidious voice that seemed actually\r\nwhispering beside her. The Persian hound in the next room had whined\r\nuneasily from time to time, and now he pushed his way past the curtain\r\nand stalked across the thick rugs. He nuzzled his shaggy head against\r\nher knee, whimpering unhappily, looking up into her face. And when she\r\nnoticed him he reared up and flung his long body across her lap,\r\nthrusting his wet nose into her face. She caught his head in her hands\r\nand rubbed her cheek against his rough hair, crooning over him softly.\r\nEven the dog was comfort in her loneliness, and they both waited for\r\ntheir master.\r\n\r\nShe pushed him down at length, and with her hand on his collar went\r\ninto the other room. A solitary lamp burned dimly. She crossed to the\r\ndoorway and pulled aside the flap, and a small, white-clad figure rose\r\nup before her.\r\n\r\n"Is that you, Gaston?" she asked involuntarily, though she knew that\r\nthe question was unnecessary, for he always slept across the entrance\r\nto the tent when the Sheik was away.\r\n\r\n"_A votre service, Madame_."\r\n\r\nFor a few minutes she did not speak, and Gaston stood silent beside\r\nher. She might have remembered that he was there. He never stirred far\r\nbeyond the sound of her voice whenever she was alone in the camp. He\r\nwas always waiting, unobtrusive, quick to carry out her requests, even\r\nto anticipate them. With him standing beside her she thought of the\r\ntime when they had fought side by side--all difference in rank eclipsed\r\nin their common danger. The servant had been merged into the man, and a\r\nman who had the courage to do what he had attempted when he had faced\r\nher at what had seemed the last moment with his revolver clenched in a\r\nhand that had not shaken, a man at whose side and by whose hand she\r\nwould have been proud to die. They were men, these desert dwellers,\r\nmaster and servants alike; men who endured, men who did things, inured\r\nto hardships, imbued with magnificent courage, splendid healthy\r\nanimals. There was nothing effete or decadent about the men with whom\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan surrounded himself.\r\n\r\nDiana had always liked Gaston; she had been touched by his unvarying\r\nrespectful attitude that had never by a single word or look conveyed\r\nthe impression that he was aware of her real position in his master\'s\r\ncamp. He treated her as if she were indeed what from the bottom of her\r\nheart she wished she was. He was solicitous without being officious,\r\nfamiliar with no trace of impertinence, He was Diana\'s first experience\r\nof a class of servant that still lingers in France, a survival of\r\npre-Revolution days, who identify themselves entirely with the family\r\nthey serve, and in Gaston\'s case this interest in his master had been\r\nstrengthened by experiences shared and dangers faced which had bound\r\nthem together with a tie that could never be broken and had raised\r\ntheir relations on to a higher plane than that of mere master and man.\r\nThose relations had at first been a source of perpetual wonder to\r\nDiana, brought up in the rigid atmosphere of her brother\'s\r\nestablishment, where Aubrey\'s egoism gave no opportunity for anything\r\nbut conventional service, and in their wanderings, where personal\r\nservants had to be often changed. Even Stephens was, in Aubrey\'s eyes,\r\na mere machine.\r\n\r\nVery soon after she had been brought to Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s camp she had\r\nrealised that Gaston\'s devotion to the Sheik had been extended to\r\nherself, but since the night of the raid he had frankly worshipped her.\r\n\r\nIt was very airless even out-of-doors. She peered into the darkness,\r\nbut there was little light from the tiny crescent moon, and she could\r\nsee nothing. She moved a few steps forward from under the awning to\r\nlook up at the brilliant stars twinkling overhead. She had watched them\r\nso often from Ahmed Ben Hassan\'s arms; they had become an integral part\r\nof the passionate Oriental nights.
