He loved them, and when the mood was\r\non him, watched them untiringly, teaching her to recognise them, and\r\ntelling her countless Arab legends connected with them, sitting under\r\nthe awning far info the night, till gradually his voice faded away from\r\nher ears, and long after she was asleep he would sit on motionless,\r\nstaring up into the heavens, smoking endless cigarettes. Would it be\r\ngiven to her ever to watch them again sparkling against the\r\nblue-blackness of the sky, with the curve of his arm round her and the\r\nsteady beat of his heart under her cheek? A stab of pain went: through\r\nher. Would anything ever be the same again? Everything had changed\r\nsince the coming of Raoul de Saint Hubert. A weary sigh broke from her\r\nlips.\r\n\r\n"Madam is tired?" a respectful voice murmured at her ear.\r\n\r\nDiana started. She had forgotten the valet. "It is so hot. The tent was\r\nstifling," she said evasively.\r\n\r\nGaston\'s devotion was of a kind that sought practical demonstration.\r\n"_Madame veut du cafe?_" he suggested tentatively. It was his\r\nuniversal panacea, but at the moment it sounded almost grotesque.\r\n\r\nDiana felt an hysterical desire to laugh which nearly turned into\r\ntears, but she checked herself. "No, it is too late."\r\n\r\n"In one little moment I will bring it," Gaston urged persuasively,\r\nunwilling to give up his own gratification in serving her.\r\n\r\n"No, Gaston. It makes me nervous," she said gently.\r\n\r\nGaston heaved quite a tragic sigh. His own nerves were steel and his\r\ncapacity for imbibing large quantities of black coffee at any hour of\r\nthe day or night unlimited.\r\n\r\n"_Une limonade_?" he persisted hopefully.\r\n\r\nShe let him bring the cool drink more for his pleasure than for her\r\nown. "Monseigneur is late," she said slowly, straining her eyes again\r\ninto the darkness.\r\n\r\n"He will come," replied Gaston confidently. "Kopec is restless, he is\r\nalways so when Monseigneur is coming."\r\n\r\nShe looked down for a moment thoughtfully at the dim shape of the hound\r\nlying at the man\'s feet, and then with a last upward glance at the\r\nbright stars turned back into the tent. All her nervous fears had\r\nvanished in speaking to Gaston, who was the embodiment of practical\r\ncommon sense; earlier, when unreasoning terror had taken such a hold on\r\nher, she had forgotten that he was within call, faithful and devoted.\r\nShe picked up the fallen book, and lying down again forced herself to\r\nread, but though her eyes followed the lines mechanically she did not\r\nsense what she was reading, and all the time her ears were strained to\r\ncatch the earliest sound of his coming.\r\n\r\nAt last it came. Only a suggestion at first--a wave of thought caught\r\nby her waiting brain, an instinctive intuition, and she started up\r\ntense with expectancy, her lips parted, her eyes wide, hardly\r\nbreathing, listening intently. And when he came it was with unexpected\r\nsuddenness, for, in the darkness, the little band of horsemen were\r\ninvisible until they were right on the camp, and the horses\' hoofs made\r\nno sound. The stir caused by his arrival died away quickly. For a\r\nmoment there was a confusion of voices, a jingle of accoutrements, one\r\nof the horses whinnied, and then in the ensuing silence she heard him\r\ncome into the tent. Her heart raced suffocatingly. There was a murmur\r\nof conversation, the Sheik\'s low voice and Gaston\'s quick animated\r\ntones answering him, and then the servant hurried out. Acutely\r\nconscious of every sound, she waited motionless, her hands gripping the\r\nsoft mattress until her fingers cramped, breathing in long, painful\r\ngasps as she tried to stop the laboured beating of her heart. In spite\r\nof the heat a sudden coldness crept over her, and she shivered\r\nviolently from time to time. Her face was quite white, even her lips\r\nwere colourless and her eyes, fixed on the curtain which divided the\r\ntwo rooms, glittered feverishly. With her intimate knowledge every\r\nmovement in the adjoining room was as perceptible as if she had seen\r\nit. He was pacing up and down as he had paced on the night when\r\nGaston\'s fate was hanging in the balance, as he always paced when he\r\nwas deliberating anything, and the scent of his cigarette filled her\r\nroom. Once he paused near the communicating curtain and her heart gave\r\na wild leap, but after a moment he moved away. He stopped again at the\r\nfar end of the tent, and she knew from the faint metallic click that he\r\nwas loading his revolver. She heard him lay it down on the little\r\nwriting-table, and then the steady tramping began once more. His\r\nrestlessness made her uneasy. He had been in the saddle since early\r\ndawn. Saint Hubert had advised him to be careful for some weeks yet. It\r\nwas imprudent not to rest when opportunity offered. He was so careless\r\nof himself. She gave a quick, impatient sigh, and the tender light in\r\nher eyes deepened into an anxiety that was half maternal. In spite of\r\nhis renewed strength and his laughing protests at Raoul\'s warnings,\r\ncoupled with a physical demonstration on his less muscular friend that\r\nhad been very conclusive, she could never forget that she had seen him\r\nlying helpless as a child, too weak even to raise his hand. Nothing\r\ncould ever take the remembrance from her, and nothing could ever alter\r\nthe fact that in his weakness he had been dependent on her. She had\r\nbeen necessary to him then. She had a moment\'s fierce pleasure in the\r\nthought, but it faded as suddenly as it had come. It had been an\r\nephemeral happiness.\r\n\r\nAt last she heard the divan creak under his weight, but not until\r\nGaston came back bringing his supper. As he ate he spoke, and his first\r\nwords provoked an exclamation of dismay from the Frenchman, which was\r\nhastily smothered with a murmured apology, and then Diana became aware\r\nthat others had come into the room. He spoke to each in turn, and she\r\nrecognised Yusef\'s clear, rather high-pitched voice arguing with the\r\ntaciturn head camelman, whose surly intonations and behaviour matched\r\nthe bad-tempered animals to whom he was devoted, until a word from\r\nAhmed Ben Hassan silenced them both. There were two more who received\r\ntheir orders with only a grunt of acquiescence.\r\n\r\nPresently they went out, but Yusef lingered, talking volubly, half in\r\nArabic, half in French, but lapsing more and more into the vernacular\r\nas he grew excited. Even in the midst of her trouble the thought of him\r\nsent a little smile to Diana\'s lips. She could picture him squatting\r\nbefore the Sheik, scented and immaculate, his fine eyes rolling, his\r\nslim hands waving continually, his handsome face alight with boyish\r\nenthusiasm and worship. At last he, too, went, and only Gaston\r\nremained, busy with the _cafetiere_ that was his latest toy. The\r\naroma of the boiling coffee filled the tent. She could imagine the\r\nservant\'s deft fingers manipulating the fragile glass and silver\r\nappliance. She could hear the tinkle of the spoon as he moved the cup,\r\nthe splash of the coffee as he poured it out, the faint sound of the\r\ncup being placed on the inlaid table. Why was Ahmed drinking French\r\ncoffee when he always complained it kept him awake? At night he was in\r\nthe habit of taking the native preparation. Surely to-night he had need\r\nof sleep. It was the hardest day he had had since his illness. For a\r\nfew moments longer Gaston moved about the outer room, and from the\r\nsound Diana guessed that he was collecting on to a tray the various\r\nthings that had to be removed. Then his voice, louder than he had\r\nspoken before:\r\n\r\n_"Monseigneur desir d\'autre chose?"_\r\n\r\nThe Sheik must have signed in the negative, for there was no audible\r\nanswer.\r\n\r\n_"Bon soir, Monseigneur."_\r\n\r\n_"Bon soir, Gaston."_\r\n\r\nDiana drew a quick breath. While the man was still in the adjoining\r\nroom the moment for which she was waiting seemed interminable. And now\r\nshe wished he had not gone. He stood between her and--what? For the\r\nfirst time since the coming of Saint Hubert she was alone with him,\r\nreally alone. Only a curtain separated them, a curtain that she could\r\nnot pass. She longed to go to him, but she did not dare. She was pulled\r\nbetween love and fear, and for the moment fear was in the ascendant.\r\nShe shivered, and a sob rose in her throat as the memory came to her of\r\nanother night during those two months of happiness, that were fast\r\nbecoming like a wonderful dream, when he had ridden in late. After\r\nGaston left she had gone to him, flushed and bright-eyed with sleep,\r\nand he had pulled her down on to his knee, and made her share the\r\nnative coffee she detested, laughing boyishly at her face of disgust.\r\nAnd, holding her in his arms with her head on his shoulder, he had told\r\nher all the incidents of the day\'s visit to one of the other camps, and\r\nfrom his men and his horses drifted almost insensibly into details\r\nconnected with his own plans for the future, which were really the\r\nintimate confidences of a husband to a wife who is also a comrade. The\r\nmingled pain and pleasure of the thought had made her shiver, and he\r\nhad started up, declaring that she was cold, and, lifting her till his\r\ncheek was resting on hers, carried her back into the other room.\r\n\r\nBut what she had done then was impossible now. He seemed so utterly\r\nstrange, so different from the man whom she thought she had grown to\r\nunderstand. She was all at sea. She was desperately tired, her head\r\naching and confused with the terrible problems of the future. She dared\r\nnot think any more. She only wanted to lie in his arms and sob her\r\nheart out against his. She was starving for the touch of his hands,\r\nsuffering horribly.\r\n\r\nShe slid down on to her knees, burying her face in the couch.\r\n\r\n"Oh, God! Give me his love!" she kept whispering in agonised entreaty,\r\nuntil the recollection of the night, months before, when in the same\r\nposture she had prayed that God\'s curse might fall on him, sent a\r\nshudder through her.\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t mean if," she moaned. "Oh, clear God! I didn\'t mean it. I\r\ndidn\'t know.... Take it back. I didn\'t mean it."\r\n\r\nShe choked down the sobs that rose, pressing her face closer into the\r\nsilken coverings.\r\n\r\nThere was silence in the next room except for the striking of a match\r\nthat came with monotonous regularity. And always the peculiar scent of\r\nhis tobacco drifting in through the heavy curtains, forcing a hundred\r\nrecollections with the association of its perfume. Why didn\'t he come\r\nto her? Did he know how he was torturing her? Was he so utterly\r\nindifferent that he did not care what she suffered? Did he even think\r\nof her, to wonder if she suffered or not? The fear of the future rushed\r\non her again with overwhelming force. The uncertainty was killing her.\r\nShe raised her head and looked at the travelling clock beside the\r\nreading-lamp. It was an hour since Gaston had left him. Another hour of\r\nwaiting would drive her mad. She must know what he was going to do. She\r\ncould bear anything but this suspense. She had reached the limit of her\r\nendurance. She struggled to her feet, drawing the thin wrap closer\r\naround her. But even then she stood irresolute, dreading the fulfilling\r\nof her fears; she had not the courage voluntarily to precipitate her\r\nfate. She clung to her fool\'s paradise. Her eyes were fixed on the\r\nclock, watching the hands drag slowly round the dial. A quarter of an\r\nhour crept past. It seemed the quarter of a lifetime, and Diana brushed\r\nher hand across her eyes to clear away the dazzling reflection of the\r\nstaring white china face with its long black minute hand. No sound of\r\nany kind came now from the other room. The silence was driving her\r\nfrantic. She was desperate; she must know, nothing could be worse than\r\nthe agony she was enduring.\r\n\r\nShe set her teeth and, crossing the room, slipped noiselessly between\r\nthe curtains. Then she shrank back suddenly with her hands over her\r\nmouth. He was leaning forward on the divan, his elbows on his knees,\r\nhis face hidden in his hands. And it was as a stranger that he had come\r\nback to her, divested of the flowing robes that had seemed essentially\r\na part of him; an unfamiliar figure in silk shirt, riding breeches and\r\nhigh brown boots, still dust-covered from the long ride. A thin tweed\r\ncoat lay in a heap on the carpet--he must have flung it off after\r\nGaston went, for the valet, with his innate tidiness, would never have\r\nleft it lying on the floor.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him hungrily, her eyes ranging slowly over the long\r\nlength of him and lingering on his bent head. The light from the\r\nhanging lamp shone on his thick brown hair burnishing it like bronze.\r\nShe was shaking with a sudden new shyness, but love gave her courage\r\nand she went to him, her bare feet noiseless on the rugs.\r\n\r\n"Ahmed!" she whispered.\r\n\r\nHe lifted his head slowly and looked at her, and the sight of his face\r\nsent her on to her knees beside him, her hands clutching the breast of\r\nhis soft shirt.\r\n\r\n"Ahmed! What is it?... You are hurt--your wound----?" she cried, her\r\nvoice sharp with anxiety.\r\n\r\nHe caught her groping hands, and rising, pulled her gently to her feet,\r\nhis fingers clenched round hers, looking down at her strangely. Then he\r\nturned from her without a word, and wrenching open the flap of the\r\ntent, flung it back and stood in the open doorway staring out into the\r\nright. He looked oddly slender and tall silhouetted against the\r\ndarkness. A gleam of perplexity crept into her frightened eyes, and one\r\nhand went up to her throat.\r\n\r\n"What is it?" she whispered again breathlessly.\r\n\r\n"It is that we start for Oran to-morrow," he replied. His voice sounded\r\ndull and curiously unlike, and with a little start Diana realised that\r\nhe was speaking in English. Her eyes closed and she swayed dizzily.\r\n\r\n"You are sending me away?" she gasped slowly.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause before he answered.\r\n\r\n"Yes."\r\n\r\nThe curt monosyllable lashed her like a whip. She reeled under it,\r\npanting and wild-eyed. "Why?"\r\n\r\nHe did not answer and the colour flamed suddenly into her face. She\r\nwent closer to him, her breast heaving, trying to speak, but her throat\r\nwas parched and her lips shaking so that no words would come.\r\n\r\n"It is because you are tired of me?" she muttered at last hoarsely,\r\n"--as you told me you would tire, as you tired of--those other women?"\r\nHer voice died away with an accent of horror in it.\r\n\r\nAgain he did not answer, but he winced, and his hands that were hanging\r\nat his sides clenched slowly.\r\n\r\nDiana flung one arm across her face to shut him out from her sight. Her\r\nheart was breaking, and she longed with a feeling of sick misery to\r\ncrawl to his feet, but a remnant of pride kept her back.\r\n\r\nHe spoke at length in the same level, toneless voice. "I will take you\r\nto the first desert station outside of Oran, where you can join the\r\ntrain. For your own sake I must not be seen with you in Oran, as I am\r\nknown there. If you should by any chance be recognised or your identity\r\nshould leak out, you can say that for reasons of your own you extended\r\nyour trip, that your messages miscarried, anything that occurs to you.\r\nBut it is not at all likely to happen. There are many travellers\r\npassing through Oran. Gaston can do all business and make all\r\narrangements for you. He will take you to Marseilles, and if you need\r\nhim he will go with you to Paris, Cherbourg, or London--whichever you\r\nwish. As you know, you can trust him absolutely. When you do not need\r\nhim any longer, he will come back to me. I--I will not trouble you any\r\nmore. You need never be afraid that I will come into your life again.\r\nYou can forget these months in the desert and the uncivilised Arab who\r\ncrossed your path. To keep out of your way is the only amends I can\r\nmake."\r\n\r\nShe flung up her head. Quick, suspicious jealousy and love and pride\r\ncontending nearly choked her. "Why don\'t you speak the truth?" she\r\ncried wildly. "Why don\'t you say what you really mean?--that you have\r\nno further use for me, that it amused you to take me and torture me to\r\nsatisfy your whim, but the whim is passed. It does not amuse you any\r\nlonger. You are tired of me and so you get rid of me with all\r\nprecautions. Do you think the truth can hurt me? Nothing that you can\r\ndo can hurt me now. You made me the vile thing I am for your pleasure,\r\nand now for your pleasure you throw me on one side.... How many times a\r\nyear does Gaston take your discarded mistresses back to France?" Her\r\nvoice broke into a terrible laugh.\r\n\r\nHe swung round swiftly and flung his arms about her, crushing her to\r\nhim savagely, forgetting his strength, his eyes blazing. "God! Do you\r\nthink it is easy to let you go, that you are taunting me like this? Do\r\nyou think I haven\'t suffered, that I\'m not suffering now? Don\'t you\r\nknow that it is tearing my heart out by the roots to send you away? My\r\nlife will be hell without you. Do you think I haven\'t realised what an\r\ninfinitely damned brute I\'ve been? I didn\'t love you when I took you, I\r\nonly wanted you to satisfy the beast in me. And I was glad that you\r\nwere English that I could make you suffer as an Englishman made my\r\nmother suffer, I so loathed the whole race. I have been mad all my\r\nlife, I think--up till now. I thought I didn\'t care until the night I\r\nheard that Ibraheim Omair had got you, and then I knew that if anything\r\nhappened to you the light of my life was out, and that I would only\r\nwait to kill Ibraheim before I killed myself."\r\n\r\nHis arms were like a vice hurting her, but they felt like heaven, and\r\nshe clung to him speechless, her heart throbbing wildly. He looked down\r\nlong and deeply into her eyes, and the light in his--the light that she\r\nhad longed for--made her tremble. His brown head bent lower and lower,\r\nand his lips had almost touched her when he drew back, and the love in\r\nhis eyes faded into misery.\r\n\r\n"I mustn\'t kiss you," he said huskily, as he put her from him gently.\r\n"I don\'t think I should have the courage to let you go if I did. I\r\ndidn\'t mean to touch you."\r\n\r\nHe turned from her with a little gesture of weariness.\r\n\r\nFear fled back into her eyes. "I don\'t want to go," she whispered\r\nfaintly.\r\n\r\nHe paused by the writing-table and took up the revolver he had loaded\r\nearlier, breaking it absently, spinning the magazine between his finger\r\nand thumb, and replaced it before answering.\r\n\r\n"You don\'t understand. There is no other way," he said dully.\r\n\r\n"If you really loved me you would not let me go," she cried, with a\r\nmiserable sob.\r\n\r\n"_If_ I loved you?" he echoed, with a hard laugh. "_If_ I\r\nloved you! It is because I love you so much that I am able to do it. If\r\nI loved you a little less I would let you stay and take your chance."\r\n\r\nShe flung out her hands appealingly. "I want to stay, Ahmed! I love\r\nyou!" she panted, desperate--for she knew his obstinate determination,\r\nand she saw her chance of happiness slipping away.\r\n\r\nHe did not move or look at her, and his brows drew together in the\r\ndreaded heavy frown. "You don\'t know what you are saying. You don\'t\r\nknow what it would mean," he replied in a voice from which he had\r\nforced all expression. "If you married me you would have to live always\r\nhere in the desert. I cannot leave my people, and I am--too much of an\r\nArab to let you go alone. It would be no life for you. You think you\r\nlove me now, though God knows how you can after what I have done to\r\nyou, but a time would come when you would find that your love for me\r\ndid not compensate for your life here. And marriage with me is\r\nunthinkable. You know what I am and what I have been. You know that I\r\nam not fit to live with, not fit to be near any decent woman. You know\r\nwhat sort of a damnable life I have led; the memory of it would always\r\ncome between us--you would never forget, you would never trust me. And\r\nif you could, of your charity, both forgive and forget, you know that I\r\nam not easy to live with. You know my devilish temper--it has not\r\nspared you in the past, it might not spare you in the future. Do you\r\nthink that I could bear to see you year after year growing to hate me\r\nmore? You think that I am cruel now, but I am thinking what is best for\r\nyou afterwards. Some day you will think of me a little kindly because I\r\nhad the strength to let you go. You are so young, your life is only just\r\nbeginning. You are strong enough to put the memory of these last months\r\nout of your mind--to forget the past and live only for the future. No\r\none need ever know. There can be no fear for your--reputation. Things\r\nare forgotten in the silence of the desert. Mustafa Ali is many hundreds\r\nof miles away, but not so far that he would dare to talk. My own men\r\nneed not be considered, they speak or are silent as I wish. There is\r\nonly Raoul, and there is no question of him. He has not spared me his\r\nopinion. You must go back to your own country, to your own people, to\r\nyour own life, in which I have no place or part, and soon all this will\r\nseem only like an ugly dream."\r\n\r\nThe sweat was standing out on his forehead and his hands were clenched\r\nwith the effort he was making, but her head was buried in her hands,\r\nand she did not see the torture in his face, she only heard his soft,\r\nlow voice inexorably decreeing her fate and shutting her out from\r\nhappiness in quiet almost indifferent tones.\r\n\r\nShe shuddered convulsively. "Ahmed! I go!" she wailed.\r\n\r\nHe looked up sharply, his face livid, and tore her hands from her face.\r\n"Good God! You don\'t mean--I haven\'t--You aren\'t----" he gasped hoarsely,\r\nlooking down at her with a great fear in his eyes.\r\n\r\nShe guessed what he meant and the color rushed into her face. The\r\ntemptation to lie to him and let the consequences rest with the future\r\nwas almost more than she could resist. One little word and she would be\r\nin his arms ... but afterwards----? It was the fear of the afterwards\r\nthat kept her silent. The colour slowly drained from her face and she\r\nshook her head mutely.\r\n\r\nHe let go her wrists with a quick sigh of relief and wiped the\r\nperspiration from his face. Then he laid his hand on her shoulder and\r\npushed her gently towards the inner room. For a moment she resisted,\r\nher wide, desperate eyes searching his, but he would not meet her look,\r\nand his mouth was set in the hard straight line she knew so well, and\r\nwith a cry she flung herself on his breast, her face hidden against\r\nhim, her hands clinging round his neck. "Ahmed! Ahmed! You are killing\r\nme. I cannot live without you. I love you and I want you--only you. I\r\nam not afraid of the loneliness of the desert, it is the loneliness of\r\nthe world outside the shelter of your arms that I am afraid of. I am\r\nnot afraid of what you are or what you have been. I am not afraid of\r\nwhat you might do to me. I never lived until you taught me what life\r\nwas, here in the desert. I can\'t go back to the old life, Ahmed. Have\r\npity on me. Don\'t shut me out from my only chance of happiness, don\'t\r\nsend me away. I know you love me--I know! I know! And because I know I\r\nam not ashamed to beg you to be merciful. I haven\'t any shame or pride\r\nleft. Ahmed! Speak to me! I can\'t bear your silence.... Oh! You are\r\ncruel, cruel!"\r\n\r\nA spasm crossed his face, but his mouth set firmer and he disengaged\r\nher clinging hands with relentless fingers. "I have never been anything\r\nelse," he said bitterly, "but I am willing that you should think me a\r\nbrute now rather than you should live to curse the day you ever saw me.\r\nI still think that your greater chance of happiness lies away from me\r\nrather than with me, and for your ultimate happiness I am content to\r\nsacrifice everything."\r\n\r\nHe dropped her hands and turned abruptly, going back to the doorway,\r\nlooking out into the darkness. "It is very late. We must start early.\r\nGo and lie down," he said gently, but it was an order in spite of the\r\ngentleness of his voice.\r\n\r\nShe shrank back trembling, with piteous, stricken face and eyes filled\r\nwith a great despair. She knew him and she knew it was the end. Nothing\r\nwould break his resolution. She looked at him with quivering lips\r\nthrough a mist of tears, looked at him with a desperate fixedness that\r\nsought to memorise indelibly his beloved image in her heart. The dear\r\nhead so proudly poised on the broad shoulders, the long strong limbs,\r\nthe slender, graceful body. He was all good to look upon. A man of men.\r\nMonseigneur! Monseigneur! _Mon maitre et seigneur._ No! It would\r\nnever be that any more. A rush of tears blinded her and she stepped\r\nback uncertainly and stumbled against the little writing-table. She\r\ncaught at it behind her to steady herself, and her fingers touched the\r\nrevolver he had laid down. The contact of the cold metal sent a chill\r\nthat seemed to strike her heart. She stood rigid, with startled eyes\r\nfixed on the motionless figure in the doorway--one hand gripping the\r\nweapon tightly and the other clutching the silken wrap across her\r\nbreast. Her mind raced forward feverishly, there were only a few hours\r\nleft before the morning, before the bitter moment when she must leave\r\nbehind her for ever the surroundings that had become so dear, that had\r\nbeen her home as the old castle in England had never been. She thought\r\nof the long journey northward, the agonised protraction of her misery\r\nriding beside him, the nightly camps when she would lie alone in the\r\nlittle travelling tent, and then the final parting at the wayside\r\nstation, when she would have to watch him wheel at the head of his men\r\nand ride out of her life, and she would strain her eyes through the\r\ndust and sand to catch the last glimpse of the upright figure on the\r\nspirited black horse. It would be The Hawk, she thought suddenly. He\r\nhad ridden Shaitan to-day, and he always used one or other of the two\r\nfor long journeys. It was The Hawk he had ridden the day she had made\r\nher bid for freedom and who had carried the double burden on the return\r\njourney when she had found her happiness. The contrast between that\r\nride, when she had lain content in the curve of his strong arm, and the\r\nride that she would take the next day was poignant. She closed her\r\nteeth on her trembling lip, her fingers tightened on the stock of the\r\nrevolver, and a wild light came into her sad eyes. She could never go\r\nthrough with it. To what end would be the hideous torture? What was\r\nlife without him?--Nothing and less than nothing. She could never give\r\nherself to another man. She was necessary to no one. Aubrey had no real\r\nneed of her; his selfishness wrapped him around with a complacency that\r\nabundantly satisfied him. One day, for the sake of the family he would\r\nmarry--perhaps was already married if he had been able to find a woman\r\nin America who would accept his egoism along with his old name and\r\npossessions. Her life was her own to deal with. Nobody would be injured\r\nby its termination. Aubrey, indeed, would benefit considerably. And\r\nhe----? His figure was blurred through the tears that filled her eyes.\r\n\r\nSlowly she lifted the weapon clear of the table with steady fingers and\r\nbrought her hand stealthily from behind her. She looked at it for a\r\nmoment dispassionately. She was not afraid. She was conscious only of\r\nan overwhelming weariness, a longing for rest that should still the\r\ngnawing pain in her breast and the throbbing in her head.... A flash\r\nand it would be over, and all her sorrow would melt away.... But would\r\nit? A doubting fear of the hereafter rushed over her. What if suffering\r\nlived beyond the border-line? But the fear went as suddenly as it had\r\ncome, for with it came remembrance that in that shadowy world she would\r\nfind one who would understand--her own father, who had shot himself,\r\nmad with heartbroken despair, when her mother died in giving her birth.\r\n\r\nShe lifted the revolver to her temple resolutely.\r\n\r\nThere had been no sound to betray what was passing behind him, but the\r\nextra sense, the consciousness of imminent danger that was strong in\r\nthe desert-bred man, sprang into active force within the Sheik. He\r\nturned like a flash and leaped across the space that separated them,\r\ncatching her hand as she pressed the trigger, and the bullet sped\r\nharmlessly an inch above her head. With his face gone suddenly ghastly\r\nhe wrenched the weapon from her and flung it far into the night.\r\n\r\nFor a moment they stared into each other\'s eyes in silence, then, with\r\na moan, she slipped from his grasp and fell at his feet in an agony of\r\nterrible weeping. With a low exclamation he stooped and swept her up\r\ninto his arms, holding her slender, shaking figure with tender\r\nstrength, pressing her head against him, his cheek on her red-gold\r\ncurls.\r\n\r\n"My God! child, don\'t cry so. I can bear anything but that," he cried\r\nbrokenly.\r\n\r\nBut the terrible sobs went on, and fearfully he caught her closer,\r\nstraining her to him convulsively, raining kisses on her shining hair.\r\n"_Diane, Diane,_" he whispered imploringly, falling back into the\r\nsoft French that seemed so much more natural. "_Mon amour, ma\r\nbien-aimee. Ne pleures pas, je t\'en prie. Je t\'aime, je t\'adore. Tu\r\nresteras pres de moi, tout a moi._"\r\n\r\nShe seemed only half-conscious, unable to check the emotion that,\r\nunloosed, overwhelmed her. She lay inert against him, racked with the\r\nlong shuddering sobs that shook her. His firm mouth quivered as he\r\nlooked down at his work. Gathering her up to his heart he carried her\r\nto the divan, and the weight of her soft slim body sent the blood\r\nracing madly through his veins. He laid her down, and dropped on his\r\nknees beside her, his arm wrapped round her, whispering words of\r\npassionate love.\r\n\r\nGradually the terrible shuddering passed and the gasping sobs died\r\naway, and she lay still, so still and white that he was afraid. He\r\ntried to rise to fetch some restorative, but at the first movement she\r\nclung to him, pressing closer to him. "I don\'t want anything but you,"\r\nshe murmured almost inaudibly.\r\n\r\nHis arm tightened round her and he turned her face up to his. Her eyes\r\nwere closed and the wet lashes lay black against her pale cheek. His\r\nlips touched them pitifully.\r\n\r\n"Diane, will you never look at me again?" His voice was almost humble.\r\n\r\nHer eyes quivered a moment and them opened slowly, looking up into his\r\nwith a still-lingering fear in them. "You won\'t send me away?" she\r\nwhispered pleadingly, like a terrified child.\r\n\r\nA hard sob broke from him and he kissed her trembling lips fiercely.\r\n"Never!" he said sternly. "I will never let you go now. My God! If you\r\nknew how I wanted you. If you knew what it cost me to send you away.\r\nPray God I keep you happy. You know the worst of me, poor child--you\r\nwill have a devil for a husband."\r\n\r\nThe colour stole back slowly into her face and a little tremulous smile\r\ncurved her lips. She slid her arm up and round his neck, drawing his\r\nhead down. "I am not afraid," she murmured slowly. "I am not afraid of\r\nanything with your arms round me, my desert lover. Ahmed! Monseigneur!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE END\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of the Project Gutenberg EBook of The Sheik, by E. M.
